PICU QBank — Neuro MASTER — SAQ (300 items)

Format: Structured short-answer questions with key bullets, ICU targets, physiologic explanation, sources.

Difficulty: Intermediate + Hard SEAP level only.

Coverage: CPP physiology · BTF Tier 1 · Marshall CT · ESETT · THAPCA · Pediatric brain-death checklist · 20-30-40 rule · SIADH vs CSW · Anti-NMDA encephalitis · HIE MRI patterns · Decompressive craniectomy · Na correction · Sarnat staging · Therapeutic hypothermia trials · SCIWORA · HSV encephalitis · TB meningitis · ADEM IPMSSG criteria · MOGAD vs NMOSD · Lundberg waves · Cerebral microdialysis · Pyridoxine-dependent epilepsy · Lead encephalopathy · ANE.

SAQ 1 MODERATE
Define cerebral perfusion pressure (CPP) and list the age-stratified minimum targets in pediatric severe TBI per current BTF guidance. Explain the physiologic rationale for age stratification.
Show structured answer
Definition: CPP = MAP − ICP. Represents the net pressure gradient driving cerebral blood flow.
Age-stratified minimum targets (BTF 2019 / consensus):
  • Infants < 2 y: ≥ 40 mm Hg
  • Young children 2–6 y: ≥ 40–50 mm Hg
  • School-age 6–12 y: ≥ 50 mm Hg
  • Adolescents > 12 y: ≥ 60 mm Hg
Physiologic rationale:
  • Resting MAP and cerebral autoregulation curve shift rightward with maturation.
  • Cerebrovascular reactivity, autoregulation limits, and basal CBF differ developmentally (peak CBF at age 4–8 y).
  • Applying adult thresholds risks supraphysiologic CPP in younger children, with increased ARDS and hyperemic injury.
  • CPP target is a floor, not a goal: above target, additional vasopressor exposure does not improve outcome.
  • Modern direction: CPPopt (PRx-based individualization) when multimodal monitoring is available.
Source: Rogers Ch 62, Zimmerman Ch 118; Kochanek BTF 2019.
SAQ 2 MODERATE
List the components of BTF 2019 Tier 1 therapy for raised ICP in pediatric severe TBI. Include thresholds and targets.
Show structured answer
Tier 1 components — applied stepwise/concurrently when ICP > 20 mm Hg sustained > 5 min:
  • Adequate sedation and analgesia — opioid + sedative; avoid propofol > 4 mg/kg/h or > 48 h (PRIS).
  • Neuromuscular blockade if shivering, ventilator dyssynchrony, or stimulation-triggered ICP spikes.
  • CSF drainage via EVD when available.
  • Hyperosmolar therapy:
    • 3% saline 3–5 mL/kg bolus (or infusion to maintain Na 150–160; cap ≤ 170 and osm ≤ 360).
    • Mannitol 0.25–1 g/kg if euvolemic and adequate hemodynamics.
  • Head-of-bed 30°, neutral neck position, avoid jugular compression.
  • PaCO₂ 35–38 mm Hg (normocapnia).
  • Strict normothermia 36–37°C; treat fever aggressively.
  • Glucose 100–180 mg/dL (avoid hypo and hyperglycemia).
  • Hb ≥ 7 g/dL (higher if low PbtO₂).
  • cEEG: identify and treat seizures (non-convulsive common after TBI).
  • Age-stratified CPP target maintained with crystalloid + vasopressor as needed (avoid overshoot).
Targets: ICP < 20 mm Hg; CPP age-appropriate; Na 150–160; osm ≤ 360; PaCO₂ 35–38; T 36–37°C; glucose 100–180.
Source: Rogers Ch 62, Zimmerman Ch 118; Kochanek BTF 2019.
SAQ 3 HARD
Describe the Marshall CT classification for traumatic brain injury and outline the prognostic implications of each grade.
Show structured answer
GradeDefinitionPrognostic note
IDiffuse injury, no visible CT pathologyBest outcome among severe TBI; rule out non-visualized injuries
IICisterns present, midline shift 0–5 mm, no high/mixed density lesion > 25 mLIntermediate; mortality < 15%
IIIDiffuse injury with swelling — cisterns compressed or absent, shift 0–5 mmMarker for elevated ICP; worse outcome; mortality ~30%
IVDiffuse injury with shift > 5 mm, no mass > 25 mLWorse outcome; mortality > 50%
VEvacuated mass lesion (surgical)Outcome depends on residual injury; lower than VI
VINon-evacuated mass > 25 mL not surgicalWorst non-surgical category; high mortality
Physiologic interpretation:
  • Compressed basal cisterns indicate effacement of the perimesencephalic CSF space → elevated ICP, herniation risk.
  • Midline shift > 5 mm implies significant mass effect with secondary injury risk.
  • Pediatric extrapolation: not perfectly validated; pediatric-specific imaging (AHT subdurals, growing fracture) requires separate appraisal.
Source: Zimmerman Ch 118; Marshall 1991 (Journal of Neurosurgery).
SAQ 4 MODERATE
Summarize the ESETT trial design and findings. State the practice-changing conclusion and how it influences your choice of second-line agent for benzodiazepine-refractory convulsive status epilepticus in a pediatric ICU.
Show structured answer
  • Trial: ESETT (Established Status Epilepticus Treatment Trial), Kapur et al., NEJM 2019.
  • Design: Multicenter, blinded, response-adaptive randomization in adult and pediatric benzodiazepine-refractory convulsive status. Three arms: fosphenytoin 20 PE/kg, levetiracetam 60 mg/kg, valproate 40 mg/kg.
  • Primary outcome: Seizure cessation + improved responsiveness at 60 min.
  • Findings: Roughly half of patients responded; no statistically significant difference between agents in efficacy or safety.
  • Practice-changing conclusion: All three are acceptable second-line agents. Choice is individualized.
Practical algorithm in PICU:
  • Levetiracetam 60 mg/kg often preferred — rapid administration, fewer interactions, no cardiac monitoring required.
  • Fosphenytoin 20 PE/kg for known phenytoin responders; needs cardiac monitoring.
  • Valproate 40 mg/kg — avoid in mitochondrial disease, hepatic dysfunction, pancreatitis history, urea cycle disorders, neonates < 2 years (hepatotoxicity).
  • If first second-line fails, proceed to a second second-line agent before refractory therapy (midazolam infusion / pentobarbital / ketamine).
Source: Rogers Ch 64; Kapur NEJM 2019.
SAQ 5 MODERATE
Summarize the THAPCA-OH and THAPCA-IH trial findings and their impact on pediatric post-arrest temperature management.
Show structured answer
  • THAPCA-OH (Moler NEJM 2015): out-of-hospital pediatric cardiac arrest; n=295; hypothermia 33°C × 48 h vs normothermia 36.8°C × 48 h. No significant difference in 12-month survival with good functional outcome (VABS-II ≥ 70).
  • THAPCA-IH (Moler NEJM 2017): in-hospital pediatric cardiac arrest; n=329; identical design. Similar negative finding.
  • Conclusion: Either 33°C or 36.8°C is acceptable for pediatric post-arrest TTM. Active fever prevention is essential in both.
Practical PICU application:
  • Maintain temperature 33–37.5°C for at least 48 h then strict normothermia for 5 days total.
  • cEEG ≥ 24 h post-arrest.
  • Defer neuroprognostication until ≥ 72 h off sedation, multimodal data.
  • Avoid hyperthermia at all times — fever associated with worse outcomes.
Source: Rogers Ch 66; Moler NEJM 2015 / 2017; AHA 2019 PALS.
SAQ 6 HARD
Outline the pediatric brain-based death determination checklist for a 5-year-old child after severe TBI. Include prerequisites, examination, apnea test thresholds, and indications for ancillary testing.
Show structured answer
  • Prerequisites (all must be met):
    • Known catastrophic, irreversible brain injury consistent with brain death.
    • Core temperature ≥ 36°C.
    • Hemodynamic stability — age-appropriate MAP, off active titration ideally.
    • No reversible metabolic / endocrine derangement (glucose, Na, ammonia, hepatic, renal).
    • No CNS depressant drug effect — sedatives, paralytics cleared (TOF 4/4).
    • No acute electrolyte / acid-base derangement.
  • Examinations:
    • Two examinations by two independent qualified physicians.
    • Inter-exam interval: 12 hours (children 30 days–18 years); 24 hours (term newborns > 48 h to 30 days).
    • Coma with no spontaneous movement, no response to noxious stimuli (cranial nerve distribution).
    • Brainstem reflexes absent: pupillary, corneal, oculocephalic, oculovestibular (cold caloric), cough, gag.
  • Apnea test:
    • Pre-oxygenate FiO₂ 1.0; ensure hemodynamic stability and core temp ≥ 36°C.
    • Baseline PaCO₂ ~ 35–45.
    • Disconnect from ventilator with O₂ via T-piece or apneic CPAP.
    • Observe for spontaneous respiration for 8–10 min.
    • Positive (consistent with brain death) when: PaCO₂ ≥ 60 mm Hg AND ≥ 20 above baseline AND no respiratory effort.
    • Abort if hypoxemia, hypotension, dysrhythmia — proceed to ancillary testing.
  • Ancillary testing required when:
    • Clinical exam or apnea test cannot be safely completed.
    • Confounders persist.
    • Modalities: 4-vessel cerebral angiography, radionuclide cerebral blood flow imaging, CT angiography (where validated), MR angiography. EEG is no longer recommended as a primary ancillary test in current pediatric guidance.
Source: Rogers Ch 68; Shemie 2023 Canadian Brain-Based Death Guideline; Nakagawa 2011 pediatric guideline; Greer 2023 World Brain Death Project.
SAQ 7 MODERATE
List the bedside parameters that should prompt elective intubation in a child with progressive neuromuscular weakness (e.g., Guillain-Barré). Provide explicit thresholds and explain the underlying physiology.
Show structured answer
  • Forced vital capacity (FVC) < 20 mL/kg (or < 50% predicted, declining).
  • Maximal inspiratory pressure (NIF) less negative than –30 cmH₂O.
  • Maximal expiratory pressure (MEP) < 40 cmH₂O — impaired cough/secretion clearance.
  • Bulbar dysfunction — pooled secretions, dysphagia, aspiration risk.
  • Hypercapnia > 50 mm Hg on ABG (late sign).
  • Hypoxemia (very late in NMD).
  • Rapid clinical progression between examinations.
  • Autonomic instability (BP swings, paroxysmal bradycardia).
Physiologic explanation:
  • NMD respiratory failure begins with ventilation insufficiency (rising PaCO₂) before oxygenation fails.
  • Reduced MEP impairs cough → secretion retention, atelectasis, pneumonia.
  • Bulbar weakness causes aspiration and airway compromise.
  • Waiting for hypoxia delays intervention to a precarious phase; elective intubation in a controlled setting is preferred.
"20-30-40 rule": FVC < 20 mL/kg; NIF less negative than –30; MEP < 40.
Source: Rogers Ch 60.
SAQ 8 HARD
A 9-year-old POD2 from suprasellar surgery has hyponatremia (Na 122). Provide a structured comparison of SIADH and cerebral salt wasting (CSW), including volume status, urine indices, weight trajectory, and management.
Show structured answer
FeatureSIADHCSW
MechanismInappropriate ADH secretionCNS-mediated natriuresis (ANP/BNP excess)
Volume statusEuvolemic / mildly hypervolemicHypovolemic
WeightStable or increasedDecreased
Urine outputLow to normalHigh (polyuria)
Urine NaElevated (often > 40)Markedly elevated
Urine osmolarityInappropriately concentratedElevated
Serum uric acidLowLow / normal
HematocritStableHemoconcentration
BUNNormal/lowOften elevated
TreatmentFluid restriction; 3% saline if symptomatic; avoid free waterVolume + sodium replacement (isotonic / hypertonic saline); never restrict fluids
Sodium correction rules:
  • Symptomatic severe hyponatremia (seizures, coma, Na < 125 with neuro symptoms): 3% saline 3–5 mL/kg bolus to raise Na 4–6 mmol/L acutely.
  • Asymptomatic: slow correction; rate < 8–10 mmol/L per 24 h to avoid osmotic demyelination.
  • Monitor Na q2–4h initially; daily weights, strict I/O, urine indices.
Source: Rogers Ch 61; Zimmerman Ch 59 supplement.
SAQ 9 HARD
Outline the clinical features, CSF findings, MRI patterns, antibody profile, and treatment for pediatric anti-NMDA receptor encephalitis.
Show structured answer
  • Clinical phases:
    • Prodrome — viral-like illness.
    • Psychiatric / behavioral — psychosis, agitation, hallucinations.
    • Movement disorder — orofacial dyskinesias, dystonia, choreoathetosis.
    • Seizures — focal or generalized.
    • Autonomic dysfunction — labile BP/HR, hyperthermia, hypoventilation.
    • Decreased level of consciousness, catatonia.
  • CSF: Lymphocytic pleocytosis, mildly elevated protein, oligoclonal bands; CSF anti-NMDA receptor (NR1 subunit) antibodies confirmatory (often higher titer in CSF than serum).
  • MRI: Often unremarkable; sometimes hippocampal T2/FLAIR hyperintensity or cortical/subcortical changes.
  • EEG: Slowing; "extreme delta brush" pattern is characteristic.
  • Oncology screen: Pelvic ultrasound / CT/MRI for ovarian teratoma (up to 50% of post-pubertal female cases); testicular exam in boys.
  • First-line immunotherapy:
    • IV methylprednisolone 30 mg/kg/day × 5 days.
    • IVIG 2 g/kg total over 5 days.
    • Plasma exchange (alternative or combined).
    • Removal of teratoma if present (curative in many cases).
  • Second-line: Rituximab and/or cyclophosphamide if refractory at 10–14 days.
  • Supportive PICU care: Airway protection, seizure control, autonomic management, sedation for dyskinesias (avoid antipsychotics — worsen extrapyramidal symptoms), nutrition.
Source: Zimmerman Ch 67 supplement; Titulaer Lancet Neurology 2013.
SAQ 10 MODERATE
Describe the major MRI patterns of injury in pediatric hypoxic-ischemic encephalopathy (HIE) and their prognostic implications.
Show structured answer
  • Watershed pattern (border-zone) — cortex and subcortical white matter in arterial border zones.
    • Typical of partial prolonged asphyxia.
    • Variable cognitive outcome; relatively better motor function preservation.
  • Basal ganglia / thalamic (deep grey) pattern — bilateral involvement of posterior putamen, ventrolateral thalamus, perirolandic cortex, posterior limb of internal capsule (PLIC).
    • Typical of acute profound asphyxia.
    • Severe motor impairment (dyskinetic CP); poor outcome.
    • Loss of normal PLIC signal is a key marker.
  • Total / global pattern — diffuse involvement of cortex, deep grey, and white matter.
    • Severe injury; worst prognosis.
  • Imaging timing:
    • DWI restriction peaks 3–5 days; pseudonormalization by 7–10 days.
    • MR spectroscopy: lactate peak predicts adverse outcome.
    • Conventional T1/T2 changes evolve later.
Source: Rogers Ch 66; Miller Pediatrics 2005; Barkovich classification.
SAQ 11 MODERATE
List the indications and contraindications for decompressive craniectomy in pediatric severe TBI. Note where pediatric evidence diverges from adult evidence.
Show structured answer
  • Indications:
    • Refractory intracranial hypertension despite optimized BTF tier-1 therapy.
    • Mass lesion with midline shift requiring evacuation (always craniectomy if swelling expected).
    • Diffuse swelling with effaced cisterns and rising ICP.
    • Early consideration in pediatric AHT and unilateral mass lesions.
    • Pediatric data (Taylor 2001 — small RCT) supports early decompression in selected pediatric patients with improved outcomes.
  • Contraindications / relative:
    • Fixed dilated pupils for prolonged time with no chance of recovery.
    • Catastrophic brain injury with brainstem damage.
    • Severe coagulopathy uncorrected.
    • Persistent uncontrollable hypotension.
  • Pediatric vs adult evidence:
    • Adult DECRA (Cooper 2011) showed worse outcomes with bifrontal craniectomy at the lower threshold.
    • Adult RESCUEicp (Hutchinson 2016) showed reduced mortality but increased severe disability at higher threshold.
    • Pediatric data are more favorable, possibly due to greater plasticity and lower baseline mortality.
  • Operative considerations:
    • Wide hemicraniectomy preferred (frontotemporoparietal); minimum 12 cm diameter in adolescents.
    • Duraplasty with cranial flap stored sterile or in abdominal subcutaneous pocket.
    • Cranioplasty at 3–6 months when swelling resolves.
Source: Rogers Ch 62, Zimmerman Ch 118; Taylor 2001; Cooper DECRA 2011; Hutchinson RESCUEicp 2016.
SAQ 12 HARD
Outline the principles and limits of sodium correction for symptomatic hyponatremia in a child with status epilepticus due to acute hyponatremia (Na 118).
Show structured answer
  • Immediate goal: Stop seizures by raising Na 4–6 mmol/L acutely with hypertonic saline.
  • 3% saline dosing: 3–5 mL/kg bolus over 10–20 min (raises Na ~ 3–5 mmol/L). May repeat once.
  • Ongoing correction:
    • Do not exceed 8–10 mmol/L rise per 24 h to avoid osmotic demyelination syndrome (ODS / central pontine myelinolysis).
    • Reduce rate further (max 6 mmol/L) in patients at higher risk: chronic hyponatremia, malnutrition, alcohol use, liver disease, hypokalemia.
  • Monitoring:
    • Check Na q2h until stable, then q4–6h.
    • Hourly UOP; daily weight; CVP if line available.
    • Reassess underlying cause (SIADH, CSW, iatrogenic, water intoxication).
  • Pitfalls:
    • Rapid correction risks ODS — delayed quadriparesis, dysphagia, coma; treat with re-lowering Na if overcorrected.
    • Hypotonic IV fluids in injured brain — discontinue D5W/0.45 NS.
    • Failing to identify and reverse underlying cause (water intoxication, SIADH-inducing drug).
Targets: Acute rise 4–6 mmol/L for seizure cessation; maintain < 8–10 mmol/L per 24 h; halt correction once neurologic symptoms resolve.
Source: Rogers electrolyte / neuro endocrine chapters; expert consensus (Sterns 2015; AACE/ACE 2013).
SAQ 13 MODERATE
Describe the Sarnat staging of neonatal hypoxic-ischemic encephalopathy and explain how the stage influences treatment and prognosis.
Show structured answer
StageLevel of consciousnessToneReflexesSeizuresEEG / Outcome
I (mild)HyperalertNormal / mildly increasedBrisk Moro & tendon reflexes; sympathetic predominanceRareNormal EEG; generally good outcome; cooling not indicated
II (moderate)Lethargic / obtundedHypotonicDiminished Moro and tendon; parasympathetic predominanceCommon, often within 24 hEEG: discontinuous/voltage-suppressed; cooling indicated; intermediate prognosis — therapeutic hypothermia improves outcome
III (severe)ComatoseFlaccidAbsentUncommon (suppressed)EEG: severely abnormal, burst-suppression, isoelectric; cooling considered; poor prognosis
Clinical implication:
  • Stage 2 (moderate) has the most robust evidence base for therapeutic hypothermia benefit.
  • Stage 1 generally has good spontaneous recovery without cooling.
  • Stage 3 has high mortality; cooling considered but benefit less robust.
  • Continuous video EEG (or amplitude-integrated EEG) is essential to detect subclinical seizures, which are very common in moderate–severe HIE.
Practical targets:
  • Begin cooling within 6 hours of birth (or insult).
  • Target temperature 33–34°C × 72 h.
  • Slow rewarming ≤ 0.5°C/h.
  • Strict normothermia during and after rewarming.
Source: Rogers Ch 66; Sarnat 1976 original description; AAP / AHA guidance.
SAQ 14 MODERATE
Summarize the three landmark trials supporting therapeutic hypothermia in moderate-to-severe neonatal HIE. State the magnitude of benefit and the practical implementation steps.
Show structured answer
  • CoolCap (Gluckman, Lancet 2005): n=234; selective head cooling vs standard care for moderate-to-severe HIE. Reduced death/severe disability at 18 months in moderate (not severe) HIE.
  • NICHD whole-body cooling (Shankaran, NEJM 2005): n=208; whole-body hypothermia 33.5°C × 72 h. Reduced death and moderate-to-severe disability at 18–22 months (RR ~0.72).
  • TOBY (Azzopardi, NEJM 2009): n=325; whole-body hypothermia 33.5°C × 72 h. Improved survival without neurologic abnormality at 18 months; long-term follow-up at 6–7 y showed improved cognitive outcomes.
  • Meta-analyses (Edwards 2010; Tagin 2012): pooled NNT to prevent one death or major disability ≈ 7–9.
Implementation criteria (general):
  • ≥ 36 weeks gestational age.
  • Within 6 hours of birth/insult.
  • Evidence of moderate–severe encephalopathy (Sarnat 2/3 OR clinical features + abnormal aEEG).
  • Evidence of perinatal hypoxic-ischemic insult (Apgar ≤ 5 at 10 min, cord/early gas pH ≤ 7.0 or base deficit ≥ 16, or need for prolonged resuscitation).
Mechanism of neuroprotection:
  • Reduces cerebral metabolic rate (5–7% per 1°C drop).
  • Attenuates excitotoxic cascade (NMDA/AMPA, Ca²⁺ overload).
  • Reduces ROS, lipid peroxidation, apoptosis.
  • Stabilizes BBB and reduces edema.
Source: Rogers Ch 66; Gluckman 2005; Shankaran 2005; Azzopardi 2009; Tagin 2012 meta-analysis.
SAQ 15 HARD
Define SCIWORA. Explain the pediatric anatomic basis for its higher incidence in children compared with adults, and outline the diagnostic and management approach.
Show structured answer
  • Definition: Spinal Cord Injury Without Radiographic Abnormality — cord injury with neurologic findings but no fracture or dislocation on plain radiographs or CT (commonly extended to include normal MRI vertebral imaging; however, MRI typically shows cord signal abnormality).
  • Pediatric anatomic predisposition:
    • Disproportionately large head + weak neck musculature → high mechanical lever arm.
    • Ligamentous laxity allows transient cord stretch beyond vertebral injury threshold.
    • Horizontal facet joints permit translation.
    • Wedge-shaped vertebrae and incomplete ossification → flexibility without bony injury.
    • Upper cervical involvement most common (< 8 years: predominantly C1–C4; > 8 years: C5–C7 more common).
  • Diagnostic approach:
    • Maintain cervical immobilization until cleared (clinical + imaging).
    • MRI of cervical spine — gold standard; identifies cord edema, contusion, hemorrhage, ligamentous injury.
    • CT to exclude fracture (poor sensitivity for cord and ligamentous injury alone).
    • Neurologic exam serially; document ASIA grading.
    • Imaging may be normal at presentation despite clear cord injury.
  • Management:
    • Immobilization for several weeks (rigid collar, often 8–12 weeks) given risk of delayed deterioration.
    • Hemodynamic management — MAP target ≥ 85–90 mm Hg for 5–7 days to optimize cord perfusion (extrapolated from adult SCI).
    • Avoid hypothermia < 36°C in the acute spinal cord injury patient (no proven neuroprotective benefit in pediatric SCI).
    • Steroids: methylprednisolone NOT recommended in current pediatric SCI guidance (extrapolated from NASCIS controversies and lack of pediatric evidence).
    • Surgical: consultation with pediatric spine for instability assessment.
    • Anticipate progressive deterioration in first 4 days; rehabilitation early.
Source: Rogers Ch 62; AANS/CNS pediatric SCI guidelines; Pang 2004 SCIWORA review.
SAQ 16 MODERATE
Describe HSV encephalitis in children: typical clinical features, CSF findings, MRI pattern, diagnostic confirmation, treatment, and prognosis.
Show structured answer
  • Clinical: Fever, headache, altered mental status, focal seizures (especially temporal-lobe origin), behavioral change, focal deficits. Neonates: more diffuse and severe presentation.
  • CSF: Lymphocytic pleocytosis (10–500), elevated RBC count (hemorrhagic component), elevated protein, normal glucose. May be initially normal in first 24–48 h.
  • MRI: Unilateral or asymmetric bilateral T2/FLAIR hyperintensity of temporal lobes, insula, and limbic structures; hemorrhagic component on SWI/GRE.
  • Diagnostic confirmation: CSF HSV PCR (HSV-1 and HSV-2). Sensitivity is high but may be negative very early (first 24–48 h); repeat if clinical suspicion persists.
  • Treatment: IV acyclovir at higher pediatric doses (15 mg/kg q8h for ages 3 months–12 years; 10 mg/kg q8h > 12 years; neonatal doses higher 20 mg/kg q8h) × 21 days. Initiate empirically before PCR confirmation. Monitor renal function (acyclovir crystal nephropathy); maintain hydration. Foscarnet for acyclovir-resistant strains.
  • Adjunctive considerations: Corticosteroids debated (may help cerebral edema; meta-analyses suggest possible benefit). Antiseizure prophylaxis (levetiracetam) for cortical involvement. ICP management for raised ICP.
  • Prognosis: Mortality 5–15% with treatment (untreated > 70%). Long-term sequelae common: epilepsy, behavioral/cognitive impairment. Outcome best with early treatment.
  • Complication: NMDA receptor encephalitis can develop weeks to months post-HSV encephalitis (post-HSV anti-NMDA syndrome).
Source: Zimmerman Ch 67 supplement; IDSA encephalitis guidelines; pediatric HSV trial data.
SAQ 17 HARD
Describe the management of pediatric tuberculous meningitis (TBM), including drug regimen, duration, role of steroids, and management of complications.
Show structured answer
  • Initial 4-drug intensive phase (2 months):
    • Isoniazid 10–15 mg/kg/day (with pyridoxine to prevent peripheral neuropathy).
    • Rifampin 10–20 mg/kg/day.
    • Pyrazinamide 30–40 mg/kg/day.
    • Plus either ethambutol 15–20 mg/kg/day OR streptomycin 20–40 mg/kg/day IM.
  • Continuation phase (7–10 months): isoniazid + rifampin (total duration 9–12 months for TBM).
  • Adjunctive corticosteroids: Dexamethasone 0.4 mg/kg/day IV initially, tapered over 6–8 weeks (or prednisolone equivalent). Improves survival (Thwaites NEJM 2004) and reduces sequelae.
  • Drug resistance: Screen for MDR-TB; add or substitute second-line agents (linezolid, fluoroquinolones, bedaquiline in select cases) when resistance.
  • Hydrocephalus:
    • Common in pediatric TBM (basal cisternal exudate impairs CSF reabsorption / outflow).
    • External ventricular drain emergently if obstructive or symptomatic communicating; VP shunt or endoscopic third ventriculostomy for definitive treatment if persistent.
  • Vascular complications: Cerebral infarcts (most often basal ganglia/internal capsule from inflammatory vasculopathy of perforators). Manage with anticoagulation in select cases; aspirin not routine.
  • Other complications: Cranial neuropathies (especially VI, III), endocrine (SIADH, panhypopituitarism, central DI), seizures.
  • HIV co-infection: Anti-retroviral therapy timing balanced against IRIS risk; typically start ART 4–8 weeks after starting TBM treatment.
Source: Zimmerman Ch 67 supplement; WHO TBM consensus; Thwaites NEJM 2004.
SAQ 18 MODERATE
Outline the diagnostic criteria for pediatric ADEM (International Pediatric MS Study Group, IPMSSG 2013) and contrast with first-attack pediatric multiple sclerosis.
Show structured answer
  • ADEM (IPMSSG 2013) requires ALL:
    • First multifocal, clinical CNS event with presumed inflammatory demyelinating cause.
    • Encephalopathy (alteration in consciousness, behavior, or cognition beyond what would be expected from fever, illness).
    • MRI findings consistent with demyelination — large (> 1–2 cm), poorly demarcated, asymmetric lesions; often bilateral; can involve subcortical and central white matter, deep gray matter, brainstem, spinal cord.
    • No new clinical and MRI findings > 3 months after symptom onset.
  • Distinguishing pediatric MS first attack:
    • Typically > 10 years old.
    • Often LACKS encephalopathy.
    • MRI: smaller, well-demarcated, ovoid lesions; periventricular, Dawson fingers; older "T1 black holes" suggest prior episodes.
    • CSF oligoclonal bands more commonly positive in MS.
    • Recurrent attacks (clinically or radiographically) ultimately diagnostic.
  • Anti-MOG antibody disease overlaps with ADEM phenotype: serum/CSF MOG-IgG positivity confers a distinct disease course; may relapse.
  • Treatment of ADEM:
    • IV methylprednisolone 30 mg/kg/day (max 1 g) × 3–5 days, then oral taper 4–6 weeks.
    • IVIG 2 g/kg total over 2–5 days for refractory cases.
    • Plasma exchange for severe refractory cases.
  • Prognosis: Generally favorable in monophasic ADEM (~70–80% recovery); 10–20% may evolve to MS or relapsing demyelinating disease.
Source: Zimmerman Ch 67 supplement; Krupp IPMSSG 2013 consensus.
SAQ 19 HARD

SAQ 20 HARD
Describe the Lundberg ICP wave classification. State the morphology, duration, clinical significance, and management implication of each type.
Show structured answer
WaveMorphology / DurationSignificanceManagement
A waves (plateau)Sustained elevation to 50–100 mm Hg, lasting 5–20 minutes, with abrupt onset and resolutionPathological. Reflect severely impaired compensatory reserve. May herald herniation. Indicate cerebrovascular vasodilation in a pressure-passive territory.Treat urgently. Optimize Tier 1: sedation, CSF drainage, hyperosmolar, normocapnia. Consider Tier 2 (decompressive craniectomy, barbiturate coma) if recurrent.
B wavesOscillations of 10–20 mm Hg occurring 0.5–2 cycles/minLess specific. May reflect impaired autoregulation or unstable vasomotor tone. May precede A waves.Monitor closely; consider optimizing CPP, sedation, ventilation; assess autoregulation (PRx).
C wavesLow-amplitude oscillations (~ 6 cycles/min, ~ 4–8 mm Hg)Often normal physiologic (Mayer wave–related) variation in arterial blood pressure transmitted to ICP. Not pathologic alone.None specific; ensure baseline ICP not elevated.
Underlying mechanisms:
  • A waves require both reduced compensatory reserve and intact (possibly dysregulated) cerebrovascular response — vasodilation increases CBV → ICP spike.
  • B waves linked to respiratory and vasomotor oscillations.
  • C waves linked to systemic blood pressure oscillations.
Source: Rogers Ch 59 / Zimmerman Ch 63 supplement; Lundberg 1960 classic description.
SAQ 21 HARD
Describe cerebral microdialysis monitoring in neurocritical care: principles, key analytes, normal and pathologic values, and clinical interpretation patterns.
Show structured answer
  • Principle: A catheter with a semipermeable membrane is placed in brain parenchyma. Perfusate flows through the catheter; small molecules in the interstitial fluid diffuse across the membrane and are collected for analysis.
  • Key analytes:
    • Glucose — energy substrate; reduced in hypoperfusion, increased metabolic demand.
    • Lactate — anaerobic metabolism byproduct.
    • Pyruvate — glycolysis intermediate; aerobic pathway proxy.
    • Lactate/pyruvate (L/P) ratio — gold-standard marker of aerobic vs anaerobic metabolism.
    • Glutamate — excitotoxicity marker.
    • Glycerol — membrane-breakdown marker.
  • Normal values (approximate):
    • Glucose 1.0–1.5 mmol/L (lower than serum due to interstitial dilution).
    • Lactate < 4 mmol/L.
    • L/P ratio 15–25.
    • Glutamate < 10 µmol/L.
    • Glycerol < 80 µmol/L.
  • Pattern interpretation:
    PatternL/PPyruvateGlucoseInterpretation
    Ischemia↑ (> 40)↓ or normal↓Cellular energy failure; consider raising CPP, optimizing Hb, normocapnia
    Mitochondrial dysfunction↑NormalNormalMitochondrial failure with adequate substrate; difficult to reverse
    HyperglycolysisNormal↑↑Increased metabolic demand without ischemia
    Glutamate excitotoxicityVariableVariableVariableGlutamate ↑; consider NMDA antagonism rationale
    Membrane injuryVariableVariableVariableGlycerol ↑; reflects cell death/lipid peroxidation
  • Clinical use: Adjunctive to ICP/CPP/PbtO₂; integrates metabolic state with hemodynamics and oxygenation.
  • Limitations: Probe location matters (peri-lesion vs uninjured cortex); slow analysis cycle; not all centers have access.
Source: Zimmerman Ch 63 supplement; Hutchinson microdialysis consensus 2015.
SAQ 22 MODERATE
Describe pyridoxine-dependent epilepsy: genetic basis, clinical presentation, biochemical markers, diagnostic trial, and long-term management.
Show structured answer
  • Genetic basis: Autosomal recessive ALDH7A1 mutations (antiquitin deficiency). Antiquitin enzyme normally degrades a metabolite in lysine catabolism (α-aminoadipic semialdehyde, α-AASA); its accumulation inactivates pyridoxal-5'-phosphate (active vitamin B6).
  • Clinical: Onset typically in neonatal period or early infancy with refractory seizures of varied semiologies (often myoclonic, focal, mixed). Other features may include encephalopathy, abnormal tone, irritability. Family history of unexplained neonatal seizures.
  • Biochemical markers:
    • Elevated urine α-aminoadipic semialdehyde (α-AASA).
    • Elevated plasma and CSF pipecolic acid.
    • Confirmation by ALDH7A1 sequencing.
  • Diagnostic / therapeutic trial:
    • IV pyridoxine 100 mg under continuous EEG monitoring with airway and resuscitation readiness (apnea/hypotension possible).
    • Dramatic seizure cessation and EEG improvement within minutes is diagnostic.
    • If no response and clinical suspicion remains: trial pyridoxal-5'-phosphate (P5P) 30 mg/kg for PNPO deficiency.
    • Folinic acid 5 mg/kg for folinic acid–responsive seizures (allelic to pyridoxine-dependent epilepsy).
  • Long-term management:
    • Oral pyridoxine 15–30 mg/kg/day lifelong.
    • Lysine-restricted diet and arginine supplementation reduce α-AASA accumulation and improve neurodevelopmental outcomes (adjunctive).
    • Folinic acid supplementation may help in subset.
    • Genetic counseling; carrier testing.
    • Anticipate neurodevelopmental delay even when seizures controlled — early intervention.
Source: Zimmerman Ch 67 supplement; Stockler-Ipsiroglu pyridoxine-dependent epilepsy consensus.
SAQ 23 MODERATE
Describe pediatric lead poisoning encephalopathy: presentation, blood lead level (BLL) thresholds for treatment, chelation regimen, and supportive ICU management.
Show structured answer
  • Presentation:
    • Subacute or acute encephalopathy: irritability, ataxia, vomiting, seizures, decreased consciousness; raised ICP.
    • Other features: microcytic hypochromic anemia, abdominal pain, peripheral neuropathy, growth/developmental delay.
    • Risk factors: older housing (pre-1978 paint), lead-contaminated soil/dust, imported products (cosmetics, ceramics, toys), occupational exposure of caregivers.
  • BLL thresholds:
    • BLL ≥ 70 µg/dL with encephalopathy OR any encephalopathy regardless of level → inpatient combination parenteral chelation.
    • BLL 45–69 µg/dL without encephalopathy → oral DMSA (succimer).
    • BLL 5–44 µg/dL → environmental investigation, nutritional measures; chelation generally not indicated.
    • CDC reference value (2021): 3.5 µg/dL — public health action level.
  • Combination parenteral chelation regimen (encephalopathy):
    • Dimercaprol (BAL) 75 mg/m² IM q4h, first dose given FIRST.
    • 4 hours after BAL, start CaNa2EDTA 1500 mg/m²/day as continuous IV infusion (or divided IV doses).
    • Course typically 5 days.
    • Rationale for BAL-first: avoids EDTA redistribution of lead into brain.
  • Supportive ICU management:
    • Raised ICP management per general principles: head-up, 3% saline, sedation.
    • Seizure control: levetiracetam, BZD.
    • Cardiac monitoring during BAL.
    • Adequate hydration to support EDTA-induced lead-zinc excretion.
    • Iron deficiency anemia — treat (iron deficiency increases lead absorption).
    • Avoid further lead exposure — environmental investigation and remediation; relocate child if necessary.
    • Report to public health.
  • Long-term: Recheck BLL after chelation; rebound is common as lead redistributes from bone. Repeat chelation as needed. Multidisciplinary follow-up (developmental pediatrics, social work, environmental health).
Source: Rogers toxicology / Zimmerman supplement; CDC / AAP guidance; ATSDR.
SAQ 24 HARD
Describe acute necrotizing encephalopathy of childhood (ANE): clinical presentation, imaging features, pathophysiology, prognostic markers, and emerging treatment approaches.
Show structured answer
  • Clinical: Fulminant para-infectious encephalopathy. Typical course: viral prodrome (especially influenza A/B, HHV-6, parainfluenza, mycoplasma, more recently SARS-CoV-2) → acute encephalopathy → rapid neurologic deterioration with seizures, coma, raised ICP within 24–72 h.
  • Imaging:
    • Symmetric thalamic lesions with central necrosis ± hemorrhage on MRI (T2/FLAIR hyperintensity with central hypointensity).
    • Other typical sites: brainstem tegmentum, cerebellar white matter, periventricular white matter, putamina, internal capsule.
    • Concentric pattern of edema-necrosis-hemorrhage on susceptibility-weighted imaging.
  • Pathophysiology:
    • Para-infectious cytokine storm — elevated IL-6, IL-10, TNF, ferritin, AST/ALT.
    • BBB disruption with leakage and necrosis.
    • Hyperinflammation similar to other macrophage activation/cytokine release syndromes.
    • Familial recurrent forms (ANE1) associated with RANBP2 gene mutations.
  • Prognostic markers:
    • Elevated ferritin ≥ 500 ng/mL — high inflammation.
    • Brainstem involvement on MRI — worse outcome.
    • Need for aggressive ICU support.
    • Mortality 30–50%; severe neurologic disability in survivors.
  • Treatment:
    • Aggressive ICU support: airway, ICP control (BTF-style approach), seizure control, hemodynamic optimization.
    • Immunomodulation: high-dose IV methylprednisolone (30 mg/kg/day × 3–5 days); IVIG; plasma exchange.
    • Cytokine-directed therapy: tocilizumab (IL-6 antagonist) increasingly reported; anakinra (IL-1 antagonist) considered.
    • Antiviral therapy targeting the precipitating infection when possible (e.g., oseltamivir for influenza).
    • Outcome data limited; treatment is largely consensus-based.
  • Family considerations: Genetic testing (RANBP2) for recurrent forms; counseling about recurrence risk with future viral illnesses.
Source: Zimmerman Ch 67 supplement; Mizuguchi 1995 ANE description; Wolf 2013 pediatric review; recent COVID-related ANE case series.
SAQ 25 MODERATE
Outline the recognition and acute management of a MELAS stroke-like episode in a 10-year-old. Address why standard stroke treatments are not used and the rationale for L-arginine.
Show structured answer
  • Recognition: Subacute focal deficits (hemiparesis, aphasia, hemianopia, encephalopathy) not respecting vascular territories; recurrent or migrainous quality; family history of mitochondrial disease; lactate elevation; MRI cortical/subcortical T2 hyperintensity often posterior, without DWI restriction matching arterial distribution.
  • Investigation: Plasma and CSF lactate; MR spectroscopy (lactate peak); genetic testing for m.3243A>G (most common) and other mtDNA / nuclear mutations.
  • Acute therapy:
    • IV L-arginine 0.5 g/kg bolus over 30–60 min, followed by 0.5 g/kg/day infusion for 3–5 days; mechanism — restores nitric oxide bioavailability for vasodilation.
    • Alternative: citrulline (precursor to arginine; better bioavailability in some protocols).
    • Seizure control: levetiracetam (avoid valproate).
    • Maintain euglycemia and normothermia; avoid fever (worsens energy crisis).
    • Avoid: tPA (no thrombus), aspirin (no benefit), valproate (mitochondrial inhibitor, hepatic failure risk).
  • Long-term: Mitochondrial cocktail (CoQ10, riboflavin, thiamine, L-carnitine, vitamin C, E) — modest evidence; arginine/citrulline prophylaxis; multidisciplinary care; avoid mitochondrial toxins (linezolid, statins, valproate, metformin).
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman metabolic chapters; Koga MELAS L-arginine RCT 2005, 2010.
SAQ 26 MODERATE
Describe posterior reversible encephalopathy syndrome (PRES): triggers, imaging, BP target during management, and prognosis.
Show structured answer
  • Triggers:
    • Hypertensive crisis (eclampsia, renovascular hypertension, glomerulonephritis).
    • Calcineurin inhibitors (cyclosporine, tacrolimus) — solid organ and HSCT recipients.
    • Chemotherapy and immunotherapy (high-dose).
    • Autoimmune disease (SLE), TTP, HUS.
    • Massive transfusion.
  • Imaging: Symmetric bilateral parieto-occipital subcortical/cortical T2/FLAIR hyperintensity (vasogenic edema); usually no diffusion restriction unless severe ischemic conversion; less commonly involves frontal, brainstem, cerebellum.
  • Management:
    • Control BP gradually — reduce MAP by 10–25% over hours, NOT precipitously (over-aggressive lowering causes ischemia).
    • Use IV titratable agents (nicardipine, labetalol; avoid sodium nitroprusside in pediatrics — cyanide toxicity).
    • Remove offending agent (hold calcineurin inhibitor, chemotherapy).
    • Treat seizures (levetiracetam first-line; cEEG monitoring).
    • Avoid fluid overload.
    • Treat underlying disease (eclampsia management, renal replacement if needed).
  • Prognosis: Generally favorable with prompt management; residual deficits may occur in severe cases; recurrence possible if trigger not addressed.
Source: Rogers Ch on hypertensive encephalopathy; Hinchey 1996 NEJM original PRES.
SAQ 27 MODERATE
Describe posterior fossa syndrome (cerebellar mutism syndrome): clinical features, time course, mechanism, and recovery expectations. Include the cerebellar cognitive-affective syndrome component.
Show structured answer
  • Clinical features:
    • Mutism (typically delayed onset 1–2 days post-op, not immediate post-anesthesia).
    • Hypotonia, ataxia, dysphagia.
    • Emotional lability (crying, laughter inappropriate to context).
    • Apathy, decreased initiative.
    • Sometimes cranial nerve palsies, dysarthria after speech returns.
  • Time course:
    • Onset: 1–4 days post-op (most commonly 24–72 h).
    • Mutism duration: weeks to months.
    • Recovery of speech: gradual; often persistent dysarthria.
    • Cognitive and affective sequelae often persist (Schmahmann cerebellar cognitive-affective syndrome).
  • Mechanism: Disruption of the dentato-thalamo-cortical pathway, often from injury to dentate nuclei or superior cerebellar peduncles during vermian/midline approaches. Risk factors: midline approach, vermian splitting, tumor type (medulloblastoma highest).
  • Frequency: ~25% of pediatric posterior fossa surgeries; higher with medulloblastoma resections.
  • Management: Supportive — speech therapy, PT/OT, NG or PEG feeding initially, family counseling about timeline, neuropsychology assessment.
  • Cerebellar cognitive-affective syndrome (Schmahmann): Executive dysfunction, visuospatial impairment, language deficits, personality change (disinhibition or flattening) — recognized long-term sequelae of cerebellar injury.
Source: Rogers Ch 61 / Zimmerman Ch 59 supplement; Schmahmann 1998 review; Catsman-Berrevoets posterior fossa syndrome.
SAQ 28 HARD
Describe the recognition and management of acute intrathecal baclofen withdrawal. Compare to NMS, malignant hyperthermia, and serotonin syndrome.
Show structured answer
  • Recognition:
    • Acute return of severe spasticity (often exceeding baseline).
    • Hyperthermia.
    • Autonomic instability (tachycardia, hypertension or hypotension, diaphoresis).
    • Mental status change (agitation, hallucinations, decreased LOC).
    • Seizures.
    • Rhabdomyolysis with myoglobinuria, AKI, DIC if untreated.
  • Confirm: Pump interrogation (flow rate, alarms, reservoir, catheter integrity); X-ray for catheter disconnection; imaging of catheter.
  • Management:
    • EMERGENT restoration of intrathecal baclofen — pump replacement, catheter repair, OR temporary intrathecal infusion via lumbar drain by neurosurgery/anesthesia.
    • High-dose oral baclofen (insufficient CNS penetration but adjunctive).
    • Benzodiazepines for sedation, spasticity control, seizures.
    • Cyproheptadine (5-HT2A antagonist) has been reported helpful.
    • Aggressive cooling, IV fluids, rhabdomyolysis management (urinary alkalinization, monitor compartments, AKI).
    • ICU supportive care; mechanical ventilation if needed.
  • Differential vs other hyperthermic syndromes:
    FeatureITB WithdrawalNMSMHSerotonin Syndrome
    TriggerPump failure / catheterNeuroleptic exposureAnesthetic exposureSerotonergic drugs
    OnsetHours to daysDays to weeksMinutes-hoursMinutes-hours
    RigiditySpasticity (return to baseline ↑)Lead-pipeSevereLess; clonus prominent
    ReflexesVariableHyporeflexiaVariableHyperreflexia
    Specific RxRestore ITBBromocriptine, dantroleneDantroleneCyproheptadine
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman pharmacology; ITB withdrawal reviews.
SAQ 29 HARD
Outline pediatric status dystonicus: definition, precipitants, complications, and management.
Show structured answer
  • Definition: Increasingly severe and frequent dystonic spasms requiring urgent hospital admission; life-threatening if untreated.
  • Precipitants:
    • Medication change / withdrawal (especially anti-dystonic, antiparkinsonian).
    • Intercurrent infection or febrile illness.
    • Surgery or anesthesia.
    • Deep brain stimulator malfunction.
    • Trauma, sleep deprivation, emotional stress.
  • Underlying conditions: DYT1, secondary dystonia (cerebral palsy, mitochondrial disease, neurodegeneration with brain iron accumulation, drug-induced).
  • Complications:
    • Hyperthermia.
    • Rhabdomyolysis with myoglobinuria.
    • Acute kidney injury.
    • Respiratory failure (oromandibular dystonia or chest wall fatigue).
    • Compartment syndrome.
    • Aspiration pneumonia.
    • Autonomic dysfunction.
    • Cardiovascular collapse.
  • Management:
    • PICU admission with continuous monitoring.
    • Deep sedation — propofol short-term (caution PRIS), midazolam infusion.
    • Neuromuscular blockade if refractory.
    • Aggressive cooling.
    • IV fluids; urinary alkalinization for rhabdomyolysis; monitor CK, renal function.
    • Oral anti-dystonic therapy optimization: trihexyphenidyl (anticholinergic), baclofen, tetrabenazine, clonidine, gabapentin, benzodiazepines.
    • Refractory: intrathecal baclofen, deep brain stimulation (globus pallidus internus), or bilateral pallidotomy.
    • Identify and treat precipitating cause.
Source: Rogers Ch pediatric movement disorders; Allen/Lin status dystonicus consensus.
SAQ 30 HARD
Describe pediatric moyamoya disease: pathophysiology, presentation, diagnostic imaging, and definitive treatment options.
Show structured answer
  • Pathophysiology: Progressive idiopathic stenosis of the supraclinoid internal carotid arteries with formation of compensatory collateral vessels at the base of the brain (the 'puff of smoke' appearance). Affects children and young adults. Bilateral in moyamoya disease; unilateral or with associated condition = moyamoya syndrome.
  • Associations:
    • Neurofibromatosis type 1.
    • Down syndrome.
    • Sickle cell disease.
    • Post-radiation (after CNS or skull base radiation).
    • Cranial reconstruction or trauma.
    • Genetic — RNF213 variant in East Asian populations.
  • Presentation:
    • Pediatric: ischemic events (TIA, stroke) — recurrent, often precipitated by hyperventilation (crying, infection).
    • Adult: increasing hemorrhagic events.
    • Headache, seizures, cognitive decline.
  • Diagnosis:
    • MRI/MRA: bilateral terminal ICA stenosis/occlusion with characteristic collateral vessels.
    • Catheter angiography (Suzuki staging I–VI).
    • CTA, perfusion imaging (rest and acetazolamide challenge) — quantify perfusion reserve.
  • Management:
    • Medical: aspirin 1–5 mg/kg/day; avoid hypotension, hypovolemia, hyperventilation, anemia, oral contraceptives; treat sickle cell, hypertension; statins not first-line in pediatrics.
    • Surgical revascularization — cornerstone for symptomatic patients:
      • Indirect: encephalo-duro-arterio-synangiosis (EDAS), encephalo-myo-synangiosis (EMS) — collateralizes from external carotid via dura/muscle to brain over months.
      • Direct: STA-MCA bypass — immediate but technically demanding; preferred in adults.
      • Combined often used.
    • Perioperative care: maintain normothermia, normocapnia, normovolemia, avoid hypotension.
    • Long-term: follow-up imaging at 6 months and yearly.
Source: Rogers Ch 65 / Zimmerman Ch 66 supplement; Smith pediatric moyamoya consensus.
SAQ 31 HARD
Describe the West Haven classification of hepatic encephalopathy and the PICU management priorities for grade III–IV encephalopathy in acute liver failure.
Show structured answer
  • West Haven Grading:
    GradeFeatures
    0Normal
    ISlight personality change, mild confusion, sleep disturbance
    IILethargy, marked personality change, intermittent disorientation, asterixis
    IIISomnolent but arousable, marked disorientation, gross asterixis, hyperreflexia
    IVComa, with or without decerebrate posturing
  • PICU management priorities (Grade III–IV):
    • Airway protection — intubate.
    • Sedation: short-acting agents (fentanyl, propofol short-term); AVOID benzodiazepines (prolonged effect in liver failure, worsen encephalopathy).
    • ICP management:
      • HOB 30°, neutral neck.
      • 3% saline 3–5 mL/kg target Na 150–155.
      • Mannitol cautiously (renal dysfunction common).
      • Normocapnia 35–38; brief hyperventilation only as bridge.
      • Strict normothermia.
    • Ammonia reduction:
      • Lactulose 1 mL/kg NG TID-QID titrate to 2–3 stools/day.
      • Rifaximin enterally if tolerated.
      • CRRT with high-flux dialyzer for ammonia > 150 µmol/L or rising despite scavengers.
    • Hemodynamic support: noradrenaline; avoid systemic hypotension.
    • Coagulopathy: FFP only for procedures or active bleeding (avoid prophylactic correction — masks INR trend critical for transplant assessment); platelets if < 50 with bleeding; cryoprecipitate if fibrinogen low.
    • Glucose: monitor q1h; D10 or D20 infusion to maintain 4–6 mmol/L.
    • Renal support: CRRT if AKI, hyperammonemia, hyponatremia, or fluid overload.
    • Urgent transplant evaluation — King's College criteria or pediatric ALF criteria for listing.
    • Avoid hepatotoxic drugs and drugs requiring hepatic metabolism.
    • Watch for cerebral herniation — single most common cause of death in pediatric ALF.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman acute liver failure chapters; pediatric ALF Working Group.
SAQ 32 HARD
Outline the emergency management of severe neonatal hyperammonemia (≥ 200 µmol/L) in suspected urea cycle disorder.
Show structured answer
  • Step 1 — Stop protein intake: NPO, no enteral feeds, no parenteral amino acids initially.
  • Step 2 — Suppress catabolism: IV D10 + intralipid 2 g/kg/day to provide ~ 100 kcal/kg/day; insulin if hyperglycemic.
  • Step 3 — Nitrogen scavengers (Ammonul = sodium phenylacetate + sodium benzoate): Loading dose 250 mg/kg IV over 90–120 min, then 250 mg/kg/day continuous infusion. Adjust per ammonia and product label.
  • Step 4 — Arginine HCl: 250 mg/kg IV bolus then 250 mg/kg/day infusion (NOT in NAGS deficiency, which uses N-carbamylglutamate; arginine harmful in arginase deficiency).
  • Step 5 — Cofactor supplementation: Carnitine 100 mg/kg, biotin, hydroxocobalamin (B12) as empiric while differential evolves.
  • Step 6 — Dialysis: Hemodialysis preferred over CRRT for rapid ammonia removal. Indications: ammonia > 250 µmol/L, rising despite scavengers, severe encephalopathy. Hemodialysis 4–6× more effective than CRRT for ammonia clearance.
  • Step 7 — Diagnostic workup:
    • Plasma amino acids (elevated glutamine and alanine; specific elevations: citrulline, argininosuccinate, ornithine direct to specific UCD).
    • Urine orotic acid: elevated in OTC (X-linked, common); normal in CPS-1 and NAGS deficiency.
    • Plasma acylcarnitines, urine organic acids (rule out organic acidemia, fatty acid oxidation defects).
    • Lactate, pyruvate (mitochondrial).
    • Genetic testing for confirmation.
  • Avoid: Valproate (mitochondrial inhibitor — fatal hepatic failure), steroids (increase catabolism), protein-containing fluids, sedation that obscures neuro exam.
  • Long-term: Protein-restricted diet with essential amino acids, oral scavengers (sodium phenylbutyrate or glycerol phenylbutyrate), liver transplant for severe forms.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman metabolic emergencies; Urea Cycle Disorders Consortium.
SAQ 33 HARD
Describe ifosfamide encephalopathy: clinical features, risk factors, mechanism, and treatment.
Show structured answer
  • Clinical features:
    • Onset typically within 24–72 h of ifosfamide administration.
    • Spectrum from mild confusion to coma.
    • Hallucinations (visual), agitation, cerebellar signs, choreoathetosis, mutism, seizures.
    • Reversible in most cases over days; rare deaths.
  • Risk factors:
    • Low serum albumin.
    • Renal dysfunction (creatinine elevation).
    • Prior cisplatin exposure.
    • High dose (≥ 5–10 g/m²).
    • Pelvic disease (impaired drug clearance).
    • Concurrent CNS-active drugs.
    • History of prior ifosfamide encephalopathy.
  • Mechanism: Chloroacetaldehyde (toxic metabolite of ifosfamide) inhibits mitochondrial respiration and depletes glutathione in CNS; thiamine depletion contributes.
  • Treatment:
    • Discontinue ifosfamide immediately.
    • IV methylene blue 1–2 mg/kg q4–6 h (electron acceptor; may restore mitochondrial respiration).
    • Thiamine 100 mg IV, B-complex vitamins.
    • Supportive ICU care; seizure control.
    • Albumin correction (if low) reduces free fraction.
    • Prophylactic methylene blue (50 mg q6h orally) for future cycles in patients with prior encephalopathy.
    • Most cases resolve within 24–72 h after discontinuation.
  • Prevention: Identify risk factors before treatment; correct albumin, hydration, renal function; consider prophylactic methylene blue.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman pediatric oncology critical care; Zulian and Salvi ifosfamide reviews.
SAQ 34 HARD
Describe Immune Effector Cell-Associated Neurotoxicity Syndrome (ICANS) following CAR-T therapy: pathophysiology, grading, and management.
Show structured answer
  • Pathophysiology: Following CAR-T (chimeric antigen receptor T-cell) infusion, cytokine release syndrome (CRS) and BBB disruption allow cytokines (IFN-γ, IL-6, IL-1, GM-CSF) and activated T cells to enter CNS, causing endothelial activation, edema, and neuroinflammation. Onset typically 3–10 days post-infusion; may co-occur with or follow CRS.
  • ASTCT Consensus Grading (Lee 2019):
    GradeICE scoreLOCSeizureMotorICP/Edema
    17–9Awake spontaneouslyNoneNoneNone
    23–6Rouses to voiceNoneNoneNone
    30–2Rouses to tactileFocal/clusters resolved with interventionNoneFocal edema on MRI
    40; unarousableUnarousableLife-threatening prolonged seizure/SEDeep focal motor weakness or hemiparesisDiffuse edema; raised ICP; cerebral herniation
    ICE = Immune effector Cell-associated Encephalopathy score (10 points): orientation, naming, command, writing, attention.
  • Management:
    • Grade 1: supportive; ICE q12h; aspiration precautions.
    • Grade 2: dexamethasone 10 mg q6–12 h (CNS-penetrating); ICU monitoring.
    • Grade 3: methylprednisolone 1 g/day or equivalent; ICU; cEEG; consider anakinra 100 mg SC daily; tocilizumab if concurrent CRS but does not treat ICANS alone (poor CNS penetration).
    • Grade 4: methylprednisolone 1 g BID or higher; intubation; cEEG; ICP management (hyperosmolar therapy, HOB 30°, normocapnia, normothermia); anakinra; siltuximab considered.
    • Tocilizumab role: treats CRS; does not penetrate BBB to treat established ICANS.
    • Steroid pulse is mainstay for established ICANS.
    • Supportive: seizure control (levetiracetam first-line), avoid prophylactic phenytoin/fosphenytoin (drug interactions).
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman pediatric oncology critical care; Lee ASTCT 2019 consensus; Topp tisagenlecleucel pediatric experience.
SAQ 35 MODERATE
Outline the acute management of pediatric arteriovenous malformation (AVM) rupture with intracerebral or subarachnoid hemorrhage.
Show structured answer
  • Initial stabilization:
    • Airway protection; intubate if GCS ≤ 8.
    • Cerebral perfusion management — avoid hypotension, but avoid hypertension (rebleed risk); aim age-appropriate MAP / CPP.
    • HOB 30°, neutral neck, normocapnia 35–38, normothermia.
    • 3% saline or mannitol for raised ICP.
    • Reverse coagulopathy: vitamin K, FFP if active bleeding or surgical intervention planned; platelets to ≥ 100 with intervention; protamine for heparin.
    • Seizure prophylaxis: levetiracetam (cortical/subdural blood).
    • Stop offending agent (anticoagulant, antiplatelet).
    • Cerebellar AVM hemorrhage — consider posterior fossa decompression for mass effect (low threshold).
  • Imaging:
    • Non-contrast CT (acute hemorrhage extent).
    • CTA / MRA to identify AVM, associated aneurysm, venous drainage.
    • Catheter angiography is gold standard for definitive characterization (Spetzler-Martin grading).
  • Definitive management:
    • Microsurgical resection — preferred for accessible low-grade AVMs (Spetzler-Martin I–III); high obliteration rate.
    • Stereotactic radiosurgery — for deep / eloquent AVMs; delayed obliteration over 2–3 years.
    • Endovascular embolization — adjunctive to surgery or radiosurgery; or definitive for selected AVMs.
    • Combined approaches common.
    • Associated aneurysm in feeding arteries may require urgent treatment to prevent rebleeding.
  • Vasospasm: Rare in AVM hemorrhage compared to aneurysmal SAH; routine nimodipine not established in pediatric AVM SAH.
  • Long-term: Antiepileptic continuation; surveillance imaging for residual AVM; family counseling about recurrence and obliteration rates.
Source: Rogers Ch 65 / Zimmerman Ch 66 supplement; pediatric AVM consensus.
SAQ 36 MODERATE
Describe pediatric idiopathic intracranial hypertension (IIH / pseudotumor cerebri): diagnostic criteria, management, and indications for surgical intervention.
Show structured answer
  • Diagnostic criteria (Friedman/Wall pediatric criteria):
    • Symptoms of intracranial hypertension (headache, transient visual obscurations, pulsatile tinnitus, diplopia).
    • Papilledema on examination.
    • Normal neurologic examination apart from CN VI palsy.
    • Normal neuroimaging (MRI/MRV to exclude venous sinus thrombosis, mass, hydrocephalus).
    • Elevated CSF opening pressure (> 25 cmH₂O in non-obese, > 28 in obese, or > 28 with sedation per ICHD).
    • Normal CSF composition.
    • Exclusion of secondary causes.
  • Associations:
    • Obesity (especially pubertal).
    • Medications: tetracyclines, retinoids, growth hormone, steroid withdrawal, lithium.
    • Endocrine: thyroid disease, Cushing.
    • Anemia / polycythemia.
    • Venous sinus thrombosis (rule out — secondary cause, not IIH).
  • Management:
    • Weight loss for obese patients.
    • Acetazolamide 25 mg/kg/day divided BID-TID (carbonic anhydrase inhibitor; reduces CSF production).
    • Topiramate alternative.
    • Furosemide adjunct.
    • Stop offending medications.
    • Serial visual field testing (formal automated perimetry) — frequency by severity.
    • Lumbar puncture therapeutic — limited duration of relief.
  • Surgical intervention indications:
    • Progressive visual loss despite maximum medical therapy.
    • Severe visual field defects.
    • Optic atrophy.
    • Intolerable medication side effects.
  • Surgical options:
    • Optic nerve sheath fenestration — preserves vision; preferred when vision is primary concern.
    • CSF diversion: ventriculoperitoneal or lumboperitoneal shunt — for refractory ICH.
    • Venous sinus stenting — emerging for selected patients with stenosis on venography.
Source: Rogers neuro / Friedman Wall pediatric IIH criteria.
SAQ 37 HARD
Outline the spectrum of pediatric arteriopathies causing arterial ischemic stroke. Include features that distinguish each category.
Show structured answer
  • Focal cerebral arteriopathy (FCA) / transient cerebral arteriopathy:
    • Most common arteriopathy in previously healthy children (~30% per VIPS).
    • Unilateral M1 or distal ICA narrowing.
    • Often post-infectious (especially varicella zoster).
    • Monophasic or transiently progressive then stabilizes.
    • Treatment: aspirin; corticosteroids in inflammatory variants.
  • Moyamoya disease / syndrome:
    • Bilateral supraclinoid ICA progressive stenosis with characteristic collaterals.
    • Associations: NF1, Down syndrome, sickle cell, post-radiation.
    • Progressive; surgical revascularization indicated.
  • Arterial dissection:
    • Spontaneous or post-trauma (chiropractic manipulation, sports).
    • Internal carotid or vertebral; long tapered narrowing on MRA; T1 fat-sat MRI shows intramural hematoma.
    • Treatment: antiplatelet or anticoagulation 3–6 months.
  • Primary CNS vasculitis (PACNS) of childhood:
    • Small or medium-large vessel subtype.
    • Multifocal progressive arteriopathy.
    • Angiogram: 'string of beads' in medium-large vessel form; biopsy in small vessel form.
    • Treatment: corticosteroids + cyclophosphamide or rituximab; maintenance 18–24 months.
  • Sickle cell vasculopathy:
    • Progressive arteriopathy with elevated TCD velocities and Suzuki-like changes.
    • STOP / SWiTCH / TWiTCH / SIT inform chronic transfusion vs hydroxyurea.
    • Acute stroke: exchange transfusion.
  • Other: Fibromuscular dysplasia, post-radiation arteriopathy, reversible cerebral vasoconstriction syndrome, infectious arteriopathy (bacterial endocarditis, fungal in immunocompromised), thrombophilic states.
Source: Rogers Ch 65 / Zimmerman Ch 66; VIPS study (Wintermark 2016); pediatric stroke consensus.
SAQ 38 HARD
Describe management of pediatric cervical artery dissection: workup, antithrombotic choice, and follow-up.
Show structured answer
  • Diagnostic workup:
    • MRI / MRA cervical and intracranial; T1 fat-saturated MRI is the gold standard (shows crescent of intramural hematoma).
    • CT angiography as alternative.
    • Catheter angiography rarely needed in pediatrics.
    • Investigate predisposing factors: connective tissue disease (Marfan, Ehlers-Danlos), recent trauma or manipulation, family history.
  • Acute management:
    • Antithrombotic therapy:
      • Antiplatelet (aspirin 1–5 mg/kg/day) OR anticoagulation (LMWH then warfarin).
      • CADISS trial (adults) showed no superiority of one over the other for stroke prevention; pediatric extrapolation usually favors aspirin as first choice except large-vessel or fluctuating cases.
      • Treat 3–6 months; reassess imaging.
    • Avoid further neck manipulation; restrict contact sports temporarily.
    • Endovascular stenting reserved for hemodynamic compromise, recurrent stroke despite medical therapy.
  • Follow-up imaging:
    • MRA at 3 and 6 months; most dissections recanalize.
    • Persistent occlusion or pseudoaneurysm requires individualized management.
  • Counseling:
    • Avoid chiropractic manipulation lifetime.
    • Genetic counseling if connective tissue disorder.
Source: Rogers Ch 65 / Zimmerman Ch 66; CADISS adult trial; pediatric stroke arteriopathy guidance.
SAQ 39 HARD
Outline the escalating treatment algorithm for FIRES (Febrile Infection-Related Epilepsy Syndrome).
Show structured answer
  • Diagnosis:
    • Previously healthy child 4–15 years.
    • Febrile illness preceding by 1–14 days.
    • Refractory status epilepticus with rapid evolution.
    • Workup: rule out infection (CSF, serology), autoimmune (NMDA, MOG, AQP4, autoimmune panels), metabolic, genetic.
    • Diagnosis of exclusion.
  • Standard AEDs:
    • BZD first-line.
    • Levetiracetam, fosphenytoin, valproate, lacosamide, perampanel.
  • Continuous infusions:
    • Midazolam 0.1–2 mg/kg/h.
    • Pentobarbital with EEG burst-suppression target.
    • Ketamine (NMDA antagonist).
    • Propofol short-term (PRIS caution).
  • Ketogenic diet:
    • Very effective in FIRES (50–70%).
    • Enteral or parenteral ketogenic formulations.
    • Target serum BHB 2–5 mmol/L, urine ketones 3+.
  • Immunotherapy / cytokine-directed:
    • High-dose IV methylprednisolone 30 mg/kg/day × 5 days.
    • IVIG 2 g/kg over 2–5 days.
    • Plasma exchange.
    • Anakinra (IL-1 antagonist) 2–5 mg/kg/day SC — emerging as key therapy for cytokine-driven FIRES.
    • Tocilizumab (IL-6 antagonist) in select cases.
  • Other:
    • Vagal nerve stimulation when stable.
    • Surgical evaluation for focal-onset epilepsy.
    • Cannabidiol (Epidiolex) emerging.
  • Outcome:
    • Mortality 10–30%.
    • Most survivors develop refractory epilepsy and cognitive sequelae.
    • Multidisciplinary long-term care; family counseling.
Source: Zimmerman Ch 67 supplement; Nabbout 2017 FIRES consensus; Lai 2020 IL-1 in FIRES.
SAQ 40 HARD
Describe Leigh syndrome: clinical features, imaging, genetic basis, and management principles.
Show structured answer
  • Clinical:
    • Onset infancy to early childhood; subacute neurodegeneration.
    • Developmental regression, hypotonia, dystonia, ataxia.
    • Cardiomyopathy, respiratory failure, brainstem involvement.
    • Lactic acidosis (variable).
    • Eye findings: nystagmus, optic atrophy.
  • Imaging:
    • Symmetric bilateral T2 hyperintensity in basal ganglia and brainstem (substantia nigra, periaqueductal gray, dentate nuclei).
    • MR spectroscopy: elevated lactate peak.
  • Genetic basis:
    • Heterogeneous: mtDNA mutations (MT-ATP6 NARP/Leigh, MT-ND mutations); nuclear mutations (SURF1, NDUFA, NDUFS, POLG); pyruvate dehydrogenase deficiency.
    • Mitochondrial respiratory chain complex deficiency final common pathway.
  • Management:
    • Supportive; no specific therapy proven for most forms.
    • Mitochondrial cocktail: CoQ10, riboflavin, thiamine, L-carnitine, vitamin C, E.
    • Targeted: ketogenic diet for PDH deficiency; biotin for biotinidase; thiamine for PDH and complex I.
    • Avoid mitotoxic drugs: valproate (POLG hepatic failure), linezolid, statins, metformin, propofol prolonged use.
    • AEDs of choice: levetiracetam.
    • Aggressive treatment of intercurrent illness (crisis precipitants).
    • Nutritional support; PT/OT; respiratory monitoring.
    • Multidisciplinary care including palliative; many forms life-limiting.
    • Genetic counseling.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman metabolic chapters; Leigh syndrome consensus.
SAQ 41 HARD
Describe POLG-related disorders including Alpers syndrome: clinical features and the critical pharmacologic contraindication.
Show structured answer
  • POLG (DNA polymerase γ) mutations: Affect mtDNA replication and repair; cause a spectrum of disease.
  • Alpers-Huttenlocher syndrome:
    • Onset 2 months–8 years.
    • Triad: refractory seizures (often myoclonic, EPC, occipital), liver dysfunction, developmental regression.
    • MRI: occipital cortical T2 hyperintensity, cerebellar atrophy.
    • Often fatal in 1–4 years.
  • Other phenotypes: Childhood ataxia-neuropathy-spinopathy syndromes, sensory ataxic neuropathy with dysarthria and ophthalmoplegia (SANDO), progressive external ophthalmoplegia.
  • CRITICAL CONTRAINDICATION:
    • Valproate is contraindicated — precipitates fulminant hepatic failure (high mortality).
    • Always screen for POLG before valproate in unexplained refractory epilepsy with any liver dysfunction.
  • Diagnosis:
    • POLG sequencing.
    • mtDNA depletion may be present.
    • Liver biopsy: microvesicular steatosis, mtDNA depletion.
  • Management:
    • Withdraw valproate immediately if exposed.
    • N-acetylcysteine for hepatic protection.
    • Supportive ICU care; CRRT for ammonia.
    • Levetiracetam for seizures.
    • Liver transplant controversial — recurrence likely with progressive brain disease.
    • Genetic counseling; family screening.
    • Palliative care discussion early; many forms life-limiting.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman metabolic chapters; POLG consensus.
SAQ 42 MODERATE
Describe Reye syndrome: clinical features, diagnostic criteria, and metabolic mimics that must be considered.
Show structured answer
  • Clinical features:
    • Two-phase illness: viral prodrome (varicella, influenza B most classic), then days later, acute encephalopathy with vomiting.
    • Progressive encephalopathy: irritability → lethargy → coma → posturing.
    • Hepatic dysfunction WITHOUT jaundice: AST/ALT ≥ 3× normal, normal/mildly elevated bilirubin.
    • Hyperammonemia.
    • Hypoglycemia (especially in young children).
    • Coagulopathy.
  • Diagnostic criteria (CDC):
    • Acute non-inflammatory encephalopathy with altered consciousness.
    • Hepatopathy: AST/ALT or ammonia ≥ 3× normal, OR liver biopsy with consistent histology.
    • No other reasonable explanation.
    • CSF normal (< 8 WBC).
  • Aspirin link:
    • Use of aspirin during viral illness associated with Reye syndrome.
    • Incidence has fallen dramatically since aspirin warnings (1980s).
  • Pathophysiology:
    • Mitochondrial dysfunction (decreased β-oxidation, urea cycle activity).
    • Resulting fatty acid accumulation, ammonia rise, cerebral edema.
  • Metabolic mimics:
    • Medium-chain acyl-CoA dehydrogenase deficiency (MCAD) — most common mimic.
    • Other fatty acid oxidation defects (VLCAD, LCHAD).
    • Urea cycle disorders (OTC partial expression in females).
    • Mitochondrial disorders.
    • Carnitine deficiency.
  • Workup:
    • Plasma acylcarnitines, urine organic acids, plasma amino acids, ammonia, carnitine levels.
    • Genetic testing for inherited metabolic disorders.
  • Management:
    • Supportive ICU care.
    • Hyperammonemia management.
    • ICP management for severe encephalopathy.
    • Glucose support (D10 IV).
    • Treat underlying inborn error of metabolism if identified.
    • Avoid further aspirin / NSAIDs in viral illness.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman pediatric metabolic emergencies; CDC Reye criteria.
SAQ 43 HARD
Describe methotrexate-related acute and chronic neurotoxicity, including 'stroke-like syndrome' and management.
Show structured answer
  • Acute neurotoxicity (stroke-like syndrome / chemotherapy-related leukoencephalopathy):
    • Onset hours to days after intrathecal or high-dose IV methotrexate.
    • Features: confusion, aphasia, hemiparesis, seizures.
    • MRI: bilateral DWI restriction in centrum semiovale and splenium of corpus callosum — distinctive pattern.
    • Most resolve over days to weeks; some progress to permanent deficits.
  • Chronic leukoencephalopathy:
    • Long-term sequela of high cumulative dose intrathecal or systemic MTX.
    • White matter changes on MRI; cognitive impairment, declining school performance.
    • Especially in children < 5 years.
  • Risk factors: High dose, intrathecal route, concurrent CNS radiation, slow MTX clearance, low folate, prior MTX neurotoxicity.
  • Acute management:
    • Hold or stop methotrexate.
    • IV leucovorin (folinic acid) rescue at higher doses.
    • Adjuncts in case reports: dextromethorphan (NMDA modulation), aminophylline (adenosine), high-dose folinic acid.
    • Supportive ICU care, seizure control.
    • Most resolve in days to weeks.
  • Severe high-dose IV MTX with renal failure / accumulation:
    • Glucarpidase (carboxypeptidase G2) cleaves MTX rapidly.
    • Aggressive hydration and urinary alkalinization.
    • Hemodialysis ineffective for MTX clearance.
  • Prevention:
    • Monitor MTX levels; ensure adequate hydration and urinary alkalinization.
    • Folinic acid rescue protocols.
    • Avoid drug interactions that impair MTX clearance (NSAIDs, certain antibiotics).
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman pediatric oncology critical care; Bhojwani methotrexate neurotoxicity review.
SAQ 44 HARD
Describe osmotic demyelination syndrome (central pontine myelinolysis): risk factors, prevention thresholds, and clinical features.
Show structured answer
  • Pathophysiology:
    • Rapid correction of chronic hyponatremia → osmotic shifts → demyelination in oligodendrocyte-rich regions.
    • Central pons most affected (CPM); extra-pontine sites: basal ganglia, thalamus, cerebellum, cortex.
  • Risk factors:
    • Chronic hyponatremia (> 48 hours duration).
    • Severe hyponatremia (Na < 120 mmol/L).
    • Hypokalemia (concurrent).
    • Malnutrition, alcohol use, liver disease, hypophosphatemia.
    • Post-liver transplant.
    • Burn patients with severe hyponatremia.
  • Prevention — Sodium correction limits:
    • Rise < 8–10 mmol/L per 24 h in standard cases.
    • Rise < 6 mmol/L per 24 h in high-risk cases.
    • Hourly Na measurement during active correction.
    • Free water replacement if correcting too fast.
    • DDAVP clamp strategy in severe/chronic cases to prevent overshoot.
  • Clinical features (typically 2–7 days after rapid correction):
    • Quadriparesis (often delayed, biphasic).
    • Dysarthria, dysphagia.
    • Locked-in syndrome in severe pontine cases.
    • Movement disorders if extra-pontine.
    • Variable cognitive change.
  • Imaging:
    • MRI T2 hyperintensity central pons; restricted diffusion early.
    • Extra-pontine involvement of basal ganglia, thalamus.
  • Management:
    • Once established, often irreversible — supportive care, rehab.
    • If detected early (within 24–48 h of overcorrection), consider re-lowering sodium with DDAVP and D5W (controversial efficacy).
    • Address contributing factors: nutrition, electrolytes.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman electrolyte emergencies; Sterns ODS prevention.
SAQ 45 HARD
Outline the multimodal framework for pediatric post-arrest neuroprognostication.
Show structured answer
  • Principle: No single modality reliably predicts outcome; multimodal integration over time.
  • Timing:
    • Defer prognostication for at least 72 h off sedation when possible.
    • Longer for patients undergoing TTM or with confounders (hypothermia, NMB, sedation, hypothermia, metabolic).
  • Modalities:
    • Clinical exam (off sedation):
      • Pupillary reactivity, corneal reflex, motor response.
      • Absent brainstem reflexes at 72 h is concerning but not definitive.
    • Continuous EEG:
      • Reactive background — favorable.
      • Burst-suppression, isoelectric, periodic discharges, electrographic SE — concerning.
      • Status myoclonus on EEG — usually unfavorable.
    • Somatosensory evoked potentials (SSEPs):
      • Bilaterally absent N20 cortical responses at ≥ 72 h post-arrest — highly specific for poor outcome (adult data; pediatric data more limited).
    • MRI brain (preferably 4–7 days):
      • Watershed pattern only — relatively favorable.
      • Basal ganglia / thalamic involvement — worse.
      • Global cortical and deep gray involvement — worst.
      • DWI restriction pseudonormalizes by 7–10 days.
    • Biomarkers:
      • Neuron-specific enolase (NSE) — pediatric thresholds less established than adult.
      • S100B — emerging.
  • Family communication:
    • Multidisciplinary meetings (PICU, neurology, palliative care, social work, chaplaincy).
    • Communicate uncertainty honestly.
    • Avoid early definitive prognostication.
    • Revisit serially as data accumulate.
    • Engage family values and quality-of-life considerations.
Source: Rogers Ch 66; AHA 2019 PALS; Topjian pediatric post-arrest neuroprognostication statement.
SAQ 46 HARD
Outline the management of delayed cerebral ischemia from vasospasm following aneurysmal subarachnoid hemorrhage.
Show structured answer
  • Recognition:
    • Peak day 4–14 post-bleed.
    • New neurologic deficit (focal weakness, aphasia, decreased LOC).
    • Transcranial Doppler: elevated MCA mean velocity (> 120 cm/s; > 200 severe).
    • Lindegaard ratio (MCA/extracranial ICA): > 3 suggests spasm vs hyperemia.
  • Imaging:
    • CT angiography to confirm vasospasm.
    • CT perfusion identifies areas at risk.
    • Catheter angiography is gold standard; therapeutic possibilities available.
  • Medical management:
    • Nimodipine 60 mg PO q4h (adult standard; pediatric extrapolation 30 mg q4h for school age).
    • Maintain euvolemia (NOT triple-H — abandoned due to harm).
    • Induced hypertension if symptomatic and aneurysm secured (norepinephrine to target MAP / SBP).
    • Maintain hemoglobin 9–10 g/dL.
    • Normothermia, normoglycemia, normocapnia.
    • Magnesium and statins not shown to benefit (CONSCIOUS trials negative).
  • Endovascular rescue:
    • For refractory symptomatic vasospasm.
    • Intra-arterial vasodilators (verapamil, nicardipine).
    • Balloon angioplasty for proximal spasm.
  • Aneurysm management:
    • Secure aneurysm before triple-H or hypertension (clipping vs coiling).
    • Reduces rebleed and enables aggressive vasospasm management.
  • Other complications to monitor:
    • Hydrocephalus.
    • Hyponatremia (CSW vs SIADH — important distinction).
    • Cardiac (neurogenic stunned myocardium, Takotsubo).
    • Seizures.
    • Hyperglycemia, fever.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman supplements; Connolly AHA SAH guideline.
SAQ 47 MODERATE
Describe Bickerstaff brainstem encephalitis: clinical features, pathophysiology, and treatment.
Show structured answer
  • Clinical features:
    • Descending paralysis: cranial nerve involvement (ophthalmoplegia, facial weakness, bulbar dysfunction).
    • Ataxia.
    • Hyperreflexia (distinguishing from Miller Fisher syndrome which has hyporeflexia).
    • Decreased level of consciousness — encephalopathy (distinguishing from GBS / MFS which are typically lucid).
    • Often follows viral illness or Campylobacter jejuni infection.
  • Pathophysiology:
    • Antibody-mediated (anti-GQ1b ganglioside) — same family as MFS.
    • GQ1b antibodies bind oculomotor and cerebellar pathways.
    • Overlaps with MFS (some patients evolve from MFS to BBE).
  • Diagnostic workup:
    • Anti-GQ1b antibody (sensitivity ~ 70%).
    • CSF: lymphocytic pleocytosis with elevated protein.
    • MRI: variable; brainstem T2 hyperintensity in some cases.
    • EEG: slowing.
  • Treatment:
    • IVIG 2 g/kg over 5 days — first-line.
    • Plasma exchange — alternative.
    • Steroids — limited evidence.
    • Supportive: airway protection, autonomic monitoring, ICU care.
  • Prognosis:
    • Generally favorable with treatment.
    • Most patients recover over weeks to months.
    • Some residual deficits possible.
  • Differential:
    • GBS / MFS (no encephalopathy).
    • Botulism (descending, dilated pupils, no encephalopathy).
    • Brainstem stroke.
    • Wernicke encephalopathy.
    • Anti-NMDA encephalitis.
Source: Zimmerman Ch 67 supplement; Odaka BBE criteria 2003.
SAQ 48 HARD
Describe Susac syndrome: clinical triad, MRI features, diagnostic approach, and treatment.
Show structured answer
  • Pathophysiology:
    • Autoimmune endotheliopathy affecting precapillary arterioles.
    • Affects brain, retina, and cochlea (small vessel autoimmunity).
  • Clinical triad:
    • Encephalopathy: subacute cognitive change, headache, behavioral, focal deficits.
    • Branch retinal artery occlusion (BRAO): visual loss, often peripheral; sometimes asymptomatic.
    • Sensorineural hearing loss: bilateral, sudden or subacute, low frequencies first.
    • Triad rarely simultaneous; partial presentations are common.
  • MRI features:
    • Multifocal T2 hyperintense lesions in white matter and corpus callosum body.
    • 'Snowball' appearance — round lesions in corpus callosum.
    • Spares optic radiation.
    • Distinguishing from MS: corpus callosum lesions in MS typically more peripheral (callosal-septal interface); Susac affects the body.
  • Other diagnostic studies:
    • Fluorescein angiography: arteriolar wall hyperfluorescence (Gass plaques) — characteristic.
    • Audiometry: low-frequency sensorineural hearing loss.
    • CSF: mild lymphocytic pleocytosis and elevated protein.
    • Brain biopsy in atypical cases.
  • Treatment:
    • Induction: high-dose IV methylprednisolone, then oral prednisone taper.
    • IVIG 2 g/kg.
    • Second-line: rituximab, cyclophosphamide, mycophenolate.
    • Long-term immunosuppression 18–24 months (relapse common).
    • Hearing aids / cochlear implants if needed.
    • Antiplatelet therapy (aspirin) often added.
  • Prognosis:
    • Variable; relapses common.
    • Residual cognitive, visual, hearing deficits common.
    • Early diagnosis and aggressive immunosuppression improve outcome.
Source: Zimmerman Ch 67 supplement; Susac syndrome consensus criteria.
SAQ 49 MODERATE
Outline the workup of opsoclonus-myoclonus-ataxia syndrome (OMAS) in a child, with emphasis on the search for occult neuroblastoma.
Show structured answer
  • Clinical recognition:
    • Triad: opsoclonus + myoclonus + ataxia.
    • Behavioral changes, irritability, sleep disturbance.
    • Onset typically 1–4 years.
  • Tumor workup (mandatory):
    • Urine catecholamines: VMA, HVA.
    • MIBG (metaiodobenzylguanidine) scintigraphy — high sensitivity for neuroblastoma.
