    • Cross-sectional imaging: CT or MRI of chest, abdomen, pelvis (look for adrenal or paraspinal mass).
    • Repeat imaging if initial negative — small occult neuroblastoma possible.
  • Other workup:
    • CSF: anti-neuronal antibodies (anti-Hu, anti-Ri uncommon in pediatric OMAS; OMS often antibody-negative).
    • EEG, MRI brain — usually unremarkable.
  • Treatment:
    • Tumor resection if found.
    • Immunotherapy: corticosteroids (high-dose or pulsed), IVIG, rituximab, cyclophosphamide.
    • ACTH historical option.
    • Long-term immunomodulation often required.
  • Prognosis:
    • Neurodevelopmental sequelae common (cognitive, behavioral, motor).
    • Early aggressive treatment improves outcome.
    • Relapse common.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman pediatric paraneoplastic; OMAS Consensus.
SAQ 50 HARD
Describe alternating hemiplegia of childhood (AHC): genetic basis, clinical features, and management.
Show structured answer
  • Genetic basis:
    • ~70–80% caused by de novo mutations in ATP1A3 (sodium-potassium ATPase α3 subunit).
    • Other genes (ATP1A2) rarely.
  • Clinical features:
    • Episodes of hemiplegia alternating between sides (right then left, or simultaneous bilateral).
    • Duration minutes to days.
    • Triggered by stress, fatigue, bathing, certain foods.
    • Sleep typically resolves attacks.
    • Abnormal eye movements (nystagmus, gaze deviation).
    • Dystonic episodes.
    • Autonomic features.
    • Developmental delay (often severe).
    • Onset typically < 18 months.
  • Diagnosis:
    • Clinical criteria (Aicardi modified).
    • ATP1A3 sequencing.
    • Rule out epilepsy (EEG usually unrevealing), hemiplegic migraine, stroke.
  • Management:
    • Trigger avoidance.
    • Flunarizine (calcium channel blocker; off-label) — most established preventive.
    • Topiramate as adjunct.
    • Sleep induction during prodrome; benzodiazepines for severe attacks.
    • Supportive multidisciplinary care.
    • PT/OT/speech therapy.
    • Educational accommodations.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman; AHC Foundation guidance.
SAQ 51 HARD
Describe Rasmussen encephalitis: diagnostic criteria, immunotherapy options, and the role of hemispherectomy.
Show structured answer
  • Bien 2005 criteria:
    • Part A: clinical focal seizures with or without epilepsia partialis continua + unilateral neurologic deficit + EEG with unihemispheric slowing.
    • Part B: imaging (progressive unihemispheric cortical atrophy and gray/white matter T2 hyperintensity) + pathology (chronic encephalitis with T-cell-dominant infiltrates, microglial nodules, no infectious cause).
    • Diagnosis: 3/3 part A OR 2/3 part B.
  • Clinical course:
    • Stage 1: prodromal with mild focal seizures.
    • Stage 2: acute with frequent focal seizures, often EPC, progressive hemiparesis and cognitive decline (typically over months to years).
    • Stage 3: residual fixed deficits with reduced seizure frequency.
  • Immunotherapy:
    • IVIG (monthly pulses or maintenance).
    • Plasma exchange.
    • Tacrolimus, rituximab.
    • Effects: may slow progression but rarely halt; quality of evidence limited.
  • Hemispherectomy:
    • Functional disconnection or anatomic hemispherectomy.
    • Definitive treatment for seizures; 70–80% seizure freedom in selected patients.
    • Trade-off: anticipated hemiparesis (already developing); preserved or recovered cognition over time, especially in younger patients.
    • Earlier surgery may preserve cognitive function better.
    • Functional hemispherotomy: less morbidity than anatomic, similar efficacy.
  • Multidisciplinary care:
    • Pediatric neurology, neurosurgery, neuropsychology, rehab medicine.
    • Family counseling.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman pediatric epilepsy; Bien 2005 criteria.
SAQ 52 HARD
Describe Wilson disease: clinical presentations, diagnostic workup, and treatment options.
Show structured answer
  • Genetics: Autosomal recessive ATP7B mutations; copper-transporting ATPase; copper accumulates systemically.
  • Clinical presentations:
    • Hepatic (younger): asymptomatic transaminitis, hepatitis, cirrhosis, acute liver failure.
    • Neurologic (adolescent and older): dysarthria, dysphagia, drooling, parkinsonism, dystonia, tremor, choreoathetosis.
    • Psychiatric: behavioral change, mood disorder, psychosis.
    • Kayser-Fleischer rings (slit lamp): nearly always in neurologic disease.
    • Other: hemolytic anemia, renal tubular acidosis, cardiomyopathy.
  • Diagnostic workup:
    • Serum ceruloplasmin: typically low (< 200 mg/L); can be normal in 5–15%.
    • Serum copper: variably low; can be normal.
    • 24-hour urinary copper: elevated (> 1.6 µmol/24 h); diagnostic if > 4 µmol/24 h.
    • Slit-lamp examination for KF rings.
    • Hepatic copper on biopsy: elevated (> 250 µg/g dry weight).
    • ATP7B sequencing for confirmation and family screening.
    • Brain MRI: T2 hyperintensity in basal ganglia, midbrain; 'face of the giant panda' in midbrain.
  • Treatment:
    • Chelation:
      • Penicillamine — traditional but may cause initial neurologic worsening in 50% of neurologic patients.
      • Trientine — alternative; lower risk of worsening; preferred in many centers.
      • Ammonium tetrathiomolybdate — emerging; less worsening.
    • Zinc: Reduces intestinal copper absorption; maintenance after chelation, or first-line in asymptomatic patients.
    • Low-copper diet: Avoid liver, shellfish, mushrooms, chocolate, nuts.
    • Liver transplant: For acute liver failure or end-stage liver disease; can normalize copper.
    • Long-term: Lifelong therapy and monitoring (urine copper, AST/ALT, neuro exam).
    • Family genetic counseling; sibling screening.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman pediatric neurology; AASLD Wilson disease guidance.
SAQ 53 MODERATE
Describe Sydenham chorea: diagnostic criteria, treatment, and long-term management.
Show structured answer
  • Clinical:
    • Acute or subacute onset of chorea (irregular, jerky, distractible, non-stereotyped involuntary movements).
    • Most prominent in arms, face, tongue.
    • Often unilateral or asymmetric.
    • Behavioral/emotional changes (irritability, mood lability, OCD symptoms).
    • Cognitive symptoms in some.
    • Latent period 1–6 months after streptococcal infection.
  • Diagnostic criteria:
    • Major criterion in Jones criteria for acute rheumatic fever.
    • Confirm streptococcal infection (ASO titer, anti-DNase B; recent throat culture/test).
    • Exclude other causes: Wilson disease, drug-induced, anti-NMDA, lupus, vascular.
  • Cardiac workup:
    • Echocardiogram for valvulitis (~ 25–50% with carditis).
    • Repeat at intervals.
  • Treatment:
    • Penicillin (treat streptococcal infection if active).
    • Chorea suppression: valproic acid (first-line in many guidelines), carbamazepine, or haloperidol (efficacy vs side-effect profile).
    • Severe / refractory: methylprednisolone pulse, IVIG, or PLEX (anti-basal-ganglia antibody hypothesis).
  • Secondary prevention:
    • Long-term penicillin prophylaxis to prevent recurrent ARF (oral or IM penicillin G benzathine every 3–4 weeks).
    • Duration: typically 10 years or until age 21 for chorea without carditis; lifelong if cardiac involvement.
  • Prognosis:
    • Usually self-limited (weeks to months).
    • 20–30% recurrence; risk during pregnancy and OCP use ('chorea gravidarum').
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman pediatric infectious / cardiology; Sydenham chorea consensus.
SAQ 54 MODERATE
Describe pediatric brain abscess management, including empiric antimicrobial choice, source control, and surgical timing.
Show structured answer
  • Common pathogens by source:
    • Sinusitis / dental / facial: streptococci, anaerobes, Staphylococcus.
    • Otitis / mastoid: streptococci, anaerobes, Enterobacteriaceae.
    • Hematogenous (endocarditis, cyanotic CHD): streptococci.
    • Post-trauma / surgery: Staphylococcus aureus (incl MRSA), Enterobacteriaceae.
    • Immunocompromised: fungal (Aspergillus, Candida, Mucor), Toxoplasma, Nocardia.
  • Empiric antibiotic regimens:
    • Standard: ceftriaxone + metronidazole.
    • Add vancomycin if MRSA risk or trauma/post-op.
    • Immunocompromised: broad coverage including anti-fungal (voriconazole/amphotericin), anti-Toxoplasma if features.
    • Duration: 6–8 weeks IV typically after drainage; longer for fungal.
  • Surgical management:
    • Indications for drainage: abscess > 2.5 cm, mass effect, midline shift, neurologic deficit, location accessible.
    • Stereotactic aspiration: preferred for deep / eloquent location.
    • Open excision: accessible cortical lesions, multiloculated, post-traumatic with foreign body.
    • Repeat aspiration may be needed if reaccumulation.
    • Culture all drainage; specific antimicrobial therapy guided by results.
  • Source control:
    • ENT for sinus / ear / dental sources.
    • Echocardiogram for endocarditis source.
    • Cardiology / cardiac surgery for cyanotic CHD with right-to-left shunt.
    • Penetrating trauma: surgical exploration / debridement.
  • Adjunctive measures:
    • Dexamethasone (controversial): for significant mass effect; risks include reduced antibiotic penetration.
    • ICP management as needed.
    • Antiepileptic prophylaxis (levetiracetam) for cortical lesions.
    • Serial imaging to confirm response.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman CNS infections; IDSA brain abscess consensus.
SAQ 55 HARD
Describe rhinocerebral mucormycosis: risk factors, recognition, and management.
Show structured answer
  • Causative organisms: Rhizopus, Mucor, Cunninghamella, Lichtheimia — angioinvasive filamentous fungi.
  • Risk factors:
    • Diabetes mellitus, especially DKA with acidosis (most classic).
    • Immunocompromise: HSCT, prolonged neutropenia, hematologic malignancy.
    • Deferoxamine therapy (iron promotes growth).
    • Burns, trauma.
    • Solid organ transplant.
  • Clinical presentation:
    • Rhinocerebral: nasal congestion, facial pain, fever, black eschar in nasal turbinate / palate (necrosis).
    • Ophthalmologic: proptosis, ophthalmoplegia, vision loss (cavernous sinus invasion).
    • Neurologic: cranial nerve palsies, infarction from angioinvasion.
    • Other forms: pulmonary, cutaneous, GI, disseminated.
  • Diagnosis:
    • Direct examination of tissue: broad non-septate hyphae with right-angle branching on histology.
    • Culture (often negative despite invasive disease).
    • PCR available in some centers.
    • Imaging: aggressive sinusitis with bony erosion, intracranial extension, vascular invasion.
  • Management:
    • EMERGENCY surgical debridement — repeated as needed; cornerstone of therapy.
    • Amphotericin B liposomal 5–10 mg/kg/day; high dose preferred for CNS.
    • Step-down: isavuconazole or posaconazole (have anti-Mucorales activity).
    • Address underlying condition: insulin, normalize acidosis, GVHD management, deferoxamine alternative.
    • Iron chelation cessation (if using deferoxamine).
    • Adjuncts: hyperbaric oxygen (limited evidence).
    • Duration: months; tailored to response.
    • Mortality: 30–70%; depends on early recognition and aggressive surgery.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman immunocompromised infections; IDSA mucormycosis.
SAQ 56 HARD
Describe mitochondrial DNA depletion syndromes: subtypes, presentations, and management principles.
Show structured answer
  • Pathophysiology:
    • Nuclear gene mutations affecting mtDNA maintenance, replication, or repair.
    • Reduced mtDNA copy number in affected tissues.
    • Mitochondrial respiratory chain dysfunction.
  • Major subtypes by gene and phenotype:
    GenePhenotype
    POLGAlpers-Huttenlocher, ataxia-neuropathy syndromes, PEO
    DGUOKHepatocerebral form; liver failure, encephalopathy
    MPV17Hepatocerebral; failure to thrive, neurologic regression
    SUCLA2, SUCLG1Encephalomyopathy with methylmalonic aciduria
    RRM2BSevere encephalomyopathy; renal tubulopathy
    TYMPMNGIE (mitochondrial neurogastrointestinal encephalopathy)
    TK2Pure myopathy
  • Diagnosis:
    • Plasma lactate, plasma amino acids, urine organic acids.
    • CK, AST/ALT, ammonia.
    • Muscle biopsy: ragged-red fibers, COX-negative staining, mtDNA quantification (low copy number).
    • Liver biopsy in hepatocerebral forms: mtDNA depletion.
    • Nuclear gene sequencing of suspected genes; whole exome sequencing for unknown.
  • Management:
    • Supportive: nutritional support, hydration during illness, treat infections promptly.
    • Mitochondrial cocktail: CoQ10, riboflavin, thiamine, L-carnitine.
    • Specific therapies:
      • TYMP / MNGIE: dideoxynucleoside replacement; HSCT investigational.
      • TK2 deficiency: nucleoside supplementation (deoxycytidine + deoxythymidine) — emerging therapy.
      • Hepatic forms: liver transplant — controversial in presence of brain disease.
    • Avoid mitotoxic drugs (valproate, statins, linezolid, metformin, propofol prolonged).
    • Genetic counseling.
    • Many forms life-limiting; engage palliative care early.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman metabolic; mtDNA depletion consensus.
SAQ 57 HARD
Describe macrophage activation syndrome (MAS) in pediatric rheumatologic disease: diagnostic criteria, pathophysiology, and treatment.
Show structured answer
  • Pathophysiology:
    • Hyperinflammatory cytokine storm syndrome.
    • Uncontrolled activation of macrophages and CD8+ T cells.
    • Massive cytokine release: IL-1, IL-6, IL-18, IFN-γ, TNF, ferritin.
    • Most common in systemic juvenile idiopathic arthritis (sJIA); also in SLE, Kawasaki, infections, malignancy.
    • Similar to secondary HLH.
  • Ravelli 2016 diagnostic criteria for MAS in sJIA:
    • Febrile patient with sJIA AND
    • Ferritin > 684 ng/mL AND
    • Any 2 of:
      • Platelets ≤ 181 × 10⁹/L.
      • AST > 48 U/L.
      • Triglycerides > 1.9 mmol/L (156 mg/dL).
      • Fibrinogen ≤ 3.6 g/L.
  • Additional features (HLH-2004 criteria for secondary HLH):
    • Fever, splenomegaly, cytopenias ≥ 2 lineages, hyperferritinemia, hypertriglyceridemia / hypofibrinogenemia, hemophagocytosis on bone marrow biopsy, low NK activity, elevated soluble IL-2R.
  • Treatment:
    • Corticosteroids: High-dose dexamethasone or pulse methylprednisolone — first-line.
    • Anakinra (IL-1 antagonist): Increasingly first-line in MAS/sJIA; effective and well tolerated.
    • Cyclosporine: Calcineurin inhibitor; alternative or adjunctive.
    • Etoposide: Per HLH-2004 protocol for severe / refractory cases.
    • Emapalumab (anti-IFN-γ): newer option for refractory secondary HLH.
    • Ruxolitinib (JAK inhibitor): emerging.
    • Supportive ICU care.
    • Treat triggers (infection, drug, malignancy).
  • Prognosis:
    • Mortality 10–25% in MAS/sJIA.
    • Higher in primary HLH and EBV-HLH.
    • Earlier recognition and treatment improve outcome.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman pediatric rheumatology; Ravelli 2016 MAS criteria; HLH-2004 protocol.
SAQ 58 HARD
Describe atypical hemolytic uremic syndrome (aHUS) with neurologic involvement: pathophysiology, diagnosis, and treatment with eculizumab.
Show structured answer
  • Pathophysiology:
    • Complement-mediated thrombotic microangiopathy.
    • Uncontrolled alternative complement pathway activation.
    • Mutations in complement factor H, I, B, MCP/CD46, C3, thrombomodulin (~ 50% identifiable).
    • Anti-factor H autoantibodies in subset.
    • Endothelial damage causes thrombosis in multiple organs.
  • Distinguishing from typical (STEC-) HUS:
    • STEC-HUS: prodromal bloody diarrhea, STEC PCR/culture positive, ~ 90% of pediatric HUS.
    • aHUS: lacks STEC; may have family history, recurrence, atypical features.
    • aHUS can be triggered by infection, pregnancy, drugs.
  • Neurologic features (~ 25–50% in aHUS):
    • Seizures.
    • PRES-like presentation.
    • Stroke (ischemic or hemorrhagic).
    • Encephalopathy.
    • Coma.
  • Workup:
    • Standard HUS workup: CBC, smear, LDH, haptoglobin, renal function.
    • STEC PCR / stool culture (rule out typical HUS).
    • ADAMTS13 (rule out TTP — < 10% activity).
    • Complement panel: C3, C4, factor H, factor I, MCP/CD46 expression.
    • Anti-factor H antibodies.
    • Genetic testing for complement mutations.
  • Treatment with eculizumab:
    • Anti-C5 monoclonal antibody; transformative therapy.
    • Rapid hematologic and renal recovery.
    • Dosing: weight-based, weekly induction × 4–5 doses, then biweekly maintenance.
    • Meningococcal vaccination required (or prophylactic antibiotics) — eculizumab blocks terminal complement.
    • Duration of therapy: indefinite for most; some trials of discontinuation in stable patients.
    • Ravulizumab (longer-acting C5 inhibitor) — newer alternative.
  • Adjunctive:
    • Plasma exchange may bridge until eculizumab.
    • Supportive care: dialysis if AKI, transfusion if needed.
    • Family genetic counseling.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman pediatric renal; aHUS consensus; eculizumab pediatric experience.
SAQ 59 MODERATE
Outline the management of central diabetes insipidus following pituitary/hypothalamic surgery, including the triphasic response.
Show structured answer
  • Recognition of central DI:
    • Polyuria (UOP > 4–5 mL/kg/h sustained).
    • Hypotonic urine (urine SG < 1.005, urine osmolarity < serum osmolarity).
    • Rising serum sodium and osmolarity.
    • No diuretic exposure; glucose normal (rule out osmotic diuresis).
  • Triphasic response (~ 30% of pituitary surgeries):
    • Phase 1 (DI): Immediate postoperatively to ~ 5 days; ADH deficiency.
    • Phase 2 (SIADH): Days 5–14; release of stored ADH from dying posterior pituitary cells; risk of hyponatremia if fluids unrestricted.
    • Phase 3 (DI again): Permanent DI from posterior pituitary destruction.
    • Not all patients exhibit all phases — monitor closely.
  • Acute DI management:
    • DDAVP 0.5–1 µg IV or 1–2 µg intranasal; lower doses in younger children.
    • Replace free water deficit cautiously over 48 hours — avoid rapid Na correction causing cerebral edema.
    • Match fluid intake to urine output + insensible losses.
    • Monitor serum sodium q2h initially.
  • SIADH phase management:
    • Free water restriction.
    • Stop DDAVP.
    • Monitor Na; symptomatic hyponatremia may need 3% saline.
  • Anterior pituitary screening (essential):
    • Cortisol (most critical) — empiric stress-dose hydrocortisone if hypotensive or AM cortisol low.
    • T4, TSH.
    • Growth hormone, sex hormones — outpatient.
  • Long-term:
    • Permanent DDAVP if persistent DI.
    • Hormone replacement as needed.
    • Endocrinology follow-up.
    • Family education on sick-day management and symptoms.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman Ch 59 supplement; post-pituitary surgery consensus.
SAQ 60 MODERATE
Describe convexity (cortical) subarachnoid hemorrhage in pediatrics: differential diagnosis, workup, and management.
Show structured answer
  • Definition: Focal SAH limited to cortical sulci, sparing basal cisterns; non-aneurysmal.
  • Pediatric differential diagnosis:
    • Reversible cerebral vasoconstriction syndrome (RCVS): thunderclap headache; transient segmental vasoconstriction (may be missed on initial angiography; repeat in 1 week).
    • Cerebral venous thrombosis with cortical vein involvement.
    • Dural arteriovenous fistula.
    • Cerebral cortical vein thrombosis.
    • Trauma (often missed history).
    • PRES with hemorrhagic conversion.
    • Vasculitis (PACNS, infectious).
    • Cocaine, sympathomimetic drug use.
    • Reversible cerebral vasoconstriction in postpartum / drug-induced settings.
    • Cortical microvascular disease.
  • Workup:
    • CT/CTA with venous phase.
    • MRI/MRV/MRA.
    • Catheter angiography if suspicion remains (best for small AVF, RCVS).
    • Repeat imaging at 1 week if RCVS suspected.
    • Lumbar puncture: opening pressure (to rule out RCVS hyperpressure features) — caution after SAH.
    • Coagulation workup.
    • Drug screen.
    • Inflammatory workup if vasculitis suspected.
  • Management:
    • Treat underlying cause.
    • RCVS: calcium channel blocker (nimodipine, verapamil) controversial; supportive care; trigger removal.
    • Antiepileptic prophylaxis (levetiracetam) for cortical bleeding.
    • Avoid hypertension and hypotension.
    • Surgical for AVF or vascular malformations identified.
    • Anticoagulation if cortical vein thrombosis.
  • Prognosis:
    • Generally good if cause identified and treated.
    • Repeat imaging at 1 month and 3 months to confirm resolution.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman supplements; convexity SAH review.
SAQ 61 HARD
Outline the recognition and emergency management of glutaric aciduria type I encephalopathic crisis.
Show structured answer
  • Background: GCDH (glutaryl-CoA dehydrogenase) deficiency; autosomal recessive; accumulation of glutaric and 3-hydroxyglutaric acid; striatal neurotoxicity.
  • Crisis trigger: Intercurrent febrile illness, fasting, dehydration, surgery — typically in early infancy (4–18 months).
  • Recognition:
    • Macrocephaly (often pre-existing).
    • Acute encephalopathy + dystonia + dyskinesia after a febrile illness.
    • MRI: bilateral putaminal injury, frontotemporal atrophy with widened opercular cisterns ('bat-wing' sign).
    • Subdural collections from atrophy — can mimic AHT.
    • Urine organic acids: elevated glutaric and 3-hydroxyglutaric acid.
  • Emergency management:
    • Stop protein intake.
    • D10 IV continuous + intralipid 2 g/kg/day for anti-catabolic effect (target glucose 5–8, insulin if needed).
    • IV carnitine 100 mg/kg load then 100 mg/kg/day infusion.
    • Riboflavin (cofactor for some forms).
    • Treat fever aggressively (acetaminophen, cooling).
    • Treat the trigger (antibiotics if infection).
    • Avoid valproate (mitotoxic).
    • Monitor for cerebral edema and ICP.
  • Long-term management:
    • Lysine-restricted diet with arginine supplementation.
    • Daily carnitine.
    • Sick-day protocol: low threshold for ER, aggressive D10 IV, protein restriction.
    • MedicAlert; family education.
    • Genetic counseling.
    • Newborn screening detects most cases.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman metabolic; GA-1 consensus.
SAQ 62 HARD
Describe maple syrup urine disease (MSUD): biochemistry, clinical presentation, emergency management.
Show structured answer
  • Biochemistry: Deficiency of branched-chain α-ketoacid dehydrogenase complex (BCKDH) → accumulation of branched-chain amino acids (leucine, isoleucine, valine) and their ketoacids (BCKAs). Leucine is the most neurotoxic.
  • Subtypes:
    • Classic: severe neonatal onset.
    • Intermediate: presents in infancy/childhood.
    • Intermittent: episodic crises with normal interval.
    • Thiamine-responsive: improves with thiamine.
    • E3-deficient: associated with lactic acidosis.
  • Clinical presentation:
    • Classic: poor feeding, irritability, opisthotonus, encephalopathy within first week of life.
    • Maple syrup / burnt sugar odor in urine, sweat, cerumen (sotolone).
    • Untreated: cerebral edema, seizures, coma, death.
  • Diagnosis:
    • Plasma amino acids: elevated leucine, isoleucine, valine; presence of alloisoleucine (pathognomonic).
    • Urine organic acids: branched-chain ketoacids.
    • Confirmed by molecular testing (BCKDHA, BCKDHB, DBT, DLD).
  • Emergency management:
    • Stop protein intake (NPO).
    • D10 IV continuous + intralipid for anti-catabolic effect (provide adequate caloric intake without protein).
    • Add isoleucine and valine supplementation (paradoxical — needed because they fall too low when leucine is removed; required for protein synthesis to mobilize leucine into protein).
    • Emergent hemodialysis if leucine ≥ 1500 µmol/L or severe encephalopathy.
    • Thiamine trial 100–300 mg/day (thiamine-responsive form).
    • Cerebral edema management (head-up, hyperosmolar, ICP monitoring if severe).
    • Treat triggering illness.
  • Long-term:
    • BCAA-restricted, isoleucine-/valine-supplemented diet under metabolic team.
    • Frequent monitoring of plasma amino acids.
    • Sick-day protocol.
    • Liver transplant for severe / refractory disease (corrects the metabolic defect since enzyme is hepatic).
    • Newborn screening detects most cases.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman metabolic; MSUD consensus.
SAQ 63 HARD
Describe congenital hyperinsulinism: diagnosis and management to protect the developing brain.
Show structured answer
  • Background: Most common cause of severe persistent hypoglycemia in infancy. Brain at risk for permanent injury — must maintain euglycemia.
  • Genetics:
    • KATP channel mutations: ABCC8 (SUR1), KCNJ11 (Kir6.2) — most common; usually severe and diazoxide-unresponsive.
    • Glutamate dehydrogenase (GLUD1): hyperinsulinism-hyperammonemia syndrome; diazoxide-responsive.
    • Glucokinase (GCK), HADH, HNF4A, MCT1, UCP2 — others.
  • Diagnosis (during hypoglycemia):
    • Detectable insulin (suppressed when normal).
    • Suppressed ketones (β-hydroxybutyrate).
    • Suppressed free fatty acids.
    • Glycemic response to glucagon (rises > 1.7 mmol/L).
    • Genetic testing.
  • Brain protection:
    • Maintain glucose > 3.5 mmol/L (some protocols target > 4 mmol/L).
    • High glucose infusion rate often needed (15–25 mg/kg/min).
    • Central line for high concentration glucose.
    • Frequent monitoring (q1h initially).
  • Pharmacologic management:
    • Diazoxide first-line (opens KATP channels): 5–15 mg/kg/day; about half respond.
    • Octreotide (somatostatin analogue): for diazoxide-unresponsive; 5–35 µg/kg/day continuous SC infusion.
    • Glucagon infusion (bridge).
    • Cornstarch / continuous feeds for older infants.
  • Imaging and surgery:
    • 18F-DOPA PET/CT differentiates focal (single lesion, often resectable for cure) vs diffuse disease (whole pancreas).
    • Focal: partial pancreatectomy curative.
    • Diffuse refractory: near-total (95–98%) pancreatectomy.
  • Long-term:
    • Diabetes risk post-pancreatectomy.
    • Pancreatic exocrine insufficiency post-pancreatectomy.
    • Neurodevelopmental follow-up.
    • Genetic counseling.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman pediatric endocrinology; hyperinsulinism consensus.
SAQ 64 HARD
Compare neuroleptic malignant syndrome (NMS) and malignant hyperthermia (MH): mechanisms, triggers, recognition, and treatment.
Show structured answer
  • NMS:
    • Mechanism: dopamine D2 antagonism → muscle rigidity, hyperthermia, autonomic instability.
    • Triggers: neuroleptics (haloperidol, chlorpromazine, atypical), antiemetics (metoclopramide, prochlorperazine).
    • Onset: hours to days after exposure or dose increase.
    • Features: 'lead-pipe' rigidity, hyperthermia, autonomic instability, altered mental status, elevated CK, hyporeflexia.
    • Treatment: stop offending agent; supportive care; benzodiazepines for agitation; dantrolene (1–2.5 mg/kg IV q6h); bromocriptine 2.5–5 mg q6h (D2 agonist); amantadine alternative; aggressive cooling, fluids, rhabdomyolysis management.
    • Mortality 5–20%.
  • MH:
    • Mechanism: uncontrolled Ca²⁺ release from skeletal muscle SR (RYR1, CACNA1S mutations) → sustained muscle contraction, hypermetabolism.
    • Triggers: halogenated volatile anesthetics (sevoflurane, isoflurane, halothane, enflurane, desflurane), succinylcholine.
    • Onset: minutes during or after anesthesia.
    • Features: masseter rigidity (early with sux), hypercarbia, tachycardia, hyperthermia (often late), generalized rigidity, hyperkalemia, rising CK, acidosis.
    • Treatment: STOP triggering agent; switch to TIVA; 100% O₂ high flow; DANTROLENE 2.5 mg/kg IV bolus, repeat q5–10 min (up to 10 mg/kg+); cooling; treat hyperkalemia; manage acidosis, rhabdomyolysis.
    • Family genetic counseling; non-triggering anesthesia for future.
  • Key distinguishing features:
    FeatureNMSMH
    TriggerNeuroleptic / antiemeticVolatile anesthetic / sux
    OnsetHours-daysMinutes-hours intra/post-op
    Rigidity typeLead-pipeSevere generalized, masseter early
    ReflexesHyporeflexiaVariable
    HypercarbiaLateEarly (hallmark)
    GeneticNoYes (RYR1, CACNA1S)
    Specific RxDantrolene + bromocriptineDantrolene + stop trigger
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman pharmacology; MHAUS guidelines; NMS Information Service.
SAQ 65 MODERATE
Describe psychogenic nonepileptic seizures (PNES) in children and adolescents: diagnostic approach and management framework.
Show structured answer
  • Recognition (features suggesting PNES):
    • Side-to-side head movements.
    • Eyes closed with active resistance to opening.
    • Asynchronous limb movements.
    • Pelvic thrusting.
    • Fluctuating responsiveness.
    • Gradual onset.
    • Lack of postictal confusion.
    • Occurrence in presence of audience.
    • Triggered by emotional context.
    • Resistance to multiple AEDs without typical pharmacologic effects.
  • Diagnostic approach:
    • Video EEG capture of typical event is the gold standard — typical event without electrographic correlate.
    • Provocative techniques (suggestion, saline placebo) controversial in pediatrics.
    • Psychiatric evaluation: depression, anxiety, trauma, family stress.
    • Rule out: physiologic nonepileptic events (syncope, cardiac arrhythmia, parasomnias, movement disorders, breath-holding spells).
  • Communication of diagnosis:
    • Honest, non-stigmatizing language.
    • Explain: 'The episodes are real and your distress is real, but the cause is psychological, not electrical. Treatment is different.'
    • Avoid pejorative terms ('pseudoseizures', 'fake').
    • Engage family in supportive role.
  • Management framework:
    • Collaborative care: pediatric neurology + psychiatry/psychology.
    • Cognitive behavioral therapy is first-line.
    • Address comorbid depression, anxiety, trauma (PTSD).
    • Treat underlying triggers: school refusal, abuse, family dysfunction.
    • Avoid AEDs (no electrographic basis; AEDs can cause iatrogenic harm).
    • Wean existing AEDs cautiously if pure PNES diagnosis confirmed.
    • Multidisciplinary team approach with school involvement.
    • Long-term outcome: 70% improve with appropriate care.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman pediatric neurology / psychiatry; PNES consensus.
SAQ 66 HARD
Describe pediatric narcolepsy type 1 (with cataplexy): pathophysiology, diagnostic criteria, and treatment.
Show structured answer
  • Pathophysiology:
    • Autoimmune destruction of hypothalamic hypocretin (orexin) neurons.
    • Strong HLA DQB1*06:02 association.
    • Triggers: viral infection (H1N1, influenza), Pandemrix vaccine in Europe (molecular mimicry).
    • Loss of orexin → instability of wake/sleep boundary.
  • Clinical features (classic tetrad):
    • Excessive daytime sleepiness (EDS) — primary symptom; sleep attacks throughout day.
    • Cataplexy — sudden bilateral loss of muscle tone triggered by strong emotion (laughter, surprise); preserved consciousness; pathognomonic.
    • Sleep paralysis — inability to move at sleep-wake transitions.
    • Hypnagogic / hypnopompic hallucinations — vivid dreaming on falling asleep / waking.
    • Additional pediatric features: weight gain (often dramatic), precocious puberty, behavioral issues.
  • Diagnostic criteria (ICSD-3):
    • EDS for ≥ 3 months AND one of:
      • Cataplexy + multiple sleep latency test (MSLT) with mean sleep latency ≤ 8 min + ≥ 2 sleep-onset REM periods (SOREMPs).
      • OR CSF hypocretin-1 ≤ 110 pg/mL (or < 1/3 normal).
  • Treatment:
    • Behavioral: scheduled naps (2–3 daily), regular sleep schedule, good sleep hygiene.
    • Sodium oxybate (γ-hydroxybutyrate): most effective for cataplexy and EDS; given at bedtime and 2.5–4 h later.
    • Modafinil / armodafinil: wake-promoting; first-line for EDS in some centers.
    • Pitolisant (H3 receptor antagonist): emerging.
    • Stimulants (methylphenidate, amphetamines): wake-promoting; for EDS.
    • SSRIs / SNRIs (venlafaxine, fluoxetine): for cataplexy.
    • Solriamfetol: emerging for EDS.
  • Long-term:
    • Multidisciplinary care: sleep specialist, neurology, psychology, nutrition.
    • School accommodations.
    • Family education and support.
    • Driving counseling when older.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman supplements; ICSD-3 narcolepsy criteria.
SAQ 67 HARD
Describe diffuse intrinsic pontine glioma (DIPG) / H3 K27M-mutant diffuse midline glioma: presentation, diagnosis, treatment.
Show structured answer
  • Background:
    • WHO 2021 classification: H3 K27M-mutant diffuse midline glioma (formerly DIPG, but now includes thalamic and spinal cord midline tumors with same molecular signature).
    • Most aggressive pediatric brain tumor.
    • Median age at diagnosis ~6–7 years.
    • Molecular: H3F3A K27M (or HIST1H3B/C) mutation; ATRX, TP53 co-mutations common.
  • Clinical presentation:
    • Classic triad evolving over weeks:
      • Cranial nerve palsies (especially CN VI, VII).
      • Cerebellar signs (ataxia, dysmetria, nystagmus).
      • Long tract signs (hemiparesis, hyperreflexia).
    • Morning headaches and vomiting from raised ICP (less common; tumor doesn't usually obstruct ventricular system).
    • Behavioral changes.
  • Diagnosis:
    • MRI: expansion of pons with T2/FLAIR hyperintensity; minimal/no enhancement (typically); may engulf basilar artery.
    • Stereotactic biopsy increasingly performed for molecular characterization (controversial historically; now safer with experienced centers).
    • Cell-free DNA from CSF emerging as non-invasive molecular profiling.
  • Treatment:
    • Focal radiotherapy (54 Gy / 30 fractions over 6 weeks): standard of care; provides temporary clinical improvement; no survival benefit.
    • Re-irradiation at progression: limited benefit.
    • Chemotherapy: historically minimal benefit (temozolomide, bevacizumab).
    • Targeted therapy (clinical trials): ONC201 (DRD2 antagonist) — emerging activity; panobinostat (HDAC inhibitor); MEK inhibitors.
    • Convection-enhanced delivery and focused ultrasound: investigational delivery strategies.
    • CAR-T cell therapy (GD2): clinical trial activity.
    • Strong recommendation: clinical trial enrollment.
  • Multidisciplinary care:
    • Pediatric oncology, neurosurgery, radiation oncology.
    • Palliative care from diagnosis — essential.
    • Steroids for peritumoral edema and symptom relief.
    • Speech, swallow, PT/OT.
    • Psychosocial support for child and family.
    • End-of-life planning.
  • Prognosis:
    • Median survival 9–12 months despite treatment.
    • 2-year survival < 10%.
    • Long-term survivors rare and not well understood.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman pediatric oncology; DIPG consensus; WHO 2021 classification.
SAQ 68 MODERATE
Describe pediatric posterior fossa ependymoma: presentation, molecular subtypes, and management.
Show structured answer
  • Epidemiology:
    • Third most common pediatric brain tumor.
    • ~ 70% of pediatric ependymomas are posterior fossa.
    • Peak age < 5 years.
  • Clinical presentation:
    • Insidious headache, vomiting (raised ICP from obstructive hydrocephalus).
    • Cerebellar signs: ataxia, dysmetria.
    • Cranial nerve palsies (extending through foramina).
    • Torticollis sometimes.
  • Imaging:
    • Fourth ventricle mass extending through foramina (Luschka, Magendie) — 'plastic' tumor.
    • Heterogeneous enhancement.
    • Calcifications and cysts in some cases.
    • Hydrocephalus common.
  • Molecular subtypes (methylation-based):
    • PF-A: Younger patients; lateral location; worse prognosis; H3 K27 trimethylation loss.
    • PF-B: Older children/adolescents; midline; better prognosis; chromosomal changes.
    • Both treated similarly currently; subtype helps prognosticate.
  • Management:
    • Emergent CSF diversion (EVD or VP shunt) for hydrocephalus.
    • Gross total resection (GTR) is the single most important prognostic factor — aim for completeness even if requires staged operations.
    • Adjuvant focal radiation (54–59 Gy) is standard:
      • For children ≥ 1 year (defer in < 1 year due to severe neurotoxicity).
      • Defer or modify in < 3 years where possible.
    • Chemotherapy role evolving: less effective than radiation; used in selected cases (especially < 3 years to defer radiation).
    • Anaplastic ependymoma (grade III): more aggressive course.
  • Complications:
    • Posterior fossa syndrome (cerebellar mutism) post-op.
    • Cranial nerve palsies.
    • Hydrocephalus requiring shunt.
    • Late effects of radiation: cognitive, endocrine, growth, secondary malignancy.
  • Outcomes:
    • 5-year survival 60–75% with GTR + radiation.
    • Multiple recurrences common.
    • Salvage with repeat surgery, re-irradiation, chemotherapy.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman pediatric oncology; PF ependymoma molecular consensus.
SAQ 69 HARD
Describe cerebral toxoplasmosis in immunocompromised pediatric host: diagnosis, treatment, and IRIS considerations.
Show structured answer
  • Epidemiology:
    • Toxoplasma gondii reactivation in HIV/AIDS (typically CD4 < 100), HSCT, solid organ transplant.
    • Most common cause of focal CNS lesions in HIV/AIDS.
    • Less common in immunocompetent (congenital toxoplasmosis or acquired ocular).
  • Clinical features:
    • Subacute encephalopathy.
    • Focal neurologic deficits.
    • Headache, fever.
    • Seizures.
    • Raised ICP from mass effect.
  • Imaging:
    • Ring-enhancing lesions, often multiple.
    • Corticomedullary junction and basal ganglia distribution.
    • Surrounding edema.
    • MRI more sensitive than CT.
  • Diagnosis:
    • Toxoplasma IgG positive (chronic infection); IgM rarely positive in reactivation.
    • CSF Toxoplasma PCR (high specificity, moderate sensitivity).
    • HIV testing and CD4 count.
    • Brain biopsy if no response to empiric therapy at 2 weeks.
  • Differential:
    • CNS lymphoma (often single lesion, periventricular, may have ring enhancement; SPECT/PET more avid).
    • Fungal abscess (Aspergillus, Candida, Cryptococcus, Mucor).
    • Mycobacterial (TB tuberculoma).
    • Nocardia.
  • Treatment:
    • Induction: pyrimethamine 200 mg load then 50–75 mg/day + sulfadiazine 4 g/day + folinic acid (leucovorin) 10–25 mg/day for 6–8 weeks. Folinic acid is essential to prevent pyrimethamine bone marrow suppression.
    • Alternative: trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole 5 mg/kg q12h.
    • Alternative: pyrimethamine + clindamycin + folinic acid (if sulfa allergy).
    • Adjunctive corticosteroids for significant mass effect (controversial — can mask CNS lymphoma).
    • Antiepileptic prophylaxis (levetiracetam) if cortical involvement.
  • Maintenance / secondary prophylaxis:
    • Continue maintenance dose until CD4 > 200 for ≥ 6 months on ART.
    • Pyrimethamine 25–50 mg/day + sulfadiazine 2–4 g/day + folinic acid.
  • IRIS (Immune Reconstitution Inflammatory Syndrome):
    • Risk when starting ART in patients with active opportunistic infection.
    • For toxoplasmosis: usually start ART within 2 weeks of toxoplasmosis treatment (less IRIS risk than TB/cryptococcus).
    • Recognize IRIS: paradoxical worsening with immune recovery.
    • Manage with continued OI treatment + steroids for severe IRIS.
Source: Zimmerman supplement; IDSA toxoplasmosis guidelines.
SAQ 70 HARD
Describe pediatric optic pathway gliomas in neurofibromatosis type 1: presentation, indications for treatment, and therapeutic options.
Show structured answer
  • Background:
    • 15–20% of children with NF1 develop optic pathway glioma (OPG).
    • Pilocytic astrocytoma; WHO grade I; usually indolent.
    • Most common pediatric optic glioma.
    • Sporadic OPGs occur in non-NF1 children; behavior may differ.
  • Presentation:
    • Vision loss (often unilateral, progressive).
    • Decreased visual acuity, color vision, visual fields.
    • Optic disc pallor or papilledema.
    • Proptosis (orbital location).
    • Endocrine dysfunction (chiasmal/hypothalamic location): precocious puberty, GH deficiency, central DI.
    • Many asymptomatic — found on MRI surveillance in NF1.
  • Imaging:
    • MRI: enhancing fusiform enlargement of optic nerve(s), chiasm, or optic tracts.
    • Surveillance MRI recommended in NF1 children every 1–2 years until ~ 8 years.
  • Indications for treatment:
    • Progressive vision loss (most important).
    • Progressive tumor growth.
    • Endocrine dysfunction (hypothalamic involvement).
    • Hydrocephalus.
    • Most NF1 OPGs do NOT progress and do NOT require treatment.
  • First-line chemotherapy:
    • Carboplatin + vincristine: standard first-line; ~ 60–70% tumor stabilization.
    • Vinblastine: alternative; similar efficacy.
    • Duration: 1–2 years typically.
  • Targeted therapy:
    • Selumetinib (MEK1/2 inhibitor): approved for inoperable pediatric NF1 low-grade glioma; significant tumor response and visual improvement in many.
    • Trametinib emerging.
    • BRAF inhibitors (dabrafenib, vemurafenib) for non-NF1 BRAF V600E-mutant low-grade gliomas (NF1 OPGs don't have BRAF mutations).
  • Avoid:
    • Radiation therapy in NF1 children — greatly increased risk of secondary malignancy (sarcomas, secondary gliomas) AND moyamoya-like vasculopathy.
  • Surgery:
    • Rarely curative for diffuse infiltrative OPGs.
    • Considered for exophytic or single eye blind for tumor cytoreduction.
  • Multidisciplinary care:
    • Pediatric oncology, ophthalmology, endocrinology, neurosurgery.
    • Visual evoked potentials in non-cooperative children.
    • Endocrine monitoring.
    • Long-term surveillance.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman pediatric oncology; NF1 OPG consensus.
SAQ 71 HARD
Describe Sturge-Weber syndrome: presentation, diagnosis, and management strategies.
Show structured answer
  • Pathophysiology:
    • Neurocutaneous disorder with somatic GNAQ mutation in mosaic distribution.
    • Leptomeningeal capillary-venous angiomatosis (typically unilateral hemispheric).
    • Port-wine stain (facial capillary malformation) in trigeminal V1 distribution.
    • Ipsilateral ocular involvement (glaucoma, choroidal hemangioma).
  • Clinical features:
    • Port-wine stain V1 — increased risk if extends to forehead/eyelid.
    • Epilepsy (often drug-resistant focal, onset infancy).
    • Progressive hemiparesis contralateral to leptomeningeal lesion.
    • Cognitive decline; intellectual disability.
    • Stroke-like episodes / 'venous strokes' from venous stasis.
    • Ipsilateral glaucoma (40–70%).
    • Migraines.
  • Imaging:
    • MRI with contrast: leptomeningeal enhancement on affected hemisphere.
    • Atrophy of affected hemisphere.
    • CT: gyriform 'tramtrack' calcifications.
    • Choroidal hemangioma on orbital MRI.
  • Diagnosis:
    • Clinical + imaging.
    • Somatic GNAQ mutation can be confirmed on biopsy of port-wine stain (research / specialized).
  • Management:
    • Antiseizure: Aggressive AED therapy — levetiracetam, oxcarbazepine, others.
    • Aspirin antiplatelet: 3–5 mg/kg/day — reduces stroke-like events and may reduce seizure burden by improving vascular stasis.
    • Surgical: Hemispherectomy / functional hemispherotomy for drug-resistant epilepsy with established hemiparesis; excellent outcomes similar to Rasmussen surgery.
    • Glaucoma management: Lifelong ophthalmologic surveillance; topical agents, surgery as needed.
    • Port-wine stain treatment: Pulsed dye laser for cosmetic improvement.
    • Cognitive remediation, educational accommodations.
    • Migraine management.
    • Multidisciplinary care: Pediatric neurology, neurosurgery, ophthalmology, dermatology, developmental pediatrics, school services.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman pediatric neurology; SWS consensus.
SAQ 72 HARD
Describe acute hemorrhagic leukoencephalitis (Weston Hurst syndrome) as severe variant of ADEM.
Show structured answer
  • Background:
    • Fulminant hemorrhagic variant of ADEM with rapid progression and high mortality (~ 30–50%).
    • Pathology: perivenular demyelination with hemorrhage, severe inflammation, often necrosis.
    • Post-infectious or post-vaccination; immune complex-mediated.
  • Clinical presentation:
    • Often 1–2 weeks after viral illness or vaccination.
    • Sudden onset, rapidly progressive.
    • Severe encephalopathy progressing to coma over hours-days.
    • Multifocal neurologic deficits, focal seizures, raised ICP.
    • Often fatal within days if untreated.
  • Imaging:
    • MRI: multifocal large hemorrhagic white matter lesions (subcortical, periventricular).
    • Mass effect / edema prominent.
    • SWI/GRE: hemosiderin / hemorrhage.
    • DWI: variable.
  • CSF:
    • Pleocytosis (often prominent, mixed).
    • Elevated protein.
    • RBC count elevated.
    • Negative infectious workup.
  • Diagnosis:
    • Clinical syndrome + imaging + CSF + rule out infection (HSV PCR, viral, bacterial).
    • Biopsy in atypical cases.
  • Treatment (aggressive):
    • High-dose IV methylprednisolone 30 mg/kg/day × 5 days (max 1 g).
    • IVIG 2 g/kg total over 2–5 days.
    • Plasma exchange.
    • Cyclophosphamide for refractory.
    • ICP management: hyperosmolar therapy, ventriculostomy, decompressive craniectomy in select cases.
    • Aggressive multidisciplinary ICU care.
    • Seizure control (levetiracetam, cEEG).
  • Outcomes:
    • High mortality (30–50%).
    • Survivors often with significant deficits.
    • Early aggressive treatment improves outcome.
    • Distinction from classical ADEM matters for prognosis and intensity of treatment.
Source: Zimmerman supplement; AHL / Weston Hurst literature.
SAQ 73 HARD
Describe the framework for weaning multimodal neuromonitoring after severe pediatric TBI.
Show structured answer
  • Phase 1 — Active monitoring (early):
    • ICP, CPP, PbtO₂, cEEG, microdialysis if available.
    • BTF Tier 1–2 protocols active.
    • Targets: ICP < 20, age-appropriate CPP, PbtO₂ ≥ 15–20, normothermia, normocapnia, glucose 100–180, Na 150–160, Hb ≥ 9–10.
  • Phase 2 — Sustained control criteria:
    • ICP < 15 sustained × 24–48 hours on Tier 1 alone (no Tier 2 needed).
    • No clinical or electrographic seizures.
    • Stable hemodynamics without escalating support.
    • Improving or stable multimodal parameters (PbtO₂, L/P ratio).
  • Phase 3 — Graduated de-escalation (over 24–72 hours):
    • Reduce hyperosmolar therapy: allow Na to drift down slowly (< 8–10 mmol/L per 24 hours) — avoid abrupt withdrawal causing rebound ICH.
    • Wean sedation in steps (e.g., 25% reduction every 12–24 hours) — observe for ICP rise, awakening, withdrawal.
    • cEEG continued during sedation weaning.
    • Maintain normothermia (cooling can be removed when stable).
    • Resume oral feeds when extubation possible.
  • Phase 4 — Monitor removal:
    • EVD: clamp for 24 hours; if no ICP rise and CT shows no ventricular enlargement, remove.
    • ICP monitor: remove after 24 hours of clamped EVD or sustained ICP < 15 on minimal therapy.
    • PbtO₂ probe: remove with ICP monitor.
    • cEEG: continue 24–48 hours after sedation withdrawal to capture emergent seizures.
  • Reassessment triggers (re-escalate if):
    • Clinical deterioration (LOC, pupil change, new deficit).
    • Rebound ICP elevation.
    • New seizures.
    • New focal deficits.
    • Repeat CT changes.
  • Family communication:
    • Update on trajectory and plan.
    • Discuss anticipated extubation, transfer to non-ICU bed, rehabilitation.
    • Engage rehab medicine.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman Ch 118; BTF 2019; clinical practice consensus.
SAQ 74 HARD
Describe the management of pediatric subdural empyema, including surgical timing and antibiotic regimen.
Show structured answer
  • Background: Subdural empyema is a neurosurgical emergency. Sources: paranasal sinusitis (most common, frontal sinus), otitis/mastoiditis, post-neurosurgical infection, post-traumatic, hematogenous in cyanotic CHD.
  • Imaging: MRI with DWI is gold standard — crescent or lentiform fluid collection between dura and arachnoid with peripheral enhancement, restricted diffusion within pus (distinguishes from sterile collection).
  • Surgical management:
    • Emergent neurosurgical drainage — craniotomy preferred over burr hole for thick / loculated empyema; burr-hole drainage acceptable for thin / liquid collection.
    • Multiple operations may be needed.
    • Source control: ENT for sinus / mastoid; cardiology for cyanotic CHD source.
  • Antibiotic regimen:
    • Empiric: vancomycin + ceftriaxone + metronidazole IV — covers staphylococci (incl MRSA), streptococci (including microaerophilic), anaerobes, gram-negatives.
    • Adjust per cultures.
    • Duration: 4–6 weeks IV typically.
  • Adjunctive: ICP management, antiepileptic prophylaxis (levetiracetam — cortical inflammation), serial imaging.
  • Mortality and outcome: 10–20% with prompt surgery; significant residual deficits possible.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman CNS infections.
SAQ 75 HARD
Describe the management of infantile spasms in tuberous sclerosis complex.
Show structured answer
  • Background: Infantile spasms (IS) and West syndrome (IS + hypsarrhythmia + developmental regression) — common in TSC. Early control improves cognitive outcome.
  • First-line in TSC: Vigabatrin 50–150 mg/kg/day; superior to ACTH for TSC-associated IS.
  • Vigabatrin monitoring: Irreversible peripheral visual field defects; mandatory ophthalmology / ERG before, during, and after treatment.
  • Duration: Typically 6 months; balance spasm control vs vision toxicity.
  • Second-line if no response in 2 weeks: ACTH (4–8 weeks course), oral prednisolone, topiramate, ketogenic diet, sirolimus / everolimus (mTOR inhibitors).
  • Surgical: Consider tuber resection if focal seizure-onset zone identified by ictal SPECT, MEG, intracranial EEG.
  • mTOR inhibitors: Everolimus / sirolimus address underlying TSC pathway; useful for SEGAs, AMLs, facial angiofibromas, and refractory epilepsy.
  • Multidisciplinary care: Pediatric neurology, neurosurgery, nephrology, dermatology, cardiology, developmental pediatrics, ophthalmology.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman pediatric epilepsy; TSC consensus.
SAQ 76 HARD
Describe X-linked adrenoleukodystrophy: presentation, diagnosis, and HSCT considerations.
Show structured answer
  • Genetics: ABCD1 gene mutation (X-linked); peroxisomal ALD protein dysfunction → very-long-chain fatty acid (VLCFA) accumulation.
  • Phenotypes:
    • Childhood cerebral form (3–12 years, most severe).
    • Adolescent cerebral form.
    • Adrenomyeloneuropathy (AMN) — adult-onset myelopathy.
    • Addison-only (adrenal insufficiency only).
    • Cerebral form in heterozygous females rare but possible.
  • Childhood cerebral ALD presentation:
    • Behavioral / cognitive decline, declining school performance.
    • Vision and hearing loss.
    • Gait disturbance, weakness.
    • Adrenal insufficiency in 80% (often pre-existing).
    • Progressive over months without treatment.
  • Imaging: Posterior parieto-occipital white matter T2 hyperintensity with peripheral contrast enhancement; Loes scoring system grades severity.
  • Diagnosis: Serum VLCFA elevated (C26:0, C24:0/C22:0 ratio); ABCD1 sequencing; newborn screening now available in many states.
  • HSCT considerations:
    • Halts cerebral progression if performed early (Loes score < 10, ideally < 8; asymptomatic or minimally symptomatic).
    • Does NOT reverse established neurologic damage.
    • Mortality and morbidity of HSCT — risk vs benefit.
    • Gene therapy (Skysona / elivaldogene autotemcel) approved for cerebral ALD in select patients.
  • Adrenal replacement: Lifelong hydrocortisone for adrenal insufficiency.
  • Multidisciplinary care: Endocrinology, neurology, ophthalmology, audiology, social work, mental health.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman metabolic; X-ALD newborn screening; ELA-ALD consensus.
SAQ 77 HARD
Describe Niemann-Pick disease type C (NPC): clinical features, diagnosis, and miglustat therapy.
Show structured answer
  • Genetics: Autosomal recessive; NPC1 (95%) or NPC2 (5%) gene mutations affecting intracellular cholesterol trafficking.
  • Distinct from NPC type A/B: Which are acid sphingomyelinase deficiencies (different mechanism, different treatment).
  • Clinical features:
    • Neonatal: cholestatic liver disease, hepatosplenomegaly.
    • Early childhood: developmental delay, ataxia, vertical supranuclear gaze palsy (highly characteristic; downgaze first impaired).
    • School-age: gait deterioration, cognitive decline, ataxia, dystonia.
    • Gelastic cataplexy (laughter-induced muscle tone loss).
    • Seizures.
    • Adolescents / adults: psychiatric symptoms; progressive dementia.
  • Diagnosis:
    • Filipin staining of cultured fibroblasts: free cholesterol accumulation (pathognomonic).
    • NPC1 / NPC2 sequencing.
    • Plasma oxysterols (cholestane-3β,5α,6β-triol; 7-ketocholesterol) elevated — screening biomarkers.
  • Treatment with miglustat:
    • Substrate reduction therapy: glucosylceramide synthase inhibitor; reduces sphingolipid synthesis.
    • Approved for neurologic manifestations of NPC.
    • Stabilizes or slows progression; does not reverse established damage.
    • Dose ~ 200 mg TID (adults; pediatric per BSA).
    • Side effects: GI symptoms, weight loss, peripheral neuropathy, tremor.
  • Emerging therapies: Arimoclomol (heat shock protein 70 inducer), gene therapy in trials.
  • Supportive care: Multidisciplinary; cataplexy management; seizure control; nutrition; PT/OT.
  • Family genetic counseling.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman metabolic; NPC consensus.
SAQ 78 HARD
Describe spinal muscular atrophy type 1: clinical features and the transformation by disease-modifying therapies.
Show structured answer
  • Background: Autosomal recessive SMN1 gene deletion / mutation; severe form causes loss of motor neurons in spinal cord anterior horn. Historically the leading genetic cause of infant mortality.
  • SMA1 clinical features:
    • Onset before 6 months.
    • Never sits independently.
    • Severe hypotonia, frog-leg posture.
    • Areflexia.
    • Tongue fasciculations.
    • Paradoxical breathing (intact diaphragm, weak intercostals).
    • Preserved facial expression and extraocular movements (cranial-bulbar spared).
    • Bulbar weakness with feeding difficulty.
    • Historically: respiratory failure death by age 2.
  • Disease-modifying therapies (transformative):
    • Nusinersen (Spinraza): antisense oligonucleotide; promotes SMN2 inclusion of exon 7 → functional SMN protein. Intrathecal administration; loading × 4, then maintenance every 4 months. Approved all ages.
    • Onasemnogene abeparvovec (Zolgensma): AAV9 gene therapy; single IV dose; delivers functional SMN1 gene. Weight limit ~ 13.5 kg in many centers due to dose and immune response. Side effect: hepatotoxicity, thrombocytopenia, myocarditis.
    • Risdiplam (Evrysdi): oral small molecule; modifies SMN2 splicing; daily dosing. Approved all ages.
  • Pre-symptomatic treatment: Newborn screening detects SMA early; pre-symptomatic treatment achieves near-normal function. Standard of care in screening states.
  • Multidisciplinary care:
    • Pediatric neurology, pulmonology, nutrition, PT, OT, speech, orthopedics, cardiology.
    • Respiratory support: cough assist, BiPAP, tracheostomy if severe.
    • Nutrition: G-tube often needed.
    • Scoliosis monitoring and management.
    • Family genetic counseling.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman pediatric neuromuscular; SMA consensus.
SAQ 79 HARD
Describe pediatric multiple sclerosis: diagnostic criteria, treatment, and considerations specific to children and adolescents.
Show structured answer
  • Epidemiology: Pediatric MS (< 18 years) ~ 3–5% of all MS; mostly adolescents (10–17 years).
  • Phenotype: Relapsing-remitting in > 95% (more relapses than adult; slower disability progression).
  • Diagnostic criteria (McDonald 2017):
    • Dissemination in space (≥ 1 lesion in 2 of 4 typical regions: periventricular, juxtacortical, infratentorial, spinal cord).
    • Dissemination in time (clinical or MRI evidence of new lesion compared to baseline, or simultaneous enhancing and non-enhancing lesions).
    • CSF oligoclonal bands (positive in 70–90% pediatric MS) can substitute for DIT.
  • Differential:
    • ADEM (encephalopathy, monophasic, post-infectious).
    • NMOSD (AQP4 positive; LETM; optic neuritis).
    • MOGAD (MOG positive; relapsing optic neuritis / TM / ADEM).
    • Acute disseminated encephalomyelitis variants.
  • Acute attack treatment:
    • IV methylprednisolone 30 mg/kg/day (max 1 g) × 5 days.
    • Oral taper variable.
    • IVIG or plasma exchange for refractory.
  • Disease-modifying therapy (DMT):
    • Approved pediatric: fingolimod (FDA approved ≥ 10 years).
    • Other commonly used: dimethyl fumarate, glatiramer acetate, natalizumab, ocrelizumab (off-label or in trials).
    • Newer agents in trials: cladribine, ofatumumab.
    • Avoid in MOGAD / NMOSD.
  • Pediatric considerations:
    • Cognitive impact: lifelong; school accommodations.
    • Mental health: depression, anxiety common.
    • Reproductive counseling for adolescents.
    • Vaccination: keep up to date BEFORE starting DMT.
    • Family education and engagement.
    • Transition to adult MS care.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman; pediatric MS consensus; McDonald 2017.
SAQ 80 HARD
Describe pediatric Aicardi-Goutières syndrome: pathophysiology, recognition, and emerging targeted therapy.
Show structured answer
  • Pathophysiology: Type I interferonopathy. Mutations in genes regulating nucleic acid sensing / processing → inappropriate accumulation of self nucleic acids → chronic type I interferon production → CNS and systemic inflammation.
  • Genes: TREX1, RNASEH2A, RNASEH2B, RNASEH2C, SAMHD1, ADAR, IFIH1; autosomal recessive (TREX1 can be AD).
  • Clinical features:
    • Onset infancy.
    • Microcephaly (acquired) or progressive microcephaly.
    • Developmental regression.
    • Spasticity, dystonia.
    • Seizures (variable).
    • Chilblains (acrocyanosis at fingers, toes, ears; cold-induced).
    • Hepatomegaly, thrombocytopenia in some.
    • Sterile pyrexia.
    • Glaucoma in some.
  • Imaging:
    • Basal ganglia and white matter calcifications.
    • White matter abnormalities.
    • Cerebral atrophy.
  • CSF:
    • Chronic lymphocytic pleocytosis (mimicking meningitis).
    • Elevated interferon-alpha activity (diagnostic).
  • Differential: Congenital TORCH infections (CMV, toxoplasmosis, rubella, HSV) — AGS clinically mimics but is non-infectious. Rule out infections first.
  • Treatment:
    • JAK inhibitors (baricitinib): emerging targeted therapy that blocks downstream interferon signaling. Trials showing clinical and biomarker improvement.
    • Supportive care: PT, OT, AED for seizures, nutrition.
    • Multidisciplinary care.
    • Family genetic counseling (autosomal recessive usually).
  • Distinction from other interferonopathies: Same family as STING-associated vasculopathy (SAVI), CANDLE syndrome, others — all respond to JAK inhibition.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman supplements; AGS / interferonopathy consensus.
SAQ 81 HARD
Describe pediatric anti-MuSK myasthenia gravis: distinguishing features and rituximab role.
Show structured answer
  • Background: Anti-muscle-specific kinase (MuSK) antibody is the second most common autoantibody in myasthenia gravis. Most AChR-seronegative MG with positive anti-MuSK.
  • Distinguishing features from AChR MG:
    • Bulbar predominance: oculobulbar, facial, neck flexor, tongue, jaw weakness.
    • Respiratory weakness more common.
    • Less prominent ocular involvement.
    • Focal muscle atrophy (especially tongue) in chronic cases.
    • Female predominance.
  • Diagnostic considerations:
    • Test both AChR and MuSK in suspected MG.
    • Pyridostigmine often LESS effective in MuSK MG (may even worsen — cholinergic crisis risk).
    • Edrophonium test response variable.
    • Decremental response on repetitive nerve stimulation; single-fiber EMG abnormalities.
    • Anti-LRP4 antibody in some MuSK / AChR-negative cases.
  • Treatment:
    • Pyridostigmine: cautious use; reduce or stop if worsening.
    • Corticosteroids: prednisone 1 mg/kg/day, careful titration.
    • IVIG / plasma exchange for acute exacerbation / crisis.
    • Rituximab: Particularly effective in MuSK MG; preferred steroid-sparing agent for refractory disease. Anti-CD20 depletes B cells producing antibody.
    • Other steroid-sparing: azathioprine, mycophenolate, cyclosporine (latter cautious).
    • Thymectomy: Less clearly beneficial in MuSK MG than AChR MG; not routinely recommended.
  • Pediatric considerations: Rare in pediatric population but recognized. Plasma exchange and IVIG safe and effective. Long-term immunosuppression with growth and infection monitoring.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman pediatric neuromuscular; MuSK MG consensus.
SAQ 82 MODERATE
Describe acute post-varicella cerebellitis: presentation, management, and recognition of cerebellar swelling complication.
Show structured answer
  • Background: Post-varicella cerebellitis is the most common form of acute pediatric cerebellitis. Post-infectious (immune-mediated) inflammation of cerebellum following viral illness, typically 1–3 weeks after varicella.
  • Other associated viruses: EBV, mycoplasma, influenza, enterovirus, rotavirus.
  • Clinical features:
    • Sudden onset truncal ataxia, dysmetria, dysarthria, nystagmus, hypotonia.
    • Otherwise alert with intact cognition.
    • Typically no fever at presentation (post-infectious).
    • Age range 2–10 years.
  • Imaging:
    • MRI: cerebellar T2 hyperintensity, swelling, sometimes mass effect.
    • Rare: severe cerebellar swelling causing obstructive hydrocephalus from fourth ventricular compression.
  • CSF: Mild lymphocytic pleocytosis with mildly elevated protein; normal glucose.
  • Differential:
    • ADEM (has encephalopathy).
    • Posterior fossa tumor.
    • Brainstem stroke (rare).
    • Drug intoxication (anticonvulsants, others).
    • Inborn errors (cerebellar ataxias).
    • Acute cerebellar ataxia (idiopathic).
  • Management:
    • Most cases self-limited; supportive care.
    • Inpatient observation if dysphagia, vomiting, or severe presentation.
    • IV methylprednisolone (30 mg/kg/day × 5 days) for severe or persistent disease.
    • IVIG for refractory.
    • Antiepileptic if seizures.
  • Cerebellar swelling complication:
    • Rare but life-threatening.
    • Massive cerebellar swelling → obstructive hydrocephalus + brainstem compression.
    • Requires emergent neurosurgical decompression (suboccipital craniectomy and/or external ventricular drain).
    • Recognition: declining LOC, signs of raised ICP, focal brainstem signs.
  • Recovery: Most recover fully over weeks; some residual mild dysmetria.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman pediatric neurology; cerebellitis consensus.
SAQ 83 HARD
Describe pediatric brain death determination in the context of post-cardiac-surgery hypothermia.
Show structured answer
  • Background: Post-cardiac-surgery hypothermia (DHCA, post-arrest TTM) is a confounder for brain-death determination.
  • Confounder requirements:
    • Core temperature ≥ 36°C for clinical exam and apnea test.
    • Hemodynamic stability — age-appropriate MAP without high-dose vasopressors.
    • No CNS depressant drugs at clinically significant levels.
    • No neuromuscular blockade (TOF 4/4).
    • Correct severe metabolic / endocrine / acid-base derangements.
  • Timing after rewarming:
    • Wait until core temperature ≥ 36°C and clinically stable.
    • For TTM 33°C × 24–48 h, then rewarming over 12+ hours, brain-death determination typically deferred until ≥ 24 hours after rewarming complete.
    • Allow drug clearance from sedatives.
  • Pediatric Shemie 2023 / Nakagawa 2011 criteria:
    • Two independent examinations by two qualified physicians.
    • Inter-exam interval: 12 hours (30 d–18 y); 24 hours (term newborn > 48 h to 30 d).
    • Apnea test: PaCO₂ ≥ 60 mm Hg AND ≥ 20 above baseline with no respiratory effort.
    • Ancillary cerebral blood flow imaging required if exam/apnea cannot be safely completed or confounders persist.
  • Ancillary testing options:
    • CTA / CT perfusion with established protocol.
    • Radionuclide cerebral perfusion scan.
    • MR angiography.
    • 4-vessel cerebral angiography (gold standard but invasive).
    • EEG no longer recommended as primary ancillary per current pediatric guidance.
  • Family communication:
    • Multidisciplinary meetings.
    • Explain confounder exclusion process.
    • Honest about timing of definitive determination.
    • Engage palliative care and chaplaincy.
    • Organ donation discussion separate from declaration team.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman Ch 68; Shemie 2023 Canadian guideline.
SAQ 84 HARD
Describe management of refractory pediatric epilepsy: surgical evaluation pathway and multimodal pre-surgical workup.
Show structured answer
  • Definition: Drug-resistant epilepsy — failure of ≥ 2 appropriately chosen and tolerated antiepileptic drugs to achieve sustained seizure freedom. Approximately 30% of pediatric epilepsy.
  • Surgical candidacy assessment:
    • Identifiable seizure-onset zone amenable to resection.
    • Resection compatible with preservation of essential cortical function.
    • Multidisciplinary epilepsy surgery conference review.
    • Family / patient understanding and consent.
  • Pre-surgical workup (multimodal):
    • Long-term video EEG monitoring: localize seizure-onset zone via ictal and interictal EEG.
    • MRI (3T epilepsy protocol): identify structural lesion (cortical dysplasia, tumor, mesial temporal sclerosis, vascular malformation).
    • PET FDG: identify hypometabolism corresponding to seizure-onset zone.
    • Ictal SPECT (subtracted from interictal): perfusion changes during seizure localize onset.
    • MEG: magnetic source imaging localizes interictal discharges with high spatial accuracy.
    • fMRI: language and motor cortex localization.
    • Diffusion tensor imaging: white matter tract mapping.
    • Neuropsychological evaluation: baseline cognitive function and lateralization (Wada test alternative).
    • Wada test: intracarotid amobarbital procedure for hemispheric language and memory lateralization (less common with fMRI).
  • Invasive monitoring (stage 2):
    • Subdural grids and strips: cortical surface recording, motor / language mapping by stimulation.
    • Stereo-EEG (sEEG): depth electrodes for 3D localization including deep structures (insular, mesial temporal, frontoorbital).
    • Stereotactic depth electrodes for hippocampal involvement.
  • Surgical procedures:
    • Focal resection (lesionectomy, temporal lobectomy, frontal/parietal resection).
    • Multiple subpial transections (eloquent cortex involvement).
    • Functional hemispherotomy / anatomic hemispherectomy (unilateral hemispheric pathology).
    • Corpus callosotomy (drop attacks; palliative).
    • Vagal nerve stimulator (palliative; bilateral / multifocal disease).
    • Responsive neurostimulation (closed-loop; focal seizure-onset zone in eloquent cortex).
    • Deep brain stimulation (emerging).
    • Laser interstitial thermal therapy (LITT) minimally invasive option.
  • Outcomes: Seizure-freedom rate varies by procedure — temporal lobectomy 60–70%, hemispherectomy 70–80%, multifocal pathology lower.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman pediatric epilepsy; ILAE consensus.
SAQ 85 HARD
Outline pediatric prolonged disorders of consciousness (DOC) after severe acquired brain injury: assessment, classification, and rehabilitation.
Show structured answer
  • Spectrum:
    • Coma: unarousable; eyes closed; no sleep-wake cycles.
    • Unresponsive wakefulness syndrome (UWS, formerly vegetative state): wakefulness without awareness; sleep-wake cycles; no consistent purposeful behavior.
    • Minimally conscious state (MCS): minimal but definite behavioral evidence of awareness.
      • MCS+ : language-mediated behaviors (following simple commands).
      • MCS- : limited behaviors (visual pursuit, contingent emotion).
    • Emerged from MCS: functional communication, functional object use.
  • Coma Recovery Scale - Revised (CRS-R):
    • Validated assessment with 6 subscales: auditory, visual, motor, oromotor/verbal, communication, arousal.
    • Serial assessment captures trajectory.
    • Identifies covert awareness in some patients with command-following on imaging.
  • Neurodiagnostic adjuncts:
    • EEG: reactivity, sleep architecture.
    • fMRI / EEG with motor imagery tasks: detect covert awareness ('Owen paradigm' in adults; pediatric adaptations).
    • MRI: structural injury extent.
    • Cognitive evoked potentials (P300, MMN).
  • Pediatric-specific considerations:
    • Greater neuroplasticity than adult; recovery can occur over months to years.
    • Outcome harder to predict than adult — avoid premature determination.
    • Standardized pediatric assessment instruments emerging (CRS-P).
  • Rehabilitation:
    • Early intensive multidisciplinary rehab.
    • Sensory stimulation, PT, OT, speech, music therapy.
    • Pharmacotherapy: amantadine for promoting recovery (limited evidence in pediatric); other neurostimulants.
    • Address medical complications: tone management, seizure control, nutrition, infection, autonomic dysfunction (paroxysmal sympathetic hyperactivity), bowel/bladder, sleep.
    • Family-centered care: education, support, training for transition home.
  • Long-term goals:
    • Functional improvement.
    • Quality of life for patient and family.
    • Ethical engagement: shared decision-making, ongoing reassessment.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman pediatric rehabilitation; DOC consensus.
SAQ 86 HARD
Describe Dravet syndrome: genetic basis, clinical features, AED selection (including drugs to AVOID), and emerging therapies.
Show structured answer
  • Genetics: SCN1A gene mutations (haploinsufficiency of Nav1.1 voltage-gated sodium channel, predominantly expressed in GABAergic interneurons → reduced inhibitory tone → seizures). Most de novo. Some PCDH19, GABRA1, SCN8A, SCN2A.
  • Clinical features:
    • Onset: 6 months, often with prolonged febrile or hemiclonic seizures.
    • Year 2: multiple seizure types (focal, generalized, myoclonic, atypical absence, atonic).
    • Status epilepticus common.
    • Developmental regression / plateau.
    • Photosensitivity.
    • Temperature sensitivity — fever, hot baths trigger seizures.
    • Ataxia, gait abnormalities by school age.
    • Increased sudden unexpected death in epilepsy (SUDEP) risk.
  • First-line AEDs:
    • Valproate.
    • Clobazam.
    • Stiripentol (approved as adjunct for refractory Dravet).
    • Cannabidiol (Epidiolex; FDA approved).
    • Fenfluramine (Fintepla; FDA approved).
    • Topiramate as adjunct.
  • AVOID (worsen seizures and developmental outcome):
    • Carbamazepine.
    • Oxcarbazepine.
    • Phenytoin / fosphenytoin.
    • Lamotrigine.
    • Vigabatrin.
    • Tiagabine.
    • Why: sodium-channel blockers further reduce Nav1.1-dependent inhibitory interneuron function.
  • Acute seizure management: aggressive temperature management, midazolam for prolonged seizures, status epilepticus protocols.
  • Sick-day plan: low threshold for antipyretics; cooling; emergency rescue medication; ER access.
  • Emerging therapies:
    • Antisense oligonucleotides targeting SCN1A (clinical trials).
    • Gene therapy.
    • Adjunctive cannabidiol and fenfluramine well-established.
  • Multidisciplinary care: Neurology, developmental pediatrics, speech, PT/OT, education, mental health, family support.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman pediatric epilepsy; Dravet consensus.
SAQ 87 HARD
Outline the management of pediatric VP shunt malfunction.
Show structured answer
  • Recognition:
    • Headache, vomiting, lethargy, irritability.
    • Declining school performance, cognitive change.
    • New seizures.
    • Bulging fontanelle (infant) or papilledema.
    • Shunt 'pumping' sometimes noted by parents.
    • Recurrent stereotyped symptoms.
  • Workup:
    • Imaging: CT head (or 'fast' MRI sequence in cooperative child to avoid radiation) — compare ventricular size to baseline; new ventriculomegaly = obstruction.
    • Shunt series X-ray: assess catheter for discontinuity, kinking, migration.
    • Shunt tap (CSF analysis) when infection suspected — by neurosurgery typically.
    • Reservoir tap allows measurement of opening pressure and CSF flow.
  • Differential of shunt failure:
    • Proximal obstruction (most common): choroid plexus, blood, debris.
    • Distal obstruction: omental wrap, peritoneal pseudocyst, peritoneal scarring.
    • Catheter discontinuity: from growth or trauma.
    • Valve failure.
    • Infection (typically subacute, with fever and lab abnormalities).
    • Over-drainage: slit ventricle syndrome (collapsed ventricles).
    • Hydrocephalus 'resolved' — re-tap and observe.
  • Management:
    • Neurosurgical consultation emergent.
    • Shunt revision: replace proximal or distal catheter, valve as indicated.
    • External ventricular drain temporarily if surgery delayed.
    • Endoscopic third ventriculostomy (ETV) considered for selected aqueductal stenosis to avoid future shunt.
    • Treat raised ICP medically while preparing for surgery (head-up, hyperosmolar, sedation).
  • Long-term: Multidisciplinary care; family education on warning signs and when to bring to ER.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman neurosurgery.
SAQ 88 HARD
Compare LGS, Dravet, and Doose syndromes: distinguishing clinical features and treatment.
Show structured answer
FeatureLGSDravetDoose
Age onset1–7 years6 months1–5 years
TriggerNone specificFever, hot bathsNone specific
GeneticVariable (TSC, others)SCN1A (~80%)Variable, SLC6A1 in some
Seizure typesTonic, atonic, atypical absence, othersHemiclonic febrile, multiple typesMyoclonic, astatic (drops), atypical absence
EEGSlow spike-wave 1.5–2.5 Hz, paroxysmal fast activityGeneralized spike-wave with photosensitivityGeneralized 2–3 Hz spike-wave
CognitionImpaired, slow regressionRegression, severeVariable; some preserved
First-line AEDsValproate, lamotrigine, rufinamide, clobazam, cannabidiolValproate, clobazam, stiripentol, cannabidiol, fenfluramineValproate, ethosuximide, ketogenic diet
AVOIDCarbamazepine, phenytoin (worsen absence)Sodium channel blockers (carbamazepine, lamotrigine, phenytoin, oxcarbazepine, vigabatrin)Carbamazepine (worsens drops)
Ketogenic dietEffectiveEffectiveParticularly effective
PrognosisRefractory; severe cognitive sequelaeRefractory; SUDEP riskBetter than LGS; ~50% remit

Recognition of specific syndromes drives appropriate AED selection (especially avoiding harmful agents in Dravet) and family counseling regarding prognosis.

Source: Rogers / Zimmerman pediatric epilepsy.
SAQ 89 HARD
Describe pediatric VP shunt infection: pathogens, management strategy, and antibiotic duration.
Show structured answer
  • Pathogens:
    • Staphylococcus epidermidis (~ 50%): biofilm-forming, often subacute.
    • Staphylococcus aureus (incl MRSA, especially with skin breach / abdominal source).
    • Gram-negative (E. coli, Klebsiella, Pseudomonas) — typically from abdominal source.
    • Cutibacterium acnes (formerly Propionibacterium): biofilm-forming, indolent.
    • Other: streptococci, mycobacteria (immunocompromised).
  • Presentation:
    • Shunt malfunction (most common — biofilm on catheter / valve).
    • Fever, irritability.
    • Headache, lethargy.
    • Abdominal pain, distention (peritonitis from VP shunt).
    • Erythema along shunt tract.
    • Pseudocyst around peritoneal catheter (palpable abdominal mass).
  • Workup:
    • CSF from shunt reservoir tap: cell count, glucose, protein, Gram stain, culture (aerobic and anaerobic).
    • Blood cultures.
    • Imaging: CT or MRI for ventricular size; ultrasound for peritoneal pseudocyst.
    • Inflammatory markers (CRP, ESR, procalcitonin).
  • Management — externalization strategy:
    • Externalize the shunt — convert to external ventricular drain to allow continuous CSF drainage and washing of infection.
    • OR: remove shunt entirely, place separate EVD.
    • Antibiotic-loaded catheter not adequately treats biofilm — physical removal essential.
  • Antibiotics:
    • Empiric: vancomycin (for staph including MRSA, S. epidermidis) + ceftazidime or cefepime (for gram-negatives, Pseudomonas).
    • Adjust per cultures.
    • Intrathecal antibiotics (vancomycin) considered for refractory cases.
  • Duration:
    • Continue IV antibiotics until CSF sterile for 2–3 consecutive days.
    • Reimplant shunt after CSF sterilization.
    • Continue antibiotics for 7–14 days post-reimplant.
    • Total course typically 2–4 weeks IV.
  • Long-term: Shunt revision; consideration of endoscopic third ventriculostomy if anatomy favorable to avoid future shunt.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman neurosurgery.
SAQ 90 HARD
Describe Rett syndrome: genetics, clinical features, diagnostic criteria, and emerging therapy (trofinetide).
Show structured answer
  • Genetics: MECP2 gene mutations (X-linked dominant). Classic Rett: most are de novo missense or nonsense MECP2 mutations. Males with MECP2 mutations typically have severe / lethal neonatal encephalopathy; classical Rett primarily affects females.
  • Clinical features (classical Rett):
    • Stage 1 (1–4 years): regression after relatively normal first 6–18 months — loss of acquired skills.
    • Stage 2 (1–4 years): rapid destruction phase — loss of purposeful hand use, stereotyped hand movements (wringing, washing, clapping), gait abnormalities, breathing irregularities (hyperventilation, breath-holding), seizures.
    • Stage 3 (preschool to school age): pseudostationary — some recovery / stabilization; motor improvements may occur.
    • Stage 4 (later school age and beyond): late motor deterioration — scoliosis, gait deterioration, rigidity.
  • Additional features:
    • Deceleration of head growth (acquired microcephaly).
    • Breathing irregularities (hyperventilation, breath-holding, air-swallowing).
    • Bruxism.
    • Autonomic dysfunction.
    • QT prolongation (cardiac surveillance).
    • Seizures.
    • Constipation.
    • Cold extremities.
    • Scoliosis.
  • 2010 Revised Diagnostic Criteria:
    • Period of regression followed by recovery / stabilization.
    • All 4 main criteria: partial / complete loss of purposeful hand skills; partial / complete loss of acquired spoken language; gait abnormalities; stereotypic hand movements.
    • Exclusion criteria: traumatic injury, congenital brain abnormalities, severe perinatal injury, postnatal infections.
    • Supportive criteria for atypical Rett.
  • Trofinetide therapy:
    • Trofinetide (Daybue): IGF-1 derivative; FDA approved 2023 for Rett syndrome (≥ 2 years).
    • Mechanism: synthetic analog of N-terminal tripeptide of IGF-1; modulates astrocyte / glial function, neuroinflammation, synaptic function.
    • Liquid formulation; weight-based oral dose BID.
    • Modest but significant improvements in clinician-rated and parent-rated outcomes (LAVENDER trial).
    • Side effects: GI symptoms (diarrhea most common).
    • First disease-modifying therapy for Rett.
  • Other management:
    • AEDs for seizures.
    • Cardiology surveillance (QT).
    • Scoliosis monitoring; surgery if severe.
    • GI: constipation management.
    • PT, OT, speech.
    • Family education and support.
    • Multidisciplinary care.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman pediatric neurology; Rett syndrome consensus.
SAQ 91 HARD
Outline pediatric mucopolysaccharidosis (MPS) Type IH (Hurler syndrome): clinical features, diagnosis, and HSCT considerations.
Show structured answer
