  • Background: MPS-I — α-L-iduronidase (IDUA) deficiency → glycosaminoglycan (heparan / dermatan sulfate) accumulation. Severe form: Hurler (MPS-IH); attenuated: Hurler-Scheie (MPS-IH/S), Scheie (MPS-IS).
  • Hurler clinical features:
    • Coarse facial features by 1 year.
    • Dysostosis multiplex (skeletal abnormalities — claw hand, kyphosis, short stature).
    • Corneal clouding.
    • Hepatosplenomegaly.
    • Cardiac valve disease (regurgitation), cardiomyopathy.
    • Recurrent respiratory infections, sleep apnea (airway abnormalities).
    • Hearing loss (chronic otitis, sensorineural).
    • Cognitive decline starting around age 2; severe ID.
    • Joint stiffness, contractures.
    • Hernias (inguinal, umbilical).
    • Premature death untreated (5–10 years).
  • Diagnosis:
    • Urine GAG screen: elevated; presence of heparan and dermatan sulfate.
    • Leukocyte / fibroblast IDUA enzyme activity: markedly reduced.
    • IDUA gene sequencing.
    • Newborn screening for MPS-I now available in many states.
  • HSCT considerations:
    • Allogeneic HSCT before age 2 (ideally) for severe Hurler.
    • Provides functional enzyme via donor leukocytes that cross-correct other cells.
    • Slows / prevents CNS disease.
    • Cross-corrects systemic disease (cardiac, hepatic, etc.).
    • Mortality and morbidity of HSCT — 10–15% transplant-related mortality.
    • Post-HSCT: cognitive function depends on age of transplant — earlier is better.
    • Skeletal and cardiac valve disease less responsive to HSCT than CNS — multidisciplinary management.
    • Newer: ex-vivo lentiviral gene therapy in trials.
  • Enzyme replacement therapy (laronidase):
    • Treats systemic features (joint range, organomegaly, cardiac valve).
    • Does NOT cross BBB — no benefit for CNS disease.
    • Indicated for attenuated forms (Hurler-Scheie, Scheie) or as bridge / adjunct to HSCT in severe Hurler.
    • Continued post-HSCT in some centers until donor chimerism / enzyme levels adequate.
  • Multidisciplinary management: Metabolic, hematology / HSCT, cardiology, ENT, orthopedics, ophthalmology, audiology, neurology, developmental pediatrics.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman metabolic; MPS-I consensus.
SAQ 92 HARD
Compare Angelman and Prader-Willi syndromes — same chromosomal region with opposite imprinting.
Show structured answer
  • Chromosomal region: Both involve chromosome 15q11-q13 imprinted region. Angelman: maternal expression of UBE3A. Prader-Willi: paternal expression of multiple genes in the region (SNRPN, MAGEL2, NDN, etc.).
  • Genetic mechanisms:
    MechanismAngelmanPrader-Willi
    Most commonMaternal deletion 15q11-q13 (~ 70%)Paternal deletion 15q11-q13 (~ 70%)
    2nd most commonUBE3A mutation (10%)Maternal uniparental disomy (25%)
    Less commonPaternal UPD; imprinting defectImprinting defect
  • Clinical features comparison:
    FeatureAngelmanPrader-Willi
    Tone (early)HypotoniaSevere neonatal hypotonia
    GrowthMicrocephaly (acquired)Short stature; failure to thrive then obesity
    CognitionSevere IDMild-moderate ID
    BehaviorFrequent unprovoked laughter ('happy disposition'), excitableHyperphagia from age 2, behavioral rigidity, skin-picking, anxiety
    EatingNormalHyperphagia → severe obesity
    EyesStrabismusAlmond-shaped
    HandsStereotyped hand-flappingSmall, narrow
    GaitAtaxic, jerky 'puppet-like'Normal except late
    SpeechSeverely impaired, often absentBetter than expected for cognition
    SeizuresCommon; characteristic EEG pattern (high-amplitude rhythmic δ with intermittent sharp waves)Less common
    SleepDisturbedOSA from obesity
    EndocrineNormalGH deficiency, hypogonadism
  • Diagnosis:
    • DNA methylation analysis of SNRPN locus — distinguishes both AS and PWS by characteristic methylation pattern.
    • FISH for 15q11-q13 deletion.
    • Microsatellite markers for UPD.
    • UBE3A sequencing for AS (when methylation normal).
    • Mother's DNA for imprinting defect studies.
  • Management:
    • Both: multidisciplinary care; developmental services.
    • AS: AEDs (multiple); melatonin for sleep; AAC for communication; PT/OT/speech.
    • PWS: growth hormone replacement; rigorous weight management (locked food, scheduled meals, supervision); behavioral management; psychiatric (anxiety, OCD); sex hormone replacement at puberty.
  • Family genetic counseling:
    • Most are sporadic.
    • UBE3A point mutations and imprinting center deletions can recur — counsel accordingly.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman pediatric genetics.
SAQ 93 MODERATE
Describe pediatric Lennox-Gastaut syndrome: features, etiologies, and treatment options.
Show structured answer
  • Definition (triad):
    • Multiple seizure types (atonic, tonic, atypical absence, GTC, focal).
    • EEG with slow spike-and-wave (< 2.5 Hz) and paroxysmal fast activity during sleep.
    • Cognitive impairment.
  • Etiologies:
    • Symptomatic / structural (~ 75%): perinatal injury, tuberous sclerosis complex, cortical dysplasia, infection, trauma, neurocutaneous disorders.
    • Genetic (~ 5–10%): SCN8A, SLC6A1, GRIN2A, KCNQ2, etc.
    • Cryptogenic (~ 15–25%).
    • Evolution from infantile spasms / West syndrome.
  • Clinical features:
    • Onset 1–7 years (peak 3–5).
    • Drop attacks (atonic seizures) — high injury risk.
    • Status epilepticus (tonic or atypical absence).
    • Cognitive plateau / regression.
    • Behavioral / psychiatric issues.
  • Treatment (broad-spectrum AEDs):
    • Valproate (often first; avoid in young infants for hepatotoxicity).
    • Lamotrigine — effective but slow titration (rash risk).
    • Topiramate.
    • Rufinamide (approved for LGS).
    • Clobazam.
    • Cannabidiol (Epidiolex; FDA approved for LGS).
    • Fenfluramine (Fintepla; approved for LGS in addition to Dravet).
    • Felbamate (severe / refractory; aplastic anemia and liver toxicity risk).
    • Brivaracetam.
  • Avoid (worsen absence and tonic):
    • Carbamazepine, oxcarbazepine, phenytoin.
    • Tiagabine.
    • Gabapentin / pregabalin.
  • Non-pharmacologic:
    • Ketogenic diet: effective, especially for atonic drops.
    • Corpus callosotomy: palliative for drop attacks.
    • VNS: ~ 30–60% with ≥ 50% seizure reduction.
    • Focal resection: for identifiable seizure-onset zone.
    • DBS / RNS: emerging.
  • Multidisciplinary management:
    • Neurology, neuropsychology, developmental pediatrics, family support.
    • Educational accommodations.
    • Behavioral and psychiatric support.
    • SUDEP risk counseling.
  • Prognosis: Refractory; significant cognitive sequelae; lifelong epilepsy.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman pediatric epilepsy.
SAQ 94 HARD
Outline emergency management of late-onset urea cycle disorder unmasked by catabolic stress.
Show structured answer
  • Recognition:
    • Older child / adolescent with new-onset encephalopathy.
    • Precipitants: surgery, anesthesia, high-protein meal, fasting, valproate, steroid administration, infection.
    • Symptoms: vomiting, irritability, confusion, ataxia, seizures, coma.
    • Plasma ammonia elevated (often markedly).
    • Family history of unexplained child deaths or 'liver problems' (especially in maternal male relatives for OTC).
    • Respiratory alkalosis often (ammonia stimulates respiratory drive).
  • Emergency management:
    • Step 1 — Stop catabolic state:
      • NPO, no protein.
      • D10 IV continuous + intralipid 2 g/kg/day for high caloric support.
      • Insulin if hyperglycemic.
    • Step 2 — Nitrogen scavengers (Ammonul):
      • Sodium phenylacetate + sodium benzoate.
      • 250 mg/kg IV bolus over 90–120 min, then 250 mg/kg/day infusion.
      • Adjusts per ammonia and product label.
    • Step 3 — Arginine HCl:
      • 250 mg/kg IV bolus over 90 min, then 250 mg/kg/day infusion.
      • NOT in NAGS deficiency (use N-carbamylglutamate) or arginase deficiency.
    • Step 4 — Dialysis:
      • Hemodialysis preferred over CRRT for rapid ammonia removal.
      • Indications: ammonia > 250 µmol/L, rising despite scavengers, severe encephalopathy.
    • Step 5 — Diagnostic workup:
      • Plasma amino acids: elevated glutamine and alanine; specific patterns (citrulline, argininosuccinate, ornithine) localize the block.
      • Urine orotic acid: elevated in OTC and downstream; normal in CPS-1 and NAGS.
      • Plasma acylcarnitines, urine organic acids (rule out organic acidemia).
      • Genetic testing.
    • Avoid:
      • Valproate (mitochondrial inhibitor — fatal hepatic failure).
      • Steroids (increase catabolism — worsen).
      • Protein-containing fluids.
  • Long-term:
    • Protein-restricted diet.
    • Oral scavengers (sodium phenylbutyrate or glycerol phenylbutyrate).
    • Liver transplant for severe forms.
    • Sick-day protocol with sick day rules.
    • MedicAlert bracelet.
    • Family genetic counseling.
    • Multidisciplinary metabolic clinic.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman metabolic; UCD consortium.
SAQ 95 MODERATE
Describe acute intermittent porphyria: triggers, recognition, and emergent management.
Show structured answer
  • Background: Autosomal dominant HMBS gene mutations → porphobilinogen deaminase deficiency → accumulation of δ-aminolevulinic acid (ALA) and porphobilinogen (PBG) during attacks.
  • Triggers:
    • Drugs (CYP450 inducers — heme synthesis stimulants):
      • Barbiturates, phenytoin, carbamazepine, valproate.
      • Sulfonamides.
      • Hormonal (estrogens, progesterones).
      • Rifampin, griseofulvin.
    • Fasting / caloric restriction.
    • Alcohol.
    • Surgery / stress.
    • Infection.
    • Hormonal changes (cyclic in females).
  • Acute attack features (4 Ps):
    • Pain: Severe abdominal pain (often diffuse, no peritoneal signs).
    • Polyneuropathy / peripheral: Motor weakness, autonomic dysfunction (tachycardia, BP swings).
    • Psychiatric: Agitation, confusion, hallucinations, depression.
    • Port-wine colored urine: Dark / red urine on standing (oxidation of porphobilin).
    • Plus: seizures, hyponatremia (SIADH).
  • Diagnosis (during attack):
    • Urine porphobilinogen (PBG) markedly elevated (must be obtained during acute attack).
    • Urine ALA elevated.
    • Plasma porphyrins also elevated.
    • Genetic testing: HMBS sequencing.
  • Emergency management:
    • Stop offending drug.
    • Hospitalize.
    • Carbohydrate loading: D10 IV (10% glucose) — suppresses heme synthesis pathway.
    • IV hemin / heme arginate: 3–4 mg/kg/day × 4 days (preferred over carbohydrate alone for severe attacks). Provides negative feedback on heme synthesis pathway.
    • Pain control: opioids preferred (NSAIDs and acetaminophen safe).
    • Treat hyponatremia (water restriction; correct slowly to avoid ODS).
    • Treat seizures: levetiracetam, gabapentin (safe); avoid phenytoin, carbamazepine, valproate (porphyrogenic).
    • Tachycardia: β-blockers if needed.
    • Address underlying triggers.
  • Long-term:
    • Avoid triggers.
    • Givosiran (RNA interference therapy targeting ALA synthase 1): approved for prevention of recurrent attacks; reduces attack rate.
    • Multidisciplinary care; family genetic counseling.
    • MedicAlert bracelet.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman metabolic; APF AIP consensus; givosiran NEJM.
SAQ 96 HARD
Describe pediatric Chiari I malformation: presentation, indications for decompression, and surgical approach.
Show structured answer
  • Anatomy: Cerebellar tonsils descending below foramen magnum (typically defined as ≥ 5 mm in children > 5 years; ≥ 4 mm in younger).
  • Common associations:
    • Syringomyelia (~ 40–60%).
    • Tethered cord.
    • Scoliosis (especially with syringomyelia).
    • Craniosynostosis syndromes.
  • Symptomatic presentations:
    • Occipital / suboccipital headache, worse with cough / Valsalva ('Valsalva headache') — most characteristic.
    • Neck pain.
    • Sensory disturbance / weakness (from syringomyelia).
    • Cranial nerve dysfunction (lower cranial nerves: dysphagia, dysarthria, sleep apnea).
    • Vertigo, nystagmus.
    • Sleep-disordered breathing.
    • Brainstem signs (rare).
  • Indications for surgical decompression:
    • Symptomatic Chiari I with: cough-triggered headache, syringomyelia, neurologic deficits, sleep apnea, scoliosis from syringomyelia.
    • Asymptomatic Chiari I generally observed; refer for symptoms or imaging progression.
  • Surgical approach:
    • Suboccipital craniectomy with C1 laminectomy.
    • Posterior fossa dural opening / decompression.
    • Duraplasty with graft (allograft or autograft).
    • Some surgeons leave dura intact; others open and expand — varies by surgeon.
    • Goal: enlarge foramen magnum and posterior fossa, relieve crowding, restore CSF flow.
  • Outcomes:
    • Headache relief: 70–80%.
    • Syringomyelia improvement: 50–70%.
    • Scoliosis stabilization (with syringomyelia improvement).
    • Complications: CSF leak, pseudomeningocele, meningitis, cerebellar swelling, hydrocephalus.
  • Pre-operative workup:
    • MRI brain + cervical spine + thoracic spine for syrinx.
    • Sleep study if sleep-disordered breathing suspected.
    • Polysomnography especially with central sleep apnea features.
    • Multidisciplinary planning.
  • Long-term:
    • Surveillance MRI.
    • Some require revision surgery for persistent / recurrent syrinx.
    • Multidisciplinary follow-up.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman neurosurgery.
SAQ 97 HARD
Describe pediatric lysosomal storage disorders: classification, common presentations, and approach to diagnosis.
Show structured answer
  • Background: Lysosomal storage disorders (LSDs) result from deficiencies in lysosomal enzymes / transporters → accumulation of substrate within lysosomes → progressive cellular dysfunction.
  • Classification by substrate:
    ClassExamples
    Mucopolysaccharidoses (MPS)Hurler, Hunter, Sanfilippo, Morquio, Maroteaux-Lamy, Sly
    SphingolipidosesTay-Sachs, Sandhoff, Gaucher, Niemann-Pick A/B, Krabbe, MLD, Fabry
    Glycoproteinosesα-mannosidosis, β-mannosidosis, fucosidosis, sialidosis, galactosialidosis
    MucolipidosesML-II (I-cell), ML-III
    Glycogen storage disease type IIPompe disease (acid α-glucosidase)
    Niemann-Pick CCholesterol trafficking (not enzymatic)
    Neuronal ceroid lipofuscinoses (NCL)Batten disease subtypes (CLN1-CLN14)
  • Common pediatric presentations:
    • Coarse facial features + organomegaly + dysostosis multiplex → MPS, mannosidosis, ML-II.
    • Macrocephaly + regression → Canavan, GM1 gangliosidosis, mucopolysaccharidoses.
    • Cherry-red macular spot → Tay-Sachs, Sandhoff, Niemann-Pick A, GM1 gangliosidosis, sialidosis.
    • Hepatosplenomegaly + neurodegeneration → Niemann-Pick A, Gaucher (severe), GM1 gangliosidosis.
    • Vertical gaze palsy → Niemann-Pick C.
    • Peripheral neuropathy + regression → Krabbe, MLD.
    • White matter disease → MLD, Krabbe, Canavan, X-ALD.
    • Adrenal insufficiency + white matter → X-ALD.
    • Vision and hearing loss + regression → NCL (Batten).
    • Cardiomyopathy + hypotonia (infantile) → Pompe.
  • Diagnostic approach:
    • Clinical pattern recognition critical for initial workup.
    • Urine screen: GAGs (MPS), oligosaccharides (oligosaccharidoses), sialic acid (sialidoses).
    • Enzyme assays in leukocytes / fibroblasts.
    • Specific biomarkers: oxysterols (NPC), VLCFA (X-ALD), urine sulfatides (MLD).
    • Genetic testing for confirmation.
    • Imaging: characteristic patterns for specific disorders.
    • Skin / conjunctival / liver / brain biopsy in difficult cases.
    • Newborn screening expanding (Pompe, MPS-I, Krabbe, X-ALD in many states).
  • Treatment principles (varies by disorder):
    • Enzyme replacement therapy (ERT): MPS-I, MPS-II, MPS-IVA, MPS-VI, Pompe, Gaucher, Fabry.
    • Substrate reduction therapy: NPC (miglustat), MPS-III (in trials).
    • HSCT: severe MPS-I (Hurler) before age 2, Krabbe pre-symptomatic, X-ALD pre-cerebral.
    • Gene therapy: emerging for many (Pompe, MPS-I, MLD, others in trials).
    • Supportive care for all.
    • Multidisciplinary management essential.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman metabolic; lysosomal storage disorder consensus.
SAQ 98 HARD
Describe the management of pediatric drowning with cardiac arrest, including TTM, neuroprotection, and prognostication.
Show structured answer
  • Initial:
    • Standard PALS BLS/ALS during transport and on arrival.
    • 'Not dead until warm and dead' if cold-water drowning with hypothermia.
    • ECMO consideration for severe hypothermic arrest at experienced center.
  • Targeted temperature management (TTM):
    • THAPCA trials: either 33°C or 36.8°C × 48 h acceptable.
    • Strict normothermia × 5 days total (avoid fever).
    • Cooling: cooling blanket, IV cold fluids, intravascular cooling devices.
  • Post-arrest bundle (AHA 2019 PALS):
    • SpO2 94–99%; avoid hyperoxia and hypoxia.
    • Age-appropriate BP; avoid hypotension.
    • PaCO2 35–45 mm Hg; avoid hyperventilation.
    • Glucose 100–180 mg/dL; avoid hypo- and hyperglycemia.
    • Na 140–155 (avoid hyponatremia in TBI).
    • cEEG ≥ 24 h; treat seizures aggressively (often non-convulsive).
    • Mechanical ventilation with lung-protective strategy.
  • Multimodal prognostication (≥ 72 h off sedation):
    • Clinical exam.
    • cEEG: reactive background and absence of malignant patterns favorable.
    • SSEPs (when feasible): bilaterally absent N20 highly specific for poor outcome in adults (pediatric data more limited).
    • MRI brain (4–7 days): pattern of injury (watershed favorable, basal ganglia/thalamic worse, global worst).
    • NSE biomarker (less established pediatric thresholds).
    • Topjian AHA pediatric post-arrest statement: integration over time required; avoid single-modality decisions.
  • Family communication:
    • Multidisciplinary meetings.
    • Honest uncertainty acknowledgment.
    • Engage palliative care early.
    • Avoid premature definitive prognostication.
    • Cultural and spiritual support.
  • Pediatric specifics:
    • Greater neuroplasticity may yield better recovery.
    • Recovery from minimally conscious state can occur over months to years.
    • Coma Recovery Scale-Revised for serial assessment.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman post-arrest; AHA 2019 PALS; THAPCA.
SAQ 99 HARD
Describe the recognition and management of organophosphate / carbamate poisoning in a child.
Show structured answer
  • Pathophysiology: Acetylcholinesterase inhibition → acetylcholine accumulation → muscarinic, nicotinic, and CNS effects. Organophosphates (insecticides, nerve agents) bind irreversibly; carbamates reversibly.
  • Recognition (cholinergic toxidrome):
    • Muscarinic (SLUDGE/Killer Bs):
      • Salivation, Lacrimation, Urination, Defecation.
      • GI distress, Emesis.
      • Bronchorrhea, Bronchospasm.
      • Bradycardia.
    • Nicotinic: muscle fasciculations, weakness, paralysis, tachycardia (sympathetic ganglia).
    • CNS: agitation, seizures, coma, central respiratory failure.
    • Death from respiratory failure (bronchorrhea + bronchospasm + paralysis + central depression).
  • Specific agents:
    • Organophosphate insecticides: malathion, parathion, chlorpyrifos.
    • Carbamates: aldicarb, methomyl, carbofuran.
    • Nerve agents: sarin, soman, tabun, VX (military / terrorist use).
    • Aging time variable: sarin ~ 5 hours; dimethyl agents (malathion) ~ 3 hours; phosphorothioates (parathion) hours-days.
  • Emergency management:
    • Provider safety: PPE essential (decontaminate self before patient contact).
    • Decontamination: remove clothing (bag in double containers), wash skin with soap and water.
    • Airway: intubate if needed for respiratory failure or bronchorrhea / weakness.
    • Atropine: 0.05 mg/kg (min 0.1 mg, max 2 mg) IV, repeat every 5 minutes; titrate to clearance of bronchorrhea and pulmonary compliance (NOT pulse). Adult doses may need 20+ mg total in severe cases.
    • Pralidoxime (2-PAM): 25–50 mg/kg IV bolus over 30 minutes, then continuous infusion 10–20 mg/kg/h. Reactivates AChE before aging. Must give before aging completes — variable by agent.
    • Benzodiazepines: midazolam IV for seizures (high doses may be needed for nerve agent).
    • Continuous infusions if prolonged toxicity (long-acting OPs).
  • Adjunctive:
    • Activated charcoal if oral ingestion within 1 hour.
    • Treat hyperthermia.
    • Cardiac monitoring.
    • Watch for intermediate syndrome (proximal weakness 1–4 days post-acute resolution).
    • Watch for delayed neuropathy (1–3 weeks).
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman toxicology; AAPCC, MHAUS for mass casualty.
SAQ 100 HARD
Describe button battery ingestion in pediatrics: complications, recognition, and management.
Show structured answer
  • Background: Modern button batteries (3V lithium cells, often 20 mm CR2032 used in remotes / hearing aids / toys) cause electrolysis-induced tissue burns when lodged.
  • Complications:
    • Esophageal (most dangerous):
      • Esophageal ulceration within 2 hours.
      • Perforation, mediastinitis, fistula formation.
      • Tracheoesophageal fistula.
      • Aortic-esophageal fistula (catastrophic hemorrhage; high mortality).
      • Spondylodiscitis.
      • Vocal cord paralysis.
    • Gastric: Less urgent if asymptomatic, but ulceration possible.
    • Small intestinal: Rare but can cause perforation.
    • Nasal: Septal perforation; orbital cellulitis; CSF leak.
    • Aural: Tympanic membrane perforation; ossicle erosion; facial nerve damage.
  • Recognition:
    • Drooling, vomiting, dysphagia, refusing food.
    • Stridor / hoarseness.
    • Chest / abdominal pain.
    • Hematemesis or melena.
    • Often witnessed ingestion or unwitnessed but family finds missing battery.
    • Pediatric ingestion peaks in toddlers; spike at holidays (toys).
  • Imaging:
    • Plain X-ray AP and lateral: shows 'double-rim' or 'halo' sign on AP, 'step-off' on lateral (button battery vs coin).
    • If location uncertain or symptomatic, get neck-chest-abdomen views.
  • Management:
    • Esophageal: Emergent endoscopic removal within 2 hours of ingestion.
    • While preparing for OR: honey 5 mL every 10 min (up to 6 doses) for > 1 year — coats battery, neutralizes alkaline burns, slows tissue damage. Sucralfate slurry alternative.
    • NOT for < 1 year (botulism risk from honey).
    • Gastric or small intestine: Most pass; serial X-rays; watch for symptoms.
    • Nasal / aural: Immediate ENT removal.
    • Post-removal: Evaluate for esophageal injury (endoscopy at time of removal; possibly repeat in 1–2 days). Antibiotic prophylaxis for esophageal injury. Surveillance for fistula formation.
  • Family education / prevention:
    • Secure all battery compartments.
    • Store batteries out of reach.
    • Recognize ingestion symptoms.
    • Emergency action if ingestion suspected.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman pediatric emergency; National Battery Ingestion Hotline.
SAQ 101 HARD
Outline the management of severe tricyclic antidepressant (TCA) overdose.
Show structured answer
  • Pathophysiology:
    • Sodium channel blockade (cardiac): wide QRS, arrhythmias.
    • α-adrenergic blockade: hypotension.
    • Anticholinergic: mental status changes, tachycardia, mydriasis, hyperthermia.
    • Decreased seizure threshold.
    • GABA receptor effects.
  • Cardiotoxic features:
    • QRS widening > 100 ms: increased risk of arrhythmia.
    • QRS > 160 ms: high risk of seizure and arrhythmia.
    • R wave in aVR > 3 mm: classic finding.
    • Risk of torsades de pointes from QT prolongation.
    • Bradyarrhythmia or tachyarrhythmia.
  • Initial management:
    • Airway protection if altered mental status.
    • Continuous cardiac monitoring.
    • IV access.
    • Activated charcoal if presentation within 1 hour and airway protected.
  • Sodium bicarbonate (cornerstone):
    • Indication: QRS > 100 ms, ventricular arrhythmia, hypotension.
    • Dose: 1–2 mEq/kg IV bolus; repeat as needed.
    • Goal: serum pH 7.50–7.55; QRS narrowing.
    • Mechanism: increased extracellular Na overcomes channel blockade; alkalinization reduces TCA binding to channels.
    • Continuous infusion (150 mEq NaHCO3 in 1 L D5W at 250 mL/h adjusted) for ongoing instability.
  • Seizures:
    • Benzodiazepines (lorazepam, midazolam).
    • Levetiracetam.
    • AVOID phenytoin (Na channel blocker — worsens TCA cardiotoxicity).
  • Hypotension:
    • IV fluids.
    • Norepinephrine first-line vasopressor (vs dopamine — preserved α effect with TCA-induced α blockade).
    • Sodium bicarbonate also helps hypotension.
  • Refractory cardiotoxicity:
    • IV lipid emulsion (20% ILE 1.5 mL/kg bolus then 0.25 mL/kg/min).
    • ECMO for refractory cardiac arrest.
    • Glucagon (less established).
  • Hyperthermia: Aggressive cooling; sedation.
  • Disposition:
    • Asymptomatic with no QRS prolongation × 6 hours: medical clearance OK.
    • Any QRS widening or symptoms: cardiac monitoring 24 hours.
    • Psychiatric evaluation when stable.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman toxicology.
SAQ 102 HARD
Describe high-altitude cerebral edema (HACE) and high-altitude pulmonary edema (HAPE): recognition, management, and prevention.
Show structured answer
  • Background:
    • Altitude sickness spectrum: acute mountain sickness (AMS) → HACE / HAPE (severe).
    • Typically > 3500 m altitude; risk increases with rapid ascent.
    • Pathophysiology: hypoxia-induced vasodilation (HACE), pulmonary vasoconstriction with leak (HAPE).
  • HACE recognition:
    • AMS symptoms progressing to:
      • Ataxia (cardinal sign).
      • Altered mental status.
      • Severe headache (poor response to analgesics).
      • Decreased level of consciousness.
      • Death if untreated.
    • MRI: vasogenic cerebral edema, especially splenium of corpus callosum (microbleeds on SWI).
  • HAPE recognition:
    • Exertional dyspnea, then dyspnea at rest.
    • Frothy sputum (sometimes blood-tinged).
    • Crackles bilaterally.
    • Hypoxia.
    • Pulmonary edema on CXR (often patchy, asymmetric).
    • Can occur with HACE.
  • Treatment (HACE and HAPE):
    • Descent: Most effective intervention; 500–1000 m descent often dramatically improves symptoms.
    • Supplemental oxygen.
    • Hyperbaric chamber (Gamow bag): Portable; simulates lower altitude when descent impossible.
    • Dexamethasone: 4–8 mg IV/PO q6h for HACE; helps HAPE adjunct.
    • Nifedipine: 30 mg PO q12h sustained release for HAPE (reduces pulmonary artery pressure).
    • Phosphodiesterase inhibitors (sildenafil, tadalafil) for HAPE: reduces pulmonary vasoconstriction.
    • Supportive care: Mechanical ventilation, ICU monitoring for severe cases.
  • Prevention:
    • Gradual ascent (max 300–500 m gain per night above 3000 m).
    • Rest day every 2–3 days.
    • Acetazolamide 2.5 mg/kg/dose (max 125 mg) PO BID starting day before ascent — accelerates acclimatization through metabolic acidosis stimulating ventilation.
    • Avoid alcohol; hydrate well.
    • Awareness of symptoms.
  • Pediatric considerations:
    • Children may not verbalize symptoms well; observe for behavioral / activity changes.
    • Acetazolamide pediatric dosing.
    • Ascent rates more conservative.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman environmental medicine.
SAQ 103 HARD
Describe the recognition and management of pediatric exertional heat stroke.
Show structured answer
  • Definition: Severe heat illness with core body temp > 40°C (104°F) and altered mental status, from exertion in heat.
  • At-risk populations:
    • Adolescent athletes (football, wrestling, track) — especially during weight cuts.
    • Military trainees.
    • Outdoor laborers.
    • Cystic fibrosis (impaired sweat).
    • Heat-vulnerable medications (diuretics, anticholinergics, lithium).
  • Risk factors:
    • Dehydration.
    • Inadequate acclimatization (sudden hot environment exposure).
    • Obesity.
    • Inappropriate clothing.
    • High humidity (reduces evaporative cooling).
    • Recent illness, fever.
    • Prior heat illness episode.
    • Use of stimulants (caffeine, ADHD meds, supplements).
  • Clinical features:
    • Core body temp > 40°C.
    • Altered mental status: confusion, agitation, ataxia, seizures, coma.
    • Hot, dry skin (in classical) or sweaty skin (in exertional — sweating may persist).
    • Tachycardia, hypotension.
    • Tachypnea.
    • Rhabdomyolysis with myoglobinuria.
    • AKI.
    • Liver dysfunction.
    • DIC.
    • Cardiogenic shock.
  • Treatment (rapid cooling is critical):
    • Cold water immersion (gold standard for exertional heat stroke): immerse in ice water bath; cool to 38.5°C in 30 minutes.
    • Alternative methods: ice-water lavage (gastric, bladder, peritoneal); evaporative cooling (cool mist + fan); cooling blankets.
    • Endovascular cooling for severe / refractory.
    • IV fluids: Normal saline; titrate to urine output (avoid overload causing pulmonary edema).
    • Rhabdomyolysis management: Aggressive IV fluids; urinary alkalinization with bicarbonate (target urine pH ≥ 7); monitor CK, creatinine.
    • Benzodiazepines: For seizures and agitation.
    • Avoid antipyretics: Not effective (heat stroke is not pyrogen-mediated).
    • Avoid dantrolene: Not effective.
    • ICU monitoring: Cardiac, neurologic, renal, hepatic.
    • Hemodialysis if severe AKI or electrolyte derangement.
  • Outcomes:
    • Mortality 5–10% with prompt aggressive cooling; up to 50% without.
    • Long-term: cardiac, hepatic, renal sequelae possible.
  • Prevention:
    • Acclimatization (gradual heat exposure 10–14 days).
    • Adequate hydration.
    • Cooling breaks during exercise.
    • Avoid extreme heat training.
    • Pre-exercise wet bulb globe temperature (WBGT) monitoring.
    • Education on warning signs.
    • Medical clearance after episode before return to play.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman environmental medicine.
SAQ 104 HARD
Describe pediatric beta-blocker overdose management, particularly the role of high-dose insulin euglycemia therapy (HIE).
Show structured answer
  • Pharmacology of toxicity:
    • β1-blockade: bradycardia, hypotension, hypoglycemia (especially propranolol).
    • β2-blockade: bronchospasm, peripheral vasoconstriction.
    • Membrane-stabilizing activity (Na channel blockade): especially propranolol — wide QRS, cardiac arrhythmias.
    • Lipophilic agents (propranolol, metoprolol, carvedilol): CNS effects — seizures, coma.
    • Reduced cyclic AMP → reduced cardiac contractility.
  • Clinical features:
    • Bradycardia, hypotension, hypoglycemia.
    • Lethargy, seizures (lipophilic agents).
    • Bronchospasm.
    • QRS widening with propranolol.
    • AV blocks.
    • Cardiac arrest possible.
  • Initial management:
    • Airway protection if needed.
    • Continuous cardiac monitoring.
    • IV access.
    • Activated charcoal if within 1 hour.
    • Glucose for hypoglycemia.
    • Atropine — often inadequate alone for beta-blocker toxicity.
  • Glucagon (first-line):
    • Bypasses β-receptor; activates adenyl cyclase directly.
    • Increases cAMP, restores cardiac contractility.
    • Dose: 0.05–0.1 mg/kg IV bolus (adult 5–10 mg); repeat or infusion 1–10 mg/h.
    • Side effects: nausea/vomiting (common); reflex hypoglycemia.
  • High-dose insulin euglycemia therapy (HIE) — most effective:
    • Mechanism: insulin provides positive inotropic effect and improves myocardial glucose uptake at high doses.
    • Insulin 1 U/kg IV bolus, then continuous infusion 0.5–1 U/kg/h (some up to 10 U/kg/h).
    • Concurrent D10 or D20 IV at high rate to maintain euglycemia (4–8 mmol/L); often need 1–2 g/kg/h glucose.
    • Monitor glucose q15 min initially.
    • Replace K (insulin shifts K intracellular); replace Mg as needed.
    • Effects may take 30–60 minutes to manifest.
  • Calcium:
    • Calcium gluconate or chloride IV — partial Ca channel blockade contributes to toxicity.
    • Dose: 30–60 mg/kg calcium gluconate IV slow.
  • Lipid emulsion therapy:
    • For lipophilic agents (propranolol, metoprolol, carvedilol).
    • 20% lipid emulsion 1.5 mL/kg IV bolus, then 0.25 mL/kg/min × 30–60 min.
    • 'Lipid sink' theory.
  • NaHCO3: For QRS widening (Na channel blockade from propranolol).
  • Refractory:
    • Vasopressors: norepinephrine, epinephrine.
    • Cardiac pacing.
    • ECMO for refractory shock / cardiac arrest.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman toxicology.
SAQ 105 HARD
Describe pediatric primary amebic meningoencephalitis from Naegleria fowleri.
Show structured answer
  • Background:
    • Free-living thermophilic amoeba (Naegleria fowleri); warm freshwater (lakes, hot springs, rivers, swimming pools, poorly chlorinated, neti pots with tap water).
    • Found globally; cases in southern US, Asia, Australia, Europe.
    • Entry via nasal mucosa (during diving, swimming); ascends along olfactory nerve through cribriform plate to brain.
    • Pathology: hemorrhagic necrotizing meningoencephalitis with frontal lobe predominance.
  • Clinical features (rapidly progressive):
    • Onset 1–9 days after exposure.
    • Severe frontal headache.
    • Nausea, vomiting, fever.
    • Stiff neck, photophobia.
    • Rapidly progressive encephalopathy, seizures, coma.
    • Death within 1–18 days from onset (median ~ 5 days).
    • Olfactory dysfunction in some.
  • Imaging:
    • MRI: cerebral edema, frontal predominance, basal ganglia involvement, hemorrhagic foci.
    • CT may show edema only.
  • CSF:
    • Bloody / xanthochromic; elevated opening pressure.
    • PMN predominance (mimics bacterial meningitis).
    • Low glucose; elevated protein.
    • Negative bacterial cultures.
    • WET MOUNT: motile trophozoites visible (KEY DIAGNOSTIC FINDING — must request specifically and examine fresh).
    • Wright stain: morphology.
    • PCR (Naegleria-specific) confirmatory.
  • Treatment (aggressive combination):
    • Amphotericin B (deoxycholate or liposomal): IV high dose + intrathecal/intraventricular.
    • Miltefosine: oral; obtain via CDC emergency access — has improved survival.
    • Rifampin: oral.
    • Azithromycin: IV.
    • Fluconazole: IV (synergistic with amphotericin).
    • Therapeutic hypothermia (32–34°C) reported in survivors.
    • Aggressive ICP management.
    • Critical: contact CDC immediately (1-770-488-7100) for miltefosine and consultation.
  • Survivors and outcome:
    • Mortality > 95% even with treatment.
    • Survivors (rare) typically had aggressive multi-drug therapy + miltefosine + therapeutic hypothermia + early diagnosis.
    • Long-term sequelae common in survivors.
  • Prevention:
    • Avoid getting water in nose during freshwater swimming (especially warm water).
    • Use nose clips when swimming in warm freshwater.
    • Don't disturb sediment.
    • Use only distilled or sterile water for nasal irrigation (neti pots).
    • Properly chlorinate pools.
Source: Zimmerman supplement; CDC PAM guidance.
SAQ 106 HARD
Describe acute carbamazepine overdose management, including multi-dose activated charcoal and indications for hemodialysis.
Show structured answer
  • Pharmacology of toxicity:
    • Na channel blockade: cardiac (QRS widening, arrhythmia), CNS (seizures).
    • Anticholinergic effects: tachycardia, mydriasis, dry mucous membranes.
    • CNS depression: drowsy, coma.
    • Enterohepatic recycling: prolonged toxicity due to bile excretion and reabsorption.
    • Cerebellar effects: ataxia, nystagmus (horizontal).
    • SIADH from chronic therapy.
  • Clinical features:
    • Drowsiness, ataxia, nystagmus.
    • Coma.
    • Seizures.
    • QRS widening, ventricular arrhythmias.
    • Hypotension.
    • Hyperthermia.
    • Mydriasis.
    • Hyponatremia.
  • Levels:
    • Therapeutic: 4–12 µg/mL.
    • Toxic: > 20 µg/mL.
    • Severe: > 40 µg/mL.
    • Level alone doesn't determine treatment — clinical status priority.
  • Management:
    • Initial: Airway protection, cardiac monitoring, IV access, supportive care.
    • Multi-dose activated charcoal (MDAC):
      • Carbamazepine has significant enterohepatic recycling.
      • Repeated activated charcoal doses interrupt this recycling.
      • Initial dose 1 g/kg, then 0.5 g/kg every 4 hours.
      • Requires intact / protected airway.
      • Continues until level < 20 µg/mL or clinical improvement.
    • Sodium bicarbonate:
      • For QRS widening > 100 ms.
      • 1–2 mEq/kg IV bolus; repeat as needed.
      • Mechanism similar to TCA overdose (Na channel blockade reversal).
    • Seizures:
      • Benzodiazepines (lorazepam, midazolam).
      • Levetiracetam.
      • AVOID phenytoin (also Na channel blocker — additive toxicity).
    • Hemodialysis / hemoperfusion:
      • Indications: severe toxicity (level > 40 µg/mL), refractory cardiovascular toxicity, hepatic failure, severe encephalopathy.
      • Hemoperfusion historically preferred but hemodialysis with high-flux filter often used.
      • Combined MDAC + extracorporeal removal in severe cases.
    • Hypotension:
      • IV fluids.
      • Norepinephrine.
      • Sodium bicarbonate.
    • Hyperthermia: Aggressive cooling.
    • Lipid emulsion: For severe refractory cardiac toxicity (lipophilic drug).
  • Disposition:
    • ICU monitoring 24+ hours.
    • Psychiatric evaluation when stable.
    • Toxicology consultation recommended.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman toxicology.

SAQ 108 HARD
Outline the management of pediatric serotonin syndrome.
Show structured answer
  • Background: Excess serotonergic activity at 5-HT2A receptors. Caused by serotonergic drug overdose, combination of serotonergic agents, or accidental ingestion.
  • Hunter criteria (diagnosis):
    • Serotonergic agent + ≥ 1 of:
      • Spontaneous clonus.
      • Inducible clonus + agitation or diaphoresis.
      • Ocular clonus + agitation or diaphoresis.
      • Tremor + hyperreflexia.
      • Hypertonia + temp > 38°C + ocular or inducible clonus.
  • Common offending agents / combinations:
    • SSRIs (fluoxetine, sertraline, etc.).
    • SNRIs (venlafaxine, duloxetine).
    • MAOIs.
    • TCAs.
    • Tramadol.
    • Meperidine.
    • Triptans.
    • Linezolid (MAO inhibitor effect).
    • St. John's wort.
    • Lithium.
    • Combination ingestions or overdoses.
  • Clinical features (triad):
    • Autonomic dysfunction: tachycardia, hypertension, hyperthermia, mydriasis, sweating, diarrhea.
    • Neuromuscular excitation: clonus (lower limbs prominent), hyperreflexia, tremor, rigidity (lower limb > upper).
    • Mental status changes: agitation, restlessness, confusion, hallucinations.
  • Management:
    • Stop offending agent(s).
    • Supportive care:
      • IV fluids.
      • Aggressive cooling (cold IV fluids, cooling blanket, evaporative cooling); avoid antipyretics (not effective).
      • Cardiac monitoring.
      • Continuous SpO2 monitoring.
    • Sedation:
      • Benzodiazepines (lorazepam, midazolam) — first-line for agitation and muscle hyperactivity.
      • Sedation may need to be deep to control hyperthermia.
    • Cyproheptadine (5-HT2A antagonist):
      • Specific antidote for serotonin syndrome.
      • Dose: 4–8 mg PO/NG q6h (max 32 mg/day in adults; pediatric: 0.25 mg/kg/dose).
      • Only available oral / NG.
    • Hyperthermia (severe, > 41°C):
      • Intubation + neuromuscular blockade (rocuronium) — paralysis stops muscle heat production.
      • Avoid succinylcholine (rhabdomyolysis worse).
      • Active cooling continued.
    • Avoid:
      • Antipyretics (not effective; serotonin syndrome is not pyrogen-mediated).
      • Dantrolene (used for malignant hyperthermia; not for serotonin syndrome).
      • Bromocriptine (used for NMS).
      • Physostigmine (no role).
    • ICU monitoring: Mortality 5–15% in severe cases.
  • Distinguish from NMS:
    • Serotonin syndrome: clonus, hyperreflexia, lower-limb predominant, rapid onset (hours), recent serotonergic exposure.
    • NMS: lead-pipe rigidity, hyporeflexia, slower onset (days), neuroleptic exposure.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman toxicology.
SAQ 109 HARD
Describe the multidisciplinary management of pediatric hypothermic cardiac arrest from cold-water drowning.
Show structured answer
  • Pre-hospital:
    • Standard PALS with modifications for hypothermia.
    • Begin CPR; continue during transport.
    • Gentle handling — fragile myocardium in severe hypothermia (avoid jarring).
    • 'Not dead until warm and dead' — continued CPR despite asystole if cold (core T < 30°C).
    • Activate cooling-capable referral center if ECMO available.
  • In-hospital:
    • Aggressive rewarming during continued CPR:
      • Passive rewarming: remove wet clothes, warm blankets.
      • Active external rewarming: forced warm air (Bair Hugger), heated blankets.
      • Active internal rewarming:
        • Warm IV fluids (40°C).
        • Warm gastric lavage.
        • Warm bladder irrigation.
        • Peritoneal dialysis with warm fluid.
        • Hemodialysis warming.
      • ECMO (V-A): best for severe hypothermic arrest at experienced centers; allows rapid core rewarming + circulatory support during myocardial recovery.
    • Goal rewarming rate: 1–2°C/h initially; faster if ECMO.
  • PALS modifications:
    • Epinephrine may be ineffective at core T < 30°C — may withhold or space doses.
    • Defibrillation: 3 attempts then continue CPR until rewarmed to ≥ 30°C, then standard.
    • Bradyarrhythmias common during rewarming — observe before treating.
  • Post-ROSC (if achieved):
    • TTM 33–36.8°C × 48 h (THAPCA).
    • Strict normothermia × 5 days total.
    • cEEG monitoring.
    • Avoid hyperoxia, hyperthermia, hypotension, hypoglycemia.
    • Multimodal neuroprognostication ≥ 72 h off sedation.
  • Cold-water drowning outcomes:
    • Better outcomes than warm-water drowning at equivalent downtime.
    • Hypothermia BEFORE hypoxic-ischemic insult is protective (rapid cooling).
    • Remarkable neurologic outcomes possible with prolonged downtime + aggressive resuscitation in select cases.
    • Outcomes depend on: downtime, water temperature, prompt CPR, ECMO availability.
  • Family communication:
    • Honest about uncertainty during acute resuscitation.
    • Multidisciplinary meetings during ICU course.
    • Engage palliative care if neurologic recovery uncertain.
    • Avoid premature decisions during early hypothermic phase.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman environmental injury; AHA cold-water drowning guidance.
SAQ 110 HARD
Compare autoimmune encephalitis variants: anti-NMDAR, anti-LGI1, anti-GABA-B, anti-AMPA, anti-CASPR2, anti-glycine receptor.
Show structured answer
AntibodyPhenotypeTumor associationDistinguishing features
Anti-NMDARMulti-stage: psych prodrome → dyskinesia → seizures → autonomic → comaOvarian teratoma (50% post-pubertal females)Orofacial dyskinesias; extreme delta brush EEG; younger patients; post-HSV phenomenon recognized
Anti-LGI1Limbic encephalitis: amnesia, complex partial seizures, faciobrachial dystonic seizures (FBDS)Thymoma (uncommon, less in pediatric)FBDS pathognomonic; hyponatremia from SIADH 40–60%; PNES often misdiagnosed
Anti-GABA-BLimbic encephalitis with severe / refractory seizures, memory loss, confusionSCLC in adults; rare paraneoplastic in pediatricOften status epilepticus; refractory seizures
Anti-AMPA (GluR1/2)Limbic encephalitis with psych/behavioral and seizuresThymoma, breast, lung, ovary in adultsFrequent paraneoplastic; tumor screen important

Anti-glycine receptor (PERM)Progressive encephalomyelitis with rigidity, myoclonus, hyperekplexia, autonomicLymphoma in some adultsStiff person spectrum; brainstem dysfunction

  • Common features: CSF antibody is most reliable diagnostic test; multi-domain neuropsychiatric symptoms; respond to first-line immunotherapy (steroids + IVIG + PLEX) and second-line (rituximab, cyclophosphamide).
  • Workup principles: CSF and serum antibody panel; brain MRI; EEG (look for characteristic patterns); tumor screen by age and antibody profile; psychiatric symptoms should not delay autoimmune evaluation.
  • Treatment principles: Tumor resection if present often curative or markedly beneficial; immunotherapy escalation if no response in 10–14 days; long-term immunomodulation for relapsing course.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman supplements; autoimmune encephalitis consensus.
SAQ 111 HARD
Describe subacute sclerosing panencephalitis (SSPE): pathogenesis, stages, diagnosis, treatment.
Show structured answer
  • Pathogenesis:
    • Slow viral encephalitis from defective measles virus persistence.
    • Wild-type measles virus mutates, loses outer envelope, persists intracellularly.
    • Higher risk if measles infection in first 2 years of life.
    • Latency typically 6–8 years (range 1–15+ years).
    • Now rare with vaccination but resurges with vaccine coverage decline.
  • Clinical stages (Jabbour):
    • Stage I (1–6 months): behavioral / cognitive changes, declining school performance, personality changes.
    • Stage II: myoclonus (especially axial — head drops; brief), seizures (mixed types), progressive cognitive decline.
    • Stage III: rigidity, spasticity, decerebration, autonomic dysfunction.
    • Stage IV: vegetative state.
    • Typically fatal in months to a few years from onset.
  • Diagnosis:
    • EEG: pathognomonic 'Radermecker complexes' — periodic high-amplitude generalized slow waves with brief silent intervals; correspond to myoclonic jerks; ~ 4–8 second cycle.
    • CSF: elevated measles antibody titer (oligoclonal bands positive); CSF/serum measles antibody ratio elevated (intrathecal synthesis).
    • Serum measles IgG elevated.
    • MRI: progressive cortical and white matter atrophy; T2 hyperintensities in periventricular and deep white matter; eventual diffuse atrophy.
    • Brain biopsy / autopsy: inclusion bodies (Dawson bodies), gliosis, neuronophagia.
  • Treatment:
    • No curative therapy.
    • Isoprinosine (inosiplex) oral — modest evidence; widely used.
    • Intraventricular interferon-alpha — small studies suggest delay progression.
    • Ribavirin investigational.
    • Symptomatic: anticonvulsants for seizures; antispastics; nutrition; supportive.
    • Palliative care engagement; family support.
  • Prevention: Measles vaccination (MMR or MMRV) is critical; high vaccine coverage prevents SSPE.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman supplements; SSPE consensus.
SAQ 112 HARD
Describe sport-related concussion: recognition, management, and graded return-to-play protocol.
Show structured answer
  • Definition: Concussion is mild traumatic brain injury (mTBI) induced by biomechanical forces, with characteristic features: rapid onset of short-lived impairment of neurologic function that resolves spontaneously; functional disturbance rather than structural; may or may not involve LOC; typically NO abnormality on standard structural imaging.
  • Recognition (any post-injury):
    • Loss of consciousness (not required).
    • Confusion / disorientation.
    • Amnesia (anterograde / retrograde).
    • Headache.
    • Dizziness, balance problems.
    • Nausea, vomiting.
    • Visual disturbances.
    • Sensitivity to light / sound.
    • Difficulty concentrating.
    • Emotional lability.
    • Sleep disturbance.
    • Symptoms often delayed onset.
  • Sideline assessment tools:
    • SCAT5 (Sport Concussion Assessment Tool) — symptom checklist, cognitive (orientation, memory, concentration), balance (BESS), neurologic.
    • Child SCAT5 for younger athletes.
    • Remove from play immediately if suspected concussion.
    • NO same-day return to play.
  • Initial management:
    • Remove from play.
    • Medical evaluation.
    • Rule out cervical injury, intracranial pathology (CT if red flags: GCS < 15, focal deficit, seizure, vomiting, severe headache, basilar skull fracture signs).
    • Initial cognitive and physical rest (24–48 hours).
    • Symptom monitoring.
    • Family education on warning signs.
  • Graded return-to-play (CISG / AAP protocol):
    • Stage 1: Symptom-limited activity (daily activities not provoking symptoms).
    • Stage 2: Light aerobic exercise (walking, stationary cycling; HR < 70% max).
    • Stage 3: Sport-specific exercise (running, skating; no head impact).
    • Stage 4: Non-contact training drills (passing drills; can resume strength training).
    • Stage 5: Full-contact practice (after medical clearance).
    • Stage 6: Return to play (normal game competition).
    • Minimum 24 hours between stages; if symptoms recur, drop back to previous stage and wait 24 hours.
  • Return to learn:
    • Cognitive rest initially; gradual return to school.
    • Academic accommodations (extended testing time, reduced workload, breaks).
    • Often parallel with return-to-play stages.
  • Second impact syndrome: Catastrophic if second concussion before first resolved. Strict return-to-play protocol prevents.
  • Post-concussion syndrome: Persistent symptoms > 4 weeks; multidisciplinary management (concussion clinic, vestibular therapy, headache, cognitive remediation).
  • Chronic traumatic encephalopathy (CTE): Recognized in athletes with repeated concussions; long-term neurodegeneration concern.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman supplements; CISG consensus; AAP / AAN pediatric concussion.
SAQ 113 HARD
Outline inborn errors of metabolism causing pediatric stroke.
Show structured answer
  • Homocystinuria (CBS deficiency):
    • Autosomal recessive cystathionine β-synthase deficiency.
    • Features: marfanoid habitus, lens subluxation (downward — distinguishes from Marfan upward), intellectual disability, thromboembolic events (arterial and venous strokes, MI, PE).
    • Diagnosis: elevated plasma homocysteine, elevated methionine, low cystine; CBS gene sequencing.
    • Treatment: pyridoxine (B6) — responsive subset; folate, B12, betaine; low-methionine diet.
  • Fabry disease:
    • X-linked α-galactosidase A deficiency.
    • Features: angiokeratomas (skin), acroparesthesias, GI symptoms, renal failure, cardiomyopathy, stroke (small vessel).
    • Diagnosis: low α-galactosidase A enzyme activity (males); GLA gene sequencing (carrier females).
    • Treatment: enzyme replacement therapy (agalsidase alfa or beta); migalastat for selected genotypes.
  • Sulfite oxidase deficiency / molybdenum cofactor deficiency:
    • Severe neonatal encephalopathy with seizures.
    • Ectopia lentis.
    • Stroke-like episodes in milder forms.
    • Diagnosis: urine sulfite test, low urine uric acid; MOCS1/MOCS2/GEPH or SUOX gene sequencing.
    • Treatment: experimental cyclic pyranopterin monophosphate (cPMP) for some MoCD types.
  • Methylmalonic acidemia / propionic acidemia:
    • Basal ganglia strokes during metabolic crises.
    • MRI: bilateral globus pallidus injury.
    • Treatment: protein restriction, B12 (for cobalamin-responsive forms), L-carnitine.
  • MELAS / mitochondrial:
    • Stroke-like episodes not respecting vascular territories.
    • L-arginine acute therapy.
    • Avoid valproate.
  • Glutaric aciduria type I:
    • Striatal necrosis during metabolic crises.
    • Bilateral putamen injury.
    • Treatment: lysine-restricted diet, carnitine, sick day protocol.
  • Organic acidurias:
    • Methylmalonic, propionic, isovaleric — basal ganglia strokes.
  • Workup of pediatric stroke should include:
    • Plasma homocysteine, methionine.
    • Plasma amino acids.
    • Plasma acylcarnitine profile.
    • Urine organic acids.
    • Lactate.
    • Lysosomal enzyme panel for Fabry.
    • Mitochondrial gene panel.
    • Specific gene tests directed by clinical features.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman supplements; pediatric stroke consensus.
SAQ 114 HARD
Describe vanishing white matter disease (VWM / CACH): genetics, clinical, imaging.
Show structured answer
  • Genetics:
    • Autosomal recessive.
    • Mutations in EIF2B1, EIF2B2, EIF2B3, EIF2B4, EIF2B5 — encoding 5 subunits of eIF2B (eukaryotic translation initiation factor 2B).
    • eIF2B critical for stress-response translation control.
  • Clinical:
    • Spectrum from antenatal-onset severe to adult-onset milder.
    • Classic childhood-onset:
      • Initial normal development 1–5 years.
      • Chronic progressive cerebellar ataxia and spasticity.
      • Episodic acute deteriorations triggered by febrile illness, head trauma (even minor), fright / emotional stress.
      • Acute deteriorations can cause stupor / coma.
      • Partial recovery after each episode but stepwise decline.
      • Ovarioleukodystrophy: female patients can have premature ovarian failure.
    • Death typically in adolescence in classic forms.
  • MRI:
    • Diffuse white matter T2 hyperintensity.
    • Progressive rarefaction and cystic changes (white matter resembles CSF signal).
    • Sparing of cortex, U-fibers initially.
    • Cerebellar atrophy.
    • Characteristic appearance differentiates from other leukodystrophies.
  • Management:
    • No specific disease-modifying therapy currently; experimental approaches in development.
    • Avoid triggers:
      • Aggressive fever management (acetaminophen at first sign of illness).
      • Avoid head trauma (helmet for younger children; avoid contact sports).
      • Stress reduction.
    • Supportive care: PT, OT, mobility devices.
    • Treat acute deteriorations: hydration, anti-pyretics, supportive ICU if severe.
    • Family genetic counseling; carrier testing.
    • Hormone replacement for ovarian failure in female patients.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman metabolic; VWM consensus.
SAQ 115 HARD
Describe pantothenate kinase-associated neurodegeneration (PKAN / NBIA-1): genetics, clinical, imaging, treatment.
Show structured answer
  • Genetics:
    • Autosomal recessive PANK2 gene mutations (chromosome 20p13).
    • PANK2 encodes pantothenate kinase 2 (mitochondrial enzyme in coenzyme A biosynthesis).
    • Iron accumulation in globus pallidus from disrupted CoA / iron metabolism.
    • Most common neurodegeneration with brain iron accumulation (NBIA-1).
  • Phenotypes:
    • Classic PKAN (early-onset): Onset 3–6 years; rapidly progressive; pigmentary retinal degeneration; dystonia (oromandibular, generalized); parkinsonism; cognitive decline; pyramidal signs; death typically before age 20.
    • Atypical PKAN (late-onset): Onset later, slower progression; predominantly psychiatric / dystonia; less retinal involvement.
  • MRI 'eye-of-the-tiger' sign:
    • Pathognomonic for PKAN.
    • Bilateral globus pallidus T2 hypointensity (iron — peripheral) surrounding central T2 hyperintensity (gliosis — central).
    • Best seen on T2 / SWI sequences.
    • Other NBIA subtypes have different imaging patterns.
  • Treatment:
    • Iron chelation:
      • Deferiprone — penetrates blood-brain barrier and chelates iron in basal ganglia.
      • TIRCON and FAIRPARK trials (deferiprone in PKAN): modest improvement in dystonia and PKAN severity rating.
      • Long-term therapy; monitor for neutropenia.
    • Symptomatic management of dystonia:
      • Trihexyphenidyl (anticholinergic).
      • Baclofen (oral or intrathecal for severe).
      • Tetrabenazine (chorea, dystonia).
      • Clonidine.
      • Botulinum toxin for focal dystonia.
      • Diazepam.
    • Deep brain stimulation (DBS):
      • Globus pallidus internus (GPi) DBS for refractory generalized dystonia.
      • Modest improvement; reduces severe dystonia.
    • Multidisciplinary care: neurology, movement disorders, ophthalmology, rehab, mental health.
    • Family genetic counseling.
  • Other NBIA subtypes:
    • PLA2G6-associated neurodegeneration (PLAN; NBIA-2).
    • Mitochondrial membrane protein-associated neurodegeneration (MPAN).
    • Beta-propeller protein-associated neurodegeneration (BPAN; WDR45).
    • Aceruloplasminemia.
    • Neuroferritinopathy.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman metabolic; NBIA consensus.
SAQ 116 HARD
Describe pediatric Friedreich ataxia: features, diagnosis, treatment including omaveloxolone.
Show structured answer
  • Genetics:
    • Autosomal recessive FXN gene GAA trinucleotide repeat expansion.
    • Normal: 5–33 repeats.
    • FRDA: ≥ 70 repeats (most homozygous; some compound heterozygous with point mutations).
    • Severity correlates inversely with shorter allele length.
    • Frataxin deficiency → mitochondrial iron accumulation → impaired iron-sulfur cluster biogenesis → mitochondrial dysfunction.
  • Classic features (mean onset 10–15 years; range 2–25+):
    • Neurologic:
      • Progressive ataxia (combined cerebellar + sensory from dorsal column degeneration).
      • Dysarthria.
      • Areflexia (large fiber sensory neuropathy).
      • Pyramidal signs (Babinski).
      • Vibration / proprioception loss (dorsal column).
      • Eventual wheelchair dependence (typically 10–15 years from onset).
    • Skeletal: Scoliosis (~ 60%); pes cavus (cavus foot); contractures.
    • Cardiac (leading cause of death):
      • Hypertrophic cardiomyopathy (transitions to dilated cardiomyopathy late).
      • Arrhythmias.
      • Heart failure.
      • Echocardiogram and ECG surveillance.
    • Endocrine: Diabetes mellitus 30% (insulin requiring).
    • Sensory: Optic atrophy (10–20%); hearing loss; nystagmus.
  • Diagnosis:
    • FXN gene sequencing (GAA repeat analysis).
    • Clinical criteria.
    • Nerve conduction studies: sensory neuronopathy.
    • MRI: cervical cord atrophy (especially dorsal columns); mild cerebellar atrophy.
    • Echocardiogram for cardiomyopathy.
  • Treatment:
    • Omaveloxolone (Skyclarys):
      • FDA approved 2023 — first disease-modifying therapy for Friedreich ataxia.
      • Nrf2 activator — restores mitochondrial function, reduces oxidative stress.
      • MOXIe trial showed modest improvement in mFARS (modified Friedreich Ataxia Rating Scale).
      • Oral, daily dosing.
      • Side effects: elevated LFTs, headache, nausea.
    • Supportive care: PT, OT, mobility aids, wheelchairs eventually.
    • Cardiac surveillance (annual echo); cardiac medications for HCM; ICD selected; eventual heart transplant in some.
    • Scoliosis management: bracing, surgical fusion for severe.
    • Diabetes management.
    • Speech therapy.
    • Vision / hearing assessments.
    • Genetic counseling; family screening.
    • Emerging: gene therapy in trials; idebenone (free radical scavenger; mixed results).
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman metabolic; FRDA consensus; MOXIe trial.
SAQ 117 HARD
Describe ataxia-telangiectasia: genetics, clinical features, diagnosis, management.
Show structured answer
  • Genetics:
    • Autosomal recessive ATM gene mutations (11q22-q23).
    • ATM (ataxia-telangiectasia mutated) kinase — master regulator of DNA damage response (recognizes double-strand breaks).
  • Clinical features:
    • Neurologic:
      • Progressive cerebellar ataxia (onset 1–2 years; appears with walking).
      • Oculomotor apraxia (slow / delayed initiation of saccades).
      • Dysarthria.
      • Choreoathetosis, dystonia.
      • Peripheral neuropathy (later).
      • Wheelchair dependence by age 10 in classic form.
    • Telangiectasias:
      • Develop 3–5 years.
      • Conjunctival (bilateral, characteristic).
      • Skin (especially in skin folds, ears, face).
    • Immunodeficiency (combined humoral + cellular):
      • Low IgA (~ 70%), low IgG subclasses (especially IgG2), low IgE.
      • T-cell dysfunction.
      • Recurrent sinopulmonary infections.
      • Bronchiectasis develops.
    • Cancer predisposition:
      • Lifetime risk ~ 25% — markedly increased.
      • Lymphoid malignancies most common (lymphoma, leukemia — especially T-cell ALL).
      • Solid tumors later (breast, ovarian, GI).
      • Cancer surveillance essential.
    • Radiation hypersensitivity:
      • Markedly increased toxicity from ionizing radiation (cells cannot repair DNA damage).
      • AVOID radiation when possible (CT scans, X-rays — use only if essential).
      • Radiation therapy for cancer must be modified or avoided.
    • Other: Insulin resistance / diabetes; gonadal dysfunction; growth failure.
  • Diagnosis:
    • Clinical features.
    • Elevated α-fetoprotein (AFP) — biomarker (95% of patients have elevation).
    • ATM gene sequencing.
    • Functional ATM assays (radiation-induced phosphorylation).
    • Immune workup: immunoglobulins, lymphocyte subsets.
  • Management:
    • No curative therapy.
    • Multidisciplinary care:
      • Pediatric neurology / movement disorders.
      • Immunology — immunoglobulin replacement for hypogammaglobulinemia.
      • Pulmonology — manage bronchiectasis, recurrent infections.
      • Hematology / oncology — cancer surveillance.
      • Endocrinology.
      • PT / OT / speech.
    • Avoid radiation (CT scans, X-rays minimized).
    • Treatment of cancer modified (reduced radiation, careful chemotherapy).
    • Antimicrobial prophylaxis in selected.
    • Genetic counseling — sibling testing; carrier testing.
    • Heterozygote carriers have increased breast cancer risk.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman metabolic; A-T consensus.
SAQ 118 HARD
Describe cerebral creatine deficiency syndromes: three subtypes, diagnosis, and treatment.
Show structured answer
  • Background:
    • Three subtypes: synthesis defects (AGAT, GAMT) — autosomal recessive; transporter defect (SLC6A8) — X-linked.
    • All result in cerebral creatine deficiency despite different mechanisms.
    • Phenotype: intellectual disability, autism, intractable epilepsy, movement disorder, hypotonia.
  • Subtype 1 — Arginine:glycine amidinotransferase (AGAT) deficiency:
    • GATM gene mutations.
    • First step in creatine synthesis impaired.
    • Low plasma guanidinoacetate.
    • Low cerebral creatine on MRS.
    • Treatment: oral creatine monohydrate — RESPONSIVE; substantial cognitive improvement if treated early.
  • Subtype 2 — Guanidinoacetate methyltransferase (GAMT) deficiency:
    • GAMT gene mutations.
    • Second step in creatine synthesis impaired.
    • HIGH guanidinoacetate (accumulates upstream of block — neurotoxic in addition to creatine deficiency).
    • Low cerebral creatine on MRS.
    • Treatment: oral creatine + arginine restriction + ornithine supplementation — reduces guanidinoacetate.
    • RESPONSIVE; significant clinical improvement.
  • Subtype 3 — SLC6A8 (creatine transporter) deficiency:
    • SLC6A8 gene mutations on X chromosome.
    • X-LINKED — males affected; female carriers variable mild symptoms.
    • Cellular uptake of creatine impaired despite normal synthesis.
    • Normal plasma creatine and guanidinoacetate.
    • Elevated urinary creatine / creatinine ratio (creatine not retained).
    • Low cerebral creatine on MRS.
    • Treatment: oral creatine NOT effective (transport defect prevents brain uptake) — emerging cyclocreatine and other creatine analogs investigational.
    • Most common cause of cerebral creatine deficiency.
  • Diagnostic approach (algorithm):
    • MR spectroscopy: decreased creatine peak (in all three).
    • Plasma and urine guanidinoacetate:
      • Low plasma GAA → AGAT.
      • High plasma + urine GAA → GAMT.
      • Normal GAA → SLC6A8.
    • Urine creatine / creatinine ratio: elevated in SLC6A8.
    • Confirmation by genetic testing.
    • Newborn screening for GAMT included in some states.
  • Outcomes:
    • AGAT and GAMT — early treatment can prevent / reverse intellectual disability.
    • SLC6A8 — supportive care; no effective therapy currently; gene therapy in development.
    • Family genetic counseling; cascade testing.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman metabolic; cerebral creatine deficiency consensus.
SAQ 119 HARD
Describe glycine encephalopathy / non-ketotic hyperglycinemia: genetics, presentation, diagnosis, treatment.
Show structured answer
  • Genetics:
    • Autosomal recessive deficiency of glycine cleavage system (GCS).
    • GLDC gene (P-protein; most common, ~ 70%).
    • AMT gene (T-protein).
    • GCSH gene (H-protein).
    • GCS normally degrades glycine in liver, brain, other tissues.
  • Pathophysiology:
    • Glycine accumulation in plasma and especially CSF.
    • Glycine acts at NMDA receptor (co-agonist with glutamate) AND inhibitory glycine receptor.
    • NMDA receptor overactivation → excitotoxicity → seizures, encephalopathy.
  • Classic neonatal form (most severe):
    • Onset within hours to days of life.
    • Profound encephalopathy / coma.
    • Intractable seizures (myoclonic, focal, generalized).
    • Severe hypotonia.
    • Hiccups (characteristic — often pre-natal).
    • Burst-suppression on EEG.
    • Apnea requiring mechanical ventilation.
    • High mortality (~ 30%); severe neurodevelopmental sequelae in survivors.
  • Atypical / mild forms:
    • Later onset (infantile / childhood).
    • Milder phenotype: developmental delay, seizures, movement disorder.
    • Better prognosis.
  • Diagnosis:
    • Plasma glycine: elevated.
    • CSF glycine: markedly elevated.
    • CSF/plasma glycine ratio > 0.08 (normal < 0.04) — pathognomonic.
    • Urine organic acids: NORMAL (distinguishes from ketotic hyperglycinemia in organic acidemias — methylmalonic, propionic, isovaleric acidemia).
    • EEG: burst-suppression (neonatal).
    • Genetic testing: GLDC > AMT > GCSH sequencing.
    • Enzyme assay on liver biopsy (less commonly performed).
  • Treatment:
    • Sodium benzoate: Conjugates with glycine to form hippurate; excreted in urine; reduces glycine levels. 250–750 mg/kg/day divided.
    • Dextromethorphan: NMDA receptor antagonist; reduces excitotoxicity. 3–10 mg/kg/day.
    • Ketamine: Has been used (NMDA antagonism).
    • Ketogenic diet: Emerging benefit in some patients.
    • Avoid: Valproate (worsens by inhibiting GCS), vigabatrin.
    • Supportive care for severe neonatal form: mechanical ventilation, seizure control.
    • Family discussion regarding prognosis early; engage palliative care.
  • Outcome:
    • Classic neonatal: high mortality; severe disability in survivors.
    • Atypical: better; some have near-normal IQ.
    • Family genetic counseling; sibling testing; prenatal diagnosis for future pregnancies.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman metabolic; NKH consensus.
SAQ 120 HARD
Describe PANDAS / PANS: criteria, workup, and management.
Show structured answer
  • PANDAS (Pediatric Autoimmune Neuropsychiatric Disorders Associated with Streptococcal infections) — Swedo criteria:
    • Presence of obsessive-compulsive disorder (OCD) or tic disorder (or both).
    • Pediatric onset (between 3 years and puberty).
    • Episodic course of symptom severity (relapsing-remitting).
    • Temporal association with group A streptococcal infections (positive throat culture, rapid strep, or rising antibody titers — ASO, anti-DNase B).
    • Associated neurologic abnormalities (motoric hyperactivity, adventitious movements, choreiform movements).
  • PANS (Pediatric Acute-onset Neuropsychiatric Syndrome) — broader category:
    • Abrupt (within 24–72 hours) onset of OCD or severely restricted food intake.
    • Concurrent presence of additional neuropsychiatric symptoms (2 or more):
      • Anxiety (especially separation anxiety).
      • Emotional lability / depression.
      • Irritability / aggression / oppositionality.
      • Behavioral regression.
      • Deterioration in school performance.
      • Sensory or motor abnormalities (sensory hypersensitivity, dysgraphia).
      • Sleep disturbances, enuresis, urinary frequency.
    • Symptoms not better explained by another neurologic / medical disorder.
    • Triggers may include infections (strep, mycoplasma, influenza, viral), environmental factors, others.
  • Workup:
    • Streptococcal: throat culture, rapid strep, ASO and anti-DNase B titers (rising titers more useful than absolute).
    • Infectious workup: mycoplasma, viral panel, EBV serology.
    • Autoimmune: ANA, ANCA, complement.
    • Endocrine: thyroid (autoimmune thyroiditis can mimic).
    • Movement disorder evaluation: examine for chorea, tics.
    • Psychiatric evaluation: OCD severity (CY-BOCS), tic severity (YGTSS), anxiety, depression, behavioral assessment.
    • EEG if seizure suspicion.
    • MRI brain if focal neurologic findings.
    • Laboratory: CBC, inflammatory markers (CRP, ESR).
  • Management:
    • Antibiotics:
      • If active strep infection: penicillin V 500 mg BID × 10 days (or amoxicillin 25 mg/kg BID).
      • Azithromycin alternative.
      • Some advocate longer prophylactic antibiotics in PANDAS with recurrent strep.
    • Psychiatric / psychological care:
      • CBT (cognitive behavioral therapy) with exposure and response prevention (ERP) for OCD.
      • SSRIs for OCD (sertraline, fluoxetine).
      • Antipsychotics for tics (aripiprazole, risperidone) — cautious in young.
    • Immunomodulation (for severe / refractory):
      • IVIG 2 g/kg.
      • Plasma exchange.
      • Corticosteroids (oral or pulsed methylprednisolone).
      • Rituximab in some cases.
      • Evidence base growing but not definitive — case-by-case in expert centers.
    • Multidisciplinary approach: Pediatric neurology, psychiatry, immunology, infectious disease.
    • Family education and support.
  • Controversy:
    • PANDAS / PANS criteria and pathophysiology debated.
    • Strict criteria identify subset that may benefit from immune-modulating therapy.
    • Avoid over-diagnosis and over-treatment with immunotherapy.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman supplements; PANDAS / PANS consensus.
SAQ 121 HARD
Describe pediatric ischemic stroke etiology and workup beyond standard pediatric stroke causes.
Show structured answer
  • Major etiologic categories:
    • Arteriopathies (~ 50%):
      • Focal cerebral arteriopathy (FCA / transient cerebral arteriopathy) — most common; unilateral M1/distal ICA narrowing; often post-VZV.
      • Moyamoya disease (bilateral supraclinoid ICA stenosis with collaterals; assoc. NF1, Down, sickle cell).
      • Arterial dissection (cervical or intracranial; post-trauma or spontaneous; connective tissue disorders).
      • Primary CNS vasculitis (PACNS) of childhood.
      • Sickle cell vasculopathy.
      • Post-radiation arteriopathy.
      • Fibromuscular dysplasia.
    • Cardioembolic (~ 25%):
      • Congenital heart disease (especially with R-L shunt, atrial communications, prosthetic valves).
      • Infective endocarditis.
      • Cardiomyopathy.
      • Atrial arrhythmias (especially in Fontan).
      • Cardiac surgery complications.
      • Paradoxical embolism (PFO).
    • Hematologic / thrombophilic:
      • Sickle cell disease.
      • Antiphospholipid syndrome.
      • Factor V Leiden, prothrombin G20210A.
      • Protein C, S, antithrombin deficiency.
      • Lupus anticoagulant.
      • Polycythemia.
      • Leukemia (especially L-asparaginase complication).
    • Metabolic / genetic:
      • Homocystinuria.
      • Fabry disease.
      • MELAS.
      • Sulfite oxidase / molybdenum cofactor deficiency.
      • Organic acidurias.
      • Glutaric aciduria type I.
      • Connective tissue disorders (Marfan, Ehlers-Danlos vascular type, Loeys-Dietz).
    • Infectious / inflammatory:
      • Bacterial meningitis (lacunar infarcts from vasculitis).
      • Tuberculous meningitis.
      • Varicella post-infectious arteriopathy.
      • HIV-associated arteriopathy.
      • Kawasaki disease.
    • Vascular: AVMs, aneurysms, cavernous malformations (more often hemorrhagic but can cause ischemic stroke).
    • Trauma: Cervical artery dissection, blunt trauma.
    • Idiopathic (~ 15–20% remain unexplained).
  • Workup:
    • Imaging: MRI brain with DWI; MRA brain and neck; MRV; cardiac CT or MRI if suspect cardiac source.
    • Catheter angiography if non-invasive inconclusive (PACNS, dissection, AVMs).
    • Cardiac: echocardiogram (transthoracic + bubble study; transesophageal if needed); ECG; Holter for arrhythmias.
    • Hematologic / thrombophilic: CBC, peripheral smear, coags, sickle screen, antiphospholipid (LA, ACL, anti-β2GP-I), protein C/S, antithrombin, factor V Leiden, prothrombin G20210A, MTHFR (clinical relevance debated), homocysteine, fibrinogen.
    • Metabolic: plasma amino acids, urine organic acids, lactate, acylcarnitine, α-galactosidase A (Fabry), urine sulfite (sulfite oxidase deficiency); MELAS testing if family / clinical features.
    • Inflammatory: ESR, CRP, ANA, ANCA.
    • Infectious: HIV testing, varicella serology, syphilis (VDRL); TB testing.
    • Genetic: arteriopathy / connective tissue gene panel; consider whole exome.
    • Family history detailed (premature stroke, vascular disease).
  • Multidisciplinary stroke team approach.
Source: Rogers / Zimmerman Ch 66; pediatric stroke consensus.
SAQ 122 — Anti-NMDAR Encephalitis HARD
A 13-year-old girl presents with subacute onset of psychiatric symptoms, orofacial dyskinesias, autonomic instability, and seizures. CSF shows mild lymphocytic pleocytosis; MRI is unremarkable.

CRITERIA

Apply the Graus 2016 criteria for diagnosis (probable and definite).
Show structured answer
PROBABLE anti-NMDAR encephalitis requires ALL three:
  1. Rapid onset (< 3 months) of ≥ 4 of the 6 major symptom categories: abnormal behaviour or psychiatric symptoms; speech dysfunction; seizures; movement disorder / dyskinesia; decreased level of consciousness; autonomic dysfunction or central hypoventilation.
  2. At least one of: abnormal EEG (focal or diffuse slowing or extreme delta brush) OR CSF pleocytosis or oligoclonal bands.
  3. Reasonable exclusion of alternative disorders.
DEFINITE = same as probable PLUS positive IgG anti-GluN1 antibodies in CSF.

TREATMENT

Outline the tiered immunotherapy and concurrent tumour screen.
Show structured answer
First-line:
  • IV methylprednisolone 30 mg/kg/day × 5 days.
  • IVIG 2 g/kg over 2–5 days.
  • Plasma exchange (5 sessions) — equivalent to IVIG; consider for severe disease.
Tumour screen (paraneoplastic in some):
  • Pelvic ultrasound ± MRI in females (ovarian teratoma).
  • Testicular ultrasound in males.
  • Imaging chest/abdomen if pelvic negative.
  • Repeat screen at 6 and 12 months if negative initially.
Second-line (if no improvement at 2 weeks):
  • Rituximab 375 mg/m² weekly × 4 doses.
  • Cyclophosphamide 500–750 mg/m² monthly.
  • Tumour removal if found.

PHYSIOLOGY

Explain the receptor pathophysiology and why patients improve with treatment.
Show structured answer
Mechanism: IgG antibodies bind the GluN1 subunit of the NMDA receptor → cross-link receptors → internalisation → functional NMDA hypofunction (mimicking ketamine/PCP physiology).

The process is REVERSIBLE — once antibody production is suppressed, NMDA receptors return to the membrane, explaining the typically good recovery with immunotherapy (though slow, over months).
SAQ 123 — Pediatric Brain-Death Determination HARD
A 10-year-old is being prepared for brain-death determination following severe TBI.

DEFINITION

List the prerequisites that must be satisfied before clinical examination.
Show structured answer
  • Established cause of brain injury consistent with irreversibility.
  • Core temperature ≥ 35 °C (preferably 36 °C).
  • Systolic blood pressure within acceptable range for age (typically > 5th percentile) without high-dose vasopressors.
  • No significant metabolic or endocrine derangement: glucose, electrolytes (Na, Ca, Mg, PO₄), pH within reasonable limits.
  • CNS depressant drugs cleared (especially barbiturates — level sub-therapeutic).
  • Neuromuscular blockade reversed (train-of-four 4/4).
  • Observation period: 24 h for newborns (37 weeks – 30 days); 12 h for > 30 days – 18 years.
  • Two clinical examinations by two different attending physicians separated by the observation period, each including an apnoea test.

CRITERIA

Define a positive apnoea test.
Show structured answer
Apnoea test prerequisites:
  • Core T ≥ 35 °C, SBP appropriate, euvolaemia, baseline PaCO₂ 35–45 mm Hg, PaO₂ > 200 on FiO₂ 1.0 for 10 min before disconnection.

POSITIVE test (consistent with brain death) requires ALL of:
  • NO respiratory effort during the entire observation (5–10 min).
  • Final PaCO₂ ≥ 60 mm Hg AND ≥ 20 mm Hg above baseline.
  • pH usually < 7.30.

Abort and repeat if SBP drops > 20%, SpO₂ < 85%, or arrhythmia.

NEXT STEP

When and which ancillary tests are used?
Show structured answer
Indications for ancillary testing:
  • Apnoea test cannot be completed safely.
  • Severe confounders that cannot be eliminated (barbiturates).
  • Examiner uncertainty.
  • Acceptable ancillary tests demonstrate absent cerebral blood flow:
    • 4-vessel catheter cerebral angiography (gold standard but invasive).
    • Radionuclide cerebral perfusion scintigraphy (Tc-99m HMPAO) — 'hollow skull' sign.
    • CT angiography (criteria still being refined in paediatrics).
    • Transcranial Doppler — reverberating or absent diastolic flow.
  • EEG no longer recommended as sole ancillary test (electrocerebral silence is not specific in deep barbiturate coma).
SAQ 124 — Multimodal Neuroprognostication after Pediatric Cardiac Arrest HARD
A 6-year-old is post-arrest day 4 after out-of-hospital arrest with submersion injury. Off sedation 24 h, normothermic.

CRITERIA

List the principles of multimodal post-arrest neuroprognostication.
Show structured answer
  • NEVER prognosticate from a single modality.
  • Wait ≥ 72 hours off sedation and paralytics, with normothermia and resolved confounders.
  • Integrate four core pillars: neurological exam, continuous EEG (background + reactivity), somatosensory evoked potentials (N20), and MRI (DWI severity).
  • Biomarkers (NSE, S100B) are less validated in children — supportive only.
  • Avoid 'self-fulfilling prophecy' — premature withdrawal of life support biases data and family decisions.
  • Family-centred decision-making with palliative care involvement.

PHYSIOLOGY

Explain the pattern of injury in HIE and why specific brain regions are vulnerable.
Show structured answer
Selective vulnerability:
  • Basal ganglia (especially putamen, globus pallidus), thalami, hippocampus, and watershed cortex are most metabolically active and have limited collateral supply.
  • Energy failure → membrane depolarisation → glutamate release → NMDA/AMPA over-activation → calcium influx → mitochondrial failure → excitotoxic cell death.
  • Secondary energy failure occurs 6–24 hours later — the 'therapeutic window' for hypothermia in neonates.
  • In severe arrest: deep grey nuclei + cortex affected. In milder injury: watershed cortex only.

TREATMENT

Outline targeted temperature management per current pediatric guidance.
Show structured answer
THAPCA-OH and THAPCA-IH (NEJM 2015, 2017):
  • Compared 33 °C vs 36.8 °C × 48 h followed by strict normothermia.
  • No survival-with-good-outcome difference between groups.
  • BUT both arms had aggressive fever avoidance — suggesting fever prevention is the key intervention.

Current guidance:
  • Either 33 °C OR 36.8 °C × 48 h, then strict normothermia × 5 days total.
  • Avoid hyperthermia (> 37.5 °C).
  • Continuous EEG to detect/treat seizures (often non-convulsive in pediatric post-arrest).
  • Optimise CPP, avoid hypoxia/hypotension, glucose 4–10 mmol/L.
SAQ 125 — Status Epilepticus — ILAE Definitions and ESETT MODERATE
A 7-year-old in active convulsive status epilepticus has received intranasal midazolam by EMS and IV lorazepam in ED.

DEFINITION

Define status epilepticus using the ILAE 2015 T1/T2 framework.
Show structured answer
T1 = the operational time point at which abnormally prolonged seizure activity should trigger emergency treatment (failure of natural seizure termination mechanisms).
For convulsive (tonic-clonic) SE, T1 = 5 minutes.

T2 = the time at which prolonged seizure activity will result in long-term consequences (neuronal injury, network alteration).
For convulsive SE, T2 = 30 minutes.
