Other seizure types: focal SE with impaired awareness — T1 10 min, T2 60 min; absence SE — T1 10–15 min, T2 less clearly defined.

TREATMENT

Summarise the ESETT trial findings and current second-line agents.
Show structured answer
ESETT (NEJM 2019, Kapur et al.): Multicentre blinded RCT comparing three second-line agents in convulsive SE refractory to benzodiazepines.

Compared:
  • Fosphenytoin 20 mg PE/kg IV.
  • Levetiracetam 60 mg/kg IV.
  • Valproate 40 mg/kg IV.

Result: All three were equally effective (~ 47–52% seizure cessation and improved responsiveness at 60 min). No statistically significant differences in efficacy or safety.

Implication: Choice between fosphenytoin, levetiracetam, and valproate can be based on availability, patient factors (allergies, comorbidities), and clinician familiarity.

NEXT STEP

If seizure continues despite second-line agent, outline the next steps.
Show structured answer
Refractory SE management:
  1. Intubate for airway protection.
  2. Initiate continuous infusion of midazolam (loading 0.2 mg/kg, then 0.05–2 mg/kg/h) or propofol (caution in pediatrics — PRIS risk).
  3. Add ketamine infusion (NMDA antagonist — addresses receptor trafficking of prolonged SE).
  4. Pentobarbital infusion targeting EEG burst-suppression (10–20 second interburst).
  5. cEEG monitoring is MANDATORY to confirm electrographic termination.
  6. Consider second-line AED add-on (lacosamide, perampanel).
  7. Look for underlying cause: imaging, infection workup, autoimmune panel (anti-NMDAR), metabolic, AED levels.
  8. For super-refractory SE: ketogenic diet, immunotherapy (steroids/IVIG), anakinra/tocilizumab if FIRES suspected.
SAQ 126 — Pediatric Severe TBI — Tier 1 Management MODERATE
An 8-year-old after MVC is intubated for GCS 5. EVD reads ICP 24 mm Hg.

TREATMENT

List the components of optimised Tier 1 TBI management per BTF 2019.
Show structured answer
  • Head positioning: head midline, HOB elevated 30°.
  • Sedation and analgesia: adequate to prevent ICP elevations from agitation; avoid prolonged propofol infusion (PRIS).
  • Normothermia: aggressive fever avoidance (paracetamol, cooling).
  • Hyperosmolar therapy: 3% saline 5 mL/kg bolus or mannitol 0.5–1 g/kg; titrate Na 145–155 (osm 300–320).
  • CSF drainage: via EVD if present.
  • Normal glucose: 4–10 mmol/L; avoid hypoglycaemia and hyperglycaemia.
  • Normocapnia: PaCO₂ 35–40; avoid prophylactic hyperventilation.
  • Normoxia: SpO₂ > 94%; PaO₂ > 90.
  • Seizure prophylaxis: levetiracetam first 7 days for severe TBI.
  • Anaemia correction: Hb > 80–90 g/L (target debated).

CRITERIA

List the CPP thresholds by age group per pediatric BTF.
Show structured answer
Age-stratified CPP minima (BTF 2019):
  • Infants < 2 years: ≥ 40 mm Hg.
  • Young children 2–6 years: ≥ 40–50 mm Hg.
  • School-age 6–12 years: ≥ 50 mm Hg.
  • Adolescents 13–17: ≥ 50–60 mm Hg (some guidelines use ≥ 60 in older adolescents extrapolating from adult evidence).

These are MINIMA — aim higher within tolerable range, individualise using CPPopt where possible.

NEXT STEP

If ICP persists > 20 despite Tier 1, what tier-2 options are available?
Show structured answer
Tier 2 options:
  • Hyperosmolar intensification — Na 150–155, osm 320–340.
  • Moderate hyperventilation — PaCO₂ 30–35 (NOT < 30; ischaemia risk).
  • Mild hypothermia — 35–36 °C.
  • Neuromuscular blockade if not already.
  • Pre-tier 3: rule out new surgical lesion with repeat imaging.

Tier 3 (refractory ICP):
  • Pentobarbital infusion to EEG burst-suppression.
  • Decompressive craniectomy (evidence stronger in children than RescueICP-extrapolation in adults).
  • Deep hypothermia 32–34 °C (Cool Kids — prophylactic hypothermia not beneficial; targeted for refractory ICP only).
SAQ 127 — Cerebral Autoregulation and PRx HARD
A child with severe TBI is undergoing continuous PRx monitoring. PRx is consistently +0.4–0.5 over the past 6 hours.

DEFINITION

Define PRx and explain its interpretation.
Show structured answer
PRx (Pressure Reactivity Index) = moving correlation coefficient between slow waves of arterial blood pressure (ABP) and intracranial pressure (ICP), calculated over a ~5-minute window.

Reflects the vasomotor reactivity of the cerebrovasculature.

Interpretation:
  • PRx < 0 (negative correlation): intact autoregulation — vessels constrict when MAP rises, ICP doesn't rise proportionally.
  • PRx ≈ 0: borderline.
  • PRx > 0.2–0.3: impaired autoregulation — ABP and ICP move together (pressure-passive flow).
  • PRx > 0.5: severely impaired autoregulation.

PHYSIOLOGY

Explain the rationale for CPPopt-guided management and how it is derived.
Show structured answer
CPPopt is the CPP value at which PRx is most negative (i.e., autoregulation is best preserved) for an individual patient.

Derived by plotting PRx against CPP over a 4–6 hour window — a parabolic curve emerges, with the nadir of PRx defining CPPopt.

Rationale:
  • Each patient has a unique 'optimal' MAP/CPP at which autoregulation is preserved.
  • Targeting a generic CPP (e.g., 60 mm Hg) may push some patients outside their autoregulatory range.
  • Targeting individualised CPPopt restores autoregulation, may reduce secondary injury, and improves outcomes in observational data.

TREATMENT

With PRx +0.5, how should management change?
Show structured answer
Implications of severely impaired autoregulation:
  • Pressure-passive cerebral blood flow → raising MAP may cause hyperaemic oedema, not improved perfusion.
  • Lowering MAP may cause ischaemia.
  • Determine CPPopt and target it (don't push MAP arbitrarily).
  • Optimise other determinants: PaCO₂ (normocapnia), normothermia, sedation depth, treat seizures.
  • Consider multimodal monitoring (PbtO₂, microdialysis) to confirm tissue oxygenation/metabolism.
  • Avoid haemodynamic extremes.
SAQ 128 — Neonatal HIE and Therapeutic Hypothermia MODERATE
A term newborn (Apgar 1/3/4) with severe acidosis presents with encephalopathy and seizures.

CRITERIA

List the eligibility criteria for therapeutic hypothermia in neonatal HIE.
Show structured answer
Three-step criteria:
Step A — Demonstrate perinatal event:
  • Apgar ≤ 5 at 10 min, OR
  • Cord pH < 7.0 or BE ≤ –12, OR
  • Continued need for ventilation at 10 min.

Step B — Moderate or severe encephalopathy:
  • Clinical assessment (modified Sarnat staging) OR aEEG abnormalities.

Inclusion criteria:
  • Gestational age ≥ 35–36 weeks.
  • Postnatal age < 6 hours (window for initiation).
  • No major congenital anomaly precluding survival.

PHYSIOLOGY

Explain Sarnat staging and how it stratifies severity.
Show structured answer
Modified Sarnat staging for HIE:
Stage 1 (mild): hyperalert, normal tone, exaggerated reflexes, no seizures, sympathetic hyperactivity.
Stage 2 (moderate): lethargy, hypotonia, weak suck/Moro, sluggish pupils, frequent seizures.
Stage 3 (severe): stupor/coma, flaccid tone, absent reflexes, dilated/fixed pupils, brainstem signs, prolonged seizures.

Cooling indicated for stage 2 or 3 within 6 hours.
Stage 1 generally not cooled (outcomes good without).

TREATMENT

Outline the cooling protocol and trial evidence base.
Show structured answer
Protocol:
  • Target core temperature 33–34 °C.
  • Duration 72 hours.
  • Initiated within 6 hours of birth.
  • Methods: whole-body cooling (servo-controlled blanket) or selective head cooling.
  • Slow rewarming (≤ 0.5 °C/hour).

Trials supporting use:
  • CoolCap (Lancet 2005).
  • NICHD (NEJM 2005).
  • TOBY (NEJM 2009).
  • ICE (Arch Pediatr Adolesc Med 2011).
  • Meta-analysis: NNT ~ 7 to prevent death or major disability.

Adjuncts (less established):
  • Erythropoietin — HEAL trial mixed results.
  • Magnesium — limited evidence.
  • Xenon, melatonin, allopurinol — investigational.
SAQ 129 — Pediatric AIS — Hyperacute Management HARD
A 14-year-old presents with acute right hemiparesis and aphasia 2.5 hours after onset. CT shows hyperdense left MCA sign; CTA confirms M1 occlusion.

TREATMENT

Outline acute reperfusion options in pediatric AIS.
Show structured answer
IV alteplase (tPA):
  • Limited pediatric trial data (TIPS trial halted for poor enrolment).
  • Extrapolated from adult evidence for AIS within 4.5-hour window.
  • Standard dose: 0.9 mg/kg (max 90 mg); 10% bolus, 90% over 60 min.
  • Exclusions: haemorrhage, large infarct, recent surgery, bleeding diathesis, sickle cell crisis (relative).

Mechanical thrombectomy:
  • Increasingly performed in older children/adolescents with LVO (large vessel occlusion).
  • Time window extends to 24 h with perfusion mismatch (DEFUSE 3, DAWN extrapolation).
  • Devices: stent retriever (Solitaire, Trevo) or aspiration catheter.
  • Save for centres with pediatric/adolescent experience.

NEXT STEP

What are the supportive measures during hyperacute care?
Show structured answer
Neuroprotective bundle:
  • Maintain SpO₂ > 94%.
  • PaCO₂ 35–45 (normocapnia).
  • BP: avoid extremes; treat severe hypertension (> 95th percentile + 15 mm Hg) cautiously.
  • Glucose 4–10 mmol/L.
  • Normothermia.
  • Head of bed flat or 30° depending on perfusion status.
  • Aspirin 3–5 mg/kg/day after haemorrhage excluded.
  • Continuous monitoring; ICU admission.

DEFINITION

Define large vessel occlusion (LVO) and how this affects therapy choice.
Show structured answer
LVO definition: Occlusion of:
  • Internal carotid artery (terminal).
  • Middle cerebral artery M1 or proximal M2 segments.
  • Basilar artery.
  • Anterior cerebral artery A1 (less common).

Implications:
  • LVO predicts severe deficit and poor outcome with medical therapy alone.
  • Mechanical thrombectomy provides dramatic benefit (NNT 2–3 for functional independence).
  • Time-sensitive: 'time is brain' applies; recanalisation rate decreases with each hour.
  • CTA or MRA needed to identify LVO before considering thrombectomy.
SAQ 130 — Sickle Cell Stroke — STOP, SIT, TWiTCH Trials MODERATE
A 9-year-old with HbSS has a left MCA infarct. Hb 8 g/dL, HbS 80%.

TREATMENT

Outline acute and prevention strategies in pediatric SCD stroke.
Show structured answer
Acute:
  • Emergent EXCHANGE transfusion to reduce HbS < 30% and total Hb to 10 g/dL.
  • Simple transfusion only as bridge — risks hyperviscosity.
  • tPA NOT recommended in pediatric SCD stroke (insufficient evidence, bleeding concern).
  • Supportive: SpO₂ > 94%, normothermia, normocapnia, normovolaemia.

Long-term prevention after first stroke (secondary prevention):
  • Chronic transfusion to maintain HbS < 30%.
  • Iron chelation as needed.
  • HSCT for selected patients (curative).

CRITERIA

Summarise the STOP trial findings.
Show structured answer
STOP (Stroke Prevention Trial in Sickle Cell Anemia, NEJM 1998):
  • Children aged 2–16 with HbSS and abnormal transcranial Doppler (TCD time-averaged mean velocity ≥ 200 cm/s in MCA or ICA).
  • Randomised to chronic transfusion (HbS < 30%) vs standard care.
  • Result: 92% relative risk reduction in first stroke.
  • Trial stopped early due to overwhelming benefit.
  • Implication: TCD screening from age 2; chronic transfusion for abnormal TCD prevents primary stroke.

PHYSIOLOGY

Summarise SIT and TWiTCH trials.
Show structured answer
SIT (Silent Infarct Transfusion Trial, NEJM 2014):
  • Children with silent cerebral infarcts on MRI (but normal exam and normal TCD).
  • Chronic transfusion reduced silent infarct progression by ~ 58%.
  • Implication: support for chronic transfusion in those with silent infarcts.

TWiTCH (TCD With Transfusions Changing to Hydroxyurea, Lancet 2016):
  • Children with abnormal TCDs treated with chronic transfusion ≥ 12 months and stable TCDs.
  • Compared continued transfusion vs switch to hydroxyurea (with phlebotomy for iron).
  • Non-inferiority demonstrated for TCD velocity stability.
  • Implication: after 1 year of transfusion stabilising TCDs (without prior overt stroke), hydroxyurea can replace transfusion.
SAQ 131 — Cervical Artery Dissection MODERATE
An 11-year-old presents with neck pain, partial Horner syndrome on the right, and progressive right hemiparesis 6 hours after chiropractic neck manipulation.

DX

Explain the clinical features and the mechanism of partial Horner syndrome.
Show structured answer
Carotid artery dissection with embolic stroke.

Partial Horner syndrome (ptosis + miosis, NO anhidrosis):
  • Postganglionic sympathetic fibres run on the surface of the cervical ICA en route to the dilator pupillae and Müller muscle.
  • Dissection disrupts these fibres.
  • Sweating fibres (preganglionic / external carotid pathway) are spared — hence anhidrosis of face is absent.
  • Painful Horner = dissection until proven otherwise.

NEXT STEP

What is the diagnostic imaging of choice and the pathognomonic feature?
Show structured answer
MRI with T1 fat-suppressed sequences of the neck:
  • Most sensitive technique.
  • Pathognomonic: crescent of T1-hyperintense intramural haematoma within the arterial wall (methaemoglobin signal during the first 1–2 weeks).

CTA shows the lumen:
  • Tapered narrowing.
  • Flame-shaped distal occlusion or 'string sign'.
  • Pseudo-aneurysm in some.

Catheter angiography rarely needed — reserved for surgical planning or unclear imaging.

TREATMENT

Acute and longer-term antithrombotic strategy?
Show structured answer
Acute:
  • Antiplatelet (aspirin 1–5 mg/kg/day) OR anticoagulation (LMWH/UFH bridged to warfarin).
  • CADISS trial (adults): no significant difference between antiplatelet and anticoagulation; choice based on infarct size, intraluminal thrombus, bleeding risk.
  • For paediatrics: extrapolated; many centres prefer aspirin for safety profile.
  • Treatment duration: 3–6 months.
  • Repeat imaging at 3 months; longer therapy if persistent occlusion/dissection.

Endovascular: reserved for refractory haemodynamic compromise.
Avoid further neck manipulation; counsel about contact sports.
SAQ 132 — Vitamin K Deficiency Bleeding (VKDB) HARD
A 6-week-old breastfed infant whose family declined vitamin K prophylaxis presents with intracerebral haemorrhage.

DEFINITION

Classify VKDB and outline the typical presentations.
Show structured answer
Three classifications:
  • Early VKDB — within 24 h of birth; usually maternal medication (warfarin, anticonvulsants).
  • Classic VKDB — days 2–7; inadequate intake, no prophylaxis given.
  • Late VKDB — 2–12 weeks; exclusively breastfed infants without prophylaxis, often with underlying cholestasis or fat malabsorption.

Vitamin K is cofactor for γ-carboxylation of factors II, VII, IX, X and proteins C and S.
Late VKDB has high rate of intracranial haemorrhage (~ 50%) with significant mortality and morbidity.

PHYSIOLOGY

Why is breast milk a risk factor and what is the role of bacterial colonisation?
Show structured answer
  • Breast milk is low in vitamin K (~ 1–2 µg/L vs formula ~ 50–100 µg/L).
  • Neonatal gut has limited bacterial colonisation in the first weeks → minimal endogenous vitamin K production by gut flora.
  • Combination: low dietary intake + minimal endogenous synthesis = high risk for VKDB.
  • Underlying cholestasis (biliary atresia, CF, alpha-1-AT deficiency) further impairs fat-soluble vitamin absorption.
  • Vitamin K prophylaxis at birth bridges this gap.

TREATMENT

Acute management and prevention?
Show structured answer
Acute:
  • IV vitamin K 1–2 mg slowly (corrects coagulopathy within 6–12 h).
  • FFP 10–15 mL/kg for immediate factor replacement during active bleeding.
  • 4-factor PCC for life-threatening haemorrhage.
  • Neurosurgical evaluation for SDH/ICH.
  • Investigate underlying cholestasis (signs of biliary atresia, CF screen).

Prevention:
  • Universal IM vitamin K 1 mg at birth — gold standard.
  • Oral regimens (multiple doses) are LESS effective for late VKDB — inferior protection.
  • Counsel families who refuse: provide oral schedule if absolutely refusing IM, but discuss risk.
SAQ 133 — Urea Cycle Disorders — Differential and Emergency Management HARD
A 5-day-old term boy presents with ammonia 850, primary respiratory alkalosis, normal glucose, low urea.

CRITERIA

Outline how plasma amino acids and urinary orotic acid distinguish among UCDs.
Show structured answer
Differential by biochemistry:
DisorderCitrullineUrinary orotic acid
CPS1 deficiencyLowNormal
NAGS deficiencyLowNormal
OTC deficiencyLowHIGH
Citrullinaemia type I (ASS1)HIGHHigh
ASA lyase (ASL) deficiencyElevatedHigh
Arginase deficiencyVariableVariable

OTC = X-linked; consider family history of male relatives with neonatal death.
Arginase deficiency typically presents later with spastic diplegia, not neonatal crisis.

PHYSIOLOGY

Why do UCDs cause respiratory alkalosis?
Show structured answer
  • Ammonia crosses the blood-brain barrier.
  • In astrocytes, glutamine synthase converts ammonia + glutamate to glutamine.
  • Glutamine accumulation causes astrocyte swelling and cerebral oedema.
  • Central irritation and stimulation of brainstem respiratory centres → hyperventilation → primary respiratory alkalosis.
  • This pattern is opposite to most metabolic encephalopathies (which cause acidosis); UCD is among the few neonatal disorders causing primary respiratory alkalosis.
  • A respiratory alkalosis pattern in a sick neonate without fever or sepsis should prompt ammonia measurement.

TREATMENT

Outline emergency hyperammonaemia management.
Show structured answer
Emergency protocol:
  1. Stop all protein intake.
  2. Reverse catabolism: IV D10 + insulin if needed; intralipid for calories.
  3. Nitrogen scavengers: sodium phenylacetate + sodium benzoate (Ammonul) load 250 mg/kg over 90 min, then maintenance.
  4. Arginine HCl IV — replenishes urea cycle (citrullinaemia/ASA lyase/OTC).
  5. Hemodialysis (preferred over CRRT for speed) if ammonia > 250 µmol/L or rising despite scavengers, or in coma.
  6. Treat cerebral oedema: head up 30°, careful fluid balance; avoid mannitol initially.
  7. After stabilisation: low-protein diet, oral scavengers (phenylbutyrate or glycerol phenylbutyrate), arginine, transplant evaluation.
SAQ 134 — MCAD Deficiency — Hypoketotic Hypoglycaemia MODERATE
A 14-month-old presents with hypoglycaemia and seizures during a viral illness. Urinary ketones are inappropriately low.

DX

Explain the diagnosis and the biochemical clue.
Show structured answer
Medium-chain acyl-CoA dehydrogenase (MCAD) deficiency — autosomal recessive ACADM mutations.

Biochemical clue: hypoketotic hypoglycaemia.
  • Normal physiology during fasting: lipolysis → β-oxidation → ketogenesis → ketones fuel brain.
  • MCADD: medium-chain fatty acid β-oxidation blocked → energy deficit + no ketones generated.
  • So hypoglycaemia + LOW ketones in urine = strongly suggests FAOD (MCAD, VLCAD, LCHAD, etc.).
  • Acylcarnitine profile: elevated C8 (octanoylcarnitine), C10, C8:1.

TREATMENT

Acute management and sick-day rules.
Show structured answer
Acute:
  • IV dextrose (D10) 10–12 mg/kg/min to maintain glucose > 4 mmol/L; this suppresses lipolysis.
  • Cover intercurrent illness aggressively.
  • L-carnitine 100 mg/kg/day if levels low (debated whether changes outcome).

Sick-day rules:
  • Maximum fasting tolerance: 4 hours in infants, 8 hours in toddlers, 10–12 hours in school-age.
  • Frequent feeding; uncooked cornstarch at bedtime in older children.
  • During illness: glucose drinks or seek hospital if poor oral intake.
  • Family education and emergency action plan.
  • Medical alert bracelet.

PHYSIOLOGY

Why is the ketogenic diet contraindicated in MCAD deficiency?
Show structured answer
Ketogenic diet:
  • High-fat, very-low-carbohydrate diet → forces reliance on fatty acid oxidation to generate ketones for energy.
  • In MCAD, fatty acid oxidation is BLOCKED — patient cannot generate ketones from medium-chain fats.
  • Result: energy crisis + accumulation of toxic intermediates → severe decompensation.
  • Hence keto diet is contraindicated in MCAD, VLCAD, LCHAD, CPT-II, and primary carnitine deficiency.
  • Before starting ketogenic diet for epilepsy, ALWAYS screen for FAODs with acylcarnitine profile and urine organic acids.
SAQ 135 — Pyridoxine-Dependent Epilepsy and PNPO Deficiency HARD
A neonate presents day 2 with refractory seizures unresponsive to phenobarbital.

DX

How are PDE and PNPO deficiency distinguished?
Show structured answer
Pyridoxine-dependent epilepsy (PDE):
  • Autosomal recessive ALDH7A1 mutations.
  • Antiquitin deficiency → accumulation of α-aminoadipic semialdehyde (α-AASA) and pipecolic acid → bind and inactivate active vitamin B6 (PLP).
  • Responds to PYRIDOXINE trial.

PNPO deficiency:
  • Autosomal recessive PNPO mutations.
  • Pyridox(am)ine-5'-phosphate oxidase deficiency — cannot convert pyridoxine to active PLP.
  • Pyridoxine alone DOES NOT work — patient needs active PLP form.
  • Responds to PYRIDOXAL-5'-PHOSPHATE trial.

Diagnostic strategy: trial pyridoxine first; if no response, trial PLP. Genetic confirmation.

TREATMENT

Outline the diagnostic trial and chronic treatment.
Show structured answer
Acute trial:
  • IV pyridoxine 100 mg (single dose) with continuous EEG and resuscitation equipment ready (apnoea risk during loading).
  • Response within minutes (seizure cessation, EEG normalisation) = positive for PDE.
  • If no response, IV PLP 30 mg/kg/day for PNPO deficiency.

Chronic:
  • PDE: lifelong oral pyridoxine 15–30 mg/kg/day (max 500 mg/day).
  • PNPO: lifelong oral PLP 30–50 mg/kg/day divided 4–6 times daily.
  • Adjunctive lysine-restricted diet ± L-arginine for PDE — reduces α-AASA buildup and may improve cognitive outcomes.
  • Folinic acid supplementation in selected patients.

DEFINITION

List other inborn errors of metabolism that may present with neonatal refractory seizures.
Show structured answer
  • Pyridoxine-dependent epilepsy (ALDH7A1).
  • PNPO deficiency.
  • Folinic acid-responsive seizures (FOLR1 / cerebral folate transport defect).
  • Non-ketotic hyperglycinemia (GLDC, AMT).
  • Sulfite oxidase / molybdenum cofactor deficiency.
  • Biotinidase deficiency.
  • Hypoglycaemia / hyperinsulinism.
  • UCDs (severe hyperammonaemia).
  • MSUD.
  • Mitochondrial disorders (POLG, Leigh).
  • Glycine cleavage system defects.
  • Cerebral creatine deficiency syndromes.
SAQ 136 — AHT Workup and Glutaric Aciduria Type 1 as Mimic HARD
A 5-month-old presents with seizures, bilateral subdural collections of different ages, and retinal haemorrhages. Family reports a fall from a couch.

NEXT STEP

Outline the comprehensive workup beyond stabilisation.
Show structured answer
Workup priorities:
  • Skeletal survey (and repeat at 2 weeks for healing fractures).
  • Dilated fundoscopy by ophthalmology — multilayered retinal haemorrhages extending to ora serrata are highly specific for AHT.
  • MRI brain + cervical spine — assess parenchymal injury, ligamentous injury, retinoschisis.
  • Coagulation studies — PT, APTT, fibrinogen, platelet count, von Willebrand profile, factor levels (rule out coagulation mimics).
  • Metabolic screen — urine organic acids (glutaric aciduria type 1), plasma acylcarnitine, ammonia, lactate.
  • Connective tissue evaluation if features suggestive (Ehlers-Danlos, OI, Menkes).
  • Statutory child-protection report — mandatory in most jurisdictions.
  • Social work, hospital child-protection team, multidisciplinary case review.
  • Sibling safety assessment.
  • Document and photograph findings meticulously.

DX

Why is glutaric aciduria type 1 a critical mimic and how is it distinguished?
Show structured answer
Glutaric aciduria type 1 (GA1):
  • Autosomal recessive deficiency of glutaryl-CoA dehydrogenase.
  • Accumulation of glutaric and 3-hydroxyglutaric acids — neurotoxic to striatum.
  • Phenotype: macrocephaly, characteristic frontotemporal atrophy with widened opercular cisterns ('bat-wing' appearance), subdural collections (from bridging vein stretching across enlarged cisterns), acute striatal injury during catabolic stress.
  • Subdural collections CAN BE SPONTANEOUS — important mimic of AHT.

Distinguishing features:
  • MRI: bat-wing temporal lobe atrophy in GA1; AHT typically has additional features (parenchymal contusions, cerebral oedema, multiple injury patterns).
  • Urine organic acids: glutaric and 3-hydroxyglutaric acids elevated in GA1.
  • Plasma acylcarnitine: C5DC elevated.
  • Genetic testing confirms.

This is why urine organic acids should be part of AHT workup — to avoid wrongful accusations.

TREATMENT

Outline GA1 emergency management and long-term care.
Show structured answer
Emergency management of metabolic crisis:
  • Stop protein intake.
  • D10 + insulin to suppress catabolism.
  • L-carnitine 100 mg/kg/day IV.
  • Treat infection / fever aggressively.
  • Hemodialysis if severe.

Long-term:
  • Lysine-restricted diet.
  • L-carnitine supplementation.
  • Riboflavin (cofactor).
  • Emergency protocol during illness — IV glucose if vomiting.
  • Newborn screening picks up most cases — pre-symptomatic dietary management dramatically improves outcomes.
SAQ 137 — Maple Syrup Urine Disease — Acute Management HARD
A 6-day-old infant presents with poor feeding, opisthotonus, characteristic maple-syrup odour, and plasma leucine 2400 µmol/L.

DX

Identify the metabolic defect and the diagnostic marker.
Show structured answer
Maple syrup urine disease (MSUD) — autosomal recessive defect of branched-chain α-ketoacid dehydrogenase (BCKAD) complex.

Genes: BCKDHA, BCKDHB, DBT, DLD.

Diagnostic biochemistry:
  • Elevated plasma leucine, isoleucine, valine.
  • LEUCINE most neurotoxic — correlates with encephalopathy severity.
  • Presence of ALLOISOLEUCINE in plasma is pathognomonic.
  • Urine: characteristic maple-syrup or burnt-sugar odour (from sotolon, an isoleucine metabolite).
  • Urinary 2,4-DNPH test positive.
  • Newborn screen: elevated leucine.

TREATMENT

Outline the emergency and chronic management.
Show structured answer
Acute (leucine > 1500):
  • Stop all protein intake.
  • D10 + insulin if needed — suppress catabolism.
  • Intralipid for additional calories.
  • Isoleucine and valine supplementation (paradoxically helpful — they compete with leucine for blood-brain barrier transport).
  • Hemodialysis if leucine > 1500 or encephalopathy / refractory — most rapid clearance.
  • Aim to reduce leucine to < 1000 quickly.

Chronic:
  • MSUD-specific formula (branched-chain amino acid-restricted).
  • Careful titration of natural protein based on individual leucine tolerance.
  • Frequent BCAA monitoring.
  • Sick-day rules: low-protein high-calorie diet; early hospitalisation.
  • Thiamine trial (10–20 mg/kg/day) — < 5% are thiamine-responsive.
  • Liver transplant for severe / poorly controlled cases — curative.
  • Multidisciplinary metabolic clinic.
  • Genetic counselling (AR with 25% recurrence).
SAQ 138 — POLG Disease and Valproate Contraindication HARD
A 4-year-old on valproate develops status epilepticus, hyperammonaemia, AST/ALT > 1000, INR 2.0.

DX

Diagnosis and the genetic basis.
Show structured answer
POLG-related hepatocerebral syndrome / Alpers-Huttenlocher syndrome.
  • POLG encodes the catalytic subunit of mitochondrial DNA polymerase γ.
  • Required for mitochondrial DNA replication.
  • Loss of function → progressive mtDNA depletion → multi-organ mitochondrial failure.
  • Severe pediatric form (Alpers-Huttenlocher): intractable epilepsy + cognitive regression + hepatic failure.
  • Often unmasked / dramatically accelerated by VALPROATE exposure.

TREATMENT

Outline emergency management and disease-specific avoidance.
Show structured answer
Emergency:
  • STOP valproate immediately.
  • N-acetylcysteine — considered, though efficacy in POLG-VPA hepatic failure unclear.
  • Hyperammonaemia management: lactulose, scavengers (Ammonul), CRRT for severe.
  • Coagulopathy: vitamin K, FFP, PCC.
  • Hypoglycaemia: D10 infusion.
  • Liver transplant evaluation — controversial historically given multi-organ disease and progressive CNS involvement; selected cases proceed.

Long-term: drugs to AVOID in mitochondrial disease:
  • VALPROATE (absolute in POLG — fulminant hepatic failure).
  • Aminoglycosides (cumulative ototoxicity).
  • Linezolid (peripheral and optic neuropathy).
  • Statins.
  • Propofol prolonged infusion (PRIS).
  • Certain antiretrovirals (NRTIs).
SAQ 139 — Anti-NMDAR Encephalitis Following HSV HARD
A 14-year-old, 8 weeks after completing 21 days of acyclovir for HSV encephalitis, develops new psychiatric symptoms, dyskinesias, and seizures.

DX

Explain the phenomenon and how to distinguish from HSV relapse.
Show structured answer
Post-HSV anti-NMDAR encephalitis — recognised entity.

Mechanism:
  • HSV infection causes neuronal damage exposing intracellular antigens.
  • Triggers autoimmune response with new anti-NMDAR antibodies (also less commonly anti-AMPA, anti-GABA-B).
  • Develops 1–3 months after acute HSV illness.
  • Up to 25–30% of HSV survivors develop CNS autoantibodies.

Distinguishing from HSV relapse:
  • HSV PCR negative in CSF (in post-infectious autoimmune).
  • Anti-NMDAR antibodies positive in CSF + serum.
  • Typical anti-NMDAR features: psychiatric, dyskinesia, seizures.
  • HSV relapse would show recurrent typical HSV imaging changes and positive PCR.

TREATMENT

Treatment differs — what is the approach?
Show structured answer
Management:
  • First-line immunotherapy: high-dose IV methylprednisolone, IVIG, plasma exchange.
  • Second-line for refractory: rituximab, cyclophosphamide.
  • Do NOT routinely repeat acyclovir if PCR negative — focus is on immune dysregulation.
  • Symptomatic: AEDs, low-dose antipsychotics with caution (these patients sensitive to neuroleptic side effects).
  • Long-term immunotherapy maintenance to prevent relapse.
  • Recovery typically slower than primary anti-NMDAR; full recovery may take months-years.
SAQ 140 — MOGAD vs NMOSD vs MS Distinction MODERATE
A 9-year-old presents with bilateral painful optic neuritis and longitudinally extensive transverse myelitis.

CRITERIA

How are MOGAD, NMOSD, and MS distinguished?
Show structured answer
FeatureMOGADNMOSDMS
AntibodyMOG-IgG (cell-based assay, titre > 1:160)AQP4-IgGUsually negative
Oligoclonal bandsUsually negativeNegativePOSITIVE (~ 85%)
Typical courseMonophasic or relapsingRelapsing, severe attacksRelapsing-remitting (usually)
Optic neuritisOften bilateral, anteriorOften severe, sequentialUsually unilateral
Cord lesionsLETM commonLETM characteristicShort, peripheral
Cerebral lesionsFluffy cortical/subcorticalArea postrema, hypothalamusPeriventricular, callosal

TREATMENT

Differential therapy implications?
Show structured answer
MOGAD:
  • Acute: high-dose IV methylprednisolone, prolonged oral taper (≥ 3 months to avoid steroid-taper relapse).
  • PLEX / IVIG for severe / refractory.
  • Long-term: rituximab, IVIG monthly, mycophenolate.
  • AVOID interferon-β, natalizumab.

NMOSD:
  • Acute: pulse steroids + PLEX.
  • Long-term: rituximab, eculizumab, satralizumab, inebilizumab (FDA-approved for AQP4+ NMOSD).
  • AVOID interferon-β, natalizumab (worsen NMOSD).

MS:
  • Acute: pulse steroids.
  • Long-term DMTs: interferons, dimethyl fumarate, fingolimod, natalizumab, ocrelizumab, etc.

NMOSD-specific drugs (eculizumab, satralizumab) are NOT for MOGAD. MS DMTs are NOT for MOGAD/NMOSD.
SAQ 141 — Salicylate Toxicity — Management Cornerstones HARD
A 16-year-old presents 6 hours after ingesting 100 aspirin tablets. ABG: pH 7.41, PaCO₂ 18, HCO₃ 13. Salicylate level 75 mg/dL.

PHYSIOLOGY

Explain the mixed acid-base picture.
Show structured answer
Salicylate has dual effects:
  • Direct stimulation of brainstem respiratory centre → primary respiratory ALKALOSIS (PaCO₂ ↓).
  • Uncoupling of oxidative phosphorylation → anaerobic metabolism → primary metabolic ACIDOSIS with elevated anion gap.

Net pH may be normal, alkalemic, or acidemic depending on relative magnitudes and stage.

In children, metabolic acidosis tends to predominate earlier than in adults.

Toxicity stages: tinnitus → confusion + hyperthermia → coma, seizures, cerebral oedema, pulmonary oedema → death.

TREATMENT

Outline alkalinisation strategy and hemodialysis indications.
Show structured answer
Alkalinisation:
  • IV NaHCO₃ to target serum pH ≥ 7.45 (keeps salicylate ionised in blood → less crossing into CNS).
  • Target urine pH ≥ 7.5 (5-fold increase in renal clearance per pH unit).
  • Repleted potassium (alkalinisation requires adequate K to maintain urine alkalinisation).
  • Glucose-containing fluids (neuroglycopenia common despite normal serum glucose — CSF glucose depleted).

Hemodialysis indications:
  • Acute level > 100 mg/dL or chronic > 60.
  • Severe acidosis (pH < 7.2 or worsening).
  • Altered mental status.
  • Pulmonary or cerebral oedema.
  • Renal failure.
  • Refractory to alkalinisation.

DO NOT casually intubate — compensatory hyperventilation is protective; iatrogenic hypoventilation (with sedation/paralysis) → catastrophic acidemia and rapid death. If you must intubate: match minute ventilation aggressively, pre-treat with bicarbonate.

DEFINITION

What is the 'ion trapping' principle?
Show structured answer
Ion trapping: Salicylate exists in ionised (water-soluble) and non-ionised (lipid-soluble) forms.

Only the non-ionised form crosses the BBB and cell membranes.
  • Lower blood pH (acidemia) → more non-ionised salicylate → more crosses into CNS → more brain toxicity.
  • Higher blood pH → more ionised → less CNS distribution.
  • Conversely in urine: alkaline urine traps the ionised form → cannot be reabsorbed → enhanced excretion.
Same principle applies to phenobarbital and methotrexate.
SAQ 142 — CO Poisoning — Hyperbaric Oxygen Indications MODERATE
Two siblings present with confusion and headache after exposure to a faulty gas heater. SpO₂ 100% on RA; COHb 32%.

PHYSIOLOGY

Explain why pulse oximetry is unreliable.
Show structured answer
  • CO binds Hb with ~ 250× the affinity of O₂.
  • Carboxyhaemoglobin (COHb) reduces O₂-carrying capacity AND shifts O₂-Hb dissociation curve to the LEFT (impairs unloading).
  • Pulse oximetry cannot distinguish COHb from O₂Hb (both absorb at similar wavelengths).
  • So SpO₂ reads falsely normal even with high COHb.
  • Need co-oximetry (multi-wavelength) to measure COHb directly.

TREATMENT

Outline indications for hyperbaric oxygen.
Show structured answer
Half-life of COHb:
  • Room air: 4–5 hours.
  • 100% normobaric O₂: 80 min.
  • Hyperbaric O₂ (2.4–3 ATA): 20–30 min.

Indications for HBO:
  • Loss of consciousness (any duration).
  • Severe neurological deficits (cognitive, motor, cerebellar).
  • Seizures.
  • Cardiac ischaemia (chest pain, ECG changes, troponin elevation).
  • Severe metabolic acidosis.
  • COHb > 25% (some sources 40%).
  • Pregnancy with COHb > 15%.
  • Persistent symptoms despite 4–6 hours of normobaric 100% O₂.

Continue 100% O₂ until COHb < 5% AND symptoms resolved.
Watch for delayed neuropsychiatric sequelae (DNS) — appear 2–40 days after exposure with cognitive, personality, movement disorder. HBO may reduce DNS incidence.
SAQ 143 — Cyanide Poisoning in Fire Victims HARD
A teenager rescued from a house fire has severe lactic acidosis disproportionate to other findings.

DX

What clues suggest concurrent cyanide poisoning?
Show structured answer
Clues to cyanide in fire victims:
  • Severe lactic acidosis disproportionate to CO level.
  • Narrow A-V O₂ difference (tissues can't extract O₂).
  • 'Pink' or bright red skin colour from saturated venous blood (despite desaturation).
  • Neurological + cardiovascular collapse out of proportion to CO.
  • Smell of bitter almonds (only 40% can detect).
  • Combustion of plastics, wool, polyurethane releases HCN.

Mechanism: Cyanide blocks cytochrome oxidase (complex IV) → cellular anaerobic metabolism despite O₂ availability → lactic acidosis.

TREATMENT

Specific antidotes and the contraindication to consider in fire victims.
Show structured answer
HYDROXOCOBALAMIN (Cyanokit) 70 mg/kg IV — first-line in pediatric fire victims.
  • Binds cyanide directly → cyanocobalamin (vitamin B12) excreted in urine.
  • Safe even when cyanide poisoning uncertain (doesn't reduce O₂ carrying capacity, doesn't cause hypotension, compatible with CO co-poisoning).
  • Side effects: red-orange discolouration of urine and skin (cosmetic); transient hypertension; lab interference.

Alternative — sodium thiosulfate:
  • Provides sulfur for rhodanese to convert cyanide to thiocyanate (renally excreted).
  • Slower onset.

AVOID nitrites (sodium nitrite, amyl nitrite) in fire victims with CO co-poisoning:
  • Nitrites induce methaemoglobinaemia (cyanide binds metHb instead of cytochromes).
  • This compounds the inability to carry oxygen when CO is also bound to remaining Hb.
  • Hydroxocobalamin is safer.

Continue 100% O₂, treat acidosis, manage seizures, ICU support.
SAQ 144 — Lead Encephalopathy — Combined Chelation HARD
A 3-year-old with blood lead level 95 µg/dL presents with seizures and encephalopathy.

CRITERIA

Indications for combination chelation.
Show structured answer
Indications for combined BAL + EDTA:
  • Symptomatic encephalopathy (regardless of level).
  • Blood lead level > 70 µg/dL.
  • Cerebral oedema or seizures.
  • PICU admission MANDATORY.

For asymptomatic BLL 45–69 → oral DMSA (succimer) monotherapy.
For BLL < 45 → environmental remediation, dietary, no chelation routinely.

TREATMENT

Outline the BAL-EDTA protocol and the critical 4-hour rule.
Show structured answer
Combined chelation protocol:
  1. Dimercaprol (BAL) 75 mg/m² IM q4h, DEEP IM in peanut oil (DO NOT USE in peanut allergic patients).
  2. BEGIN BAL FIRST.
  3. FOUR HOURS LATER, start CaNa₂EDTA 1500 mg/m²/day IV (24-hour infusion or split q4h).
  4. Critical: starting EDTA BEFORE BAL can paradoxically redistribute lead into the brain → worsens encephalopathy. The 4-hour delay is essential.
  5. Continue 5 days, reassess BLL.
  6. Follow with succimer (DMSA) oral 30 mg/kg/day × 5 days, then 20 mg/kg/day × 14 days; reassess.

Supportive:
  • ICP management.
  • Seizure control (avoid mannitol initially — fluid balance).
  • Identify and REMEDIATE LEAD SOURCE — child cannot return home until decontaminated.
  • Iron deficiency often co-exists — replete iron AFTER chelation initiated.
  • Public health reporting.
  • Family/sibling screening.
  • Long-term cognitive and behavioural surveillance.
SAQ 145 — Methanol Poisoning — Toxic Alcohol HARD
A teenager presents with severe metabolic acidosis, visual loss, and an osmolar gap.

DX

Diagnosis and the toxic metabolite responsible.
Show structured answer
Methanol poisoning (or ethylene glycol — similar approach).

Parent compound is relatively non-toxic — the TOXIC METABOLITES cause injury:
  • Methanol → metabolised by alcohol dehydrogenase (ADH) → FORMALDEHYDE → then formic acid (FORMATE).
  • Formate inhibits cytochrome oxidase → cellular anoxia, severe metabolic acidosis.
  • Formate is particularly toxic to optic nerve and retina → blindness.
  • Anion gap metabolic acidosis develops as parent alcohol is metabolised.
  • Osmolar gap is HIGH early (parent alcohol present) but normalises as alcohol metabolised → toxic anion gap acidosis appears.

Ethylene glycol: metabolised to glycoaldehyde → glycolic acid → glyoxylic acid → oxalic acid → calcium oxalate crystals (renal injury).

TREATMENT

Outline antidote and definitive treatment.
Show structured answer
Antidote — FOMEPIZOLE (4-methylpyrazole):
  • Competitive inhibitor of alcohol dehydrogenase.
  • Loading dose 15 mg/kg IV, then 10 mg/kg q12h.
  • Blocks formation of toxic metabolites.
  • Preferred over ethanol (which also blocks ADH but causes intoxication, hypoglycaemia, harder to dose).

HEMODIALYSIS:
  • Removes parent alcohol AND toxic metabolites.
  • Indicated for: severe acidosis, AKI, visual changes, refractory electrolyte derangement, methanol level > 50 mg/dL.

Adjuncts:
  • Folinic acid for methanol (cofactor — accelerates formate metabolism to CO₂).
  • Thiamine + pyridoxine for ethylene glycol (cofactors that shunt glyoxylate to less toxic metabolites).
  • Sodium bicarbonate for severe acidosis.
  • Treat seizures, manage electrolytes (hypocalcaemia in ethylene glycol).
SAQ 146 — Tricyclic Antidepressant Overdose HARD
A 14-year-old ingests her parent's amitriptyline. ECG shows widened QRS 130 ms, agitation, dilated pupils.

PHYSIOLOGY

Explain the multiple mechanisms of TCA toxicity.
Show structured answer
TCAs have FIVE classes of effects:
  1. Sodium channel blockade — widens QRS, predisposes to arrhythmia (terminal R wave in aVR, right-axis deviation of terminal 40 ms of QRS).
  2. Anticholinergic — dilated pupils, dry skin, urinary retention, tachycardia, hyperthermia, delirium.
  3. α1-adrenergic blockade — vasodilation, hypotension.
  4. Antihistamine — sedation.
  5. Monoamine reuptake inhibition — varies by agent.

Anticholinergic + Na channel + α-blockade + antihistamine = 'cardiotoxic anticholinergic with seizures' — classic TCA toxidrome.

TREATMENT

Outline acute management.
Show structured answer
Sodium bicarbonate IV 1–2 mEq/kg for QRS > 100 ms:
  • Sodium loading restores Na channel function.
  • Alkalinisation increases protein binding of TCA, reduces free drug.
  • Target serum pH 7.45–7.55, narrow QRS.

Other interventions:
  • Cardiac monitoring.
  • Benzodiazepines for seizures (AVOID phenytoin — worsens Na channel blockade).
  • Intubation if needed for airway protection.
  • Activated charcoal if recent ingestion and protected airway.
  • IV lipid emulsion (Intralipid) for severe cardiac toxicity refractory to bicarbonate (lipid sink hypothesis).
  • Norepinephrine for hypotension (avoid epinephrine — paradoxical worsening due to α-blockade).
  • AVOID flumazenil, AVOID physostigmine (anticholinergic reversal can cause seizures and arrhythmia in TCA overdose).
  • Watch for delayed cardiotoxicity 24–48 h.
SAQ 147 — Cerebral Microdialysis Interpretation HARD
A child with severe TBI is being monitored with a cerebral microdialysis catheter. Sample values: lactate 5.5, pyruvate 70, L/P ratio 79, glucose 0.5, glutamate elevated, glycerol elevated.

DEFINITION

Define each microdialysis biomarker and its physiologic meaning.
Show structured answer
  • Lactate: end-product of anaerobic glycolysis; rises in ischaemia, hyperglycolysis, mitochondrial dysfunction.
  • Pyruvate: end-product of aerobic glycolysis; alternative substrate for Krebs cycle.
  • L/P ratio: the most sensitive marker of cerebral metabolic state. Normal < 25; mildly elevated 25–40; severely elevated > 40 indicates ischaemia or severe mitochondrial dysfunction.
  • Glucose: < 0.8 mmol/L (interstitial) indicates inadequate substrate delivery (low CPP, severe ischaemia, hypoglycaemia).
  • Glutamate: excitatory amino acid; rises in ischaemia and excitotoxicity.
  • Glycerol: released from membrane phospholipids → marker of cell membrane breakdown.

DX

Interpret this metabolic pattern.
Show structured answer
Ischaemic energy crisis:
  • L/P > 40 indicates failure to oxidise pyruvate (mitochondrial dysfunction or limited oxygen).
  • Low interstitial glucose → inadequate substrate delivery.
  • Elevated glutamate → excitotoxicity.
  • Elevated glycerol → membrane breakdown.
  • Pattern fits ischaemia (low CPP, microcirculatory failure) with secondary energy crisis.

Distinguishing patterns:
  • Ischaemia: elevated L/P + low glucose.
  • Mitochondrial dysfunction without ischaemia: elevated L/P + normal/high glucose.
  • Hyperglycolysis: elevated L + P with normal L/P.
  • Normal: L/P < 25, glucose > 1.5.

TREATMENT

What therapeutic responses follow?
Show structured answer
  • Optimise CPP — increase MAP toward CPPopt (or higher within tolerated range).
  • Reduce CMRO₂ — deeper sedation, treat seizures, normothermia.
  • Optimise oxygen delivery — Hb, SpO₂, PaO₂.
  • Manage PaCO₂ — avoid hypocapnia (vasoconstriction worsens ischaemia).
  • Consider ICP escalation if elevated.
  • Reassess after intervention — microdialysis values lag 1–2 hours.
SAQ 148 — PbtO₂ Monitoring HARD
A child with severe TBI has a Licox probe placed showing PbtO₂ 12 mm Hg.

DEFINITION

Define PbtO₂ and its normal/therapeutic range.
Show structured answer
PbtO₂ (brain tissue oxygen pressure): Direct measurement of oxygen tension in brain parenchyma (subcortical white matter usually).

Ranges:
  • Normal: 23–35 mm Hg.
  • Mild compromise: 15–23 mm Hg.
  • Significant hypoxia: 10–15 mm Hg — increased risk of mortality.
  • Critical: < 10 mm Hg — high risk of irreversible tissue injury.

Reflects balance between O₂ delivery and consumption in the monitored region (1–2 cm³).

PHYSIOLOGY

List determinants of PbtO₂.
Show structured answer
Five main determinants:
  1. Cerebral perfusion pressure (CPP) and cerebral blood flow.
  2. Arterial oxygen content (Hb concentration × SpO₂ + dissolved O₂).
  3. Cerebral metabolic rate of O₂ consumption (CMRO₂).
  4. Diffusion characteristics (oedema, microthrombosis).
  5. Probe position (proximity to lesion influences readings).

TREATMENT

Outline interventions when PbtO₂ is low.
Show structured answer
Stepwise approach when PbtO₂ < 15:
  1. Increase MAP to optimise CPP.
  2. Increase FiO₂ to raise PaO₂.
  3. Transfuse RBCs if Hb < 80–90 g/L.
  4. Reduce CMRO₂: deeper sedation, treat fever, treat seizures.
  5. Optimise PaCO₂ (normocapnia; avoid hypocapnia).
  6. Address ICP if elevated.
  7. Consider repositioning probe if values seem discordant with clinical picture.

BOOST-II adult trial: PbtO₂-guided protocol reduced hypoxia but did not improve mortality. BOOST-3 ongoing.
SAQ 149 — FIRES Syndrome HARD
A previously well 8-year-old presents with refractory super-refractory status epilepticus 1 week after a febrile illness.

DEFINITION

Define FIRES and its current understanding.
Show structured answer
FIRES (Febrile Infection-Related Epilepsy Syndrome):
  • Catastrophic epileptic encephalopathy in previously well children.
  • Onset of refractory status epilepticus 24 h to 2 weeks after febrile illness.
  • Fever is usually resolved at SE onset.
  • Considered part of NORSE (New-Onset Refractory Status Epilepticus) spectrum — FIRES is the pediatric subset with febrile prodrome.
  • Mechanism: presumed immune-mediated / cytokine-driven, with elevated IL-1β, IL-6.
  • Outcome: high mortality (~ 10–30%); survivors typically develop chronic drug-resistant epilepsy with cognitive impairment.

TREATMENT

Outline the tiered treatment including immunotherapy.
Show structured answer
Tier 1: Standard SE management.
Tier 2: ICU sedation with midazolam, ketamine, pentobarbital.
Tier 3: Immunotherapy + Ketogenic Diet (target ketosis within days).
  • High-dose corticosteroids + IVIG.
  • Plasma exchange.
  • Cytokine-targeted therapy:
    • Anakinra (IL-1 receptor antagonist) — 2–5 mg/kg/day; increasing evidence for early use.
    • Tocilizumab (IL-6 antagonist) — case series support.
  • Ketogenic diet — initiate early; aim for serum β-OHB > 2 mmol/L.

Investigations:
  • Autoimmune encephalitis panel.
  • Genetic testing for known epileptic encephalopathy genes.
  • Cytokine panel (IL-1β, IL-6).
  • Surveillance for ovarian teratoma if applicable.

NEXT STEP

What is the long-term management consideration?
Show structured answer
After acute phase:
  • Multidrug-resistant epilepsy common — multiple AEDs, often broad-spectrum (valproate, levetiracetam, clobazam, perampanel).
  • Continue ketogenic diet long-term — significant evidence for sustained benefit.
  • Continue maintenance anakinra in some.
  • VNS for refractory epilepsy.
  • Surgical evaluation if focal substrate identified.
  • Neuropsychology, educational support, palliative care if severe.
  • Family genetic counselling and support.
SAQ 150 — ICANS — CAR-T Cell-Associated Neurotoxicity HARD
A 12-year-old, day 7 post-CAR-T therapy for relapsed ALL, develops confusion, expressive aphasia, and seizure.

DEFINITION

Define ICANS and the ASTCT grading system.
Show structured answer
ICANS (Immune effector Cell-Associated Neurotoxicity Syndrome): CNS toxicity following CAR-T cell or other engineered T-cell therapies.

ASTCT consensus grading (2019):
Based on the worst score of:
  1. ICE score (10-point cognitive assessment).
  2. Level of consciousness.
  3. Seizures.
  4. Motor findings.
  5. Elevated ICP / cerebral oedema.

Grades 1–4:
  • Grade 1: ICE 7–9, awake.
  • Grade 2: ICE 3–6, awakens to voice.
  • Grade 3: ICE 0–2, awakens only to tactile, clinical seizure resolves, focal oedema.
  • Grade 4: unarousable, refractory seizures or status, motor weakness, diffuse oedema / Cushing's triad.

TREATMENT

Outline management of grade 3–4 ICANS.
Show structured answer
General supportive:
  • ICU admission for grade ≥ 2.
  • Aspiration precautions, swallowing assessment.
  • cEEG monitoring.

Corticosteroids:
  • Grade 2: oral dexamethasone 10 mg q6h or IV equivalent.
  • Grade 3: dexamethasone 10–20 mg IV q6h.
  • Grade 4: methylprednisolone 1 g/day for 3 days.

Tocilizumab:
  • If concurrent CRS (cytokine release syndrome).
  • Does NOT cross BBB well — doesn't directly treat ICANS but treats systemic CRS contributing.

Anakinra: for refractory ICANS without CRS response to tocilizumab.

Seizure control: levetiracetam loaded then maintenance.
ICP management: if cerebral oedema — hyperosmolar therapy, hyperventilation, sedation, neurosurgical consultation.
SAQ 151 — Anti-LGI1 Limbic Encephalitis MODERATE
A 13-year-old presents with subacute memory loss, faciobrachial dystonic seizures (FBDS — brief seconds-long unilateral facial-arm dystonic jerks), and hyponatraemia.

DX

Diagnosis and the antigen target.
Show structured answer
Anti-LGI1 (Leucine-rich glioma-inactivated 1) encephalitis:
  • Most common autoimmune encephalitis in older adults; rare but recognised in pediatrics.
  • Antibodies target LGI1 — part of the voltage-gated potassium channel complex (along with CASPR2).
  • Classic triad: limbic encephalitis (memory loss, behavioural change, temporal seizures) + FBDS + hyponatraemia from SIADH.
  • FBDS often precede other features by weeks-months; recognising them allows early diagnosis.
  • MRI: medial temporal T2/FLAIR hyperintensity (similar to HSV encephalitis but bilateral and without haemorrhage).
  • CSF: minimally inflammatory typically.

TREATMENT

Treatment approach and notable feature?
Show structured answer
  • First-line: high-dose IV methylprednisolone, IVIG, plasma exchange.
  • Second-line: rituximab, cyclophosphamide.
  • FBDS often respond DRAMATICALLY to immunotherapy alone — characteristic feature.
  • AEDs help suppress FBDS but the disease itself is autoimmune.
  • Long-term immunotherapy maintenance to prevent relapse.
  • Tumour screen (thymoma, SCLC in adults; less common in pediatrics) — may screen thymus and chest.
  • Recovery often good with treatment, but memory deficits may persist.
SAQ 152 — Decompressive Craniectomy in Pediatric TBI HARD
A 12-year-old with severe TBI has refractory ICP > 25 despite optimised tier 1 and tier 2 management.

CRITERIA

List indications for decompressive craniectomy.
Show structured answer
Pediatric DC indications:
  • Refractory ICP > 20–25 mm Hg despite maximal medical management.
  • Imaging evidence of mass effect/midline shift with surgical lesion → primary craniectomy for lesion evacuation.
  • Diffuse cerebral oedema with effaced cisterns.
  • Salvageable brain (no imaging evidence of irreversible global injury, intact brainstem reflexes).
  • Performed by 6–24 hours of refractory ICP (timing controversial — earlier may be better).

Evidence base:
  • Adult: DECRA (paradoxically increased poor outcomes); RescueICP (better outcomes but more severe disability survivors).
  • Pediatric evidence (Taylor 2001, Adelson) generally favours DC more strongly than adult RCTs.

TREATMENT

Technical considerations and post-operative care.
Show structured answer
Technique:
  • Large bone flap (12–15 cm diameter or larger) — small flaps inadequate.
  • Bilateral fronto-temporo-parietal craniectomy for diffuse swelling.
  • Unilateral with duraplasty for hemispheric swelling.
  • Open dura with expansion duraplasty.
  • Bone flap preserved (subcutaneous abdominal pouch or frozen) for delayed cranioplasty.

Post-op:
  • Continue ICP monitoring (re-zero baseline).
  • Continued tier 1 management.
  • Watch for: external cerebral herniation through the defect, hygromas, hydrocephalus.
  • Helmet protection while skull defect present.
  • Cranioplasty (replacement of bone flap or PMMA prosthesis) at 6 weeks–6 months — earlier may improve outcomes.

PHYSIOLOGY

Explain why DC works physiologically.
Show structured answer
Monro-Kellie doctrine: Fixed cranial vault contains brain + CSF + blood. If one increases, another must decrease to maintain constant volume.

When all compensation exhausted, ICP rises exponentially.

DC removes the constraint of the fixed vault → swollen brain can expand outward → ICP decreases.

Allows reduction of secondary injury from herniation and global ischaemia.

Risks: paradoxical brain swelling beyond margins, infection, post-traumatic hydrocephalus.
SAQ 153 — Methotrexate Neurotoxicity HARD
A 10-year-old with ALL develops acute confusion, expressive aphasia, and right hemiparesis 6 hours after the third intrathecal methotrexate dose.

DX

Diagnosis and the typical MRI pattern.
Show structured answer
Acute methotrexate-induced leukoencephalopathy (stroke-like syndrome):
  • Onset typically hours to days after IT methotrexate (or high-dose IV).
  • Manifestations: hemiparesis, aphasia, encephalopathy, seizures.
  • MRI: bilateral DWI restriction in the centrum semiovale (deep white matter), often with periventricular extension.
  • Distinguishes from arterial stroke: bilateral, white-matter predominant, no vascular territory.
  • Reversible in most cases (over days-weeks).

TREATMENT

Acute management.
Show structured answer
  • Hold/discontinue methotrexate.
  • IV leucovorin (folinic acid) rescue.
  • Adjuncts reported: dextromethorphan (NMDA antagonist), aminophylline, high-dose folinic acid.
  • Supportive care.
  • If CSF methotrexate level elevated (rare): intrathecal glucarpidase (carboxypeptidase) hydrolyses methotrexate to inactive metabolites.
  • Most patients recover; some develop chronic leukoencephalopathy with cognitive deficits.
  • Future MTX dosing decisions individualised — may need dose reduction or switch.
SAQ 154 — Tacrolimus-Induced PRES MODERATE
A 12-year-old solid-organ transplant recipient on tacrolimus presents with confusion, seizures, and BP 150/90. MRI: bilateral parieto-occipital T2/FLAIR hyperintensity without restricted diffusion.

DX

Diagnosis and the typical clinical features.
Show structured answer
Posterior reversible encephalopathy syndrome (PRES):
  • Clinical: headache, altered mental status, seizures, visual disturbances.
  • BP often elevated but not always.
  • MRI hallmark: bilateral parieto-occipital subcortical T2/FLAIR hyperintensity (vasogenic oedema, no DWI restriction).
  • Atypical patterns: frontal, brainstem, deep grey — increasingly recognised.
  • Causes: calcineurin inhibitors (cyclosporine, tacrolimus), chemotherapy (vincristine, cisplatin), hypertension, eclampsia, autoimmune disease, sepsis.

PHYSIOLOGY

Explain the pathophysiology.
Show structured answer
Two main hypotheses:
  1. Failure of cerebral autoregulation in posterior circulation (less sympathetic innervation than anterior) → hyperperfusion and BBB disruption.
  2. Endothelial dysfunction from cytotoxic drugs → BBB disruption with vasogenic oedema.
Posterior watershed regions affected because of relatively weaker autoregulation.

TREATMENT

Management.
Show structured answer
  • Remove offending agent: hold or switch tacrolimus (e.g., to sirolimus or belatacept depending on transplant context).
  • Controlled BP reduction — 10–25% of MAP over hours; AVOID precipitous drops.
  • Seizure control with levetiracetam first-line.
  • Supportive ICU care.
  • Most cases resolve over days to weeks.
  • Long-term: surveillance for recurrence if calcineurin inhibitor reintroduced; consider alternative immunosuppression.
SAQ 155 — Hepatic Encephalopathy from Acute Liver Failure HARD
A 14-year-old presents with acute liver failure from paracetamol overdose and West Haven grade III encephalopathy.

DEFINITION

Classify hepatic encephalopathy and describe the West Haven scale.
Show structured answer
West Haven scale:
  • Grade 0: minimal HE (subclinical, only detected on neuropsychological testing).
  • Grade I: trivial lack of awareness, shortened attention span, altered sleep pattern, asterixis absent.
  • Grade II: lethargy or apathy, disorientation, slurred speech, asterixis present.
  • Grade III: somnolence to semi-stupor, gross disorientation, asterixis usually absent.
  • Grade IV: coma.

Grade III/IV in ALF correlates with risk of cerebral oedema and raised ICP.

PHYSIOLOGY

Explain the pathophysiology of cerebral oedema in ALF.
Show structured answer
  • Hyperammonaemia is the major driver.
  • Astrocytes convert ammonia + glutamate → glutamine via glutamine synthase.
  • Glutamine accumulates intracellularly → osmotic astrocyte swelling.
  • Inflammation (cytokine release from failing liver) impairs BBB function and exacerbates oedema.
  • Cerebral blood flow disturbance — initial reduction, then hyperaemia in later stages.
  • Result: cytotoxic + vasogenic cerebral oedema → raised ICP.

TREATMENT

Outline management priorities including ICP.
Show structured answer
General:
  • ICU admission, transplant evaluation early.
  • Treat cause (NAC for paracetamol).

ICP and cerebral oedema:
  • Head of bed 30°; head neutral.
  • Hyperosmolar therapy: 3% saline (target Na 145–155), mannitol if Na low.
  • Normothermia or mild hypothermia (33–34 °C — some evidence; case-by-case).
  • Normocapnia.
  • AVOID over-sedation that worsens encephalopathy assessment; but use enough to control agitation/seizures.
  • ICP monitoring: invasive monitoring controversial (coagulopathy and infection risk); some centres use.

Ammonia management:
  • Lactulose orally/PR.
  • Rifaximin.
  • CRRT with high-flux filter for severe hyperammonaemia (also clears other toxins).

Coagulopathy: vitamin K, platelets for active bleeding; avoid prophylactic FFP (interferes with prognostic markers).
Transplant: emergent evaluation; King's College criteria for paracetamol.
SAQ 156 — Cerebral Malaria HARD
A 10-year-old child returns from sub-Saharan Africa with high fever, declining LOC, seizures, and retinal haemorrhages. Blood smear: P. falciparum, parasitaemia 5%.

CRITERIA

List WHO criteria for severe falciparum malaria.
Show structured answer
WHO severe malaria criteria (any of the following with P. falciparum):
  • Impaired consciousness (GCS < 11).
  • Multiple convulsions (≥ 2 in 24 h).
  • Prostration (unable to sit/stand/feed).
  • Acidosis (HCO₃ < 15, lactate > 5, base deficit > 8).
  • Hypoglycaemia < 2.2 mmol/L.
  • Severe anaemia (Hb < 7 in children).
  • Renal impairment.
  • Jaundice (bilirubin > 50).
  • Pulmonary oedema.
  • Abnormal bleeding.
  • Shock.
  • Hyperparasitaemia (> 10% in low transmission, > 20% in high transmission).

Malarial retinopathy (whitening, haemorrhages, vessel changes) is highly specific — distinguishes cerebral malaria from other causes of coma.

TREATMENT

Outline first-line therapy.
Show structured answer
IV ARTESUNATE 2.4 mg/kg at 0, 12, 24 hours, then once daily.
  • Superior to IV quinine — SEAQUAMAT and AQUAMAT trials showed mortality reduction.
  • Continue until child can tolerate oral therapy.
  • Complete with 3-day ACT (artemisinin-based combination therapy).
  • Watch for post-artesunate delayed haemolysis — 1–3 weeks after treatment; check Hb regularly.

Supportive ICU care:
  • Airway and ventilation.
  • Glucose monitoring — hypoglycaemia common (replacement essential).
  • Treat seizures with benzodiazepines (AVOID prophylactic phenobarbital — increased mortality per Crawley et al. 2000).
  • Transfusion if severe anaemia.
  • Avoid over-hydration — FEAST trial showed bolus fluids worsen outcomes in African children with severe febrile illness; cautious maintenance.
  • Treat superinfection.

Do NOT use corticosteroids — no benefit, possibly harmful.
Do NOT use phenobarbital prophylactically.
SAQ 157 — Tuberculous Meningitis — Stages and Treatment MODERATE
A child with tuberculous meningitis presents with hydrocephalus, basal cisternal enhancement, and BMRC stage II disease.

CRITERIA

Outline the BMRC staging system for TBM.
Show structured answer
British Medical Research Council (BMRC) staging:
  • Stage I: Alert and oriented, no focal neurologic deficits; non-specific symptoms (fever, headache, malaise).
  • Stage II: Lethargic or confused, with focal neurologic deficits (cranial nerve palsies, hemiparesis).
  • Stage III: Stupor or coma, severe deficits, multiple cranial nerve palsies, abnormal posturing.

Outcome correlates STRONGLY with stage at treatment initiation — stage I has excellent outcomes; stage III mortality 30–50% with significant morbidity in survivors.
Hence early recognition and treatment are critical.

TREATMENT

Outline the antitubercular regimen and adjunctive steroid use.
Show structured answer
Intensive phase (2 months):
  • Isoniazid + Rifampicin + Pyrazinamide + Ethambutol (HRZE).
  • HIGH-DOSE rifampicin per recent trials (15–20 mg/kg/day) — better CNS penetration.
  • Pyridoxine to prevent isoniazid-induced neuropathy.

Continuation phase (7–10 months):
  • Isoniazid + Rifampicin.
  • Total ≥ 9–12 months for TBM.
  • Longer (12 months) for HIV co-infected, severe disease.

Adjunctive dexamethasone:
  • Thwaites et al., NEJM 2004 — improves survival in TBM regardless of HIV status.
  • 0.4 mg/kg/day IV tapered over 6–8 weeks.
  • Most benefit in stages II and III.

Hydrocephalus management:
  • Communicating hydrocephalus (most common) → medical first (acetazolamide, furosemide) then VP shunt if persistent.
  • Obstructive (less common) → urgent VP shunt or ETV.

Watch for IRIS, hepatotoxicity, drug interactions.
SAQ 158 — Wilson Disease — Diagnosis and Treatment MODERATE
A 14-year-old presents with parkinsonism, dysarthria, drooling, Kayser-Fleischer rings, and elevated transaminases.

CRITERIA

Outline diagnostic criteria and the Leipzig score.
Show structured answer
Leipzig diagnostic score (2003, updated):
Points assigned for:
  • Kayser-Fleischer rings (2 points).
  • Neurological symptoms (1–2 points).
  • Low ceruloplasmin (< 100 mg/L = 2; < 200 = 1).
  • Coombs-negative haemolytic anaemia (1 point).
  • Elevated 24-h urinary copper (1 point for > 1×ULN, 2 points for > 2×ULN).
  • Liver copper on biopsy (1–2 points).
  • ATP7B mutation analysis (1–4 points).

Score ≥ 4 = diagnostic; 3 = probable; ≤ 2 unlikely.

TREATMENT

Outline chelation strategy with attention to neurologic worsening.
Show structured answer
Chelation:
  • Trientine — preferred initial chelator in NEUROLOGIC Wilson disease.
  • D-penicillamine — effective but can cause paradoxical NEUROLOGIC WORSENING in 10–50% of patients (mobilises copper that worsens CNS deposition before urinary excretion). Start LOW, go SLOW.
  • Zinc — for maintenance after chelation, or for pre-symptomatic patients. Induces enterocyte metallothionein, blocking copper absorption.

Diet: avoid copper-rich foods (shellfish, organ meat, chocolate, nuts).

Liver transplant: for fulminant hepatic failure or end-stage cirrhosis.

Lifelong therapy mandatory. Monitor with urinary copper, ceruloplasmin, transaminases, clinical exam.

Cascade testing: AR with 25% recurrence; presymptomatic identification of siblings allows early initiation.
SAQ 159 — Hyperosmolar Therapy in TBI MODERATE
A child with severe TBI has refractory ICP. The team is choosing between 3% saline and mannitol.

DEFINITION

Compare 3% saline and mannitol — mechanism and considerations.
Show structured answer
3% saline (hypertonic saline):
  • Bolus dose: 3–10 mL/kg over 10–20 min.
  • Continuous infusion: 0.1–1 mL/kg/h to maintain target Na.
  • Mechanism: osmotic gradient draws water from brain into vasculature; preserves intravascular volume.
  • Targets: Na 145–155, osm 300–320 (tier 1); Na up to 160, osm up to 360 (tier 2).
  • Advantages: preserves volume status; effective even when BBB partially disrupted; can be given as continuous infusion.

Mannitol:
  • 0.25–1 g/kg IV over 15–30 min q4–6h.
  • Mechanism: same osmotic gradient.
  • Plus rheologic effect — reduces blood viscosity, improves microcirculation.
  • Diuretic effect → may cause hypovolaemia, hypotension.
  • Limit total exposure (cumulative nephrotoxicity); target serum osm < 320 (mannitol component).
  • Calculate osmol gap (measured – calculated osm) — if > 10, hold mannitol.

TREATMENT

Targets and when to escalate.
Show structured answer
Targets (tier 1):
  • Na 145–155 mmol/L.
  • Serum osm 295–320 mOsm/kg.
  • If osmol gap > 10 → hold mannitol (concern for tubular toxicity).

Targets (tier 2):
  • Na up to 160 mmol/L.
  • Serum osm up to 340 mOsm/kg.

Monitoring:
  • Serum Na, K, Cl, osm q4–6h initially.
  • Renal function (creatinine, urine output).
  • Avoid rapid Na changes (> 10/24 h) at correction time.
  • Slow rewarming after sustained hypernatraemia — risk of cerebral oedema with too-rapid lowering.
SAQ 160 — Status Dystonicus — Tiered Management HARD
A 10-year-old with DYT1 generalised dystonia presents with severe sustained dystonia, hyperthermia, rhabdomyolysis, AKI.

DEFINITION

Define status dystonicus.
Show structured answer
Status dystonicus: A pediatric neurocritical emergency defined by progressive worsening of dystonia with one or more of:
  • Bulbar dysfunction with risk of aspiration.
  • Hyperthermia (often > 39 °C).
  • Rhabdomyolysis (CK often > 10 000) with risk of AKI.
  • Respiratory compromise.
  • Autonomic instability.
  • Pain and distress.

Common precipitants: medication change/withdrawal (especially intrathecal baclofen pump failure), infection, dehydration, fatigue.
Genetic dystonia (DYT1/TOR1A, GNAO1) at increased risk.

TREATMENT

Outline the tiered management.
Show structured answer
Tier 1:
  • Optimise oral/IV anti-dystonic medications:
    • Trihexyphenidyl (start 0.05 mg/kg/day, escalate).
    • Baclofen (oral; restore intrathecal delivery if pump failure).
    • Tetrabenazine.
    • Clonidine.
    • Benzodiazepines (clonazepam, diazepam).

Tier 2:
  • PICU admission.
  • Deep sedation — midazolam infusion, propofol (caution prolonged use — PRIS).
  • Intubation for airway protection.
  • Continuous monitoring.

Tier 3:
  • Neuromuscular blockade.
  • Active cooling.
  • Aggressive rhabdomyolysis management: IVF, urinary alkalinisation, monitor K, renal function, CRRT if AKI severe.

Tier 4:
  • Bilateral globus pallidus internus DBS (GPi-DBS) — increasingly emergent in refractory cases.
  • Definitive for many primary dystonias (especially DYT1).

Throughout: hydration, nutrition, electrolytes, treat triggers (infection, source identification).
SAQ 161 — Refeeding Syndrome MODERATE
A 16-year-old with severe restrictive anorexia (BMI 13) is being prepared for inpatient refeeding.

DEFINITION

Define refeeding syndrome and its hallmark electrolyte derangement.
Show structured answer
Refeeding syndrome: Constellation of life-threatening electrolyte, fluid, and metabolic disturbances that occurs when nutrition is reintroduced to severely malnourished patients.

Hallmark electrolyte derangement: HYPOPHOSPHATAEMIA.

Also seen: hypokalaemia, hypomagnesaemia, hypocalcaemia, thiamine deficiency, fluid overload.

Pathophysiology:
  • Glucose load → insulin release.
  • Insulin drives K, PO₄, Mg into cells.
  • Existing depleted body stores → rapid intracellular shift unmasks deficiency.
  • Thiamine is consumed in metabolism — deficiency precipitates Wernicke encephalopathy.
  • Glucose load also causes fluid retention → cardiac overload.

Mortality untreated: up to 80%.

CRITERIA

List the high-risk patients per NICE criteria.
Show structured answer
High risk (any one of):
  • BMI < 16.
  • Unintentional weight loss > 15% in 3–6 months.
  • Little or no oral intake for > 10 days.
  • Low pre-refeeding K, PO₄, Mg.

OR any TWO of:
  • BMI < 18.5.
  • Unintentional weight loss > 10% in 3–6 months.
  • Little or no oral intake > 5 days.
  • History of alcohol abuse, drugs (including insulin, chemotherapy, antacids, diuretics).

TREATMENT

Outline prevention and acute management.
Show structured answer
Prevention:
  • THIAMINE 100 mg IV/IM BEFORE any glucose load and continue daily.
  • Start refeeding SLOWLY: 10–20 kcal/kg/day initially; advance over days based on tolerance.
  • Prophylactic electrolyte repletion: PO₄, K, Mg as needed.
  • Daily monitoring of PO₄, K, Mg, Ca, glucose initially.
  • Cardiac monitoring.
  • Multivitamin supplementation.
  • Fluid restriction (start 20–30 mL/kg/day) — avoid fluid overload.

Acute syndrome:
  • Slow or temporarily stop refeeding.
  • Aggressive PO₄, K, Mg repletion.
  • Thiamine 500 mg IV TID × 3 days then maintenance.
  • Cardiac monitoring (arrhythmia from electrolyte derangement).
  • Treat seizures (correct electrolytes simultaneously).
  • Resume slower refeeding once stabilised.
  • Multidisciplinary: ICU, eating disorders team, dietitian, psychiatry; family involvement.
SAQ 162 — Neuroleptic Malignant Syndrome HARD
A 16-year-old psychiatric inpatient on haloperidol presents with hyperthermia, severe rigidity, autonomic instability, and CK 35 000.

CRITERIA

Outline diagnostic criteria.
Show structured answer
NMS — DSM-5-TR diagnostic features:
Exposure to dopamine antagonist within 72 hours (or rapid withdrawal of dopamine agonist) PLUS:
  • Severe muscle rigidity ('lead pipe').
  • Hyperthermia (> 38 °C).
  • Two or more of: diaphoresis, dysphagia, tremor, incontinence, altered mental status, mutism, tachycardia, BP lability, leukocytosis, elevated CK.
  • Symptoms not attributable to another condition.

Onset typically 4–14 days after initiation or dose increase of neuroleptic.

Hallmark features (clinical tetrad):
  1. Hyperthermia.
  2. Severe muscle rigidity.
  3. Altered mental status.
  4. Autonomic instability.

DEFINITION

Distinguish NMS from serotonin syndrome and malignant hyperthermia.
Show structured answer
FeatureNMSSerotonin syndromeMalignant hyperthermia
TriggerDopamine antagonistSerotonergic drugVolatile anaesthetic or succinylcholine
OnsetDays to 2 weeksHoursMinutes-hours
RigidityLead-pipeLess, more clonusMasseter, then generalised
ReflexesNormal/diminishedHYPERREFLEXIA + clonusRigid
PupilsNormalMYDRIASISNormal
Bowel soundsNormalHYPERACTIVENormal
SkinSweatingSweating + flushedMottled
Specific treatmentStop dopamine antag; dantrolene, bromocriptineStop serotonergic; cyproheptadineStop trigger; dantrolene

TREATMENT

Outline management.
Show structured answer
General:
  • STOP all neuroleptics immediately.
  • ICU admission for continuous monitoring.
  • Aggressive cooling — cold water immersion if T > 40 °C.
  • IV fluids — manage rhabdomyolysis (target urine output 1–3 mL/kg/h, urinary alkalinisation if myoglobinuria/AKI risk).
  • Correct electrolytes (K, Ca).

Specific pharmacology:
  • DANTROLENE 1–2.5 mg/kg IV q6h — direct skeletal muscle ryanodine receptor inhibitor; reduces hyperthermia and rigidity.
  • BROMOCRIPTINE 2.5–5 mg PO q6–8h — dopamine agonist; restores dopaminergic tone.
  • AMANTADINE — alternative dopamine agonist.
  • Benzodiazepines for agitation.

Recovery and prevention:
  • Mortality 5–20%, lower with early recognition.
  • Resume neuroleptics if essential — wait ≥ 2 weeks after recovery, choose atypical (lower D2 affinity).
  • Family / patient education about risk of recurrence.
SAQ 163 — Serotonin Syndrome HARD
A 14-year-old on sertraline started tramadol 24 hours ago. Now: agitation, hyperreflexia, sustained clonus, mydriasis, hyperthermia.

CRITERIA

Outline Hunter's criteria for serotonin syndrome.
Show structured answer
Hunter's serotonin toxicity criteria:
In a patient with serotonergic medication exposure, presence of ONE of:
  • Spontaneous clonus.
  • Inducible clonus + agitation OR diaphoresis.
  • Ocular clonus + agitation OR diaphoresis.
  • Tremor + hyperreflexia.
  • Hypertonia + temperature > 38 °C + ocular or inducible clonus.

HYPERREFLEXIA and CLONUS are the cardinal features distinguishing from NMS.
Lower-limb hyperreflexia and clonus are usually more prominent than upper limb.

DX

Outline the spectrum of severity.
Show structured answer
Mild: tachycardia, shivering, sweating, mydriasis, intermittent tremor or myoclonus, hyperreflexia.
Moderate: hyperthermia (≤ 40 °C), hypertension, mental status changes (anxiety, agitation), increased bowel sounds.
Severe (life-threatening): hyperthermia > 40 °C, severe rigidity, rhabdomyolysis, DIC, metabolic acidosis, multi-organ failure, death.

TREATMENT

Management approach.
Show structured answer
General:
  • STOP all serotonergic drugs.
  • Supportive care: cooling, IV fluids, monitor.
  • Benzodiazepines (lorazepam, diazepam) — first-line for agitation and rigidity.
  • Cardiovascular support as needed.

Specific:
  • CYPROHEPTADINE — 5-HT2 antagonist; oral 4–8 mg q1–4h (max ~ 32 mg/24 h adult; pediatric dosing lower).
  • For severe / refractory: chlorpromazine has some 5-HT2 antagonism (caution with hypotension).

AVOID:
  • SSRIs, SNRIs, TCAs, MAOIs, tramadol, methylene blue (CO MAOI activity), linezolid, ondansetron, metoclopramide.
  • Antipsychotics with significant D2 blockade (dantrolene NOT useful; serotonin syndrome rigidity is not from malignant hyperthermia mechanism).

Recovery:
  • Most cases resolve within 24–72 hours of removing offending drug.
  • Wait 2 weeks (5 weeks for fluoxetine) before re-introducing serotonergic medication.
SAQ 164 — Cervical Spinal Cord Injury — Acute Management HARD
A 15-year-old with complete C6 SCI from diving is admitted to PICU. HR 50, BP 80/50.

DX

Distinguish neurogenic shock from spinal shock.
Show structured answer
Neurogenic shock: Haemodynamic dysfunction from disruption of sympathetic outflow (T1–L2).
  • Hypotension + bradycardia + warm extremities + anhidrosis below lesion.
  • Occurs in cervical and high thoracic injuries.
  • Treat: vasopressors (norepinephrine for both α and β), fluids (cautiously), atropine for severe bradycardia.

Spinal shock: Temporary loss of all motor, sensory, autonomic, and reflex function below the level of injury — appears flaccid even with eventual UMN injury.
  • Lasts hours to weeks.
  • Resolution heralded by return of reflexes (often bulbocavernosus first), then UMN signs emerge.
  • Cannot determine final neurologic outcome until spinal shock resolves.

TREATMENT

Outline acute management priorities including MAP target.
Show structured answer
Acute management priorities:
  • Airway: Cervical SCI at C5 or above → diaphragmatic paralysis (intubation often required); C3–C5 injury → loss of phrenic; consider early intubation with cervical-spine precautions (in-line stabilisation).
  • Breathing: Mechanical ventilation; cough assist; risk of aspiration; atelectasis.
  • Circulation: Treat neurogenic shock with norepinephrine (preferred — covers β for bradycardia and α for vascular tone). Phenylephrine alone NOT ideal (worsens bradycardia).
  • MAP target ≥ 85–90 mm Hg for 7 days to optimise spinal cord perfusion (extrapolated from adult evidence — AANS/CNS SCI guidelines).
  • NMS / corticosteroids: NASCIS trials of methylprednisolone — controversial; current AANS/CNS guidelines do NOT routinely recommend.

Other:
  • Imaging: CT cervical spine + MRI of cord.
  • Spinal stabilisation: cervical collar, traction, surgical fixation as indicated.
  • DVT prophylaxis (high VTE risk).
  • Bladder management (catheter).
  • Bowel programme.
  • Pressure-area care.
  • Early rehabilitation.
  • Family support.
SAQ 165 — Autonomic Dysreflexia HARD
A 14-year-old with complete T4 SCI develops severe pounding headache, BP 220/130, flushing above lesion, sweating above lesion.

DX

Diagnosis and pathophysiology.
Show structured answer
Autonomic dysreflexia: Massive sympathetic reflex response to noxious stimulus below the level of SCI (typically at T6 or above).

Pathophysiology:
  • Noxious stimulus below level of injury (distended bladder is #1 cause; faecal impaction; pressure ulcer; ingrown toenail; menstruation; sexual stimulation).
  • Afferents reach spinal cord but cannot ascend past lesion.
  • Reflex sympathetic outflow below lesion → massive vasoconstriction → hypertension.
  • Baroreflex detects hypertension → vagally mediated bradycardia + vasodilation ABOVE the lesion (flushing, sweating, headache).
  • BUT inhibitory descending pathway cannot pass lesion to vasodilate vessels below → BP remains catastrophically high.

Can cause stroke, MI, retinal haemorrhage, death if untreated.

TREATMENT

Outline immediate management.
Show structured answer
First-line — POSITIONAL + SOURCE REMOVAL:
  1. Sit patient UPRIGHT (Fowler position) — uses orthostatic effect to drop BP.
  2. Loosen tight clothing, remove abdominal binder, leg stockings.
  3. IDENTIFY AND REMOVE INCITING STIMULUS:
    • Bladder distension — straight catheterise or check Foley patency.
    • Faecal impaction — gentle digital evacuation with topical anaesthetic (further stimulation can worsen).
    • Pressure ulcer, ingrown toenail, tight clothing.
    • Pregnant patient: any labour stimulus.

Second-line — PHARMACOLOGIC BP control: only if positional + source measures fail and BP > 150/100:
  • Nifedipine immediate-release (10 mg sublingual or chewed) — rapid acting.
  • Nitrates (nitroglycerin paste, sublingual).
  • Captopril.
  • Hydralazine IV.
  • Avoid Trendelenburg (worsens).

Patient education:
  • Critical for chronic SCI patients to recognise early symptoms and act.
  • Medical alert.
  • Carry emergency information.
SAQ 166 — Botulism — Infant vs Foodborne MODERATE
A 5-month-old infant with constipation, weak cry, ptosis, descending paralysis.

DX

Distinguish infant from foodborne/wound botulism.
Show structured answer
Infant botulism:
  • Children < 12 months.
  • Pathogenesis: ingestion of C. botulinum SPORES (not pre-formed toxin) → colonisation of immature infant gut → in vivo toxin production.
  • Sources: honey (avoid in infants < 12 months), environmental dust, soil; corn syrup historically.
  • Onset gradual over days.
  • Presentation: 'floppy infant' with constipation, weak cry, poor feed, hypotonia, descending paralysis, autonomic features (dilated pupils, dry mouth).

Foodborne botulism:
  • Ingestion of PRE-FORMED toxin in improperly canned/preserved foods.
  • Older children and adults.
  • Rapid onset (12–48 h) of bilateral cranial nerve palsies → descending paralysis.
  • GI symptoms (vomiting, diarrhoea) precede neurologic.

Wound botulism:
  • Spore contamination of wound (heroin users, deep wounds).
  • In vivo toxin production at wound site.
  • Subacute onset.
  • No GI symptoms.

TREATMENT

Distinguish antitoxin choice and supportive care.
Show structured answer
Antitoxin:
  • INFANT botulism (< 1 year): Human botulism immune globulin (BabyBIG) — single IV infusion. Reduces hospital stay by ~ 3 weeks.
  • FOODBORNE / WOUND botulism (older patients): Equine heptavalent botulism antitoxin (HBAT) — IV; risk of serum sickness / anaphylaxis. Not used in infants < 1 year (BabyBIG preferred).

Avoid:
  • AMINOGLYCOSIDES (potentiate neuromuscular blockade).
  • Antibiotics routinely (release of toxin from lysed bacteria — controversial).

Supportive care:
  • Meticulous airway management — early intubation if respiratory weakness.
  • Mechanical ventilation if needed (often prolonged — weeks).
  • NG/PEG feeding.
