  • Prevent complications during paralysis (DVT, pressure ulcers, contractures, infections).
  • Wound debridement for wound botulism.
  • Antibiotics for wound source (penicillin G) — after antitoxin.
  • Public health reporting.
  • Full recovery is the rule with optimal supportive care.
SAQ 167 — GBS — EGRIS Score and Management HARD
A 16-year-old with progressive ascending weakness after Campylobacter infection has FVC 35 mL/kg.

CRITERIA

Outline EGRIS and indications for ICU/intubation in GBS.
Show structured answer
EGRIS (Erasmus GBS Respiratory Insufficiency Score): Predicts mechanical ventilation within first week.

Three components scored 0–4:
  • Days between onset of weakness and admission (≤ 3 d = 2 pts; 4–7 = 1; > 7 = 0).
  • Facial and/or bulbar weakness (yes = 1; no = 0).
  • MRC sum score on admission (≤ 35 = 4 pts; 36–49 = 3; 50–59 = 1; ≥ 60 = 0).

Score 0–7. Higher = greater risk of needing intubation.

Indications for ICU admission:
  • FVC < 20 mL/kg, NIF less negative than –30 cmH₂O, or 30% reduction from baseline.
  • Bulbar dysfunction with aspiration risk.
  • Severe autonomic instability.
  • Inability to lift head off bed.
  • Rapid progression (peak weakness within 7 days).

TREATMENT

Outline acute therapy and what to AVOID.
Show structured answer
Disease-modifying:
  • IVIG 2 g/kg total over 2–5 days OR plasma exchange (5 sessions) — equivalent efficacy.
  • Combining IVIG + PLEX has no additional benefit.
  • STEROIDS ALONE are NOT effective in GBS (unlike CIDP).

Supportive ICU care:
  • Airway protection: early intubation if FVC < 20 mL/kg, NIF less negative than –30, autonomic instability.
  • Autonomic monitoring: cardiac monitoring; treat arrhythmias and BP swings conservatively (sensitive to drugs).
  • Pain management — neuropathic pain very common; gabapentin, pregabalin first-line; opioids if severe.
  • VTE prophylaxis (LMWH).
  • Pressure-area care.
  • NG feeding if dysphagia.
  • Early physiotherapy.
  • Family / patient education about recovery trajectory (months-years).

DEFINITION

List GBS variants.
Show structured answer
  • AIDP — acute inflammatory demyelinating polyneuropathy; most common form in Europe / N America.
  • AMAN — acute motor axonal neuropathy; commonly post-Campylobacter; anti-GM1, anti-GD1a antibodies.
  • AMSAN — acute motor and sensory axonal neuropathy; more severe and prolonged.
  • Miller Fisher syndrome (MFS) — ophthalmoplegia + ataxia + areflexia; anti-GQ1b antibody.
  • Bickerstaff brainstem encephalitis (BBE) — like MFS + encephalopathy + hyperreflexia (instead of areflexia).
  • Pharyngeal-cervical-brachial variant — upper-body predominant weakness.
  • Acute autonomic neuropathy — pure autonomic involvement.
SAQ 168 — Myasthenia Gravis — AChR vs MuSK MODERATE
A 14-year-old presents with bulbar-predominant fatigable weakness. AChR antibody negative.

DX

Outline antibody subtypes and clinical correlates.
Show structured answer
Antibody subtypes:
  • Anti-AChR — most common (~ 80–90% of generalised MG); ocular MG ~ 50%.
  • Anti-MuSK (muscle-specific tyrosine kinase) — 5–10% of MG; often AChR-negative; predominantly female, often with bulbar/respiratory predominance; more severe disease; thymectomy LESS effective; rituximab particularly effective.
  • Anti-LRP4 (low-density lipoprotein-related protein 4) — newer; small subset; agrin-LRP4-MuSK pathway.
  • Seronegative MG — 5–10%; diagnose clinically + electrophysiologically (decremental response on RNS).

TREATMENT

Stepwise treatment principles.
Show structured answer
  1. Symptomatic: Pyridostigmine — acetylcholinesterase inhibitor; doses up to 60 mg q3h. Less effective in MuSK MG.
  2. Immunosuppression: Prednisone with calcium + vitamin D (watch for transient worsening on starting). Steroid-sparing — azathioprine, mycophenolate, methotrexate. Rituximab particularly effective in MuSK MG.
  3. Acute exacerbation / crisis: IVIG or plasma exchange — equivalent.
  4. Thymectomy: Indicated for AChR-positive generalised MG, especially in younger patients (MGTX trial showed benefit); not standardly recommended for MuSK MG; controversial for ocular MG.
  5. Newer agents: Eculizumab (complement inhibitor), efgartigimod (FcRn inhibitor), rozanolixizumab — for refractory disease.

AVOID in MG: aminoglycosides, fluoroquinolones, telithromycin, β-blockers, magnesium, neuromuscular blockers (increased sensitivity).

DEFINITION

Distinguish myasthenic crisis from cholinergic crisis.
Show structured answer
Myasthenic crisis: Severe weakness + respiratory failure + bulbar dysfunction — caused by undertreatment or worsening disease.

Cholinergic crisis: Weakness AND excessive cholinergic activity (miosis, bradycardia, hypersalivation, lacrimation, bronchorrhoea, diarrhoea, fasciculations) — caused by excessive anticholinesterase therapy.

Historic Tensilon (edrophonium) test — improvement = myasthenic; worsening = cholinergic. Rarely used today.

Modern approach: hold pyridostigmine, treat with IVIG/PLEX + steroids + supportive ventilation if in crisis.
SAQ 169 — Wernicke Encephalopathy MODERATE
A 16-year-old with severe restrictive anorexia develops confusion, ophthalmoplegia, and ataxia during refeeding.

DX

Outline diagnostic features and the classic triad.
Show structured answer
Classic Wernicke triad:
  1. Encephalopathy (confusion, disorientation, inattention).
  2. Ophthalmoplegia (CN VI palsy first, then horizontal nystagmus, conjugate gaze palsy).
  3. Ataxia (wide-based gait, truncal ataxia).

Only ~ 10–30% of cases have all three features at presentation. Many missed because of partial syndromes.

Risk factors: alcohol misuse, severe vomiting (hyperemesis gravidarum, post-bariatric surgery, eating disorders), prolonged TPN without thiamine, prolonged starvation.

MRI (helpful but not always abnormal): symmetric T2/FLAIR hyperintensity in mammillary bodies, dorsomedial thalami, periaqueductal grey, hypothalamus.

PHYSIOLOGY

Explain the metabolic basis.
Show structured answer
  • Thiamine (B1) is a cofactor for transketolase (pentose phosphate pathway), pyruvate dehydrogenase (entry to Krebs cycle), and α-ketoglutarate dehydrogenase (Krebs cycle).
  • Thiamine deficiency → impaired carbohydrate metabolism + lactate accumulation + impaired neurotransmitter synthesis.
  • Selective vulnerability of mammillary bodies, thalamus, periaqueductal grey — high metabolic demand.
  • Glucose load in thiamine-deficient state consumes remaining thiamine → can precipitate Wernicke (give thiamine BEFORE glucose).

TREATMENT

Outline treatment.
Show structured answer
IV THIAMINE:
  • Acute Wernicke: 500 mg IV TID × 3 days.
  • Then 250 mg IV/IM daily × 5 days.
  • Then oral maintenance 100 mg daily.

Some advocate even higher doses in acute disease.

Critical principle: Always give thiamine BEFORE any glucose load in at-risk patients (alcohol, hyperemesis, anorexia, TPN, prolonged fasting).

Long-term sequelae: Korsakoff syndrome — anterograde amnesia, confabulation. Can be permanent if untreated.

Prevention:
  • Routine thiamine in TPN, severe vomiting, anorexia inpatient care.
  • Maintain high index of suspicion.
  • Multidisciplinary management.
SAQ 170 — Osmotic Demyelination Syndrome HARD
A 9-year-old with severe hyponatraemia from polydipsia was over-corrected (Na rose by 25 mmol/L over 24 h). Day 5 he develops progressive quadriparesis, dysphagia, dysarthria.

DX

Diagnosis and the typical MRI pattern.
Show structured answer
Osmotic demyelination syndrome (ODS) — formerly central pontine myelinolysis (CPM) when restricted to pons; extrapontine when other regions.
  • Caused by overcorrection of chronic hyponatraemia.
  • Onset 2–6 days after rapid Na rise.
  • Symptoms: progressive quadriparesis, dysphagia, dysarthria, 'locked-in syndrome' in severe cases, mutism, behavioural change.
  • MRI (often days behind clinical): symmetric T2 hyperintensity in central pons (trident shape), and/or extrapontine regions (basal ganglia, thalamus, cerebellum).
  • Often irreversible; partial recovery possible.

PHYSIOLOGY

Explain pathophysiology.
Show structured answer
  • In chronic hyponatraemia (> 48 h), brain cells extrude organic osmolytes (myo-inositol, taurine, glutamate, creatine) to reduce cell volume.
  • Rapid increase in serum Na → extracellular osmolality rises faster than brain cells can re-accumulate osmolytes.
  • Cells shrink → oligodendrocyte injury and apoptosis → demyelination.
  • Centrally located pons + cerebellum + basal ganglia are particularly vulnerable.
  • Hence the importance of SLOW correction (< 8–10 mmol/L per 24 h) when hyponatraemia has been present > 24–48 h.

TREATMENT

Management.
Show structured answer
Acute (within 48 h of overcorrection):
  • Re-lower Na gradually (5–10 mmol/L drop over 24 h) using DDAVP and free water — controversial efficacy.
  • If beyond 48 h: damage usually established; conservative management.

Supportive:
  • ICU care for severe cases (airway, nutrition, mobility).
  • Physiotherapy / rehabilitation.
  • Slow recovery over months; some partial recovery; some permanent.

PREVENTION is the key:
  • Limit Na correction to < 8 mmol/L in 24 h, < 18 in 48 h.
  • Higher-risk patients (alcoholics, malnourished, hypokalaemic, liver disease): even stricter limit < 6 mmol/L per 24 h.
  • Frequent monitoring during correction.
  • For ADH suppression (free water diuresis): use DDAVP prophylactically if rapid water diuresis occurs.
SAQ 171 — Vasospasm After Aneurysmal SAH HARD
A 15-year-old with ruptured A-com aneurysm, clipped on day 1. Day 7: new right-sided weakness and confusion.

DX

Diagnosis and the typical time course.
Show structured answer
Cerebral vasospasm with delayed cerebral ischaemia (DCI):
  • Peak incidence days 4–14 after SAH; rare before day 3 and after day 21.
  • Up to 40% of SAH patients develop angiographic vasospasm; 20–30% develop symptomatic DCI.
  • Mechanism: blood breakdown products (oxyhaemoglobin, free haem) trigger arterial spasm and inflammation in proximal cerebral arteries.
  • Manifestation: new focal neurologic deficit OR decreased level of consciousness OR new infarct on imaging — that cannot be explained by other cause.

Diagnostic confirmation:
  • Transcranial Doppler (TCD) — elevated MCA mean velocity (> 120 cm/s mild; > 200 severe); Lindegaard ratio (MCA/ICA) > 3 distinguishes vasospasm from hyperaemia.
  • CT/MR perfusion — hypoperfusion in affected territory.
  • Catheter angiography — gold standard.

TREATMENT

Management of DCI.
Show structured answer
Prevention:
  • Nimodipine 60 mg PO q4h × 21 days for all SAH patients (adult evidence; pediatric extrapolation; reduces poor outcomes via neuroprotection rather than vasospasm reversal).
  • Maintain euvolaemia (avoid both hypo- and hypervolaemia; old 'triple-H' protocol abandoned).
  • Aggressive secure airway, optimise oxygenation.

Acute DCI:
  • INDUCED HYPERTENSION (formerly part of triple-H): increase MAP toward symptom resolution (typically MAP 20–25% above baseline). Norepinephrine usually first-line.
  • Maintain euvolaemia.
  • Optimise oxygenation, normocapnia, glucose, Hb.

Refractory to medical:
  • ENDOVASCULAR rescue:
    • Intra-arterial vasodilators (verapamil, milrinone, nicardipine).
    • Balloon angioplasty for proximal large-vessel spasm.

Monitoring:
  • Daily TCDs through day 14 post-SAH.
  • Continuous neurologic monitoring.
  • Watch for delayed hydrocephalus.
SAQ 172 — Neurocysticercosis MODERATE
A 12-year-old immigrant from Latin America presents with new-onset focal seizures. CT: multiple ring-enhancing lesions, some with central calcification.

DX

Outline the clinical-radiologic stages and the diagnostic criteria.
Show structured answer
Stages of NCC parenchymal cysts:
  1. Vesicular: viable cyst, thin wall, no enhancement, scolex visible (eccentric dot).
  2. Colloidal: dying parasite — wall enhancement appears, surrounding oedema.
  3. Granular-nodular: retracted lesion with peripheral enhancement.
  4. Calcified: end-stage, fully calcified non-enhancing nodule.

Del Brutto criteria use a combination of absolute, major, minor, and epidemiologic criteria for diagnosis.

TREATMENT

Outline treatment principles including when NOT to give cysticidal therapy.
Show structured answer
Treat depending on stage and location:
  • Calcified inactive lesions only: NO antiparasitic — already dead; treat seizures with AEDs.
  • Viable parenchymal cysts: albendazole 15 mg/kg/day (± praziquantel for multiple cysts) WITH corticosteroids (dexamethasone) — steroids reduce oedema reaction.
  • Single enhancing lesion: AED + steroids sometimes; albendazole shortens course.
  • Intraventricular cysts: often surgical / endoscopic removal — risk of hydrocephalus from acute death-related inflammation if treated medically without preparation.
  • Subarachnoid/racemose: prolonged therapy, often with steroids.
  • Cysticercotic encephalitis (massive cyst burden with diffuse oedema in young women): AVOID cysticidal therapy — would worsen cerebral oedema catastrophically. Treat with high-dose steroids first.

AEDs as needed; treat hydrocephalus if present (shunting).
SAQ 173 — ADEM — IPMSSG Criteria MODERATE
A 7-year-old child develops encephalopathy and multifocal neurologic deficits 2 weeks after a viral illness.

CRITERIA

Outline the 2013 IPMSSG criteria for monophasic pediatric ADEM.
Show structured answer
International Pediatric MS Study Group 2013 criteria — ADEM requires ALL of:
  1. First polyfocal clinical CNS event with presumed inflammatory demyelinating cause.
  2. Encephalopathy not explained by fever, systemic illness, or postictal state — REQUIRED for ADEM (distinguishes from CIS / first MS attack).
  3. No new clinical/MRI findings emerging 3 months or more after onset.
  4. Brain MRI abnormalities consistent with demyelination during the acute (3-month) phase.

MRI features supporting ADEM:
  • Multiple large (> 1–2 cm), poorly demarcated lesions involving cerebral white matter, deep grey matter, brainstem, cerebellum.
  • T1 hypointense ('black holes') uncommon.
  • Spinal cord lesions in 30%, often longitudinally extensive.

TREATMENT

Outline treatment.
Show structured answer
  • First-line: high-dose IV methylprednisolone 30 mg/kg/day × 3–5 days then oral taper over 4–6 weeks (abrupt taper risks relapse).
  • Refractory: IVIG 2 g/kg.
  • Severe / steroid-resistant: plasma exchange.
  • Cyclophosphamide for fulminant cases.
  • Symptomatic support: AEDs, ICP management (rare; severe ADEM can have raised ICP), rehab.
  • Long-term: monitor for relapse — multiphasic ADEM, ADEM-ON (followed by optic neuritis), evolution to MS, NMOSD, or MOGAD.
  • Check MOG-IgG and AQP4-IgG at baseline.
  • Most monophasic ADEM children have good recovery.
SAQ 174 — Pediatric Stroke Arteriopathy Classification HARD
An 8-year-old previously healthy boy has a unilateral acute MCA infarct with MRA showing focal narrowing.

CRITERIA

Outline the major arteriopathies in pediatric AIS.
Show structured answer
Pediatric AIS arteriopathy classification:
  • Focal cerebral arteriopathy of childhood (FCA, inflammatory subtype): unilateral, monophasic, distal ICA/proximal M1/A1; often post-VZV; non-progressive (stabilises within 6 months).
  • FCA dissection subtype: traumatic or spontaneous dissection.
  • Moyamoya disease/syndrome: progressive bilateral distal ICA/proximal MCA stenosis with collateral 'puff of smoke'.
  • Primary CNS vasculitis (PACNS): small/medium vessel, multifocal, progressive; raised ESR/CRP; CSF inflammation.
  • Sickle cell vasculopathy: bilateral, intracranial stenoses; abnormal TCD predicts stroke risk.
  • Vasculopathy of systemic disease: Takayasu, fibromuscular dysplasia, Ehlers-Danlos.
  • Cardioembolic without intrinsic arteriopathy: CHD, endocarditis, atrial myxoma, prosthetic valve.

TREATMENT

Outline secondary prevention by aetiology.
Show structured answer
FCA: Aspirin 1–5 mg/kg/day × 2 years; adjunct corticosteroids in inflammatory subtype (per VIPS data); acyclovir if active VZV.

Moyamoya: Aspirin + surgical revascularisation (EDAS, STA-MCA bypass); maintain hydration, normotension, normothermia, normocapnia.

PACNS: Induction with high-dose steroids + cyclophosphamide or rituximab; maintenance immunosuppression 18–24 months.

SCD: Exchange transfusion to HbS < 30%; chronic transfusion long-term; consider BMT.

Cardioembolic: Treat source (anticoagulation per indication); aspirin or warfarin depending on anatomy.

Dissection: Antiplatelet or anticoagulation 3–6 months; avoid neck manipulation.

General: ICU monitoring acutely, normothermia, normocapnia, normoglycaemia, BP within autoregulatory range.
SAQ 175 — Moyamoya — Surgical Revascularisation MODERATE
A 9-year-old with bilateral moyamoya has recurrent TIAs despite medical management.

CRITERIA

Outline Suzuki staging.
Show structured answer
Suzuki angiographic staging (1–6):
  • Stage 1: ICA bifurcation narrowing only.
  • Stage 2: Initial appearance of moyamoya collaterals.
  • Stage 3: Intensified moyamoya collaterals; major ACA and MCA branches start to disappear.
  • Stage 4: Minimised moyamoya collaterals; major branches almost gone; ophthalmic artery collaterals appear.
  • Stage 5: Reduced moyamoya collaterals; only transdural collaterals remain.
  • Stage 6: Disappearance of moyamoya collaterals; only external carotid collaterals supply brain.

TREATMENT

Outline indirect vs direct revascularisation options.
Show structured answer
Direct bypass:
  • STA-MCA (superficial temporal artery to middle cerebral artery) anastomosis.
  • Immediate increase in perfusion.
  • Technical challenges in young children with small vessels.

Indirect revascularisation (promotes pial collateral formation):
  • EDAS — encephalo-duro-arterio-synangiosis: STA dissected free and laid on brain surface.
  • EDAMS — adds muscle (temporalis).
  • EMS — encephalo-myo-synangiosis.
  • Burr hole synangiosis.
  • Pial synangiosis.
  • Multiple burr-holes.

Combined direct + indirect: increasingly used in older children/adults.

Perioperative care:
  • Continue aspirin perioperatively.
  • Maintain HYDRATION, NORMOTENSION, NORMOTHERMIA, NORMOCAPNIA — extremes of any precipitate stroke.
  • Optimise Hb (treat anaemia).
  • Avoid hyperventilation (vasoconstriction).
  • Smooth induction; avoid crying/pain (induces hyperventilation).
SAQ 176 — Catastrophic Antiphospholipid Syndrome HARD
A 14-year-old develops multifocal stroke, renal failure, and skin lesions over 2 weeks. Antiphospholipid antibodies positive.

DEFINITION

Outline diagnostic criteria for catastrophic APS.
Show structured answer
Catastrophic APS (CAPS, Asherson syndrome) — diagnostic criteria:
  1. Evidence of involvement of THREE or more organs/systems/tissues.
  2. Development of manifestations simultaneously or in less than one week.
  3. Confirmation by histopathology of small vessel occlusion in at least one organ/tissue.
  4. Laboratory confirmation of presence of antiphospholipid antibodies (lupus anticoagulant, anti-cardiolipin, anti-β2-glycoprotein I).

Definite CAPS = all 4.
Probable CAPS = 3 criteria with related features.

Mortality ~ 50%.

TREATMENT

Outline 'triple therapy' approach.
Show structured answer
CAPS triple therapy:
  1. ANTICOAGULATION (heparin → warfarin/DOAC).
  2. HIGH-DOSE CORTICOSTEROIDS (IV methylprednisolone pulses then oral).
  3. PLASMA EXCHANGE and/or IVIG.

Refractory:
  • Rituximab.
  • Eculizumab — emerging evidence for complement-mediated cases.
  • Cyclophosphamide for systemic vasculitis component.

Supportive ICU care for multi-organ failure, with attention to:
  • Cerebral perfusion.
  • Renal replacement if AKI.
  • Pulmonary management (DAH possible).
  • Cardiac (microthrombosis can cause MI).

Long-term: lifelong anticoagulation, immunosuppression maintenance, monitor for recurrence.
SAQ 177 — Hyperammonaemia — Differential and CRRT Indications HARD
An infant has ammonia 950 µmol/L on day 5 of life.

DX

Outline the differential of neonatal hyperammonaemia.
Show structured answer
Neonatal hyperammonaemia differential:
  • Urea cycle disorders (no acidosis; ± respiratory alkalosis): CPS1, NAGS, OTC (X-linked), ASS1 (citrullinaemia), ASL, arginase.
  • Organic acidemias (severe acidosis): propionic, methylmalonic, isovaleric. Glycine elevated in plasma ('ketotic hyperglycinaemia').
  • Fatty acid oxidation disorders (variable acidosis, hypoglycaemia): MCAD, LCHAD, VLCAD, CPT II.
  • Transient hyperammonaemia of newborn (THAN): often preterm infants; resolves spontaneously; cause unclear.
  • Liver failure: severe hepatic dysfunction with elevated transaminases.
  • Drugs: valproate (especially with carnitine deficiency), urinary diversion.
  • HHH syndrome (hyperammonaemia, hyperornithinaemia, homocitrullinuria).

TREATMENT

Outline acute management and CRRT indications.
Show structured answer
Acute hyperammonaemia management:
  1. STOP protein.
  2. Reverse catabolism: D10 + insulin if needed; intralipid for calories.
  3. Nitrogen scavengers IV: sodium phenylacetate + benzoate (Ammonul) load + maintenance.
  4. Arginine HCl (essential for UCDs except arginase).
  5. Treat cerebral oedema: HOB up, careful fluid management.

Indications for hemodialysis / CRRT:
  • Ammonia > 250 µmol/L OR rising despite scavengers.
  • Coma.
  • Catastrophic metabolic decompensation.

HD vs CRRT:
  • Intermittent hemodialysis: fastest clearance — preferred when available.
  • CRRT: slower clearance but better tolerated haemodynamically.
  • Some centres use hybrid (slow extended dialysis).

After acute crisis:
  • Long-term low-protein diet, scavengers (phenylbutyrate or glycerol phenylbutyrate), arginine.
  • Liver transplant for severe UCDs.
  • Genetic counselling, sibling screening.
  • Family education, emergency action plan.
SAQ 178 — Subdural Empyema HARD
A 14-year-old with severe sinusitis develops fever, headache, and meningismus. MRI: crescentic fluid collection along frontal convexity with peripheral enhancement and adjacent brain oedema.

DX

Outline diagnosis and mortality.
Show structured answer
Subdural empyema: Pus in subdural space — true neurosurgical emergency.

Sources:
  • Sinusitis (frontal sinus) — most common in adolescents (frontal subdural empyema, 'Pott's puffy tumour' when associated with frontal bone osteomyelitis).
  • Otitis/mastoiditis — temporal location.
  • Trauma/post-neurosurgery.
  • Bacteraemia (rare).

Organisms: Streptococcus (especially S. anginosus group/milleri), anaerobes (Bacteroides, Fusobacterium), Staph aureus, Gram-negatives in post-neurosurgical.

Mortality 5–20%; morbidity (seizures, deficits) much higher; outcome highly time-dependent.

TREATMENT

Outline management.
Show structured answer
EMERGENT NEUROSURGICAL DRAINAGE:
  • Craniotomy with evacuation preferred over burr-hole drainage (better source control, especially for thick pus, septations).
  • May need repeat procedures.

EMPIRIC ANTIBIOTICS (broad-spectrum until cultures):
  • Vancomycin + ceftriaxone + metronidazole.
  • For post-neurosurgical: add anti-pseudomonal coverage (cefepime or meropenem).
  • Duration: 4–6 weeks IV, can transition to oral if responding well.

Source control:
  • ENT consultation for sinus drainage in sinogenic source.
  • Mastoidectomy for otogenic source.

Supportive:
  • AEDs (high seizure rate).
  • Treat raised ICP.
  • Imaging follow-up (MRI at 2 weeks, then monthly).
  • Multidisciplinary: neurosurgery, ENT, ID.
SAQ 179 — Cavernous Sinus Thrombosis HARD
A 12-year-old with severe facial cellulitis develops bilateral periorbital oedema, chemosis, proptosis, and CN III/IV/VI palsies.

DX

Diagnosis and the key clinical features.
Show structured answer
Septic cavernous sinus thrombosis:
  • Often secondary to spreading infection from face (especially central face, 'danger triangle'), sinusitis (sphenoid, ethmoid), dental infection, otitis media.
  • Pathogens: S. aureus (most common, including MRSA), streptococci, anaerobes, Gram-negatives.
  • Features:
    • Bilateral (or sequential) eye involvement — periorbital oedema, chemosis, proptosis.
    • CRANIAL NERVES affected by location in cavernous sinus: III, IV, V1, V2, VI; CN VI most vulnerable (medial in sinus).
    • Fever, severe headache.
    • Sluggish/dilated pupil if ciliary nerves affected.
    • May spread to other sinuses, develop septic emboli to brain, meningitis.

TREATMENT

Outline management.
Show structured answer
Diagnostic confirmation:
  • MRI with MRV — gold standard.
  • CT venography alternative.
  • Blood cultures, source workup (sinus imaging, dental review).

Antibiotics:
  • Vancomycin (MRSA coverage) + ceftriaxone or cefepime + metronidazole.
  • Duration: 3–4 weeks (some recommend 6).
  • Narrow with culture results.

Anticoagulation:
  • Controversial but increasingly recommended for septic CST.
  • LMWH or UFH — start when infection controlled and risk of bleeding low.
  • Continue 3–6 months.

Source control:
  • Surgical drainage of facial/sinus/dental abscess.
  • ENT, oral surgery as appropriate.

Supportive:
  • Treat raised ICP if present.
  • Manage cranial nerve sequelae.
  • ICU monitoring.

Mortality 20–30%; persistent CN deficits common.
SAQ 180 — Hyperinsulinism — Critical Sample MODERATE
A 6-month-old has persistent hypoglycaemia requiring high glucose infusion rate.

DX

Outline the diagnostic 'critical sample' approach.
Show structured answer
Critical sample (drawn AT THE TIME OF HYPOGLYCAEMIA < 2.8 mmol/L):
  • Glucose, insulin, C-peptide.
  • β-hydroxybutyrate (ketones).
  • Free fatty acids.
  • Growth hormone, cortisol.
  • Acylcarnitine profile.
  • Ammonia.
  • Lactate.
  • Urine: ketones, organic acids.

Pattern in hyperinsulinism:
  • HYPOKETOTIC hypoglycaemia (insulin suppresses ketogenesis).
  • Suppressed FFA (insulin suppresses lipolysis).
  • Detectable insulin (or C-peptide) DURING hypoglycaemia (normally suppressed when glucose < 2.8).
  • High glucose infusion rate to maintain euglycaemia (often > 10 mg/kg/min).
  • Glucagon stimulation test: glycaemic response (insulin has prevented appropriate glycogen breakdown).

TREATMENT

Outline management.
Show structured answer
Acute:
  • Maintain euglycaemia > 3.5 mmol/L with high glucose infusion (10–25 mg/kg/min often needed; central line).
  • Avoid hypoglycaemic episodes — risk of permanent neurologic injury (parieto-occipital).

Pharmacology:
  • Diazoxide 5–15 mg/kg/day divided BID/TID — opens KATP channels in β-cells, inhibiting insulin secretion. First-line if KATP channel intact. Side effect: fluid retention (often need diuretic), hypertrichosis.
  • Octreotide — somatostatin analogue. For diazoxide-unresponsive cases. Watch for NEC in neonates.
  • Glucagon infusion as bridge.
  • Nifedipine — calcium channel blocker; small evidence.
  • Sirolimus — emerging for refractory.

Surgical:
  • For diazoxide-unresponsive disease, often KATP channel mutations (ABCC8, KCNJ11).
  • F-DOPA PET to distinguish FOCAL vs DIFFUSE forms.
  • Focal — partial pancreatectomy curative.
  • Diffuse — near-total pancreatectomy (95–98%) — risk of subsequent diabetes.

Multidisciplinary: endocrine, surgery, neurology, genetics.
SAQ 181 — Cerebral Salt Wasting vs SIADH MODERATE
A child post-suprasellar tumor resection has serum Na 124, urine Na 90, urine osm 600.

DEFINITION

Distinguish SIADH from CSW.
Show structured answer
SIADHCSW
Volume statusEuvolaemic / mildly hypervolaemicHYPOVOLAEMIC
WeightIncreased / stableDecreased
Urine outputLow / normalHIGH
Serum NaLowLow
Urine NaHigh (> 30)VERY HIGH (often > 100)
Urine osmInappropriately concentratedConcentrated
BUN/CreatinineNormal/lowElevated (volume depleted)
Uric acidLowLow (similar in both)
TreatmentFluid restrictionVolume replacement + sodium

Volume status is the key clinical discriminator — examine the patient carefully.

TREATMENT

Outline management for each.
Show structured answer
SIADH:
  • Fluid restriction (50–75% maintenance).
  • Monitor electrolytes q4–6h.
  • For symptomatic severe hyponatraemia: 3% saline 3–5 mL/kg bolus.
  • AVP antagonists (tolvaptan, conivaptan) — selected refractory cases; caution overcorrection.
  • Treat underlying cause (cancer, infection, drugs).

CSW:
  • Volume REPLACEMENT with isotonic fluids and sodium.
  • Fludrocortisone 0.05–0.2 mg/day to enhance sodium retention.
  • Continue until natriuresis resolves.
  • Distinguish carefully — fluid restriction in CSW worsens hypovolaemia and cerebral perfusion.

Critical:
  • SLOW correction in both — < 8–10 mmol/L per 24 h to avoid osmotic demyelination.
  • Check sodium frequently (q4h initially) and adjust rate.
  • If overcorrection: re-lower Na with DDAVP + free water.
SAQ 182 — Central Diabetes Insipidus After Pituitary Surgery MODERATE
A 14-year-old post-pituitary mass resection develops polyuria of 10 mL/kg/h with serum Na rising to 158.

DX

Diagnosis and the triphasic pattern.
Show structured answer
Central diabetes insipidus (DI): Inadequate AVP/ADH release from posterior pituitary.

Diagnostic criteria:
  • Polyuria (> 4 mL/kg/h or > 50 mL/kg/24 h).
  • Dilute urine (urine SG < 1.005, urine osm < 300).
  • Hypernatraemia OR rising Na with inappropriately dilute urine.
  • Response to desmopressin (DDAVP) — diagnostic and therapeutic.

Triphasic post-pituitary surgery pattern (~ 30% of cases):
  1. Phase 1 — DI: days 1–4. Loss of AVP from damaged posterior pituitary.
  2. Phase 2 — SIADH: days 4–10. Necrotic neurons release stored AVP.
  3. Phase 3 — Permanent DI: from day 10 onwards if cell death is permanent.

Monitor closely — risk of overcorrection in either direction.

TREATMENT

Outline management.
Show structured answer
DDAVP (desmopressin):
  • IV/IM: 0.5–1 µg test dose; effect within 1–2 hours.
  • Intranasal: 1–2 µg (less reliable in critically ill).
  • Oral: less commonly used acutely.

Fluid management:
  • Match urine output with hypotonic fluids if pre-DDAVP and patient hypernatraemic.
  • Replace free water deficit cautiously (over 48 h) — avoid rapid Na correction (< 0.5 mmol/L per hour) — cerebral oedema risk.
  • Once on DDAVP: maintenance fluids only; avoid free water overload (with DDAVP active, body retains water).

Monitor:
  • Hourly intake/output and urine SG.
  • Q4h serum Na initially.
  • Strict fluid balance.

Concurrent screen:
  • Anterior pituitary function (cortisol, T4, GH) — empirical stress-dose hydrocortisone often given peri-operatively until cortisol axis assessed.
  • Hypothyroidism replacement.

Long-term:
  • If permanent DI: oral or intranasal DDAVP twice daily.
  • Patient/family education: regular dosing, recognise oliguria/hyponatraemia (overdosing).
  • Medical alert.
SAQ 183 — Acute Necrotising Encephalopathy (ANE) HARD
A toddler with influenza A develops rapid encephalopathy. MRI: symmetric bilateral thalamic + brainstem T2 hyperintensities with haemorrhage/necrosis.

DX

Outline diagnosis and pathophysiology.
Show structured answer
Acute necrotising encephalopathy of childhood (ANE):
  • Rare, devastating para- or post-infectious encephalopathy.
  • Triggers: influenza A/B (most common), HHV-6, mycoplasma, COVID-19, parainfluenza.
  • Pathophysiology: cytokine storm (very high IL-6, TNF, IL-10) → BBB disruption → multifocal symmetric necrosis.
  • Imaging hallmark: bilateral SYMMETRIC thalamic lesions (always); brainstem tegmentum, putamen, cerebral and cerebellar white matter, often haemorrhagic/necrotic.
  • Lab: marked hyperferritinaemia (often > 5000), elevated cytokines.
  • Familial/recurrent forms: RANBP2 mutations (autosomal dominant) — at risk of repeated episodes triggered by febrile illness.

Mortality ~ 30%; severe disability common in survivors.

TREATMENT

Outline multimodal management.
Show structured answer
Time-critical management:
  • ICU support: airway, ventilation, ICP management.
  • High-dose IV methylprednisolone early (within 24 h ideally).
  • IVIG 2 g/kg.
  • Plasma exchange for severe cases.
  • Tocilizumab (anti-IL-6) — increasingly used in severe / refractory.
  • Antivirals: oseltamivir for influenza (within 48 h); empirical until viral cause identified.
  • Aggressive antipyresis — fever worsens cytokine cascade.
  • AEDs for seizures.

Avoid:
  • Aspirin (Reye-like concerns in viral illness).
  • Delays in immunotherapy.

Long-term:
  • Genetic testing (RANBP2) if recurrent/familial.
  • Family counselling about recurrence risk.
  • Annual flu vaccination for at-risk families.
  • Aggressive fever management with future infections.
  • Anticipatory immunotherapy at next febrile illness in known RANBP2 carriers (controversial).
SAQ 184 — Sturge-Weber Syndrome MODERATE
A 3-year-old with right-sided port-wine stain (V1 + V2) presents with status epilepticus.

DX

Outline pathophysiology and risk stratification by port-wine distribution.
Show structured answer
Sturge-Weber syndrome (encephalotrigeminal angiomatosis):
  • Caused by somatic mosaic GNAQ R183Q mutations (postzygotic; explains segmental distribution).
  • Triad: facial port-wine stain (V1 critical), leptomeningeal angiomatosis (often unilateral, posterior parietal-occipital), glaucoma.
  • Risk stratification by port-wine stain:
    • V1 involvement (forehead, eyelid): HIGH RISK of neurologic + ocular complications.
    • V2/V3 only: low neurologic risk.
    • Bilateral V1: very high risk.
  • Pathophysiology: leptomeningeal angiomatosis → chronic venous stasis → cortical hypoperfusion → progressive cortical atrophy with characteristic gyriform 'tram-track' calcifications and gliosis on CT.

TREATMENT

Outline management.
Show structured answer
Acute SE:
  • Standard pediatric SE algorithm.
  • Aggressive antipyretic management — fever worsens SWS strokes.
  • Maintain hydration and BP — dehydration and hypotension precipitate strokes.

Chronic seizure management:
  • AEDs — often refractory.
  • LOW-DOSE ASPIRIN 3–5 mg/kg/day chronically — reduces stroke-like episodes by mitigating venous stasis thrombosis (off-label but standard of care).

Surgical:
  • Hemispherectomy (functional or anatomical) for medically refractory focal epilepsy with established hemiparesis — can be transformative.
  • Earlier surgery may improve cognitive trajectory.

Ophthalmology:
  • Lifelong glaucoma surveillance (50% in V1 patients).
  • Early infant ophthalmology referral.

Cosmetic:
  • Pulsed dye laser for port-wine stain — best results before age 1.

Multidisciplinary: neurology, ophthalmology, dermatology, neuropsychology, family support.
SAQ 185 — Vein of Galen Malformation HARD
A neonate presents with high-output cardiac failure and a loud continuous cranial bruit.

DX

Outline pathology and presentation by age.
Show structured answer
Vein of Galen aneurysmal malformation (VGAM):
  • Embryologic vascular malformation: persistence of median prosencephalic vein of Markowski with arteriovenous shunting from choroidal and posterior cerebral arteries.
  • Two types: choroidal (multiple small feeders to vein wall — most common in neonates), mural (single large fistula — often diagnosed later).
  • Presentation by age:
    • NEONATAL: high-output cardiac failure (from massive AV shunt), pulmonary hypertension, multi-organ dysfunction; rare cerebral symptoms.
    • INFANCY: macrocephaly + hydrocephalus + developmental delay + heart failure progressively.
    • OLDER CHILD: headache + seizures + cognitive issues; rarely haemorrhage.

TREATMENT

Outline approach by Bicêtre score.
Show structured answer
Bicêtre neonatal evaluation score (Lasjaunias):
Scores clinical functions: cardiac, cerebral, hepatic, renal, respiratory (each 1–5, total 21).
Higher score = better function.
  • Score > 12: medical management; defer intervention.
  • Score 8–12: emergency embolisation indicated.
  • Score < 8: poor candidate; comfort care discussion.

Acute (neonatal):
  • Aggressive cardiac failure management: diuretics, afterload reduction, milrinone.
  • Mechanical ventilation if needed.
  • Manage pulmonary hypertension.

Definitive — staged endovascular embolisation:
  • Transarterial or transvenous approach.
  • Goal: gradual flow reduction over multiple sessions.
  • Ideally deferred to age 4–6 months when feasible (better outcomes).
  • Emergent intervention in neonates with refractory cardiac failure despite medical management.

Surgical resection rarely possible — endovascular only.

Long-term:
  • Monitor for hydrocephalus from venous hypertension or compression.
  • Neurodevelopmental follow-up.
  • Repeat imaging.
SAQ 186 — Hypoxic-Ischaemic Encephalopathy — MRI Patterns MODERATE
A term newborn with severe HIE has MRI on day 4 of life.

DX

Outline the typical patterns of injury by severity.
Show structured answer
HIE MRI patterns:
  • Basal ganglia / thalamic (central) pattern: involves bilateral posterolateral putamen, ventrolateral thalamus, hippocampus, corticospinal tract → seen with SEVERE acute near-total asphyxia. Worst prognosis (severe disability, dystonic CP, dyskinetic CP from basal ganglia injury).
  • Watershed (parasagittal) pattern: bilateral parasagittal cortex (parietal-occipital boundary), subcortical white matter → seen with prolonged moderate hypoperfusion. Cognitive sequelae, visual issues, motor deficits relatively less severe.
  • Mixed pattern: central + watershed — common with prolonged severe insult.
  • Focal infarction: arterial territory (e.g., MCA from antenatal/perinatal stroke).

DWI shows restriction in first 7–10 days; T2/T1 changes appear around days 7–14; cystic encephalomalacia / atrophy at 3 months.

PHYSIOLOGY

Explain selective vulnerability.
Show structured answer
  • Neurons in basal ganglia, thalamus, hippocampus are metabolically active with limited collateral supply — most vulnerable to severe global hypoxia-ischaemia.
  • Watershed regions (boundary zones between arterial territories) are vulnerable to hypoperfusion as collateral filling is poor when pressure drops.
  • Energy failure → membrane depolarisation → glutamate release → NMDA/AMPA over-activation → calcium influx → mitochondrial failure → cell death.
  • Secondary energy failure 6–24 hours later (apoptosis phase) — therapeutic window for hypothermia.
  • Hippocampus, putamen, globus pallidus, peri-Rolandic cortex are the most affected in severe HIE.
SAQ 187 — GLUT1 Deficiency Syndrome MODERATE
An infant presents with infantile-onset seizures, microcephaly, and severely low CSF glucose despite normal blood glucose.

DX

Outline diagnostic biochemistry.
Show structured answer
GLUT1 deficiency syndrome (De Vivo disease):
  • Heterozygous (mostly de novo) loss-of-function mutations in SLC2A1.
  • Reduced glucose transport across blood-brain barrier.
  • Hallmark biochemistry:
    • Hypoglycorrhachia (low CSF glucose < 2.0 mmol/L in fasted patient).
    • Normal blood glucose.
    • CSF:plasma glucose ratio < 0.45 (often ≤ 0.4) — classic.
    • Normal/low CSF lactate.
  • Clinical: infantile-onset seizures (mixed types), microcephaly developing, paroxysmal exercise-induced dyskinesia (PED), intellectual disability, complex movement disorder.
  • Confirm with SLC2A1 genetic testing.

TREATMENT

Outline first-line therapy.
Show structured answer
KETOGENIC DIET — first-line:
  • Classic ketogenic diet (4:1 fat-to-carbohydrate ratio) or modified Atkins.
  • Ketone bodies (β-OHB, acetoacetate) bypass GLUT1 — they cross the BBB via monocarboxylate transporter 1 (MCT1) to fuel the brain.
  • Often dramatic seizure control and improved movement disorder symptoms.
  • Should be continued long-term (years).
  • Initiated as early as possible to maximise cognitive benefit.

TRIHEPTANOIN (UX007):
  • Anaplerotic odd-chain triglyceride.
  • FDA-approved for long-chain FAOD; shown to reduce paroxysmal exercise-induced dyskinesia and seizures in some GLUT1 DS patients.

AVOID:
  • Phenobarbital, valproate, methylxanthines, diazepam — may further depress GLUT1.

Multidisciplinary: neurology, dietitian, metabolic, developmental services.

Family counselling: Most cases de novo, rare AD inheritance.
SAQ 188 — Mucormycosis in DKA HARD
A 14-year-old in DKA from new-onset T1DM develops black nasal eschar on day 2 of admission.

DX

Outline pathology and the susceptibility window.
Show structured answer
Rhinocerebral mucormycosis from Mucorales (Rhizopus oryzae most common, also Mucor, Rhizomucor, Cunninghamella, Lichtheimia).

Pathology:
  • ANGIO-INVASIVE — fungal hyphae invade blood vessels, cause thrombosis, infarction.
  • Tissue necrosis with characteristic black eschar.
  • Spreads from nose → sinuses → orbit → brain (especially via cavernous sinus / orbital apex).

Susceptibility:
  • DKA: low pH + high glucose + free iron (transferrin releases iron at low pH) creates ideal environment for Mucor growth.
  • Neutropenia.
  • Immunosuppression: steroids, transplant.
  • Deferoxamine therapy (iron supply).
  • COVID-19 pandemic — increased incidence noted ('CAM' — COVID-associated mucormycosis).

TREATMENT

Outline multimodal treatment.
Show structured answer
1) Surgical debridement — aggressive, often disfiguring, REPEATED.
  • Sinus, orbit, palate, sometimes intracranial.
  • Wide margins until viable tissue.
  • 'Cure' impossible without aggressive surgery.

2) Antifungal therapy:
  • Liposomal amphotericin B 5–10 mg/kg/day IV — first-line.
  • Continue until clinical improvement and source control.
  • Step-down to oral isavuconazole or posaconazole (CYP3A4 inducer-sensitive).
  • Long-term therapy.

3) Reverse predisposing factors:
  • Aggressive correction of DKA (lower glucose, correct acidosis).
  • Discontinue deferoxamine.
  • Reverse immunosuppression where possible (taper steroids, treat neutropenia).

4) Adjuncts:
  • Hyperbaric oxygen — adjunctive in some centres.
  • Iron chelation considered (caution: avoid deferoxamine which feeds Mucor; deferasirox alternative).

5) Multidisciplinary:
  • ENT, neurosurgery, ophthalmology, ICU, ID, endocrinology.

Mortality 40–80% even with optimal treatment.
SAQ 189 — ICU Delirium MODERATE
A 14-year-old ventilated 12 days has new agitation, sleep-wake disturbance, and refuses to track. CAPD score 12.

DEFINITION

Outline CAPD and subtypes of delirium.
Show structured answer
Cornell Assessment of Pediatric Delirium (CAPD):
  • Validated 8-item observational tool.
  • Score ≥ 9 = delirium.
  • Items: eye contact, purposeful actions, awareness, communication, restlessness, inconsolability, hypoactivity, response.
  • Designed to be quick for routine ICU screening.

Subtypes:
  • Hyperactive (~ 20%): agitation, hallucinations, restlessness — recognised easily.
  • Hypoactive (~ 45%): withdrawal, decreased responsiveness, often missed; worse outcomes.
  • Mixed (~ 35%): fluctuates between subtypes.

Incidence in PICU: 20–60% depending on cohort. Higher in: prolonged ventilation, sepsis, age < 5, developmental disability, prior neurodevelopmental issues.

TREATMENT

Outline non-pharmacologic and pharmacologic approach.
Show structured answer
Non-pharmacologic FIRST (ABCDEF bundle):
  • Awakening — daily sedation interruption.
  • Breathing — daily spontaneous breathing trial.
  • Choice of sedation — minimise benzodiazepines (deliriogenic); prefer dexmedetomidine.
  • Delirium screening — CAPD daily.
  • Early mobility.
  • Family engagement.
  • Plus: sleep hygiene (day/night cues, minimise nocturnal interruption), normal day/night lighting, family presence, familiar objects.
  • Treat pain, withdrawal, sleep deprivation; avoid restraints.

Reduce deliriogenic medications:
  • Benzodiazepines — most deliriogenic; replace with dexmedetomidine when possible.
  • Anticholinergics (diphenhydramine, atropine, scopolamine).
  • Steroids if minimal indication.

Pharmacologic adjuncts (for severe/refractory):
  • Low-dose atypical antipsychotics — quetiapine, risperidone, olanzapine.
  • Haloperidol — historical; less favoured given QT prolongation.
  • Melatonin for sleep regulation.

Long-term: Delirium associated with increased PICU mortality, prolonged ICU/hospital stay, post-PICU cognitive issues.
SAQ 190 — Neuromonitoring Suite in TBI HARD
A child with severe TBI is on multimodal monitoring with ICP, PRx, PbtO₂, microdialysis, and NIRS.

DEFINITION

List the main monitoring modalities and what each measures.
Show structured answer
ModalityWhat it measures
ICP (EVD or fiberoptic)Intracranial pressure; serves as basis for CPP calculation
CPP (MAP − ICP)Cerebral perfusion pressure
PRxPressure reactivity index — autoregulation status
CPPoptIndividualised optimal CPP target
PbtO₂ (Licox)Brain tissue oxygen tension (regional)
MicrodialysisBrain interstitial metabolites: lactate, pyruvate, L/P ratio, glucose, glutamate, glycerol
NIRSRegional cerebral oxygen saturation (non-invasive)
cEEGElectrographic seizures, background features
Transcranial DopplerFlow velocity, vasospasm detection, autoregulation
Jugular bulb oximetryGlobal cerebral oxygen extraction

PHYSIOLOGY

Explain the rationale for combining modalities.
Show structured answer
No single modality is sufficient:
  • ICP alone doesn't reveal regional ischaemia, metabolic state, or autoregulation.
  • CPP alone doesn't guarantee perfusion is adequate to metabolic demand.
  • PbtO₂ measures only the region around the probe (1–2 cm³).
  • Microdialysis lags ICP/CPP changes by 1–2 hours.

Combining modalities allows:
  • Detect mismatch — ICP normal but PbtO₂ low or L/P elevated suggests microvascular failure.
  • Tailor interventions — CPPopt-guided therapy individualises MAP target.
  • Confirm safe ICP-lowering interventions (e.g., hyperventilation might worsen PbtO₂).
  • Triggers for tier escalation are more nuanced.
  • Improves identification of secondary injury that would be missed by single-modality monitoring.
SAQ 191 — Acute Flaccid Myelitis (AFM) MODERATE
A 5-year-old develops acute asymmetric flaccid limb weakness 1 week after a febrile URI.

DX

Outline AFM features and the viral association.
Show structured answer
Acute Flaccid Myelitis (AFM):
  • Polio-like illness affecting anterior horn cells of the spinal cord.
  • Increasing recognition in pediatrics — temporal clusters in late summer / autumn (every 2 years in recent surveillance).
  • Strongest association: ENTEROVIRUS D68 (also enterovirus A71, West Nile, adenovirus).
  • Features:
    • Acute flaccid weakness of one or more limbs, often asymmetric.
    • Onset after febrile illness, typically 1–2 weeks earlier.
    • Preserved sensation.
    • Reflexes often absent in affected limbs.
    • Cranial nerve involvement (facial weakness, dysphagia) in some.
    • Respiratory failure if bulbar/diaphragmatic.
  • MRI: longitudinal T2 hyperintensity restricted to GREY MATTER (anterior horns) of the spinal cord — gives 'H' shape on axial.
  • CSF: lymphocytic pleocytosis, normal glucose, mildly raised protein.
  • Distinguishes from GBS (areflexic + sensory, root enhancement on MRI), transverse myelitis (whole cord on axial).

TREATMENT

Outline management approach.
Show structured answer
Supportive care is the mainstay — no specific antiviral proven.
  • Respiratory monitoring — intubation if bulbar/diaphragmatic involvement.
  • Cough assist, BiPAP as needed.
  • Aspiration precautions.
  • Bladder/bowel management.

Immunotherapy (no proven benefit, often used):
  • IVIG (commonly given based on case series).
  • Corticosteroids — controversial (theoretical concern about worsening viral infection); used in some protocols.
  • Plasma exchange in severe/refractory.
  • Fluoxetine — initially considered (in vitro EV-D68 activity); not effective clinically.

Rehabilitation early:
  • Physical and occupational therapy.
  • Swallow/speech evaluation.

Long-term:
  • Nerve transfer surgery considered after ≥ 6 months for persistent severe focal weakness.
  • Tendon transfers later.
  • Most have residual deficits; full recovery less common than GBS.

Public health: reporting; cluster investigation.
SAQ 192 — Neonatal Stroke MODERATE
A term newborn has a focal motor seizure at 24 hours of life. MRI: left MCA infarct.

DX

Outline aetiologies of perinatal arterial ischaemic stroke.
Show structured answer
Perinatal arterial ischaemic stroke (PAIS):
  • Incidence ~ 1 in 2500 term births — most common cause of pediatric AIS.
  • Often presents with focal seizures on day 1–3 of life; sometimes only with later motor asymmetry.
  • Multiple aetiologies, often multifactorial:
    • Placental thrombosis (chorionic vessel thrombi).
    • Hypoxic-ischaemic events (umbilical cord problems).
    • Maternal prothrombotic state, autoimmune (anti-phospholipid).
    • Congenital heart disease.
    • Polycythaemia.
    • Sepsis.
    • Cocaine exposure.
    • Inherited thrombophilia (often secondary contributor).
    • Catheterisation injury (umbilical artery, jugular).
  • MRI: focal restricted diffusion in arterial territory; typically LEFT MCA (~ 75% of all PAIS).

TREATMENT

Outline management.
Show structured answer
Acute:
  • Treat seizures — phenobarbital first-line; levetiracetam.
  • Supportive care: oxygenation, normocapnia, normothermia, normoglycaemia.
  • Continuous EEG monitoring.
  • NO tPA or thrombectomy in neonates (insufficient evidence; bleeding risk; vascular anatomy).
  • NO routine antithrombotic for PAIS (most lesions monophasic; thrombosis already complete).

Workup:
  • Echocardiogram (CHD).
  • Placental pathology.
  • Thrombophilia screen (controversial timing — defer some tests beyond neonatal period).
  • Maternal autoimmune workup.
  • Routine MRI/MRV.

Long-term:
  • Early physical therapy and stimulation programmes (e.g., constraint-induced movement therapy).
  • Risk of hemiplegic cerebral palsy (50–70%); cognitive issues; epilepsy.
  • School support.
  • Recurrent stroke risk low (~ 2%) — usually does not require lifelong antiplatelet.
SAQ 193 — Posterior Reversible Encephalopathy Syndrome (PRES) MODERATE
A 10-year-old on tacrolimus presents with seizures, confusion, BP 150/95. MRI: bilateral parieto-occipital T2/FLAIR without DWI restriction.

DEFINITION

Outline PRES — clinical, imaging, triggers.
Show structured answer
Posterior reversible encephalopathy syndrome (PRES):
Clinical:
  • Headache, altered mental status, seizures, visual disturbances (cortical blindness, hemianopia).
  • BP often elevated but not always.
  • Onset acute to subacute (hours-days).

Imaging:
  • Bilateral parieto-occipital subcortical T2/FLAIR hyperintensity — VASOGENIC oedema (no DWI restriction in typical cases).
  • Atypical patterns increasingly recognised: frontal, brainstem, deep grey involvement.
  • Usually reversible with prompt treatment.

Triggers:
  • Severe hypertension (most common in adults).
  • Calcineurin inhibitors (cyclosporine, tacrolimus).
  • Chemotherapy (vincristine, cisplatin, gemcitabine, intrathecal MTX).
  • Eclampsia/pre-eclampsia.
  • Autoimmune disease (SLE).
  • Sepsis.
  • Renal failure.

PHYSIOLOGY

Outline the two main hypotheses for pathophysiology.
Show structured answer
  1. Failure of cerebral autoregulation: Posterior circulation has less sympathetic innervation than anterior → less effective autoregulation. Hypertension exceeds upper limit of autoregulation → hyperperfusion + BBB disruption → vasogenic oedema, preferentially in posterior territories.
  2. Endothelial dysfunction: Cytotoxic drugs, immune dysregulation, sepsis cause endothelial injury → BBB disruption + vasogenic oedema. May explain cases without hypertension.

TREATMENT

Outline management.
Show structured answer
  • Remove offending agent: hold or switch calcineurin inhibitor (to sirolimus or belatacept depending on transplant); stop offending chemotherapy.
  • Controlled BP reduction — 10–25% of MAP over hours; AVOID precipitous drops (can cause infarct).
  • Seizure control: levetiracetam first-line; AEDs for ~ 1–2 weeks then can wean.
  • Magnesium for eclampsia.
  • Supportive ICU care.
  • Most cases reversible over days to weeks; rarely permanent injury if delayed treatment.
  • Future avoidance: surveillance if calcineurin inhibitor reintroduced; consider alternative immunosuppression if recurrent.
SAQ 194 — Cerebral Microcephaly Differentials MODERATE
A neonate has microcephaly (HC < 2 SD below mean for gestational age and sex).

DEFINITION

Outline causes of congenital and acquired microcephaly.
Show structured answer
Congenital microcephaly (present at birth):
  • Genetic primary microcephaly (microcephaly vera): MCPH genes (1–28); usually autosomal recessive; small but proportionate.
  • Syndromic genetic: trisomies 13, 18, 21; Cornelia de Lange; Smith-Lemli-Opitz; many others.
  • Congenital infections (TORCH): toxoplasmosis, others (syphilis, parvovirus, Zika, HIV), rubella, CMV, herpes — often with periventricular calcifications, hearing loss, chorioretinitis.
  • Teratogens: maternal alcohol (FAS), phenytoin, valproate, methadone, radiation.
  • Maternal disease: PKU (maternal PKU syndrome), severe hypothyroidism.
  • Severe HIE in utero.
  • Structural malformations: lissencephaly, holoprosencephaly, severe microgyria.

Acquired (post-natal) microcephaly (head circumference percentile drops):
  • Severe perinatal HIE.
  • Postnatal infection (meningitis, encephalitis).
  • Severe malnutrition.
  • Metabolic/genetic disorders (Rett, Angelman, MECP2 duplication, leukodystrophies).
  • Postnatal stroke.

NEXT STEP

Outline workup approach.
Show structured answer
History:
  • Detailed pregnancy history (teratogens, maternal illness, infections, alcohol).
  • Birth history (HIE, trauma).
  • Family history (consanguinity, microcephaly, intellectual disability, neurodegenerative disease).
  • Developmental trajectory.

Examination:
  • Anthropometrics — head circumference percentile and growth trajectory.
  • Dysmorphic features.
  • Skin: café-au-lait, hypopigmented macules, telangiectasias.
  • Neurological exam (tone, reflexes, cranial nerves, movements).
  • Hearing, ophthalmology (chorioretinitis, cataracts, abnormal fundus).

Investigations:
  • Imaging: MRI brain (gold standard).
  • Genetic: chromosomal microarray first; targeted gene panel or whole-exome/genome based on phenotype.
  • Metabolic: plasma amino acids, urine organic acids, lactate, ammonia.
  • TORCH workup (CMV PCR, toxo, rubella, HSV).
  • HIV testing.
  • Newborn screening review.

Multidisciplinary: neurology, genetics, developmental paediatrics, ophthalmology, audiology, early intervention.

Family counselling based on aetiology and recurrence risk.
SAQ 195 — Reye Syndrome MODERATE
A child with chickenpox treated with aspirin presents with vomiting, lethargy, hepatic dysfunction, and hyperammonaemia.

DEFINITION

Outline Reye syndrome and the key clinical biochemistry.
Show structured answer
Reye syndrome: Encephalopathy + hepatopathy without inflammation, classically after viral illness (influenza, varicella) with aspirin use.

Clinical:
  • Onset days after viral illness with vomiting (often severe), then progressive lethargy, agitation, encephalopathy, seizures.
  • Stages (Lovejoy): I lethargic + vomiting; II disoriented/combative; III stupor/decorticate; IV coma/decerebrate; V flaccid + apnoeic.

Biochemistry:
  • Elevated AST, ALT (often > 3× normal) with NORMAL bilirubin (distinguishing feature).
  • Hyperammonaemia (often > 100 µmol/L).
  • Hypoglycaemia.
  • Prolonged PT/INR.
  • Metabolic acidosis.

Mechanism: mitochondrial dysfunction (likely aspirin or aspirin metabolites + viral interaction interfering with hepatic mitochondrial fatty acid oxidation).

Incidence has dramatically dropped since aspirin use in children/adolescents with viral illnesses fell out of favour. Always rule out FAOD (especially MCAD deficiency) — mimics Reye.

TREATMENT

Outline management.
Show structured answer
Supportive ICU care:
  • Manage cerebral oedema and raised ICP — HOB up, hyperosmolar therapy, normothermia, normocapnia.
  • Hyperammonaemia: lactulose, nitrogen scavengers (Ammonul), CRRT for severe.
  • Hypoglycaemia: D10 maintenance, avoid hypoglycaemia.
  • Coagulopathy: vitamin K, FFP for active bleeding.
  • Seizure management.
  • Avoid further aspirin / NSAIDs in viral illness — public health message.

Critical: investigate for FAOD — MCAD, VLCAD, LCHAD can mimic Reye exactly and require specific therapy. Send acylcarnitine profile, urine organic acids.

Prevention:
  • AVOID aspirin in children/adolescents with viral illness (influenza, varicella especially).
  • Universal varicella and influenza vaccination.

Mortality reduced from ~ 50% historically to < 10% with modern critical care and earlier recognition.
SAQ 196 — Acute Hemorrhagic Leukoencephalitis (Weston Hurst) HARD
A 6-year-old develops rapid encephalopathy and multiple large white-matter haemorrhagic lesions 2 weeks after a viral illness.

DX

Diagnosis and severity considerations.
Show structured answer
Acute haemorrhagic leukoencephalitis (Weston Hurst syndrome):
  • Severe, hyperacute variant of ADEM.
  • Onset days after viral illness with rapid encephalopathy, focal deficits, seizures, raised ICP.
  • MRI: multifocal large white-matter lesions with haemorrhagic component, mass effect, oedema.
  • CSF: pleocytosis, often with neutrophils (vs lymphocytic in classic ADEM), elevated protein.
  • Pathology: perivenular demyelination + haemorrhage + neutrophil infiltrate + necrosis.
  • High mortality (~ 50%) — neurosurgical emergency.

Falls within ADEM spectrum but more severe; aggressive treatment required.

TREATMENT

Outline aggressive management.
Show structured answer
ICU management with aggressive treatment of raised ICP:
  • Hyperosmolar therapy (3% saline; mannitol).
  • Normothermia, normocapnia, HOB 30°.
  • Decompressive craniectomy may be needed for refractory mass effect.
  • Seizure management.

Immunotherapy (aggressive, multimodal):
  • HIGH-DOSE IV methylprednisolone 30 mg/kg/day × 5 days.
  • IVIG 2 g/kg.
  • PLASMA EXCHANGE.
  • CYCLOPHOSPHAMIDE for refractory cases.
  • Rituximab in some.

Supportive:
  • Mechanical ventilation if severe.
  • cEEG monitoring.
  • Rehabilitation early when stable.

Outcomes:
  • Mortality 30–50%.
  • Survivors often have severe neurologic deficits.
  • Outcome correlates with timing of immunotherapy and ICU intervention.
SAQ 197 — Reversible Cerebral Vasoconstriction Syndrome (RCVS) HARD
A teenager presents with thunderclap headache during exertion, recurrent over weeks. CTA normal initially; later angiogram shows segmental beading.

DX

Outline diagnostic features and triggers.
Show structured answer
RCVS: Recurrent thunderclap headaches plus reversible multifocal segmental cerebral artery vasoconstriction.
  • Time course: vasoconstriction develops over 1–3 weeks, resolves over 1–3 months.
  • Often presents with convexity (cortical) SAH (not basal cistern — rules out aneurysm).
  • Triggers: post-partum, vasoactive drugs (SSRIs, triptans, ergots, decongestants, cannabis, cocaine, amphetamines), Valsalva (sex, exercise, defecation, swimming, coughing).
  • Initial angiography may be normal; 'string of beads' appears later (peak 2–3 weeks).
  • CSF usually normal.

TREATMENT

Management.
Show structured answer
  • Stop offending vasoactive agents.
  • Calcium channel blockers (nimodipine 60 mg PO q4h × 3 weeks); verapamil alternative.
  • Avoid triggers during active phase.
  • Controlled BP management — avoid extremes.
  • Magnesium for refractory cases.
  • Steroids generally NOT helpful and may worsen.
  • Avoid triptans / ergots for headaches.
  • Most cases resolve in 1–3 months.
  • Repeat MRA / CTA at 4–8 weeks to confirm resolution.
SAQ 198 — Spontaneous Intracranial Hypotension MODERATE
A 14-year-old develops daily severe headache (worse upright) after minor trauma. MRI brain: diffuse smooth pachymeningeal enhancement, brain sagging, tonsillar descent.

DX

Outline imaging features (SEEPS).
Show structured answer
SIH imaging hallmarks — SEEPS mnemonic:
  • Subdural fluid collections.
  • Enhancement of pachymeninges (diffuse smooth, no nodularity).
  • Engorgement of venous structures.
  • Pituitary hyperaemia.
  • Sagging of the brain (with apparent Chiari-like tonsillar descent that resolves with patching).

Postural headache (worse upright) is the cardinal clinical feature.
Leak localisation: dynamic CT myelography, MR myelography, digital subtraction myelography (for CSF-venous fistulas).

TREATMENT

Stepwise management.
Show structured answer
  1. Conservative: bed rest, hydration, caffeine, analgesia (helps a minority).
  2. Epidural blood patch (EBP) — first intervention. Lumbar first; can be targeted to localised leak.
  3. Repeat EBP or fibrin glue for refractory.
  4. Surgical repair for persistent leak with identified site.
  5. Subdural collections from SIH usually resolve once leak sealed — avoid immediate SDH drainage.
SAQ 199 — Idiopathic Intracranial Hypertension (IIH) MODERATE
An obese 14-year-old has chronic headache, transient visual obscurations, pulsatile tinnitus, and bilateral papilloedema. LP opening pressure 38 cmH₂O.

CRITERIA

Outline diagnostic criteria.
Show structured answer
Modified Dandy criteria for IIH:
  1. Symptoms / signs of raised ICP.
  2. No localising signs except CN VI palsy.
  3. Awake and alert.
  4. Normal CSF composition.
  5. Normal imaging (no mass, no ventriculomegaly, no venous sinus thrombosis).
  6. Elevated opening pressure:
    • Adults: > 25 cmH₂O.
    • Non-sedated obese children: > 28 cmH₂O (Friedman criteria 2013).

Must exclude secondary causes — venous sinus thrombosis is the most important to rule out (CTV/MRV).

TREATMENT

Management.
Show structured answer
Medical:
  • Weight loss — only disease-modifying intervention for sustained remission.
  • Acetazolamide (carbonic anhydrase inhibitor) — first-line; high-dose if vision threatened.
  • Furosemide adjunct.
  • Topiramate alternative (also reduces appetite — dual benefit in obese patients).
  • Serial therapeutic LPs as temporising measure.

Surgical (for vision threat or refractory disease):
  • Optic nerve sheath fenestration (ONSF) — preserves vision; less effective for headache.
  • CSF diversion (VP shunt or LP shunt) — addresses both vision and headache; more complications.
  • Venous sinus stenting — for documented stenosis (especially transverse sinus).

Surveillance: serial ophthalmology, visual fields, OCT.
SAQ 200 — Status Migrainosus MODERATE
A 13-year-old has severe migraine refractory to NSAIDs and triptans for 72+ hours.

DEFINITION

Define status migrainosus.
Show structured answer
Status migrainosus (ICHD-3):
  • Debilitating migraine attack lasting > 72 hours.
  • Refractory to typical abortive therapy.
  • Causes significant disability.
  • Pain-free intervals < 4 h not considered breaking the duration.

TREATMENT

Outline inpatient management.
Show structured answer
IV pharmacological options (typically given in combination):
  • IV hydration.
  • IV magnesium sulfate 30 mg/kg.
  • IV valproate 15 mg/kg (avoid if pregnancy or hepatic disease).
  • IV ketorolac.
  • Dihydroergotamine (DHE) 0.5–1 mg q8h (Raskin protocol) — avoid in basilar/hemiplegic migraine.
  • Dopamine antagonists: prochlorperazine, metoclopramide, chlorpromazine (watch for extrapyramidal effects).
  • Diphenhydramine often pre-administered before dopamine antagonists.

Steroid bolus (dexamethasone 10 mg or methylprednisolone) to prevent recurrence — single dose.

Adjuncts: antiemetic, dark room, sleep, prevent dehydration.

Avoid: opioids (rebound headache, addiction), barbiturates routinely.

Long-term: review and optimise preventive medication; identify triggers; lifestyle factors; consider chronic migraine workup if frequent attacks.
SAQ 201 — Second-Impact Syndrome HARD
A 16-year-old returns to play despite persistent post-concussion symptoms, sustains another impact, and collapses with rapid herniation.

DEFINITION

Outline second-impact syndrome.
Show structured answer
Second-impact syndrome (SIS):
  • Catastrophic outcome of a second head injury sustained while still symptomatic from a recent concussion.
  • Mechanism: post-concussive metabolic and autoregulatory derangement → second impact triggers catastrophic loss of autoregulation → pressure-passive cerebral blood flow → diffuse cerebral oedema → herniation within minutes.
  • Almost unique to adolescents (immature autoregulation, contact sports).
  • Survival uncommon; survivors typically have severe disability.
  • Time from second impact to collapse: minutes (typical < 5 min).

TREATMENT

Outline management and prevention.
Show structured answer
Acute management:
  • Emergency airway, ventilation.
  • Aggressive ICP management: HOB 30°, hyperosmolar therapy, normocapnia, normothermia.
  • Decompressive craniectomy may be needed.
  • Multimodal monitoring.
  • Outcome generally poor despite intervention.

Prevention is the only effective strategy:
  • STRICT return-to-play protocols (Berlin/Amsterdam consensus): graduated step-wise return; each step must be symptom-free for 24 h before progression.
  • NO return to play until completely asymptomatic at baseline.
  • Education for athletes, parents, coaches.
  • Concussion protocols at all levels of sport.
  • Helmet/protective equipment.
  • 'When in doubt, sit them out.'
SAQ 202 — Concussion Return-to-Play MODERATE
A 14-year-old soccer player sustains a witnessed concussion with brief LOC.

CRITERIA

Outline the graduated return-to-play protocol.
Show structured answer
Berlin/Amsterdam Consensus Statement on Concussion in Sport:
  • Step 1: 24–48 h initial rest (limited cognitive activity); avoid screen time; sleep optimisation.
  • Step 2: Light aerobic exercise (walking, stationary cycling at low intensity).
  • Step 3: Sport-specific exercise (running, no contact).
  • Step 4: Non-contact training drills (more complex training).
  • Step 5: Full-contact practice (after medical clearance).
  • Step 6: Return to play / sport (after symptom-free at all prior steps).

Key principles:
  • Each step requires 24 h symptom-free before progression.
  • If symptoms return at any step → drop back to previous step.
  • Minimum total time: ~ 7 days for adolescents; longer if symptoms persist.
  • School/cognitive demands managed in parallel ('return to learn').
  • Younger athletes typically take longer.
  • Recurrent or persistent symptoms warrant comprehensive evaluation.

NEXT STEP

Outline 'return to learn' principles.
Show structured answer
Return to learn:
  • Early sub-symptom threshold cognitive activity facilitates recovery.
  • Strict cognitive rest no longer recommended.
  • Gradual return to school with accommodations as symptoms allow.
  • School accommodations: shortened day, frequent breaks, reduced workload, extended deadlines, quiet environment, sunglasses if photophobia, no testing initially.
  • Communication between school, family, healthcare team.
  • Most students return to full school within 1–4 weeks.
  • Persistent symptoms > 4 weeks: refer to concussion clinic for multidisciplinary care (vestibular rehab, vision therapy, cognitive remediation, psychology).
SAQ 203 — Dopa-Responsive Dystonia MODERATE
A 9-year-old has progressive lower-limb dystonia worse at end of day, relieved by sleep, and dramatic response to low-dose L-DOPA.

DX

Outline the genetic basis and clinical features.
Show structured answer
Dopa-responsive dystonia (DRD; Segawa disease):
  • Autosomal dominant GCH1 mutations — GTP cyclohydrolase 1 deficiency.
  • Reduces tetrahydrobiopterin (BH4), cofactor for tyrosine hydroxylase → reduced dopamine synthesis.
  • Classic phenotype:
    • Childhood-onset lower-limb dystonia.
    • DIURNAL FLUCTUATION (worse evening, better with sleep) — hallmark.
    • Dramatic response to low-dose L-DOPA.
    • Often misdiagnosed as cerebral palsy.
  • Differential: tyrosine hydroxylase deficiency (AR, more severe, neonatal/infantile); AADC deficiency; juvenile parkinsonism (PARKIN, PINK1).

TREATMENT

Outline therapy.
Show structured answer
  • L-DOPA / carbidopa — first-line; dramatic and sustained response.
  • Start low (1–2 mg/kg/day) and titrate.
  • Typical maintenance 4–10 mg/kg/day divided 2–3 doses.
  • Unlike Parkinson disease, no long-term motor fluctuations or dyskinesia even on chronic L-DOPA.
  • Lifelong therapy.
  • Anticholinergics (trihexyphenidyl) for residual dystonia in some.
  • Avoid dopamine antagonists.
  • Genetic counselling: AD with reduced penetrance (~ 30%); cascade testing offered (presymptomatic identification and treatment).
SAQ 204 — Friedreich Ataxia — Omaveloxolone MODERATE
A 14-year-old with confirmed Friedreich ataxia and progressive cardiomyopathy.

DEFINITION

Outline the disease and the genetic basis.
Show structured answer
Friedreich ataxia (FRDA):
  • Autosomal recessive GAA trinucleotide repeat expansion in FXN (frataxin) gene on chromosome 9q21.
  • Reduced frataxin → mitochondrial iron-sulfur cluster dysfunction → energy failure + oxidative stress.
  • Classic features: progressive ataxia (cerebellar + sensory), areflexia with upgoing plantars (mixed UMN/LMN), dysarthria, scoliosis, pes cavus, hypertrophic CARDIOMYOPATHY (leading cause of death), diabetes, hearing/visual loss.
  • Repeat size correlates with severity: larger = earlier onset, faster progression, earlier cardiomyopathy.

TREATMENT

Outline disease-modifying and supportive therapy.
Show structured answer
OMAVELOXOLONE (Skyclarys):
  • FDA-approved 2023 for FRDA in patients ≥ 16 years.
  • First disease-modifying therapy for FRDA.
  • Mechanism: Nrf2 pathway activator → increased expression of antioxidant response element genes → mitigates oxidative stress.
  • MOXIe trial: modest mFARS improvement (~ 1.6 points) at 48 weeks vs placebo.
  • Cardiac and long-term effects under investigation.

Surveillance:
  • CARDIAC: annual echo + ECG; cardiac MRI; treat HF (ACE inhibitor, β-blocker); ICD if indicated.
  • ENDOCRINE: annual HbA1c.
  • ORTHOPAEDIC: scoliosis surgery as needed.
  • AUDIOLOGY, ophthalmology.

Investigational: gene therapy with AAV9-frataxin (trials ongoing); other compounds.

Multidisciplinary clinic, genetic counselling, family cascade testing.
SAQ 205 — DMD — Modern Therapies HARD
A 6-year-old boy with confirmed DMD on corticosteroid therapy.

DEFINITION

Outline disease and current treatment landscape.
Show structured answer
Duchenne muscular dystrophy (DMD):
  • X-linked recessive; mutations in DMD gene (Xp21) producing absent dystrophin protein.
  • Becker MD (BMD): same gene, in-frame mutations producing partial dystrophin → milder.
  • Progressive proximal weakness, loss of ambulation typically by age 12–13, cardiomyopathy, respiratory failure.

Treatment landscape:
  • Corticosteroids (prednisone, deflazacort) — standard of care; slow disease progression, extend ambulation by 2–3 years.
  • Vamorolone — dissociative steroid (FDA 2023); fewer side effects.
  • Exon-skipping antisense oligonucleotides (for specific mutations):
    • Eteplirsen — exon 51 skip (~ 13% of patients).
    • Golodirsen, viltolarsen — exon 53 skip.
    • Casimersen — exon 45 skip.
  • Gene therapy: delandistrogene moxeparvovec (ELEVIDYS) — AAV-delivered micro-dystrophin; FDA-approved 2023 for ages 4–5, expanded 2024.
  • Investigational: givinostat (HDAC inhibitor; FDA 2024), CRISPR-based therapies.

NEXT STEP

Outline multidisciplinary management.
Show structured answer
  • CARDIAC: yearly echo from age 6, then cardiac MRI; ACE inhibitor + β-blocker for cardiomyopathy.
  • RESPIRATORY: spirometry, sleep study; nocturnal NIV when FVC drops below 50% predicted; cough assist.
  • ORTHOPAEDIC: scoliosis management (spinal fusion when significant), contracture management.
  • NUTRITION: bone health (calcium, vitamin D); weight management.
  • REHAB: physiotherapy, occupational therapy, orthotics.
  • NEUROCOGNITIVE assessment and educational support.
  • ANAESTHESIA precautions: avoid succinylcholine (rhabdomyolysis), inhalational anaesthetics (cardiac risk); use TIVA.
  • Genetic counselling: X-linked; mother usually carrier; affected male offspring 50%; daughters 50% carriers; carrier females can develop cardiomyopathy.
  • Family screening, prenatal diagnosis.
SAQ 206 — SMA — Disease-Modifying Therapies HARD
A 4-month-old infant has confirmed SMA type 1 (homozygous SMN1 deletion, 2 copies of SMN2).

DEFINITION

Outline the disease.
Show structured answer
Spinal muscular atrophy (SMA):
  • Autosomal recessive motor neuron disease.
  • Homozygous SMN1 deletion / mutation (chromosome 5q13).
  • Severity modified by SMN2 copy number (more SMN2 = milder).
  • Classification (clinical):
    • Type 1 (Werdnig-Hoffmann): onset < 6 months; never sits independently; previously fatal in infancy.
    • Type 2: onset 6–18 months; sits but doesn't walk.
    • Type 3 (Kugelberg-Welander): onset > 18 months; walks initially.
    • Type 4: adult onset.
    • Type 0: prenatal/neonatal onset, severe.

TREATMENT

Outline disease-modifying therapies.
Show structured answer
Three approved disease-modifying therapies (DMTs):
  1. Nusinersen (Spinraza):
    • Antisense oligonucleotide given INTRATHECALLY.
    • Modifies SMN2 splicing to include exon 7 → increased functional SMN protein.
    • Loading (4 doses) then maintenance every 4 months for life.
  2. Onasemnogene abeparvovec (Zolgensma):
    • Single IV dose AAV9 gene therapy delivering an SMN1 gene copy.
    • FDA-approved for children < 2 years.
    • One-time treatment.
    • Side effects: transient elevated transaminases, thrombocytopenia, cardiac monitoring.
  3. Risdiplam (Evrysdi):
    • Oral small molecule splicing modifier of SMN2.
    • Daily oral dosing.
    • Less invasive than IT nusinersen.

Critical: TREAT EARLY — pre-symptomatic treatment (via newborn screening) yields best outcomes.

Supportive care:
  • Respiratory (BiPAP, cough assist; tracheostomy in some).
  • Nutrition (G-tube).
  • Orthopaedics (scoliosis).
  • Multidisciplinary clinic.
  • Family genetic counselling.
SAQ 207 — Pompe Disease — ERT and CRIM Status HARD
A 4-month-old infant with cardiomyopathy, macroglossia, hypotonia, and severely elevated CK.

DX

Outline disease.
Show structured answer
Pompe disease (glycogen storage disease type II):
  • Autosomal recessive acid α-glucosidase (GAA) deficiency.
  • Lysosomal glycogen accumulation in cardiac/skeletal muscle, liver, CNS.
  • Infantile-onset (IOPD): severe cardiomyopathy with LVH, hepatomegaly, macroglossia, 'floppy baby' hypotonia, respiratory failure.
  • Late-onset (LOPD): progressive proximal myopathy + respiratory weakness; NO cardiomyopathy.
  • Untreated IOPD fatal in first year.

TREATMENT

Outline ERT, CRIM status, and immune tolerance.
Show structured answer
Enzyme replacement therapy (ERT):
  • Alglucosidase alfa (Myozyme/Lumizyme) — recombinant human GAA, IV every 2 weeks.
  • Avalglucosidase alfa (Nexviazyme) — newer with improved lysosomal uptake (FDA 2021).
  • Start as early as possible — best outcomes when initiated within 6 months and before significant muscle damage.

CRIM (cross-reactive immunologic material) status — critical:
  • CRIM-positive: have some residual enzyme; develop low-titre antibodies to ERT; respond well.
  • CRIM-negative: produce NO enzyme; develop high-titre antibodies that neutralise ERT; ERT fails.
  • CRIM-negative patients need IMMUNE TOLERANCE INDUCTION (ITI) protocol with rituximab + methotrexate ± IVIG before/during ERT initiation.

Multidisciplinary care:
  • Neuromuscular clinic.
  • Cardiology — HF therapy, arrhythmia surveillance.
  • Pulmonology — BiPAP, eventually invasive ventilation; cough assist.
  • Nutrition — G-tube feeding often required.
  • Anaesthesia consult before procedures.

Newborn screening for Pompe allows pre-symptomatic treatment — dramatically better outcomes.

Gene therapy in trials.
SAQ 208 — Botulinum Toxin in Spasticity MODERATE
A 6-year-old with spastic diplegic cerebral palsy has focal calf spasticity limiting gait.

DEFINITION

Outline mechanism and indications.
Show structured answer
Botulinum toxin (BTX):
  • Mechanism: cleaves SNARE proteins (especially SNAP-25 for BTX-A) at presynaptic motor terminals → blocks acetylcholine release → muscle weakness.
  • Indications in pediatrics:
    • Focal spasticity in cerebral palsy.
    • Dystonia (focal — cervical dystonia, blepharospasm, hemifacial spasm).
    • Sialorrhoea (parotid/submandibular injection).
    • Strabismus.
    • Bladder neurogenic instability.
    • Migraine prevention (chronic migraine in adolescents — FDA approved in adults).
  • Onset 1–2 weeks; peak effect 4–6 weeks; duration 3–4 months; redosing typically not before 12 weeks.
  • Common formulations: onabotulinumtoxinA (Botox), abobotulinumtoxinA (Dysport), incobotulinumtoxinA (Xeomin), rimabotulinumtoxinB (Myobloc — type B).
  • Dosing units NOT interchangeable between formulations.

NEXT STEP

Outline treatment planning and safety.
Show structured answer
Treatment planning:
  • Identify functional goals (e.g., gait improvement, ease of care).
  • Target specific muscles based on examination.
  • Coordinate with physiotherapy.
  • Use ultrasound or electrical stimulation guidance for accurate injection (especially for deep muscles).
  • Maximum dose per session limited by total units / body weight.
  • Often combined with serial casting / orthotics.

Side effects:
  • Local: pain at injection site, weakness of adjacent muscles.
  • Systemic (rare but serious): distant muscle weakness, dysphagia, respiratory compromise.
  • Allergic reactions.
  • Antibody formation reducing future efficacy (more with rapid re-injection).

Contraindications:
  • Active infection at site.
  • Hypersensitivity.
  • Use with caution in: neuromuscular disease (MG, LEMS — pre-existing dysfunction), aminoglycosides (potentiate weakness).

FDA black box warning regarding distant spread of toxin causing serious adverse effects — caution with high doses or pediatric use.
SAQ 209 — Tuberous Sclerosis Complex — Diagnostic Criteria MODERATE
A 7-month-old with infantile spasms has multiple cortical tubers on MRI.

CRITERIA

Outline TSC diagnostic criteria.
Show structured answer
TSC diagnostic criteria (2012 revised; restated 2021):
Genetic criteria: pathogenic TSC1 or TSC2 mutation = definitive diagnosis regardless of clinical features.

Clinical criteria: 2 major OR 1 major + ≥ 2 minor.

MAJOR (11):
  • Hypomelanotic macules ≥ 3, each ≥ 5 mm.
  • Angiofibromas ≥ 3, OR fibrous cephalic plaque.
  • Ungual fibromas ≥ 2.
  • Shagreen patch.
  • Multiple retinal hamartomas.
  • Cortical dysplasias (tubers or migration lines) ≥ 3.
  • Subependymal nodules ≥ 2.
  • Subependymal giant cell astrocytoma (SEGA).
  • Cardiac rhabdomyoma.
  • Lymphangioleiomyomatosis.
  • Renal angiomyolipoma ≥ 2.

MINOR (6):
  • Confetti skin lesions.
  • Dental enamel pits > 3.
  • Intraoral fibromas ≥ 2.
  • Retinal achromic patch.
  • Multiple renal cysts.
  • Non-renal hamartomas.

TREATMENT

Outline mTOR-targeted therapy.
Show structured answer
mTOR inhibitors (everolimus, sirolimus):
  • Target the constitutively activated mTOR pathway in TSC.
  • Indications in pediatric TSC:
    • SEGA > 1 cm not surgical candidate (everolimus).
    • Renal angiomyolipoma > 3 cm.
    • Refractory focal seizures (everolimus ≥ 2 years; EXIST-3 trial).
    • Sirolimus topical for facial angiofibromas.
    • Sirolimus systemic for lymphangioleiomyomatosis (adults).
  • Side effects: mucositis, infections, hyperlipidaemia, growth concerns, immunosuppression.

Vigabatrin first-line for TSC-related infantile spasms (uniquely effective vs other causes).

EPISTOP trial (2021): pre-emptive vigabatrin from infancy in TSC infants with epileptiform EEG reduces drug-resistant epilepsy.

Multidisciplinary surveillance:
  • Neurology: seizures, SEGA.
  • Renal: angiomyolipomas, cysts.
