  • Cardiology: rhabdomyomas (usually regress).
  • Pulmonary: LAM in adolescent/adult females.
  • Ophthalmology: hamartomas.
  • Skin.
  • Cognitive/behavioural: TSC-associated neuropsychiatric disorders (TAND).
SAQ 210 — Lennox-Gastaut Syndrome — Treatment Hierarchy MODERATE
A 6-year-old with multiple seizure types (atonic, tonic, atypical absence) and slow spike-wave EEG.

DEFINITION

Outline the triad and seizure types.
Show structured answer
Lennox-Gastaut syndrome (LGS) — diagnostic triad:
  1. Multiple seizure types — atonic ('drop attacks'), tonic (especially in sleep), atypical absence; plus often GTCs, focal, myoclonic.
  2. Characteristic EEG: slow spike-wave (1.5–2.5 Hz) + paroxysmal fast activity (especially in sleep).
  3. Cognitive impairment.

Onset: 3–8 years typically.

Aetiology:
  • Structural (cortical malformations, post-injury, tuberous sclerosis).
  • Genetic (numerous; CHD2, GABRB3, others).
  • Often evolves from West syndrome.
  • Cryptogenic in 25%.

TREATMENT

Outline pharmacological and non-pharmacological approach.
Show structured answer
Broad-spectrum AEDs:
  • Valproate.
  • Lamotrigine (caution: rash; slow titration).
  • Topiramate.
  • Rufinamide (FDA-approved for LGS).
  • Clobazam (FDA-approved for LGS).
  • Cannabidiol (Epidiolex, FDA-approved for LGS).
  • Fenfluramine (newly approved for LGS, 2022).
  • Felbamate — effective but reserved due to aplastic anaemia / hepatic failure risk.

AVOID:
  • Carbamazepine, oxcarbazepine, vigabatrin — may worsen seizures in LGS.

Non-pharmacological:
  • Ketogenic diet — highly effective; consider early.
  • Vagal nerve stimulator (VNS) — for refractory disease.
  • Corpus callosotomy — for disabling drop attacks (atonic seizures); reduces injury risk.
  • Focal resection if focal lesion identified on detailed imaging.

Adjunct: helmets for drop attacks, cognitive support, special education, family support, palliative care for severe disease.

Prognosis: generally poor for seizure control and cognitive outcome despite multimodal therapy.
SAQ 211 — Dravet Syndrome HARD
A 14-month-old with prolonged hemiclonic febrile seizures since 6 months of age, now developing afebrile seizures. SCN1A mutation de novo.

DEFINITION

Outline Dravet syndrome.
Show structured answer
Dravet syndrome (severe myoclonic epilepsy of infancy):
  • Onset typically 4–12 months with prolonged febrile seizures (often hemiclonic, long-duration).
  • Evolution: afebrile seizures by 2 years; multiple seizure types (myoclonic, atypical absence, focal, status epilepticus); developmental slowing or regression after first year.
  • Crisp-sensitive — fever, hot baths, photic stimulation, exertion can trigger.
  • Cause: 80% SCN1A mutations (loss of function in voltage-gated sodium channel α subunit affecting inhibitory interneurons preferentially).
  • Lifetime risk of SUDEP (sudden unexpected death in epilepsy) elevated.

TREATMENT

Outline first-line therapy and what to AVOID.
Show structured answer
FIRST-LINE AEDs:
  • Valproate.
  • Clobazam.
  • Stiripentol (approved as adjunct; metabolic interaction).
  • Cannabidiol (Epidiolex) — FDA-approved for Dravet.
  • Fenfluramine (Fintepla) — FDA-approved 2020 specifically for Dravet; effective for convulsive seizures.

AVOID — sodium channel blockers (worsen Dravet, paradoxically):
  • Carbamazepine.
  • Oxcarbazepine.
  • Phenytoin.
  • Lamotrigine — particularly important to avoid; can worsen significantly.

Other strategies:
  • Aggressive fever management — antipyretics at first sign, cool environment, avoid hot baths/saunas.
  • Ketogenic diet — effective in many patients.
  • Photosensitivity precautions if applicable.

Investigational:
  • STK-001 (antisense oligonucleotide for SCN1A) — early trials.
  • Gene therapy approaches.

Comprehensive care: multidisciplinary, cognitive/behavioural support, family education, emergency action plan, medical alert.
SAQ 212 — Aicardi-Goutières Syndrome HARD
A 6-month-old has progressive microcephaly, basal ganglia calcifications, severe developmental delay, CSF lymphocytosis with elevated interferon-α, but no infection.

DEFINITION

Outline the disease and the genetic basis.
Show structured answer
Aicardi-Goutières syndrome (AGS):
  • Type I interferonopathy — autoinflammatory disorder mimicking congenital infection.
  • Mutations in genes regulating innate immunity / nucleic acid metabolism: TREX1, RNASEH2A/B/C, SAMHD1, ADAR1, IFIH1.
  • Inappropriate activation of type I interferon (IFN-α) signalling — endogenous nucleic acids activate sensors that normally detect viral DNA/RNA.
  • Presentation:
    • Encephalopathy in infancy with regression / developmental delay.
    • Microcephaly developing.
    • Spasticity, dystonia.
    • Calcifications in basal ganglia and white matter on CT.
    • Lymphocytic CSF pleocytosis.
    • Elevated CSF and serum IFN-α and interferon-stimulated genes ('interferon signature').
    • Chilblains, panniculitis, mouth ulcers in some.
  • Mimics congenital infection (TORCH) clinically and on imaging — but no actual infection.

TREATMENT

Outline emerging therapy.
Show structured answer
JAK inhibitors — emerging targeted therapy:
  • BARICITINIB — blocks JAK1/2 signalling downstream of type I IFN receptor.
  • Reduces interferon signature and improves clinical features in many patients.
  • Earlier treatment may improve outcomes.

Supportive care:
  • Seizure management.
  • Spasticity / dystonia management.
  • Nutrition (often PEG).
  • Multidisciplinary care.

Family genetic counselling:
  • Inheritance varies by gene (most AR; AD for some IFIH1 and TREX1 mutations).
  • Cascade testing.
  • Prenatal options.

Prognosis: Variable based on genotype; many severe with limited lifespan, milder phenotypes possible.
SAQ 213 — Niemann-Pick Type C MODERATE
A 14-year-old has progressive ataxia, vertical supranuclear gaze palsy, splenomegaly, and gelastic cataplexy.

DX

Outline disease.
Show structured answer
Niemann-Pick type C (NPC):
  • Autosomal recessive NPC1 (95%) or NPC2 mutations.
  • Lysosomal storage disorder of cholesterol and other lipid trafficking.
  • Classic features:
    • VERTICAL SUPRANUCLEAR GAZE PALSY (VSGP) — impaired voluntary vertical saccades (especially DOWN-gaze); reflex eye movements preserved.
    • Progressive ataxia, dystonia, dysarthria.
    • Splenomegaly (often from infancy).
    • Neonatal cholestasis in some forms.
    • Cognitive decline, neuropsychiatric features.
    • GELASTIC CATAPLEXY — sudden loss of muscle tone with laughter; rare but characteristic.
    • Seizures.
  • Wide phenotypic spectrum: neonatal, late-infantile, juvenile, adult-onset.
  • Diagnosis: filipin staining of cultured fibroblasts (cholesterol storage), elevated oxysterol biomarkers (cholestane-3β,5α,6β-triol, 7-ketocholesterol), molecular testing.

TREATMENT

Outline therapy.
Show structured answer
MIGLUSTAT (Zavesca):
  • Substrate reduction therapy — inhibits glucosylceramide synthase → reduces glycosphingolipid accumulation.
  • Slows neurological progression in children and adults.
  • Approved in Europe and many countries for NPC neurological manifestations.

Hydroxypropyl-β-cyclodextrin (HPβCD):
  • Investigational; intrathecal delivery has shown benefit in trials.

Arimoclomol: HSP induction; in clinical trials.

Supportive:
  • Seizure management.
  • Dystonia treatment.
  • Nutrition (G-tube often needed).
  • Palliative care.
  • Multidisciplinary.

Family genetic counselling (AR, 25% recurrence; cascade testing).
SAQ 214 — Wernicke vs Korsakoff MODERATE
A patient with chronic alcohol misuse develops confusion, ophthalmoplegia, and ataxia. Subsequently develops anterograde amnesia.

DEFINITION

Distinguish Wernicke encephalopathy from Korsakoff syndrome.
Show structured answer
Wernicke encephalopathy: Acute, reversible thiamine deficiency with classic triad of:
  1. Encephalopathy (confusion).
  2. Ophthalmoplegia (especially CN VI, nystagmus, conjugate gaze palsy).
  3. Ataxia.
Only 10–30% have all three; many have partial syndrome — high index of suspicion needed.

Korsakoff syndrome: Chronic, often permanent consequence of inadequately treated Wernicke. Features:
  • Anterograde amnesia (severe — cannot form new memories).
  • Retrograde amnesia (variable extent).
  • Confabulation (filling memory gaps).
  • Limited insight.
  • Other cognitive functions relatively preserved.
  • Anatomic substrate: bilateral mammillary body / dorsomedial thalamus injury.

Together: 'Wernicke-Korsakoff syndrome' when both phases present.

TREATMENT

Outline treatment.
Show structured answer
IV THIAMINE immediately at suspicion:
  • Acute Wernicke: 500 mg IV TID × 3 days.
  • Then 250 mg IV/IM daily × 5 days.
  • Then oral maintenance 100 mg daily.

Critical principles:
  • ALWAYS give thiamine BEFORE any glucose load in at-risk patients (alcohol, hyperemesis, anorexia, prolonged TPN, prolonged fasting, refeeding).
  • Glucose load before thiamine consumes remaining thiamine → can precipitate Wernicke.
  • Early recognition and aggressive treatment is the only way to prevent Korsakoff syndrome.

Once Korsakoff established:
  • Less reversible — neurological deficits often permanent.
  • Long-term supportive care.
  • Continued thiamine, abstinence from alcohol, nutritional support.

Pediatric context: Less common but seen with anorexia nervosa, hyperemesis gravidarum, prolonged TPN without thiamine, cancer chemotherapy.
SAQ 215 — Refeeding Syndrome — NICE Risk Stratification MODERATE
A 16-year-old with anorexia nervosa (BMI 13) is to be admitted for nutritional rehabilitation.

CRITERIA

Outline NICE high-risk and very-high-risk criteria.
Show structured answer
NICE risk stratification for refeeding syndrome:
HIGH RISK (any ONE of):
  • BMI < 16.
  • Unintentional weight loss > 15% in 3–6 months.
  • Little / no oral intake > 10 days.
  • Low pre-refeeding K, PO₄, or Mg.

OR any TWO of:
  • BMI < 18.5.
  • Unintentional weight loss > 10% in 3–6 months.
  • Little / no intake > 5 days.
  • History of alcohol misuse, drugs (insulin, chemotherapy, antacids, diuretics).

For very high risk (BMI < 14, intake < 5 days): start refeeding at maximum 5 kcal/kg/day, advance very cautiously.

TREATMENT

Outline prevention and acute management.
Show structured answer
Prevention (essential):
  • THIAMINE 100 mg IV/IM/oral BEFORE glucose load; continue daily.
  • Start refeeding SLOWLY: 10–20 kcal/kg/day initially; advance gradually based on tolerance.
  • Prophylactic electrolyte repletion (PO₄, K, Mg) based on weight and risk.
  • Daily PO₄, K, Mg, Ca, glucose monitoring initially.
  • Cardiac monitoring.
  • Multivitamin.
  • Fluid restriction (20–30 mL/kg/day) — avoid overload.

Acute refeeding syndrome:
  • Slow or temporarily stop refeeding.
  • Aggressive PO₄, K, Mg repletion.
  • Thiamine 500 mg IV TID × 3 d.
  • Cardiac monitoring (arrhythmia).
  • Treat seizures (correct electrolytes simultaneously).
  • Resume slower refeeding once stabilised.

Mortality untreated up to 80%. Multidisciplinary: ICU, eating disorders team, dietitian, psychiatry; family involvement.
SAQ 216 — Lithium Toxicity HARD
A 14-year-old on chronic lithium was started on hydrochlorothiazide. Now: tremor, confusion, ataxia, AKI. Level 3.8 mEq/L.

PHYSIOLOGY

Outline drug interactions that raise lithium levels.
Show structured answer
Drugs that raise lithium levels:
  • Thiazides and loop diuretics — reduce sodium delivery to distal tubule → increased proximal lithium reabsorption (lithium handled similarly to Na in proximal tubule).
  • NSAIDs — reduce renal clearance via prostaglandin inhibition.
  • ACE inhibitors / ARBs — reduce renal clearance.
  • Dehydration (any cause: gastroenteritis, hot weather, sweating).
  • Acute renal injury.

Toxicity stages:
  • Mild (1.5–2.5): tremor, GI symptoms, fatigue.
  • Moderate (2.5–3.5): coarse tremor, dysarthria, ataxia, confusion.
  • Severe (> 3.5): seizures, coma, arrhythmia, AKI; can be fatal.

Chronic toxicity often presents with severe neurologic features at lower levels than acute overdose.

TREATMENT

Outline management.
Show structured answer
  1. STOP lithium immediately.
  2. STOP HCTZ; manage hypertension differently.
  3. IV fluids (0.9% NaCl) — restore volume, enhance clearance.
  4. Cardiac monitoring (lithium can prolong QT, AV block, arrhythmias).
  5. HEMODIALYSIS indications:
    • Level > 4 mEq/L regardless of symptoms.
    • Level 2.5–4 with severe symptoms (seizures, coma, AKI).
    • Refractory haemodynamic instability.
    • Beware POST-DIALYSIS REBOUND — lithium redistributes from intracellular to extracellular; repeat session often needed.
  6. AVOID activated charcoal (doesn't bind lithium effectively).
  7. Consider polyethylene glycol whole-bowel irrigation for sustained-release ingestion within 1 hour.
  8. Treat seizures with benzodiazepines.

Long-term sequelae: nephrogenic DI, CKD, hypothyroidism, cognitive effects.

Future management of bipolar: may need alternative mood stabiliser; if continuing lithium, careful drug interaction review, dose reduction, regular monitoring.
SAQ 217 — Acute Dystonic Reaction MODERATE
A teenager develops oculogyric crisis and torticollis hours after starting haloperidol.

DX

Outline diagnosis and the typical drugs.
Show structured answer
Acute dystonic reaction:
  • Caused by acute D2 dopamine receptor blockade → striatal cholinergic excess.
  • Typical onset hours to days of starting offending drug.
  • Drugs:
    • Typical antipsychotics (haloperidol, perphenazine, chlorpromazine).
    • Atypical antipsychotics (less common but reported with risperidone, olanzapine).
    • Antiemetics: metoclopramide, prochlorperazine.
    • Promethazine.
  • Manifestations:
    • Torticollis.
    • Oculogyric crisis (sustained upward gaze).
    • Trismus.
    • Laryngospasm (life-threatening).
    • Opisthotonus.
    • Pharyngeal/lingual dystonia.
  • Adolescent males at higher risk than adult women.

TREATMENT

Outline acute management.
Show structured answer
First-line — ANTICHOLINERGIC:
  • BENZTROPINE 1–2 mg IV/IM (adult dose; weight-based in children).
  • OR DIPHENHYDRAMINE 1 mg/kg IV (max 50 mg).
  • Response typically within 5–20 minutes — diagnostic and therapeutic.
  • Continue oral anticholinergic (benztropine 1–2 mg BID or trihexyphenidyl) for 24–48 hours to prevent recurrence.

Other measures:
  • Stop or reduce offending agent.
  • Consider switch to lower-D2 atypical antipsychotic if antipsychotic essential.
  • Reassure — frightening but not life-threatening usually.
  • Document reaction; avoid re-exposure.

DISTINGUISH from:
  • Tetanus (no recent neuroleptic).
  • Strychnine poisoning.
  • Hyperekplexia.
  • Status dystonicus (more sustained, severe).
  • Neuroleptic malignant syndrome (hyperthermia, rigidity, autonomic instability — different syndrome).
SAQ 218 — Drugs to Avoid in Mitochondrial Disease MODERATE
A child with confirmed mitochondrial disease (POLG mutation) requires anaesthesia and adjuvant medications.

DEFINITION

Outline drugs to AVOID and explain why.
Show structured answer
Drugs to AVOID in mitochondrial disease:
DrugReason
VALPROATEMitotoxic; in POLG, can cause FULMINANT HEPATIC FAILURE. Absolute contraindication in POLG.
Aminoglycosides (gentamicin, amikacin, tobramycin)Mitochondrial ribosomal binding → cumulative ototoxicity. Avoid if alternative available.
LinezolidMitochondrial protein synthesis inhibitor → peripheral and optic neuropathy.
StatinsCoQ10 depletion → myopathy.
Propofol prolonged infusionPropofol infusion syndrome (PRIS) — disrupts fatty acid oxidation and respiratory chain.
Some antiretrovirals (NRTIs)Mitochondrial DNA polymerase γ inhibition → mitochondrial dysfunction.
PhenytoinHepatotoxicity in POLG.
MetforminLactic acidosis risk if existing mitochondrial dysfunction.

TREATMENT

Safe alternatives.
Show structured answer
Safe(r) alternatives:
  • For seizures: levetiracetam preferred; lamotrigine, topiramate, clobazam alternatives. AVOID valproate.
  • For infection: β-lactams (ceftriaxone, piperacillin-tazobactam), fluoroquinolones (cautiously); avoid aminoglycosides if alternative exists.
  • For anaesthesia: short infusion of propofol acceptable for procedures (PRIS risk with prolonged); avoid prolonged infusions; volatile anaesthetics generally well tolerated but may cause MH-like reactions in some.
  • For sedation in ICU: midazolam, dexmedetomidine, ketamine alternatives to prolonged propofol.
  • For pain: acetaminophen, opioids OK; avoid NSAIDs if AKI risk.
  • For immunosuppression: standard agents generally OK; monitor for hepatic effects.

General principles:
  • Avoid prolonged fasting (catabolic decompensation).
  • Aggressive treatment of intercurrent illness.
  • Maintain euglycaemia and hydration.
  • Multidisciplinary care.
  • Medical alert bracelet.
SAQ 219 — Carbamazepine Overdose MODERATE
A 14-year-old ingests 50 carbamazepine tablets. Drowsy, ataxic, nystagmus. ECG: QRS 130 ms. Level 35 µg/mL.

DX

Outline mechanism of toxicity.
Show structured answer
Carbamazepine toxicity:
  • Sodium channel blockade — cardiac (QRS widening, similar to TCA) and CNS (drowsiness, ataxia).
  • Anticholinergic effects (mydriasis, urinary retention, tachycardia).
  • CNS depression.
  • Hyponatraemia (SIADH) — common with chronic toxicity.
  • Therapeutic level 4–12 µg/mL; toxic > 20.
  • Has SIGNIFICANT ENTEROHEPATIC CIRCULATION — multiple-dose activated charcoal effective.
  • Often slow absorption from large ingestion → late peak levels (12–18 h).

TREATMENT

Outline management.
Show structured answer
Decontamination:
  • MULTI-DOSE activated charcoal — particularly effective for carbamazepine due to enterohepatic recirculation; reduces half-life significantly.
  • Continue MDAC until level < 20 µg/mL or clinical improvement.
  • Requires intact/protected airway.

Acid-base and cardiac:
  • Sodium bicarbonate for QRS widening — similar to TCA management; target QRS < 100, pH 7.45–7.55.
  • Cardiac monitoring.

Seizures: benzodiazepines (NOT phenytoin — worsens sodium channel toxicity).

Severe toxicity:
  • HEMODIALYSIS or hemoperfusion for:
    • Level > 40 µg/mL with severe toxicity.
    • Refractory cardiovascular instability.
    • Status epilepticus.
    • Renal/hepatic failure.
  • Intubation if airway concerns.
  • IV lipid emulsion for severe cardiac instability (lipid sink hypothesis).

Long-term:
  • Psychiatric evaluation for intentional overdose.
  • Alternative AED if epilepsy management ongoing — avoid sodium channel blockers (use levetiracetam, valproate, etc., considering individual patient).
  • Family medication safety education.
SAQ 220 — Acute Intermittent Porphyria HARD
A 16-year-old presents with severe abdominal pain, weakness, hyponatraemia, autonomic instability, dark urine.

DX

Outline diagnosis.
Show structured answer
Acute intermittent porphyria (AIP):
  • Autosomal dominant hydroxymethylbilane synthase (HMBS / porphobilinogen deaminase) deficiency.
  • Third enzyme in haem biosynthesis.
  • Accumulation of δ-aminolevulinic acid (ALA) and porphobilinogen (PBG) → neurotoxic, drives attacks.
  • Classic features:
    • Severe abdominal pain (often without peritonitis signs).
    • Autonomic dysfunction (labile BP, tachycardia, sweating).
    • Peripheral motor neuropathy (often proximal weakness, can progress to respiratory failure).
    • Neuropsychiatric features (anxiety, depression, psychosis, seizures).
    • Hyponatraemia (SIADH).
    • Dark/reddish urine on standing (PBG oxidises to porphobilin).
  • Triggers: CYP-inducing drugs (anticonvulsants — phenytoin, phenobarbital, carbamazepine; sulfonamides; OCPs), fasting, hormonal changes, stress, alcohol, infection.
  • Diagnosis: elevated urinary ALA and PBG during attack.

TREATMENT

Outline acute management.
Show structured answer
Acute attack:
  1. Remove triggers — stop offending drugs (check porphyria drug-safety list).
  2. HEMIN (Panhematin/heme arginate) IV 3–4 mg/kg/day × 4 days — exogenous haem feedback-suppresses ALA synthase → reduces ALA/PBG production.
  3. HIGH-CARBOHYDRATE LOADING with IV dextrose (D10) 300–500 g/day — also suppresses ALA synthase.
  4. Analgesia: OPIOIDS are safe (no porphyrinogenicity); AVOID NSAIDs if AKI risk.
  5. Antiemetics: ondansetron safe; AVOID metoclopramide.
  6. Benzodiazepines safe (lorazepam, midazolam).
  7. Antihypertensives: β-blockers (propranolol) safe; AVOID dihydropyridine CCBs (nifedipine).
  8. Seizures: gabapentin and levetiracetam considered safe; AVOID phenytoin, phenobarbital, carbamazepine.
  9. Correct hyponatraemia carefully (slow correction).

Long-term prevention:
  • GIVOSIRAN (Givlaari) — siRNA targeting ALAS1, FDA-approved 2019; reduces recurrent attacks.
  • Trigger avoidance.
  • Dietary counselling (avoid prolonged fasting).
  • Monthly preventive haem infusions in severe cases.
  • Family genetic counselling (AD with variable penetrance).
SAQ 221 — Pediatric ICP Waveform Interpretation HARD
An EVD shows ICP waveform with P2 > P1.

DEFINITION

Outline normal ICP waveform components.
Show structured answer
Normal ICP waveform — three peaks:
  • P1 (percussion wave): arterial pulsation transmitted through choroid plexus / arterial inflow. Largest in compliant brain.
  • P2 (tidal wave): reflects brain compliance — increases as compliance decreases.
  • P3 (dicrotic wave): aortic valve closure / venous outflow.

Normal: P1 > P2 > P3 (descending stairsteps).

P2 > P1 indicates REDUCED compensatory reserve and impending decompensation — the cranial vault has reached its limits of compensation; small further volume increase will cause large ICP rise.

PHYSIOLOGY

Outline Lundberg waves and significance.
Show structured answer
Lundberg waves (slow waves of ICP, originally described 1960):
  • A waves (plateau waves): sustained 50–100 mm Hg elevations lasting 5–20 minutes; pathological; indicate severely reduced compensatory reserve and impending herniation; warrant urgent intervention.
  • B waves: rhythmic oscillations 0.5–2 per minute, 5–50 mm Hg amplitude; reflect changes in vasomotor tone; may be present in pathological and physiological states.
  • C waves: slow oscillations 4–8 per minute; related to respiratory and arterial pressure variation; usually physiological.

TREATMENT

Management of P2 > P1 waveform.
Show structured answer
  • P2 > P1 = warning sign of impending decompensation.
  • Escalate ICP management:
    • Optimise Tier 1: sedation depth, normocapnia, normothermia, hyperosmolar therapy, CSF drainage.
    • Consider Tier 2 / 3 escalation: pentobarbital, decompressive craniectomy, deep cooling.
    • Multimodal monitoring (PbtO₂, microdialysis) to guide individualised therapy.
  • Investigate for new structural lesion (repeat imaging).
  • Treat seizures.
  • Continuous re-evaluation; do not wait for ICP to rise into critical range.
  • Even when ICP appears 'normal', a P2-dominant waveform indicates the system is near the steep part of the volume-pressure curve.
SAQ 222 — Glutaric Aciduria Type 1 MODERATE
A 6-month-old with macrocephaly develops encephalopathy and dystonia after febrile illness.

DX

Diagnostic features and MRI hallmark.
Show structured answer
  • Autosomal recessive deficiency of glutaryl-CoA dehydrogenase.
  • Accumulation of glutaric and 3-hydroxyglutaric acids — neurotoxic to striatum.
  • Phenotype: macrocephaly, frontotemporal atrophy with widened opercular cisterns ('bat-wing' appearance), subdural collections (mimics AHT), acute striatal injury during catabolic crises.
  • Diagnosis: urinary glutaric and 3-hydroxyglutaric acids; plasma C5DC acylcarnitine elevated.
  • Newborn screening picks up most cases.

TREATMENT

Emergency and chronic management.
Show structured answer
Acute crisis:
  • Stop protein.
  • D10 + insulin to suppress catabolism.
  • L-carnitine IV 100 mg/kg/day.
  • Treat infection / fever aggressively.
  • Hemodialysis if severe.

Chronic:
  • Lysine-restricted diet.
  • L-carnitine supplementation.
  • Riboflavin (cofactor).
  • Emergency action plan; IV glucose during illness.
  • Pre-symptomatic dietary management dramatically improves outcomes.
SAQ 223 — Drowning — Outcome Predictors and TTM MODERATE
A 5-year-old after submersion incident with downtime 8 minutes.

CRITERIA

Outline outcome predictors.
Show structured answer
Submersion duration is the strongest predictor:
  • < 5 min: good outcomes likely.
  • 5–10 min: variable.
  • > 25 min: very poor (rare survival without severe disability).

Other factors:
  • Water temperature (cold < 6 °C is protective via diving reflex + metabolic suppression).
  • Time to first CPR.
  • Time to ROSC (> 25 min predicts poor outcome).
  • Initial pH and lactate.
  • Pupillary reactivity at presentation.

TREATMENT

Outline post-arrest TTM per THAPCA.
Show structured answer
THAPCA-OH and THAPCA-IH (NEJM 2015, 2017):
  • 33 °C vs 36.8 °C × 48 h, then strict normothermia × 5 d.
  • No significant difference in good-outcome survival.
  • Both arms emphasised fever avoidance — likely the key.

Current practice:
  • Choose 33 °C OR 36.8 °C × 48 h; strict normothermia thereafter.
  • Avoid hyperthermia > 37.5 °C.
  • cEEG to detect seizures.
  • Optimise CPP; avoid hypoxia/hypotension.
  • Glucose 4–10 mmol/L.
  • Multimodal neuroprognostication ≥ 72 h off sedation.
SAQ 224 — Heat Stroke MODERATE
An adolescent collapses during a midday distance run. T 41.5 °C, severe rhabdomyolysis, AKI, GCS 8.

DX

Outline diagnosis.
Show structured answer
Exertional heat stroke:
  • Core T > 40 °C with CNS dysfunction in the context of strenuous physical exertion.
  • Distinguish from classic heat stroke (passive, elderly).
  • Exertional has higher lactate, more rhabdomyolysis, more DIC risk.
  • Multi-organ injury: cerebral oedema, AKI, hepatic failure, cardiac, GI ischaemia, coagulopathy.

TREATMENT

Outline cooling strategy.
Show structured answer
Cold water immersion (CWI) — gold standard for exertional heat stroke:
  • Target core T 38.5–39 °C within 30 min.
  • Stop cooling at 38–38.5 °C to avoid overshoot.
  • 'Cool first, transport second' if available on-site.

Alternatives if CWI unavailable:
  • Ice packs to neck, axillae, groin.
  • Cool mist + fans (evaporative).
  • Cold IV fluids.

Antipyretics (paracetamol/NSAIDs) are NOT effective — and may worsen hepatic/renal dysfunction.

Supportive: aggressive fluid resuscitation, rhabdomyolysis management (urinary alkalinisation), DIC management, ICU.
SAQ 225 — Tetanus HARD
An unvaccinated 3-year-old presents with trismus, opisthotonus, autonomic storms 8 days after a puncture wound.

DX

Outline pathophysiology.
Show structured answer
  • Clostridium tetani spores germinate in anaerobic wound environment.
  • Tetanospasmin toxin → retrograde axonal transport to CNS → cleaves synaptobrevin (VAMP) at presynaptic inhibitory terminals.
  • Blocks release of GABA and glycine from inhibitory interneurons in spinal cord and brainstem.
  • Disinhibition of motor neurons → uncontrolled motor firing, sympathetic over-activity.

TREATMENT

Outline multi-pronged management.
Show structured answer
  1. NEUTRALISE circulating toxin: human tetanus immune globulin (HTIG) 3000–6000 IU IM. Does NOT reverse bound toxin.
  2. ERADICATE organism: surgical wound debridement; metronidazole IV (preferred over penicillin — fewer GABA antagonist effects).
  3. ACTIVE IMMUNISATION: tetanus toxoid (the disease itself does not produce immunity).
  4. CONTROL SPASMS: heavy sedation (benzodiazepines, propofol); magnesium sulfate infusion; intrathecal baclofen in severe.
  5. MANAGE AUTONOMIC STORM: magnesium first-line; α + β blockers (labetalol); clonidine. AVOID isolated β-blockade.
  6. Mechanical ventilation; NMB if needed; ICU for weeks.

Mortality 10–50% even with optimal care.
SAQ 226 — Snake Envenomation — Neurotoxic Snakes MODERATE
An adolescent in Australia presents with ptosis, dysarthria, descending weakness following a snake bite.

DX

Outline neurotoxic snakes and their toxin mechanisms.
Show structured answer
Neurotoxic snake families:
  • Elapidae (cobras, kraits, mambas, coral snakes, sea snakes, taipans, brown snakes).
  • Some Viperidae (Mojave rattlesnake — neurotoxic component).

Toxin mechanisms:
  • α-neurotoxins (postsynaptic): bind nAChR at NMJ, like curare-mimic; cobras, kraits.
  • β-neurotoxins (presynaptic): inhibit ACh release at NMJ; some krait venoms; longer-acting, irreversible without antivenom.
  • Both manifest as descending paralysis: ptosis (CN III earliest), bulbar dysfunction, respiratory failure.
  • Often very rapid (minutes-hours).

TREATMENT

Outline management.
Show structured answer
  • Immobilise limb at heart level; AVOID tourniquet (causes ischaemic injury, ineffective for systemic absorption).
  • DO NOT cut, suck, apply ice.
  • Pressure-immobilisation bandage (Australian practice for elapid bites — slows lymphatic spread).
  • Identify the snake species if possible (without endangering responders) — informs antivenom choice.
  • SPECIFIC ANTIVENOM as early as possible:
    • Australia: tiger snake, brown snake, taipan, mulga, death adder antivenoms — polyvalent available.
    • US: coral snake antivenom; CroFab/Anavip for crotalids.
    • Watch for serum sickness, anaphylaxis (premedication with adrenaline, antihistamines, steroids in some).
  • Airway protection if respiratory failure.
  • Mechanical ventilation supportive (paralysis often reversible with antivenom in postsynaptic; irreversible until receptor regeneration in presynaptic).
  • Monitor for delayed neuromuscular complications.
  • ICU monitoring.
  • Public health: report; education for community.
SAQ 227 — Tick Paralysis MODERATE
A child returns from a camping trip with ascending paralysis. CSF normal. Tick found on scalp.

DX

Outline diagnosis and pathology.
Show structured answer
  • Toxin (holocyclotoxin or related) secreted by gravid female tick during prolonged attachment (typically > 4–7 days).
  • Toxin blocks presynaptic ACh release at NMJ.
  • Classic ascending flaccid paralysis (mimics GBS) but NO sensory loss (vs GBS).
  • Often cranial nerve involvement.
  • Causative ticks: Dermacentor variabilis (US East), D. andersoni (US Northwest), Ixodes holocyclus (Australia — more severe).
  • Reversal within 24 hours of tick removal in most cases (Australian version may continue worsening for 24–48 h after removal).

TREATMENT

Outline management.
Show structured answer
  1. THOROUGHLY SEARCH for the tick — scalp, behind ears, neck, axillae, groin, between toes; often more than one.
  2. Remove tick(s) COMPLETELY — fine forceps, grasp close to skin, pull straight out.
  3. Supportive care during recovery: airway support if respiratory weakness; ventilation if needed.
  4. Australian tick paralysis: canine-derived antitoxin available; not US.
  5. Look for co-infections in endemic regions: RMSF (doxycycline empirically), Lyme, ehrlichiosis, babesiosis.
  6. Tick prevention education for family.

Pearl: TICK PARALYSIS = GBS until you find the tick. Look thoroughly — examine all skin folds and the scalp.
SAQ 228 — Black Widow (Latrodectus) Envenomation MODERATE
An adolescent presents with severe abdominal pain, rigidity, autonomic instability, and pinpoint puncture marks.

DX

Outline diagnosis and mechanism.
Show structured answer
Black widow envenomation (Latrodectus mactans and species):
  • α-latrotoxin binds presynaptic neurexin/latrophilin → triggers massive ACh and norepinephrine release at NMJ and autonomic terminals.
  • Clinical features:
    • Severe muscle cramping and rigidity (often abdominal — mimics acute abdomen).
    • Autonomic over-activity: hypertension, tachycardia, profuse sweating, hypersalivation.
    • Pain often severe; lasts hours-days.
    • Local skin lesion may be minimal or invisible.

TREATMENT

Outline management.
Show structured answer
  • OPIOIDS for severe pain (often severe).
  • Benzodiazepines for muscle spasm and anxiety.
  • Calcium gluconate NOT effective (historical use).
  • Equine antivenom for severe cases (high anaphylaxis risk; ICU setting only with adrenaline ready).
  • Supportive care; hypertension management.
  • Most cases self-limited; few require ICU.
  • Pediatric patients tend to have more severe envenomation.
SAQ 229 — Mushroom Poisoning — Amanita phalloides HARD
A child ingested wild mushrooms 12 hours ago. Now severe GI symptoms then rising AST/ALT and INR.

DX

Diagnosis and mechanism.
Show structured answer
Amatoxin poisoning (Amanita phalloides, A. virosa, Lepiota, Galerina):
  • Amatoxin (α-amanitin) inhibits RNA polymerase II → blocks transcription → cell death in liver and kidney.
  • Classic biphasic / triphasic course:
    • Phase 1 (6–24 h): GI symptoms (severe vomiting, diarrhoea, abdominal pain).
    • Phase 2 ('false recovery', 24–48 h): symptoms improve.
    • Phase 3 (48–72 h+): hepatic and renal failure; cerebral oedema; mortality.
  • Mortality 20–40% even with treatment.

TREATMENT

Outline management.
Show structured answer
  • Activated charcoal if early (within 1–2 h).
  • Multi-dose activated charcoal (some advocate; controversial).
  • IV fluids; correct electrolytes.
  • SILIBININ (IV silymarin from milk thistle) — inhibits hepatic uptake of amatoxin; FDA orphan drug.
  • N-acetylcysteine — supportive for hepatic injury.
  • Penicillin G high dose (historical) — competes with amatoxin for hepatic uptake; benefit unclear.
  • Supportive: monitor LFTs, INR, ammonia; treat cerebral oedema; haemodialysis for renal failure.
  • LIVER TRANSPLANT evaluation if fulminant hepatic failure (King's College criteria adapted for paediatrics).
SAQ 230 — Organophosphate Poisoning HARD
A child develops SLUDGE symptoms (salivation, lacrimation, urination, defecation, GI distress, emesis), bronchorrhoea, bradycardia.

DX

Outline mechanism and clinical features.
Show structured answer
Organophosphate / carbamate poisoning:
  • Inhibits acetylcholinesterase → accumulation of ACh at all cholinergic synapses.
  • Three components:
    • Muscarinic: SLUDGE + bronchorrhoea, bradycardia, miosis.
    • Nicotinic: fasciculations, muscle weakness, eventual paralysis (NMJ).
    • Central: agitation, seizures, coma.
  • Organophosphates: 'aging' occurs — covalent bond becomes irreversible if pralidoxime not given early (within hours).
  • Carbamates: reversible inhibition; no aging.

TREATMENT

Outline management.
Show structured answer
  1. DECONTAMINATION: remove clothing, wash skin (protect responders).
  2. HIGH-DOSE ATROPINE: 1–5 mg IV/IM, REPEAT every 5 min, DOUBLING the dose if needed, until secretions dry (often very high cumulative doses).
  3. PRALIDOXIME (2-PAM): 25–50 mg/kg IV slow over 30 min, then infusion 10–20 mg/kg/h. Reactivates enzyme before aging completes. For organophosphates, NOT for carbamates routinely.
  4. BENZODIAZEPINES for seizures.
  5. Airway protection — early intubation. Avoid succinylcholine (prolonged paralysis due to cholinesterase inhibition).
  6. Supportive ICU care.
  7. Monitor RBC cholinesterase activity for severity and response.
  8. Public health investigation (occupational, agricultural, intentional, mass casualty).
SAQ 231 — Brain Abscess — Empiric Antibiotics MODERATE
A child with cyanotic CHD presents with fever, focal seizures, and a ring-enhancing parenchymal lesion.

DX

Outline likely organisms and predisposing factors.
Show structured answer
Predisposing factors and organisms:
  • Cyanotic CHD (R-to-L shunt): bypasses pulmonary filter — Streptococci (viridans, milleri/anginosus), anaerobes.
  • Sinusitis (frontal): Streptococci, anaerobes; frontal lobe location.
  • Otitis/mastoiditis: Streptococci, Pseudomonas, anaerobes; temporal lobe / cerebellum.
  • Trauma/post-neurosurgery: Staph aureus, Gram-negatives.
  • Immunocompromised: fungi (Aspergillus), Nocardia, Toxoplasma, mycobacteria.
  • Endocarditis: Staph aureus, Streptococci.

Common in pediatric brain abscess overall: Streptococcus viridans/milleri (most common), Staph aureus, anaerobes (Bacteroides, Fusobacterium), Enterobacteriaceae; polymicrobial in > 30%.

TREATMENT

Outline antibiotic regimen and surgical indications.
Show structured answer
Empiric antibiotics:
  • Ceftriaxone + metronidazole — standard combination.
  • Add vancomycin if MRSA risk (post-neurosurgery, suspected staph).
  • Broaden Gram-negative coverage (cefepime or meropenem) if immunocompromised, post-neurosurgery, hospital-acquired.
  • Antifungal (voriconazole, amphotericin B) if immunocompromised.
  • Anti-toxoplasmosis (pyrimethamine + sulfadiazine + leucovorin) if HIV/AIDS.
  • Duration: 6–8 weeks IV total (transition to oral when responding).

Surgical drainage indications:
  • Abscess > 2.5 cm.
  • Mass effect with midline shift.
  • Posterior fossa location.
  • Multiloculated abscess.
  • Gas-containing abscess.
  • Failure to respond to antibiotics.
  • Immunocompromised host (need tissue diagnosis).
  • Neurological deterioration.

Imaging follow-up: 2 weeks, then monthly, then 3-monthly for response.
SAQ 232 — Neuroprotection in Pediatric Cardiac Surgery MODERATE
A 10-year-old undergoes complex cardiac repair with deep hypothermic circulatory arrest.

DEFINITION

Outline neuroprotective strategies during CPB.
Show structured answer
Intraoperative neuroprotection:
  • DEEP HYPOTHERMIA (18–22 °C) for circulatory arrest — reduces CMRO₂ exponentially.
  • Antegrade or retrograde cerebral perfusion when possible to maintain some cerebral blood flow during deep cooling.
  • α-stat or pH-stat acid-base management — debate over which is optimal in pediatric heart surgery (pH-stat may improve cerebral blood flow at deep cooling).
  • Hematocrit optimisation during CPB (target 24–28% in deep hypothermia).
  • Glucose control — avoid hyperglycaemia (worsens neuro outcome).
  • Cerebral oximetry (NIRS) monitoring — detect rSO₂ desaturation events.
  • EEG monitoring — detect electrographic seizures, monitor depth of anaesthesia.
  • Minimise circulatory arrest time when feasible (limit < 40–45 min).
  • Slow gradual cooling and rewarming.
  • Optimise oxygen delivery, avoid air emboli.

NEXT STEP

Outline post-operative seizure management.
Show structured answer
Post-op seizures common (5–20%):
  • cEEG monitoring for at least 48–72 h post-op — many are subclinical.
  • Load levetiracetam.
  • Address electrolytes (Na, Ca, Mg, glucose).
  • Investigate cause: stroke (embolic, watershed), haemorrhage, hyperventilation/hyperoxia-induced, metabolic.
  • MRI when stable to assess for infarct or haemorrhage.
  • Avoid valproate in mitochondrial-suspected patients.
  • Most resolve; some develop persistent epilepsy requiring chronic AED.
  • Long-term neurocognitive follow-up.
SAQ 233 — Hyperventilation Cautions in TBI MODERATE
A child with severe TBI has ICP 30 and the team considers hyperventilation.

PHYSIOLOGY

Outline the rationale and risks of hyperventilation.
Show structured answer
Mechanism:
  • Hyperventilation → low PaCO₂ → cerebral vasoconstriction → reduced cerebral blood volume → reduced ICP.
  • Effect rapid (minutes) but transient (hours) — physiological adaptation occurs.

Risks:
  • Cerebral vasoconstriction reduces flow → ISCHAEMIA, especially when CBF already compromised.
  • Increased mortality with prophylactic hyperventilation in adult TBI trials.
  • Worsens microcirculatory dysfunction.
  • Children may be particularly vulnerable.

TREATMENT

When to use and when to avoid.
Show structured answer
AVOID:
  • Prophylactic hyperventilation (PaCO₂ < 35) in first 24 h after severe TBI — Tier 1 recommendation.
  • Avoid PaCO₂ < 30 in pediatric TBI.
  • Avoid in HIE without monitoring.

USE judiciously (Tier 2):
  • Moderate hyperventilation (PaCO₂ 30–35) for refractory ICP elevations.
  • As temporary bridge while preparing definitive intervention (decompressive craniectomy, hyperosmolar therapy).
  • For impending herniation (Cushing's triad) — brief, urgent hyperventilation as bridge to surgery.

Monitor during hyperventilation:
  • PbtO₂ if available — drops in PbtO₂ indicate ischaemia.
  • Jugular bulb oxygen saturation.
  • NIRS.
  • Multimodal monitoring helps avoid harm.
SAQ 234 — Pediatric SAH — BP and Rebleeding HARD
A 15-year-old with aneurysmal SAH; aneurysm not yet secured.

TREATMENT

Outline BP management pre-securing.
Show structured answer
Pre-securing BP target:
  • SBP < 140–160 mm Hg until aneurysm secured.
  • Avoid extreme hypotension (cerebral hypoperfusion).
  • Use titratable IV agents:
    • Nicardipine — preferred (titratable, less reflex tachycardia).
    • Labetalol.
    • Esmolol.
  • AVOID sudden BP drops.

Other priorities pre-securing:
  • Pain control — opioids; reduces sympathetic surge.
  • Antiemetics for nausea.
  • Stool softeners (avoid Valsalva).
  • Quiet environment.
  • Bed rest.
  • Imaging: CT, CTA, then catheter angiography for definitive characterisation.
  • Plan aneurysm securing within 24–48 h (clipping or coiling depending on size, location, age, anatomy).

NEXT STEP

Outline post-securing management.
Show structured answer
After aneurysm secured:
  • BP can be allowed higher to support CPP and prevent vasospasm.
  • Maintain euvolaemia.
  • Nimodipine 60 mg PO q4h × 21 days (neuroprotective; not for vasospasm prevention per se).
  • Daily TCDs through day 14 to monitor for vasospasm.
  • Manage hydrocephalus (EVD).
  • Monitor for delayed cerebral ischaemia.
  • Seizure prophylaxis controversial — often given briefly.
  • Surveillance for delayed complications: hyponatraemia (CSW, SIADH), cardiac stunning, neurogenic pulmonary oedema.
SAQ 235 — Pediatric AVM — Spetzler-Martin Grading HARD
A 13-year-old presents with intracerebral haemorrhage from a frontal AVM.

CRITERIA

Outline Spetzler-Martin grading.
Show structured answer
Spetzler-Martin grade (sum of three components):
  • Size: small < 3 cm (1 pt); medium 3–6 cm (2 pts); large > 6 cm (3 pts).
  • Eloquence of adjacent brain: non-eloquent (0 pts); eloquent (1 pt — sensorimotor, language, visual, hypothalamus, thalamus, brainstem, cerebellar nuclei, deep cerebellar pathways).
  • Pattern of venous drainage: superficial only (0 pts); deep venous drainage (1 pt).

Total 1–5 (Spetzler-Martin grade); grade 6 means inoperable.

Used to estimate surgical risk; pediatric outcomes generally better than adult for same grade.

TREATMENT

Outline treatment options.
Show structured answer
Three main modalities (often combined):
  • Microsurgical resection — for accessible Spetzler-Martin grade I–III; best for cure.
  • Endovascular embolisation — often adjunctive to reduce nidus before surgery/radiation; rarely curative alone for large AVMs.
  • Stereotactic radiosurgery (gamma knife) — for deep, eloquent locations or small inaccessible AVMs; 2–3 year latency to obliteration (so still bleed risk during latency).

Acute haemorrhage management:
  • Stabilisation (airway, ICP, BP).
  • Prevent rebleeding (avoid hypertension).
  • Nimodipine for SAH-associated vasospasm.
  • Seizure prophylaxis (levetiracetam).
  • Aneurysm component (often associated with AVM) may need urgent treatment.
  • Definitive AVM treatment planned after stabilisation; multidisciplinary team.

Outcome:
  • Annual rebleeding risk after first haemorrhage ~ 5–7%/year.
  • Pediatric AVM management is balance of immediate haemorrhage risk vs procedural risk; lifelong follow-up needed.
SAQ 236 — Vagal Nerve Stimulator MODERATE
A child with drug-resistant epilepsy is considered for VNS.

DEFINITION

Outline mechanism and indications.
Show structured answer
Vagal nerve stimulator (VNS):
  • Implanted device delivering programmed intermittent electrical stimulation to left vagus nerve.
  • Mechanism uncertain — proposed effects on locus coeruleus, raphe nuclei, thalamocortical network, central GABA and norepinephrine systems.
  • NOT curative — typically achieves 30–50% seizure reduction in ~ 50% of patients; full seizure freedom uncommon (10%).
  • Indications:
    • FDA-approved for refractory focal epilepsy ≥ 4 years.
    • Off-label use for generalised epilepsy, LGS, depression, Dravet.

TREATMENT

Outline practical use and side effects.
Show structured answer
Programming:
  • Continuous cyclic stimulation (e.g., 30 sec on, 5 min off).
  • Magnet-activated extra stimulation by patient at seizure onset (abortive).
  • Gradual up-titration of intensity over weeks.

Side effects:
  • Voice changes / hoarseness (stimulation of recurrent laryngeal nerve).
  • Cough.
  • Throat discomfort.
  • Dyspnoea (especially during stimulation).
  • Sleep apnoea (worsening of OSA — need pre-implant assessment).
  • Bradycardia / asystole rare.

Other neurostimulation alternatives:
  • Responsive neurostimulation (RNS): closed-loop, focal — for focal-onset epilepsy with identified zone.
  • Deep brain stimulation (DBS) of anterior nucleus of thalamus.
  • Trigeminal nerve stimulation (external).
SAQ 237 — Hemispherectomy HARD
A child with refractory unilateral hemispheric epilepsy from Rasmussen encephalitis is considered for hemispherectomy.

DEFINITION

Outline indications and techniques.
Show structured answer
Indications for hemispherectomy:
  • Refractory unilateral hemispheric epilepsy from:
    • Rasmussen encephalitis.
    • Sturge-Weber syndrome with refractory seizures.
    • Hemimegalencephaly.
    • Perinatal arterial stroke (porencephalic cyst).
    • Hemispheric malformations of cortical development.
  • Pre-requirements:
    • Established existing hemiparesis (or developing — will not worsen significantly).
    • Language transferred to other hemisphere (in dominant hemisphere case).
    • Pre-surgical evaluation: video-EEG, MRI, fMRI, neuropsychology, Wada test.

Techniques:
  • Anatomical hemispherectomy — removal of entire hemisphere. Higher complication rates (hydrocephalus, hemosiderosis).
  • Functional hemispherectomy / hemispherotomy — disconnection of the hemisphere while preserving most tissue. Reduced complications, similar seizure outcomes.

TREATMENT

Outline outcomes and post-operative considerations.
Show structured answer
Outcomes:
  • Seizure-freedom rates 60–80% (Engel I), depending on aetiology.
  • Cognitive outcomes — many children improve cognitively after surgery (seizure burden was harming function).
  • Language can transfer (especially in young children, < 6–8 years) — Wernicke and Broca can reorganise.
  • Hemiparesis usually preserved at pre-op level; may improve with rehab.
  • Visual: contralateral homonymous hemianopia post-op.

Post-operative complications:
  • Hydrocephalus (especially anatomical hemispherectomy) — may need VP shunt.
  • Aseptic meningitis.
  • Superficial hemosiderosis (more with anatomical).
  • Infection.

Long-term:
  • Continued rehabilitation.
  • Educational support.
  • AED weaning if seizure-free.
  • Periodic neuropsychology.
SAQ 238 — Hereditary Hyperekplexia MODERATE
A neonate has exaggerated startle response and sustained stiffening on noise or touch, with apnoea.

DX

Outline diagnosis and genetic basis.
Show structured answer
Hereditary hyperekplexia (startle disease, stiff-baby syndrome):
  • Glycine receptor or glycine transporter mutations:
    • GLRA1 (α1 subunit, AD most common).
    • GLRB (β subunit, AR).
    • SLC6A5 (glycine transporter 2, AR).
  • Reduced glycinergic inhibition in brainstem and spinal cord → motor hyperexcitability.
  • Features:
    • Exaggerated startle response to unexpected stimuli (sound, touch).
    • Sustained tonic stiffening with apnoea — life-threatening.
    • Hypertonia at rest in neonatal period.
    • Nose-tap test: forehead percussion elicits prominent stiffening.
  • EEG normal.
  • Differential: epileptic startles (EEG correlate, postictal), drug withdrawal, Sandifer, neonatal jitteriness.

TREATMENT

Outline therapy.
Show structured answer
  • CLONAZEPAM — drug of choice; potentiates GABA-A inhibition (counter-balances glycinergic deficit).
  • Other benzodiazepines (diazepam, midazolam) also helpful.
  • Family education on VIGEVANO MANOEUVRE — forced flexion of head and limbs toward trunk aborts sustained stiffening apnoea.
  • Trigger avoidance in neonates (sudden noise, temperature change).
  • Most patients improve dramatically with treatment; many wean off medication later in childhood (residual exaggerated startle into adulthood).
  • Genetic counselling: depends on specific mutation/inheritance.
  • Distinguish from acquired anti-glycine receptor antibody syndrome (PERM — progressive encephalomyelitis with rigidity and myoclonus) in older patients.
SAQ 239 — AADC Deficiency HARD
A 14-month-old has progressive dystonia, oculogyric crises, autonomic dysfunction, and severe developmental delay.

DX

Outline diagnosis.
Show structured answer
Aromatic L-amino acid decarboxylase (AADC) deficiency:
  • Autosomal recessive DDC gene mutations.
  • AADC needed to synthesise:
    • Dopamine (from L-DOPA).
    • Serotonin (from 5-HTP).
    • Norepinephrine and epinephrine.
  • Severe combined monoamine neurotransmitter deficiency → profound motor + autonomic dysfunction.
  • Classic features:
    • Severe hypotonia from infancy.
    • Dystonia, developmental delay.
    • OCULOGYRIC CRISES — sustained upward eye deviation lasting hours, distressing.
    • Autonomic dysfunction (sweating, ptosis, miosis, temperature instability).
    • Sleep and feeding disturbances.
  • CSF NEUROTRANSMITTER METABOLITES are diagnostic:
    • Low HVA (dopamine metabolite).
    • Low 5-HIAA (serotonin metabolite).
    • Low/undetectable 3-O-methyldopa.
    • L-DOPA, 5-HTP elevated (substrates accumulate).

TREATMENT

Outline therapy.
Show structured answer
Symptomatic pharmacotherapy:
  • DOPAMINE AGONISTS (pramipexole, rotigotine, ropinirole).
  • VITAMIN B6 (pyridoxal-5-phosphate) — AADC cofactor; modest benefit.
  • MAO INHIBITORS (selegiline) — slow degradation of any dopamine.
  • Trihexyphenidyl for dystonia.
  • Anticholinergics for sweating.

NOT GIVEN: L-DOPA — patient cannot convert it to dopamine (AADC is the missing enzyme).

GENE THERAPY:
  • ELADOCAGENE EXUPARVOVEC (Upstaza) — AAV2-hAADC gene therapy via bilateral putaminal injection.
  • EMA approved 2022, FDA approved 2024.
  • Dramatic improvement in motor function and quality of life.

OCULOGYRIC CRISIS management: benzodiazepines (clonazepam), antihistamines, induction of sleep.

Multidisciplinary care. Family genetic counselling (AR, 25% recurrence).
SAQ 240 — Brain Tumour — Posterior Fossa Differential MODERATE
A 6-year-old has 6 weeks of morning headache, vomiting on rising, ataxia. MRI: large midline cerebellar mass.

DEFINITION

Outline pediatric posterior fossa tumour differential.
Show structured answer
Pediatric posterior fossa tumours (in approximate frequency):
  • Cerebellar pilocytic astrocytoma (WHO grade I) — most common; typically cystic with enhancing mural nodule; BRAF fusion (KIAA1549-BRAF) most common driver; gross-total resection often curative.
  • Medulloblastoma — most common malignant pediatric brain tumour; midline vermian floor of 4th ventricle; solid enhancing mass with restricted diffusion (highly cellular); molecular subgroups: WNT (best), SHH, Group 3 (worst), Group 4.
  • Ependymoma — arises from ependymal lining; often projects through foramina (Magendie, Luschka); calcifications common; PF-A and PF-B molecular subgroups (prognostic).
  • Atypical teratoid/rhabdoid tumour (ATRT) — younger children (< 3); SMARCB1/INI1 loss; aggressive.
  • Brainstem glioma — diffuse intrinsic pontine glioma (DIPG) — diffuse expansion of pons; H3 K27M mutation defines diffuse midline glioma; poor prognosis (median survival ~ 9–12 months).

NEXT STEP

Outline initial management of suspected posterior fossa tumour.
Show structured answer
  1. Stabilise — protect airway if depressed LOC.
  2. Manage raised ICP: HOB up, head neutral, sedation as needed, dexamethasone (4 mg/kg/day × 2 days) for oedema.
  3. AVOID lumbar puncture (herniation risk with obstructive hydrocephalus).
  4. Emergent EVD or endoscopic 3rd ventriculostomy for hydrocephalus.
  5. Pre-op spinal MRI to stage (especially for medulloblastoma, ependymoma which seed CSF).
  6. Maximal safe surgical resection — outcomes depend on extent of resection.
  7. Tissue diagnosis with molecular subtyping.
  8. Adjuvant therapy per histology / molecular subgroup:
    • Pilocytic astrocytoma: typically surgery alone if gross-total.
    • Medulloblastoma: craniospinal radiation + chemotherapy (in children > 3 years; chemotherapy alone in younger).
    • Ependymoma: surgery + focal radiation; chemotherapy in young children.
    • ATRT: aggressive multimodal therapy.
    • DIPG: focal radiation (chemotherapy minimal benefit); clinical trials with targeted agents.

Multidisciplinary: neurosurgery, neuro-oncology, neurology, rehab, neuropsychology, family support.
SAQ 241 — Posterior Fossa Syndrome / Cerebellar Mutism MODERATE
A 7-year-old POD2 from medulloblastoma resection becomes mute, dysphagic, hypotonic, emotionally labile.

DX

Outline diagnosis.
Show structured answer
Posterior fossa syndrome (cerebellar mutism syndrome, CMS):
  • Occurs after posterior fossa surgery in 25% of pediatric medulloblastoma cases.
  • Delayed onset typically 24–48 hours post-op (after initial post-anaesthetic recovery may seem normal).
  • Features:
    • Mutism (initially complete, then dysarthria as it improves).
    • Truncal ataxia, hypotonia.
    • Emotional lability (sudden crying, irritability).
    • Apraxia, dysphagia.
    • Cranial nerve dysfunction.
    • Often more severe than expected from surgical extent.
  • Recovery: speech returns over weeks to months but often with residual cerebellar cognitive-affective syndrome (CCAS) — executive dysfunction, behavioural changes.
  • Mechanism: damage / disruption of dentato-thalamo-cortical pathways.

TREATMENT

Outline management.
Show structured answer
  • Supportive care:
    • Nutrition (NG/PEG until swallow returns).
    • Communication aids.
    • Family education and reassurance.
  • Early speech and language therapy.
  • Physiotherapy and occupational therapy.
  • Neuropsychology — ongoing throughout recovery.
  • School re-entry planning.
  • No specific pharmacological therapy proven; some report bromocriptine, fluoxetine anecdotally.
  • Recovery is generally protracted (weeks-months); most children regain functional communication.
  • Long-term: residual cerebellar cognitive-affective syndrome common; ongoing support needed.
  • Pre-operative counselling for families about this recognised complication.
SAQ 242 — Pediatric Glucose Targets and Hyperglycaemia in TBI MODERATE
A child in neurocritical care has glucose 14 mmol/L.

PHYSIOLOGY

Outline why hyperglycaemia is harmful in TBI / ischaemic brain injury.
Show structured answer
  • Hyperglycaemia worsens outcomes in TBI, stroke, HIE, SAH, post-arrest.
  • Mechanisms:
    • Anaerobic glycolysis → lactic acidosis in ischaemic tissue → worsens cellular damage.
    • Free radical production.
    • BBB disruption.
    • Pro-inflammatory effects.
    • Activation of cell-death pathways.
  • Stress hyperglycaemia is common — release of catecholamines, cortisol, inflammatory cytokines.

TREATMENT

Outline current targets and approach.
Show structured answer
Current pediatric targets:
  • Glucose 4–10 mmol/L generally.
  • Avoid both HYPOGLYCAEMIA (< 3 mmol/L worse than mild hyperglycaemia) and severe HYPERGLYCAEMIA (> 10 mmol/L).
  • Tight glycaemic control (4–7) trials in pediatric ICU (CHiP, SPECS) did NOT show benefit and increased hypoglycaemia — abandoned routinely.
  • Acceptable: 5–10 mmol/L moderate glucose control.
  • Insulin infusion if glucose persistently > 10–12.
  • Avoid dextrose-containing IV fluids in acute ICH/SAH/TBI for first 24 h unless hypoglycaemic (use 0.9% NaCl).
  • Frequent glucose monitoring — hourly during insulin infusion.
  • HYPOGLYCAEMIA more harmful than mild hyperglycaemia — be conservative.
SAQ 243 — Hypothalamic Obesity Post-Craniopharyngioma MODERATE
A child 1 year after craniopharyngioma resection has gained 15 kg, hyperphagia, daytime fatigue, behavioural changes.

DEFINITION

Outline hypothalamic obesity.
Show structured answer
Hypothalamic obesity:
  • Severe, rapidly progressive obesity following damage to the hypothalamus.
  • Common after craniopharyngioma resection (~ 50% of patients), other suprasellar / hypothalamic tumours and treatments.
  • Mechanism: damage to ventromedial hypothalamus (satiety centre) and disruption of leptin/melanocortin signalling → hyperphagia + reduced energy expenditure + autonomic dysregulation.
  • Features:
    • Hyperphagia, sometimes nocturnal eating, food preoccupation.
    • Reduced physical activity, lethargy.
    • Sleep disturbances.
    • Behavioural / emotional changes.
    • Often coexists with hormonal deficiencies (GH, ACTH, TSH, gonadotropins, ADH).

TREATMENT

Outline management.
Show structured answer
Conservative (limited efficacy):
  • Structured diet, family-based behavioural therapy.
  • Physical activity programmes (often difficult given low energy expenditure).
  • Sleep optimisation.

Pharmacotherapy (limited evidence):
  • Metformin.
  • Octreotide — suppresses insulin secretion.
  • GLP-1 agonists (exenatide, liraglutide, semaglutide) — increasingly used.
  • Setmelanotide (MC4R agonist) — emerging for hypothalamic obesity.
  • Treat associated hormonal deficiencies (GH replacement may help body composition).

Surgical:
  • Bariatric surgery in selected cases (controversial in pediatrics with hypothalamic obesity).

Multidisciplinary: endocrinology, dietitian, psychology, family-based treatment.

Counselling: long-term challenge; expectations management; quality of life support.
SAQ 244 — Cervical Spine Clearance in Trauma MODERATE
A 4-year-old after a high-speed MVC requires C-spine evaluation.

DEFINITION

Outline pediatric C-spine clearance principles.
Show structured answer
Pediatric C-spine anatomy considerations:
  • Higher fulcrum (C2–C3) in young children — high cervical injuries more common.
  • Larger head relative to body → cervical flexion injury.
  • Increased ligamentous laxity.
  • SCIWORA (spinal cord injury without radiographic abnormality) more common — MRI needed.

Clinical clearance (per PECARN criteria for low-risk):
  • Patient must be: alert (GCS 15), no neurologic deficits, no neck pain or tenderness, no high-energy mechanism, no painful distracting injury.
  • Plus age-appropriate ability to communicate (typically ≥ 3 years).
  • If ALL criteria met → can clear clinically without imaging.

Imaging:
  • Plain X-rays (AP, lateral, odontoid view) — historically used but limited sensitivity.
  • CT for adequate evaluation of bony injury (limited radiation in pediatric — avoid if can clinically clear).
  • MRI for ligamentous injury, SCIWORA, spinal cord injury.
  • Flexion-extension views rarely useful acutely.

NEXT STEP

Outline immobilisation principles.
Show structured answer
Pediatric C-spine immobilisation:
  • Standard cervical collar (appropriate size — many adult collars too large for children).
  • For young children (< 8 years): consider OCCIPITAL RECESS / chest pad to compensate for larger head — prevents forced flexion when on flat board.
  • Maintain in-line cervical stabilisation during airway management (in-line manual stabilisation; modified jaw thrust).
  • Avoid neck flexion/extension during transport, intubation.
  • Family-friendly approach; gentle but firm.
  • Re-evaluate need for immobilisation regularly — prolonged immobilisation causes pressure ulcers, anxiety, complications.

Spinal motion restriction:
  • Soft collar acceptable if minor mechanism.
  • Long backboards: brief use for extrication / transport; not for prolonged immobilisation (pressure injury, respiratory compromise).
SAQ 245 — Pediatric Encephalitis — Empiric Therapy MODERATE
A 7-year-old with fever, behavioural change, focal seizures, mild CSF lymphocytic pleocytosis.

DX

Outline major causes of pediatric encephalitis.
Show structured answer
Pediatric encephalitis causes:
  • Viral (most common identifiable): HSV-1 (most important — treatable), enterovirus, EBV, mycoplasma (some classify as bacterial), arboviruses (West Nile, La Crosse, EEE, JEV), CMV, HHV-6, varicella zoster, rabies (rare but fatal), measles (SSPE late), influenza-associated.
  • Bacterial / atypical: Mycoplasma, Bartonella (cat scratch), Listeria, syphilis, tuberculosis, Lyme.
  • Fungal: Cryptococcus, Histoplasma, Coccidioides (immunocompromised).
  • Parasitic: cerebral malaria, toxoplasma, neurocysticercosis, primary amoebic (Naegleria).
  • Autoimmune: anti-NMDAR, anti-LGI1, anti-MOG (FLAMES variant with cortical FLAIR), ADEM, ANE, Hashimoto encephalopathy.
  • Para-infectious: ADEM, ANE.
  • Idiopathic — often no cause identified despite extensive workup (~ 50%).

TREATMENT

Outline empiric therapy.
Show structured answer
Empiric therapy pending workup:
  • Acyclovir 10 mg/kg IV q8h (15 mg/kg q8h in children < 12 years) — START IMMEDIATELY; delay is associated with worse HSV outcomes. Continue 14–21 days if HSV confirmed.
  • Broad-spectrum bacterial coverage: ceftriaxone + vancomycin (cover possible bacterial meningitis).
  • Doxycycline if rickettsial disease possible (in endemic areas).
  • Antifungals (amphotericin B, voriconazole) if immunocompromised or fungal suspected.
  • Anti-toxoplasmosis (pyrimethamine + sulfadiazine + leucovorin) if HIV/AIDS.
  • Steroids are usually NOT given empirically — confounds autoimmune workup; given selectively if autoimmune suspected (after HSV PCR result).

Workup:
  • CSF: cell count, protein, glucose; HSV PCR + other viral PCRs; bacterial culture; cryptococcal antigen; autoimmune panel.
  • MRI brain.
  • EEG.
  • Blood cultures, viral serology.
  • HIV testing.
  • Autoimmune encephalitis panel (serum + CSF).
  • Pelvic/testicular imaging in females (anti-NMDAR teratoma).

De-escalate antibiotics / antivirals when results return.
SAQ 246 — Neonatal Stroke — Acute Management MODERATE
A term newborn has focal motor seizures on day 2 of life. MRI: left MCA infarct.

DX

Outline pathology of perinatal arterial ischaemic stroke (PAIS).
Show structured answer
Perinatal arterial ischaemic stroke (PAIS):
  • Incidence ~ 1 in 2500 term births — most common cause of pediatric AIS.
  • Most occur in the perinatal period (delivery and immediate postnatal).
  • Presents typically with focal seizures on day 1–3; sometimes later with motor asymmetry.
  • Aetiologies (often multifactorial):
    • Placental thrombosis (chorionic vessel thrombi).
    • Hypoxic-ischaemic events (umbilical cord problems).
    • Maternal prothrombotic state (autoimmune, antiphospholipid).
    • Congenital heart disease.
    • Polycythaemia.
    • Sepsis.
    • Cocaine exposure.
    • Inherited thrombophilia (often secondary contributor).
    • Catheterisation injury.
  • MRI: focal restricted diffusion in arterial territory; typically LEFT MCA (~ 75% of all PAIS).

TREATMENT

Outline acute and long-term management.
Show structured answer
Acute:
  • Treat seizures — phenobarbital first-line; levetiracetam.
  • Supportive care: oxygenation, normocapnia, normothermia, normoglycaemia.
  • cEEG monitoring.
  • NO tPA or thrombectomy in neonates (insufficient evidence; bleeding risk).
  • NO routine antithrombotic for PAIS — most lesions monophasic; thrombosis often already complete by presentation.
  • Exception: anticoagulation if ongoing source (e.g., cardiac thrombus, CSVT).

Workup:
  • Echocardiogram (CHD).
  • Placental pathology.
  • Thrombophilia screen (controversial timing — defer some tests beyond neonatal period).
  • Maternal autoimmune workup.
  • MRI/MRV.

Long-term:
  • Early physical therapy and stimulation programmes.
  • Constraint-induced movement therapy.
  • Hemiplegic CP develops in 50–70%.
  • Cognitive issues common.
  • Epilepsy risk (15–20%).
  • School support / early intervention.
  • Recurrent stroke risk low (~ 2%) — usually does not require lifelong antiplatelet.
SAQ 247 — Cervical Cord Injury — MAP and CSF Drainage HARD
An adolescent with complete cervical SCI.

TREATMENT

Outline acute hemodynamic management.
Show structured answer
  • MAP target ≥ 85–90 mm Hg for 7 days post-injury (extrapolated from adult evidence) to optimise spinal cord perfusion pressure.
  • Norepinephrine preferred — covers α (vasoconstriction) and β (treats bradycardia from sympathectomy).
  • Phenylephrine alone worsens bradycardia.
  • Fluids cautiously (often hypovolaemic from sympathectomy).
  • Atropine for severe bradycardia.
  • Avoid both hypotension and excessive elevation of BP.
  • Methylprednisolone NOT routinely recommended (NASCIS controversies; AANS/CNS guidelines do not recommend).
  • Lumbar CSF drainage may improve spinal cord perfusion in some thoracic/aortic surgery contexts but not standard for traumatic SCI.

NEXT STEP

Outline supportive care priorities.
Show structured answer
  • Airway: C5 and above → diaphragmatic paralysis; intubation often required.
  • VTE prophylaxis (LMWH) — high VTE risk in SCI.
  • Bladder management (Foley initially; intermittent catheterisation long-term).
  • Bowel programme.
  • Pressure-area care.
  • Nutrition (often increased metabolic demand acutely).
  • Rehabilitation early.
  • Family support.
SAQ 248 — Pediatric Status Epilepticus Beyond Second-Line HARD
Status epilepticus persists despite benzodiazepine and a second-line AED.

TREATMENT

Outline tertiary management.
Show structured answer
Refractory SE (continues despite 2 AEDs):
  1. Intubate for airway protection.
  2. Continuous infusion sedation:
    • Midazolam (load 0.2 mg/kg; infusion 0.05–2 mg/kg/h).
    • Propofol (caution — PRIS risk in children).
    • Pentobarbital (load 5–10 mg/kg; infusion to EEG burst-suppression).
  3. Ketamine — NMDA antagonist; addresses receptor trafficking in prolonged SE.
  4. Continuous EEG mandatory to confirm electrographic termination.
  5. Add second-line AED if not already.

Super-refractory SE (continues despite anaesthetic):
  • Ketogenic diet — initiate early.
  • Immunotherapy if autoimmune suspected (steroids, IVIG, PLEX).
  • Anakinra or tocilizumab if FIRES suspected.
  • Hypothermia 33 °C considered.
  • Magnesium sulfate.
  • Lacosamide, perampanel, brivaracetam.
  • Surgical evaluation if focal substrate identified.

Underlying cause workup: autoimmune encephalitis (anti-NMDAR), metabolic, genetic, drug levels, infection, imaging.
SAQ 249 — ECMO Neurological Complications HARD
A child on VA ECMO develops new neurologic signs.

DX

Outline ECMO neurological complications.
Show structured answer
  • ICH — 10–20% of pediatric ECMO; risk highest in neonates and with VV configuration; bleeding from anticoagulation.
  • Ischaemic stroke — VA ECMO predisposes to LEFT-sided thromboembolic events (retrograde aortic flow allows clots to reach systemic circulation); carotid cannulation right ICA → right hemisphere strokes also.
  • Hypoxic-ischaemic encephalopathy from pre-ECMO arrest.
  • Cerebral oedema.
  • Watershed infarcts from low flow.
  • Brain death possible.

TREATMENT

Bedside management when new neurological signs develop.
Show structured answer
  • Bedside neuro exam, pupillary check.
  • Optimise CPP: head midline, HOB 30°, ensure adequate MAP.
  • Reduce anticoagulation if ICH suspected — reduce heparin to lower target ACT/aPTT; reverse with protamine if necessary; transfuse platelets/FFP.
  • Bridge therapy: hyperosmolar (3% saline 3–5 mL/kg) ± mannitol; brief hyperventilation.
  • Urgent imaging when possible — bedside CT or portable CT, MRI rarely possible on ECMO.
  • Multidisciplinary: neurosurgery for possible decompression, ECMO team for circuit management, family for prognosis.
  • Continuous EEG to detect seizures.
  • Transcranial Doppler can be used at bedside.
  • NIRS monitoring during ECMO can detect declining rSO₂.
SAQ 250 — Cerebellar Mutism — Cerebellar Cognitive-Affective Syndrome MODERATE
A child recovering from medulloblastoma resection has speech returning but with persistent behavioural and cognitive changes.

DEFINITION

Outline cerebellar cognitive-affective syndrome (CCAS).
Show structured answer
Cerebellar cognitive-affective syndrome (Schmahmann syndrome):
  • Often follows cerebellar mutism syndrome but can occur independently after cerebellar injury / surgery.
  • Four domains affected:
    1. Executive function (planning, set-shifting, working memory).
    2. Visuospatial cognition.
    3. Linguistic processing (agrammatism, dysprosody).
    4. Affective regulation (emotional lability, blunted affect, disinhibition).
  • Anatomic correlate: cerebellar hemispheres (especially posterior lobe) modulate cognition and behaviour via dentato-thalamo-cortical pathways to prefrontal, posterior parietal, and limbic cortex.
  • The cerebellum is not just a motor structure.

TREATMENT

Outline rehabilitation approach.
Show structured answer
  • Long-term cognitive rehabilitation.
  • Speech and language therapy.
  • Occupational therapy for executive function.
  • Educational accommodations — gradual school re-integration.
  • Neuropsychology assessment to characterise deficits and track recovery.
  • Family support and education about chronic nature.
  • Social work; school liaison.
  • Pharmacological adjuncts limited — SSRIs for mood, stimulants if attention severely impaired (case-by-case).
  • Long-term follow-up — many improve over years but residual deficits common.
SAQ 251 — Carotid-Cavernous Fistula MODERATE
A teenager develops proptosis, conjunctival chemosis, bruit over the eye, and reduced visual acuity weeks after head trauma.

DX

Outline diagnosis and classification.
Show structured answer
Carotid-cavernous fistula (CCF):
  • Abnormal communication between carotid arterial system and cavernous sinus.
  • Direct (high-flow): traumatic — laceration of intracavernous ICA with high arterial flow into sinus.
  • Indirect (dural arteriovenous fistula, low-flow): spontaneous, often older patients; dural branches of ECA/ICA shunt to cavernous sinus.
  • Classic features:
    • Pulsatile proptosis.
    • Audible bruit over eye (auscultate orbit).
    • Conjunctival chemosis and arterialisation of conjunctival veins.
    • Reduced visual acuity from venous congestion.
    • Ophthalmoplegia (CN III, IV, VI affected by cavernous sinus location).
    • Glaucoma from venous outflow obstruction.
  • Imaging: CTA, MRA, or catheter angiography.

TREATMENT

Outline treatment.
Show structured answer
  • Direct (high-flow) CCF: usually requires intervention — vision-threatening.
  • Endovascular embolisation — transvenous or transarterial approach:
    • Coils or liquid embolic agent (Onyx, glue).
    • Detachable balloons historically.
  • Indirect CCF: may spontaneously thrombose; manual carotid compression sometimes attempted; embolisation for persistent or vision-threatening.
  • Surgical alternative if endovascular unsuccessful.
  • Eye protection during course.
  • Multidisciplinary: interventional neuroradiology, ophthalmology.
SAQ 252 — Spinal Dural Arteriovenous Fistula MODERATE
An older child has progressive lower-limb weakness and bladder dysfunction. MRI cord: longitudinally extensive T2 hyperintensity with serpiginous flow voids on cord surface.

DX

Diagnosis and pathology.
Show structured answer
Spinal dural AV fistula (DAVF):
  • Most common spinal vascular malformation.
  • Abnormal connection between radicular artery and radicular vein within dura.
  • Arterialised pressure in spinal cord veins → venous hypertension → cord congestion → progressive myelopathy (Foix-Alajouanine syndrome).
  • Features:
    • Subacute or chronic progressive paraparesis.
    • Sensory loss.
    • Bowel and bladder dysfunction.
    • Often missed/misdiagnosed as transverse myelitis early.
  • MRI: longitudinally extensive cord T2 hyperintensity + serpiginous flow voids on dorsal cord surface (engorged perimedullary veins).
  • Diagnosis: spinal catheter angiography (gold standard).

TREATMENT

Outline treatment.
Show structured answer
  • Treatment is essential — untreated DAVF leads to progressive irreversible cord injury.
  • EARLY TREATMENT for best outcomes.
  • Two main options:
    • Endovascular embolisation — first-line in most centres; embolise the venous side of the fistula.
    • Surgical disconnection — clip the draining vein at its dural exit; high success rate.
  • Some patients have incomplete recovery despite successful treatment — depends on duration of symptoms before diagnosis.
  • Follow-up imaging to confirm closure.
  • Multidisciplinary: interventional neuroradiology, neurosurgery, neurology.
SAQ 253 — Spinal Cord Infarction HARD
A 14-year-old after cardiac surgery develops acute paraplegia, preserved proprioception, urinary retention.

DX

Outline anterior spinal artery syndrome.
Show structured answer
Anterior spinal artery syndrome:
  • Occlusion / hypoperfusion of anterior spinal artery (supplies anterior 2/3 of cord).
  • Clinical features:
    • Bilateral weakness below the level (motor — corticospinal tracts).
    • Loss of pain and temperature sensation below level (spinothalamic tracts).
    • Preserved proprioception, vibration, light touch (DORSAL columns spared — supplied by posterior spinal arteries).
    • Bladder and bowel dysfunction.
    • Acute onset.
  • Causes:
    • Aortic surgery (thoracic AAA repair, coarctation) — interruption of artery of Adamkiewicz.
    • Hypotension during surgery.
    • Aortic dissection.
    • Atherosclerotic occlusion.
    • Vasculitis.
    • Cocaine use.
    • Surgical positioning (rare).

TREATMENT

Outline management.
Show structured answer
  • Maintain MAP ≥ 85–90 mm Hg to optimise spinal cord perfusion pressure.
  • Lumbar drain — reduce CSF pressure to improve spinal cord perfusion (used in thoracic aortic surgery routinely).
  • Aggressive correction of anaemia, hypoxia.
  • Steroids — controversial; NASCIS evidence in traumatic SCI does not directly apply.
  • Imaging: MRI cord to confirm pattern and exclude other causes.
  • Investigate aetiology (echocardiogram, vascular imaging, thrombophilia screen).
  • Rehabilitation — bladder, bowel, mobility, contracture prevention.
  • Pain management.
  • Outcome variable — early treatment may allow some recovery.
SAQ 254 — Chiari II vs Chiari I MODERATE
Distinguishing pediatric Chiari malformations.

DEFINITION

Outline Chiari I vs II.
Show structured answer
Chiari I malformation:
  • Cerebellar tonsils descend > 5 mm below the foramen magnum.
  • Often asymptomatic; some have cough-/Valsalva-triggered headaches.
  • May have associated syringomyelia, scoliosis.
  • NOT associated with neural tube defects.
  • Imaging only — clinical syndrome requires symptoms or syrinx.

Chiari II malformation:
  • STRONGLY associated with myelomeningocele (open spinal dysraphism).
  • Complex hindbrain malformation:
    • Small posterior fossa.
    • Downward herniation of cerebellar vermis, brainstem (medulla), and 4th ventricle.
    • 'Beaked' tectum.
    • Corpus callosum dysgenesis.
    • Aqueductal stenosis.
  • HYDROCEPHALUS in > 90%.
  • Symptomatic in many infants:
    • Stridor (vocal cord paralysis from CN X dysfunction).
    • Apnoea (central).
    • Dysphagia.
    • Aspiration.

TREATMENT

Outline management.
Show structured answer
Chiari I:
  • Asymptomatic — surveillance (no surgery).
  • Symptomatic (cough headache, syrinx, scoliosis): suboccipital decompression with C1 laminectomy ± duroplasty.
  • Associated syrinx may need additional management (shunt, fenestration).

Chiari II:
  • Multidisciplinary spina bifida clinic.
  • Manage hydrocephalus: VP shunt (or ETV).
  • Posterior fossa decompression for symptomatic brainstem compression (severe stridor, apnoea, dysphagia).
  • ENT, audiology, ophthalmology, neurodevelopmental, urology, orthopaedics, rehabilitation.
  • Tracheostomy for severe vocal cord paralysis.
  • Nutrition (NG/PEG if aspiration).
  • Family planning: folic acid supplementation pre-conception for future pregnancies; recurrence risk ~ 3–5%.
SAQ 255 — Hydrocephalus — Choice of ETV vs VP Shunt MODERATE
An infant with obstructive hydrocephalus from aqueductal stenosis.

CRITERIA

Outline ETV success score.
Show structured answer
ETV Success Score (Kulkarni et al.): Predicts likelihood of ETV success at 6 months.
Three components:
  • Age: < 1 month (0 pts), 1–6 months (10), 6 months – 1 year (30), 1–10 years (40), ≥ 10 years (50).
  • Aetiology: post-infectious (0), myelomeningocele (20), IVH (20), non-tectal tumour (30), aqueductal stenosis (30), tectal tumour (50), other (30).
  • Previous shunt: Previous shunt -10, no prior shunt 0.

Higher score = better ETV candidate.
Score > 70 → ETV strongly preferred; 50–70 → ETV reasonable; < 50 → VP shunt likely better.

TREATMENT

Outline VP shunt vs ETV considerations.
Show structured answer
VP shunt:
  • Works for any hydrocephalus (communicating or non-communicating).
  • Lifetime complications:
    • Mechanical failure 30–40% in first year, 80% lifetime.
    • Infection 5–10%.
    • Overshunting / slit-ventricle.
    • Need for revisions.
  • Modern programmable valves reduce some complications.

ETV (Endoscopic Third Ventriculostomy):
  • Preferred for OBSTRUCTIVE (non-communicating) hydrocephalus in children > 1 year — creates direct communication from 3rd ventricle into basal cisterns.
  • Avoids shunt dependence — no hardware.
  • Success depends on age, aetiology, prior shunt.
  • Infants < 1 year (especially < 6 months) lower success; consider ETV + choroid plexus cauterisation (Warf protocol) — improves success.
  • Failures (re-stenosis) may occur years later — long-term follow-up needed.
SAQ 256 — Pediatric Spinal Cord Tumour MODERATE
A child has progressive lower extremity weakness, bladder dysfunction, and back pain. MRI: enhancing intramedullary cord lesion.

DX

Outline common pediatric intramedullary tumours.
Show structured answer
Pediatric intramedullary tumours (in approximate frequency):
  • Pilocytic astrocytoma (most common in pediatric cord) — often cystic with mural nodule; BRAF fusion (KIAA1549-BRAF) common.
  • Ependymoma (especially in older children/adolescents) — often well-circumscribed, central; NF2 association.
  • Ganglioglioma — BRAF V600E mutation often present.
  • High-grade glioma — rare in pediatric cord; poor prognosis.
  • Hemangioblastoma — von Hippel-Lindau association.

Extramedullary tumours: meningioma (rare in children), nerve sheath tumours (schwannoma, neurofibroma — NF1/NF2 association).

TREATMENT

Outline management.
Show structured answer
  • Detailed imaging: MRI whole spine + brain with contrast.
  • Maximal safe surgical resection — outcomes depend on extent of resection.
  • Intraoperative neurophysiological monitoring (SSEPs, MEPs) during surgery.
  • Pathology with molecular subtyping (BRAF, IDH, H3 K27M, etc.).
  • Adjuvant therapy per histology / molecular subgroup:
    • Pilocytic astrocytoma: typically surgery alone if gross-total; observation and surveillance.
    • Ependymoma: surgery + focal radiation in older children.
    • BRAF V600E lesions: MEK inhibitors (selumetinib, trametinib) emerging.
    • High-grade: multimodal with focal radiation + chemotherapy.
  • Rehabilitation: bladder management, mobility, contracture prevention.
  • Multidisciplinary: neurosurgery, neuro-oncology, urology, rehabilitation, neuropsychology, family support.
  • Long-term follow-up imaging and clinical monitoring.
SAQ 257 — Sialorrhoea in Neurologically Impaired Children MODERATE
A child with severe CP has chronic drooling causing skin breakdown and social difficulty.

TREATMENT

Outline management.
Show structured answer
Behavioural / supportive:
