  • Oral motor therapy.
  • Bib management; positioning to allow swallowing.

Pharmacologic:
  • ANTICHOLINERGICS — first-line:
    • Glycopyrrolate (oral or transdermal) — doesn't cross BBB → fewer CNS side effects.
    • Hyoscine / scopolamine patch.
    • Atropine sulfate (1% oral drops applied sublingually).
    • Side effects: dry mouth, constipation, urinary retention, blurred vision, behavioural change.
  • BOTULINUM TOXIN injection of salivary glands (parotid + submandibular, ultrasound-guided):
    • Lasts 3–4 months; can repeat.
    • Effective and well-tolerated.
    • Risk of dysphagia, dry mouth.

Surgical:
  • Salivary gland duct ligation / repositioning.
  • Selective salivary gland excision (submandibular).
  • Considered for refractory cases.

Multidisciplinary: SLT, dental, OT, dermatology for skin care, family education.
SAQ 258 — Vagal Nerve Stimulator vs Responsive Neurostimulation MODERATE
Comparing VNS and RNS for pediatric drug-resistant epilepsy.

DEFINITION

Outline differences.
Show structured answer
VNSRNS
Stimulation patternContinuous programmed cyclic (open-loop)Closed-loop — detects seizure activity and responds
TargetLeft vagus nerveCortical seizure-onset zone(s)
ApprovalFDA-approved age ≥ 4 for focal epilepsyFDA-approved adults; off-label in pediatrics
Pre-surgical workupMinimal — broad indicationRequires identified focal seizure-onset zone (V-EEG, MEG, iEEG)
Efficacy~ 50% have > 50% seizure reduction; 10% seizure-freeBetter seizure reduction in focal cohorts (50–70% reduction; ~ 20–30% seizure-free at 5 years)
Side effectsVoice change, cough, throat discomfort, sleep apnoeaImplant-related; minimal stimulation side effects

TREATMENT

Outline DBS and other emerging.
Show structured answer
Other neurostimulation:
  • DBS (anterior nucleus of thalamus) — for refractory focal epilepsy; FDA-approved adults; pediatric data emerging.
  • Centromedian thalamic DBS — for LGS and generalised epilepsies; investigational.
  • Trigeminal nerve stimulation — external; less invasive.
  • Transcranial direct/alternating current stimulation — investigational.
SAQ 259 — Coma Scale Comparisons MODERATE
Evaluating a child with altered consciousness.

DEFINITION

Outline scales used in pediatric practice.
Show structured answer
Common pediatric coma / consciousness scales:
  • Glasgow Coma Scale (GCS): Eye opening (1–4), Verbal (1–5), Motor (1–6); total 3–15. Pediatric modification of verbal score for < 5 years (recognise carer, irritable cry, inconsolable, moans, no response).
  • FOUR score (Full Outline of UnResponsiveness): includes eye response, motor, brainstem reflexes, respiration; designed for intubated patients (no verbal component).
  • Pediatric Coma Scale (Adelaide / Simpson): alternatives.
  • AVPU: Alert / Voice / Pain / Unresponsive — simple, used in pre-hospital and early triage.
  • Coma Recovery Scale–Revised (CRS-R): detailed scale for prolonged disorders of consciousness; tracks subscales (auditory, visual, motor, oromotor, communication, arousal); used for VS vs MCS distinction.
  • Coma Near-Coma scale: another scale for prolonged disorders.

CRITERIA

Outline GCS severity categories and pitfalls.
Show structured answer
Severity:
  • Mild TBI: GCS 13–15.
  • Moderate TBI: GCS 9–12.
  • Severe TBI: GCS ≤ 8.

Pitfalls:
  • Sedation, paralysis, alcohol/drugs confound GCS — verbal score unreliable.
  • Intubation precludes verbal scoring — denote as '5T' for the verbal component.
  • Eye opening difficult in periorbital swelling — denote as '1C' (closed).
  • Motor response is the most prognostically useful single component.
  • Best motor response (not worst) is scored.
  • Bilateral examination — note asymmetry.
  • Trend over time more important than single value.
  • Children < 2 years: use pediatric modification for verbal; engagement with caregivers, tracking, irritability.
SAQ 260 — Brain Death — Ancillary Tests HARD
Brain death determination in a child where apnoea test cannot be safely completed.

CRITERIA

Outline acceptable ancillary tests.
Show structured answer
Acceptable ancillary tests in pediatric brain death (demonstrate absent cerebral blood flow):
  • Radionuclide cerebral perfusion scintigraphy (Tc-99m HMPAO): 'hollow skull' sign — no intracranial perfusion. Most commonly used ancillary in pediatrics; portable.
  • 4-vessel catheter cerebral angiography: gold standard but invasive; rarely used as primary ancillary now.
  • CT angiography (CTA): demonstration of absent contrast at M4/A4 branches; criteria still being refined in paediatrics; emerging acceptable test.
  • Transcranial Doppler (TCD): reverberating ('to-and-fro') or absent diastolic flow consistent with circulatory arrest; operator-dependent; some centres use as supportive.

NO LONGER recommended as sole ancillary:
  • EEG showing electrocerebral silence — can be misleading in deep barbiturate coma; not reliable when CNS depressants present.

Ancillary tests SUPPLEMENT clinical exam when components cannot be completed — they do not replace it in routine practice.

NEXT STEP

When are ancillary tests indicated?
Show structured answer
Indications for ancillary testing:
  • Apnoea test cannot be safely completed (haemodynamic / oxygenation instability).
  • Severe pharmacologic confounders (barbiturates) that cannot be eliminated.
  • Significant facial / cervical / ocular trauma precluding brainstem reflex testing.
  • Examiner uncertainty about clinical findings.
  • To shorten observation period in selected cases (per some institutional protocols).
  • Family request to confirm clinical diagnosis (controversial).

Ancillary tests do NOT replace clinical brain-death exam — they supplement when clinical exam cannot be completed.

Test should be performed by experienced personnel and interpreted by experts familiar with brain-death criteria.
SAQ 261 — Pediatric Stroke Risk in Cardiac Disease MODERATE
A child with single-ventricle physiology palliated with Fontan has new stroke.

PHYSIOLOGY

Outline stroke risk in pediatric CHD.
Show structured answer
Mechanisms of cardiac-related stroke:
  • RIGHT-TO-LEFT SHUNT (cyanotic CHD, septal defects, Fontan with fenestration): bacteria/clots bypass pulmonary capillary filter → reach systemic circulation → cerebral emboli; predisposes to brain abscess.
  • STAGNANT CIRCULATION (Fontan): chronically elevated systemic venous pressure → stasis → thromboembolic risk.
  • ATRIAL ARRHYTHMIA / DILATATION (post-Fontan): atrial thrombus formation.
  • PROSTHETIC MATERIALS (mechanical valves, conduits): thrombogenic surface.
  • HYPOXAEMIA: polycythaemia → hyperviscosity.
  • SURGICAL EVENTS: embolisation during CPB; deep hypothermic circulatory arrest.
  • ENDOCARDITIS: septic embolisation.
  • PROTEIN-LOSING ENTEROPATHY: in Fontan → loss of anticoagulant factors.

TREATMENT

Outline acute and long-term management.
Show structured answer
Acute:
  • Standard stroke care (neuroprotection, normothermia, normocapnia, BP within autoregulatory range).
  • Endovascular thrombectomy increasingly performed in adolescents with LVO.
  • IV tPA — limited paediatric data; case-by-case in adolescents with no contraindications.
  • Investigate embolic source: TTE + TEE (with bubble study), Holter (for AF), atrial thrombus.

Long-term:
  • Antithrombotic optimisation:
    • Aspirin for many Fontan patients without thrombus history.
    • Warfarin for atrial fibrillation, valve replacement, prior thrombus.
    • DOACs emerging in pediatric cardiology (limited data).
  • Address underlying — arrhythmia management, Fontan optimisation, treat protein-losing enteropathy.
  • Optimise haemoglobin (avoid excessive polycythaemia).
  • Endocarditis prophylaxis as indicated.
  • Lifelong cardiac care.
  • Rehabilitation, neuropsychology, school support.
SAQ 262 — Acute Stroke Imaging Algorithm MODERATE
A child presents with acute focal neurologic deficit suspected stroke.

NEXT STEP

Outline imaging algorithm.
Show structured answer
Stepwise imaging:
  1. Non-contrast CT head — first-line in most settings; excludes haemorrhage; can be done rapidly.
  2. If no haemorrhage and stroke suspected:
    • CTA (head and neck) — identifies LVO, dissection, vascular abnormality.
    • OR MRI brain (DWI most sensitive for early ischaemia) + MRA — more sensitive but less available emergently.
  3. CT perfusion or MR perfusion — for selecting candidates for endovascular therapy beyond standard windows (mismatch with DWI).
  4. MRV / CTV if venous thrombosis suspected.
  5. Echocardiography (TTE then TEE if needed; bubble study) for cardiac source.
  6. Catheter angiography — gold standard for vascular anatomy; needed for endovascular intervention.

Time critical: 'time is brain' — minimise time to imaging and intervention.
Younger children: sedation may be needed for MRI; CT may be faster; balance radiation exposure against need for information.
Workup post-acute: thrombophilia, autoimmune, infection, metabolic (MELAS, homocystinuria, Fabry), genetic.
SAQ 263 — Anti-MOG Encephalitis with Seizures (FLAMES) MODERATE
A child with cortical encephalitis and seizures.

DX

Outline FLAMES.
Show structured answer
FLAMES (FLAIR-hyperintense Lesions in Anti-MOG-associated Encephalitis with Seizures):
  • Recognised phenotype within the MOGAD spectrum.
  • Cortical FLAIR-hyperintense lesions (often unilateral or bilateral focal cortical), with seizures.
  • Can resemble HSV encephalitis on imaging (but typically less haemorrhage, no temporal lobe predilection).
  • MOG-IgG positive in CSF and/or serum.
  • Often steroid-responsive — relatively good prognosis with treatment.

TREATMENT

Outline management.
Show structured answer
  • Distinguish from HSV — empirical acyclovir + CSF HSV PCR.
  • Once MOG-IgG confirmed and HSV excluded:
    • High-dose IV methylprednisolone 30 mg/kg/day × 3–5 days.
    • Prolonged oral steroid taper (over ≥ 3 months) to avoid relapse.
    • PLEX or IVIG for severe / refractory.
  • AEDs for seizure management.
  • Long-term immunotherapy for relapsing patients: rituximab, IVIG, mycophenolate.
  • Repeat MOG-IgG at 6 months — persistent positivity suggests relapsing course.
  • Multidisciplinary: neurology, ophthalmology (optic neuritis surveillance), rehabilitation.
SAQ 264 — Atypical Hemolytic Uremic Syndrome HARD
A 7-year-old with bloody diarrhoea progresses to AKI, thrombocytopenia, schistocytes. Initial workup STEC-negative; later develops seizures and decreased LOC.

DX

Outline aHUS and pediatric features.
Show structured answer
Atypical HUS (complement-mediated):
  • Triad: microangiopathic haemolytic anaemia + thrombocytopenia + acute kidney injury.
  • Distinct from typical (STEC-HUS) — STEC PCR/culture negative.
  • Caused by dysregulation of alternative complement pathway:
    • Mutations in factor H, factor I, MCP (CD46), C3, factor B.
    • Auto-antibodies against factor H.
  • Neurologic involvement in > 50% — seizures, encephalopathy, stroke (microvascular thrombosis).
  • Other organ involvement: heart, GI, pancreas, eye.
  • Relapsing course; high lifetime morbidity untreated.

TREATMENT

Outline management.
Show structured answer
  • ECULIZUMAB (Soliris): humanised monoclonal antibody against C5 — first-line for aHUS.
    • Blocks terminal complement pathway.
    • FDA-approved 2011 for aHUS.
    • Dramatically improves outcomes including neurological recovery.
    • Vaccinate against meningococcus (and provide prophylactic antibiotics) — encapsulated organism risk.
  • RAVULIZUMAB (Ultomiris): longer-acting C5 inhibitor; less frequent dosing.
  • Plasma exchange — historically used; bridges before eculizumab; less first-line now.
  • Supportive care: dialysis for AKI, transfusion as needed.
  • Long-term: lifelong eculizumab in most; genetic testing for inheritance and family screening.
SAQ 265 — Spinal Muscular Atrophy — Newborn Screening MODERATE
Discussing newborn screening for SMA.

DEFINITION

Outline rationale and process.
Show structured answer
SMA newborn screening:
  • SMA = autosomal recessive motor neuron disease; homozygous SMN1 deletion / mutation.
  • Severity modified by SMN2 copy number.
  • Without treatment, SMA type 1 fatal in infancy.
  • With pre-symptomatic disease-modifying therapy, infants can develop near-normal motor function.
  • Newborn screening detects SMN1 deletion via PCR — high sensitivity.
  • Implemented in many regions (US, Australia, Europe progressively).
  • Allows pre-symptomatic identification → treat before motor neuron loss.

TREATMENT

Outline post-screen pathway.
Show structured answer
  • Positive screen → confirmatory testing (SMN1 deletion test, SMN2 copy number determination).
  • Refer to specialised neuromuscular clinic urgently.
  • Treat as early as possible — pre-symptomatic outcomes far better.
  • Three approved DMTs:
    • Onasemnogene abeparvovec (Zolgensma) gene therapy.
    • Nusinersen (Spinraza) — intrathecal antisense oligonucleotide.
    • Risdiplam (Evrysdi) — oral.
  • Treatment choice based on age, SMN2 copy number, family preference, cost.
  • Comprehensive supportive care: respiratory, nutritional, orthopaedic, family genetic counselling.
  • Family education and long-term follow-up.
  • Cascade testing of unaffected siblings (carriers).
SAQ 266 — PALICC ARDS Pediatric Definition MODERATE
A pediatric ICU patient is being assessed for ARDS.

CRITERIA

Outline PALICC-2 ARDS definition.
Show structured answer
PALICC-2 (2023) Pediatric ARDS criteria:
  • Onset within 7 days of known clinical insult.
  • Respiratory failure not fully explained by cardiac failure or fluid overload.
  • New chest imaging abnormality.
  • Oxygenation criteria (severity stratification):
    • NIV stratification (CPAP or BiPAP ≥ 5 cmH₂O):
      • PARDS: SpO₂/FiO₂ ≤ 264 OR PaO₂/FiO₂ ≤ 300.
    • Invasive ventilation stratification (OI = oxygenation index = (FiO₂ × MAP × 100) / PaO₂):
      • Mild: OI 4–8.
      • Moderate: OI 8–16.
      • Severe: OI ≥ 16.
  • Specific subgroups: at risk (lower threshold), immunocompromised, cyanotic heart disease, chronic lung disease.
  • SpO₂-based criteria allow inclusion when arterial blood gas not available.

TREATMENT

Outline ventilation strategy.
Show structured answer
Lung-protective ventilation per PALICC-2:
  • Tidal volume 5–8 mL/kg of ideal body weight; lower for severe ARDS (3–6 mL/kg).
  • Plateau pressure < 28 (less for compliant chest wall).
  • Driving pressure ≤ 15 cmH₂O.
  • PEEP: titrate to optimise oxygenation and recruitment without overdistension; PEEP-FiO₂ tables can guide.
  • Permissive hypercapnia acceptable (pH > 7.20).
  • Permissive hypoxaemia (SpO₂ 88–92% acceptable in severe disease).
  • Prone positioning for moderate-severe ARDS (PROSEVA evidence in adults; pediatric extrapolation supportive).
  • Pulmonary vasodilators (iNO, sildenafil) — selective use.
  • Inhaled prostacyclin for refractory.
  • ECMO for refractory disease (P/F < 60, severe hypercapnia, hemodynamic instability).
  • Conservative fluid strategy.
  • Steroids — selected (controversial; methylprednisolone in early severe ARDS may help).
SAQ 267 — Septic Encephalopathy MODERATE
A 7-year-old with severe sepsis develops altered mental status.

DX

Outline septic encephalopathy.
Show structured answer
Septic encephalopathy:
  • Diffuse cerebral dysfunction during severe sepsis without direct CNS infection.
  • Common — up to 50% of septic patients have some encephalopathy.
  • Mechanism multifactorial:
    • Cytokine-mediated BBB disruption.
    • Cerebral hypoperfusion from sepsis-induced hemodynamic instability.
    • Microcirculatory dysfunction.
    • Neurotransmitter imbalance.
    • Metabolic derangements.
    • Drug effects (sedation).
  • Features: confusion, agitation, lethargy, seizures (often non-convulsive); coma in severe cases.
  • Important to distinguish from CNS infection, structural causes.

TREATMENT

Outline approach.
Show structured answer
  • Treat underlying sepsis aggressively (source control, antibiotics, hemodynamic resuscitation).
  • Optimise CPP: avoid hypotension; maintain MAP appropriate for age.
  • Cerebral monitoring: cEEG for non-convulsive seizures; multimodal monitoring in severe cases.
  • Treat seizures.
  • Address modifiable factors: pain, sedation, electrolytes, glucose, temperature.
  • Avoid neurotoxic medications.
  • Rule out other causes: MRI if focal signs; LP if meningitis suspected.
  • Most cases reversible with sepsis resolution; some have persistent cognitive deficits long-term.
  • Multidisciplinary follow-up post-PICU.
SAQ 268 — AHT — Mimickers MODERATE
Differential diagnosis when considering abusive head trauma.

CRITERIA

Outline mimickers to rule out.
Show structured answer
AHT mimickers — investigate when AHT considered:
  • Glutaric aciduria type 1 (GA1): macrocephaly + spontaneous subdural collections + bat-wing temporal atrophy. Confirm with urine organic acids.
  • Coagulopathies: factor deficiencies, von Willebrand disease, vitamin K deficiency bleeding, platelet disorders. Confirm with PT/APTT, factor levels, vWF panel.
  • Menkes disease: copper transport disorder; subdurals; pili torti; spasticity. Check copper, ceruloplasmin.
  • Ehlers-Danlos / connective tissue disease: spontaneous vascular fragility; family history.
  • Osteogenesis imperfecta: bone fragility (rib fractures); blue sclerae; family history.
  • Birth-related subdural haemorrhage: usually small, posterior, resolves in 4 weeks; not confused with multilayered retinal haemorrhages or fractures.
  • Accidental trauma: pattern of injuries should match plausible mechanism.
  • Spontaneous intracranial hypotension: subdural collections (smaller, bilateral, no retinal haemorrhages).

NEXT STEP

Outline workup approach.
Show structured answer
  • Detailed history (mechanism, witness account, family history).
  • Skeletal survey + repeat at 2 weeks.
  • Dilated ophthalmologic examination (retinal haemorrhages).
  • MRI brain + cervical spine.
  • Coagulation studies (PT, APTT, fibrinogen, platelet count, vWF, factor levels).
  • Metabolic screen: urine organic acids (GA1), acylcarnitine profile.
  • Copper / ceruloplasmin if Menkes suspected.
  • Genetic studies as guided.
  • Mandatory child-protection report — even if mimicker possible; investigation parallel.
  • Multidisciplinary: neurology, paediatrics, social work, hospital child-protection team, ophthalmology, genetics.
  • Document meticulously; preserve specimens; photographic documentation.
  • Sibling safety assessment.
SAQ 269 — Posterior Reversible Encephalopathy — Drugs MODERATE
A child on chemotherapy develops headache, seizures, BP elevation, and characteristic MRI.

DEFINITION

Outline PRES-inducing drugs.
Show structured answer
Drugs causing PRES:
  • Calcineurin inhibitors: cyclosporine, tacrolimus.
  • Chemotherapy: vincristine, cisplatin, carboplatin, gemcitabine, ifosfamide, cytarabine, intrathecal methotrexate, bevacizumab, sorafenib.
  • Immunosuppressants: rituximab, sirolimus, OKT3.
  • Erythropoietin (especially when corrects anaemia rapidly).
  • Steroids (rare).
  • IVIG.

Recognition important — drug-induced PRES typically reversible if causative agent removed.

TREATMENT

Outline management.
Show structured answer
  • Identify and STOP / hold offending drug if possible.
  • Substitute alternative when essential (e.g., tacrolimus → sirolimus or belatacept; CNS-sparing chemo regimen).
  • BP control — gradual reduction (10–25% MAP over hours); avoid precipitous drops.
  • Seizure management — levetiracetam first-line.
  • Supportive ICU care.
  • MRI follow-up to confirm resolution.
  • Resolution typically days to weeks; rarely permanent.
  • If drug essential for ongoing therapy: may consider reintroduction at lower dose with surveillance.
SAQ 270 — Pediatric Stroke — Acute Anti-thrombotic MODERATE
A 10-year-old has acute ischaemic stroke from focal cerebral arteriopathy.

TREATMENT

Outline acute anti-thrombotic principles.
Show structured answer
Acute anti-thrombotic (after haemorrhage excluded):
  • ASPIRIN 1–5 mg/kg/day — most common acute and secondary prevention agent.
  • For some aetiologies (cardioembolic with thrombus, dissection): heparin / LMWH initially → warfarin / DOAC.
  • For sickle cell stroke: exchange transfusion (not anti-thrombotic).
  • Duration depends on aetiology:
    • FCA: 2 years (often longer).
    • Dissection: 3–6 months with reimaging.
    • Cardioembolic with persistent source: indefinite anticoagulation.
    • Other arteriopathies: 2 years or longer.
  • Aetiology workup guides therapy duration.

NEXT STEP

Outline supportive measures.
Show structured answer
  • BP: avoid extremes; treat severe hypertension cautiously (10–25% MAP reduction over hours); avoid hypotension.
  • Glucose 4–10 mmol/L.
  • Normothermia (treat fever aggressively).
  • Normocapnia (35–40 mm Hg).
  • SpO₂ > 94%.
  • Hb optimised (transfusion if < 80–90 g/L in SCD < 100 g/L).
  • HOB elevation 30°.
  • Continuous EEG if seizures or altered consciousness.
  • Multidisciplinary stroke team.
  • Family communication and education.
  • Early rehabilitation.
SAQ 271 — Cerebral Aspergillosis HARD
An immunocompromised teenager develops ring-enhancing cerebral lesions; blood cultures positive for Aspergillus.

TREATMENT

First-line management.
Show structured answer
  • VORICONAZOLE IV first-line (CNS-penetrating; superior to amphotericin per adult RCT).
  • Therapeutic drug monitoring (trough 1–5.5 mg/L).
  • Alternative: liposomal amphotericin B; isavuconazole.
  • Surgical debridement for accessible / large lesions.
  • Address underlying immunodeficiency.
  • Long-term therapy (months); steroid taper if possible.
  • Mortality 50–70% even with treatment.
SAQ 272 — Cerebral Toxoplasmosis MODERATE
An immunocompromised child with ring-enhancing lesions and positive Toxoplasma IgG.

TREATMENT

Outline therapy.
Show structured answer
  • Pyrimethamine + sulfadiazine + LEUCOVORIN (folinic acid prevents marrow toxicity).
  • Alternative: pyrimethamine + clindamycin if sulfa allergy.
  • Reassess clinical / imaging at 2 weeks; if no response or atypical features → brain biopsy (differential includes CNS lymphoma, fungal abscess).
  • Address underlying — restore immune function.
  • Long-term suppressive therapy until CD4 recovery.
SAQ 273 — Primary Amoebic Meningoencephalitis HARD
A teenager develops fulminant meningoencephalitis after freshwater swimming.

DX

Diagnosis.
Show structured answer
Naegleria fowleri — free-living amoeba; enters via cribriform plate during nasal exposure to warm freshwater.
Rapidly progressive; case-fatality > 95%.
CSF: PMN predominance, low glucose, high protein, AMOEBAE on wet mount.

TREATMENT

Outline therapy.
Show structured answer
  • Amphotericin B IV + intrathecal — high doses.
  • Miltefosine (compassionate use through CDC).
  • Rifampin + azithromycin + fluconazole adjunctive.
  • Therapeutic hypothermia in some protocols.
  • ICU supportive care.
  • Public health reporting.
SAQ 274 — Eosinophilic Meningitis HARD
A child returned from a Pacific island region develops headache, fever, severe headache; CSF eosinophilic pleocytosis.

DX

Diagnosis and pathology.
Show structured answer
Angiostrongylus cantonensis (rat lungworm) — most common cause of eosinophilic meningitis worldwide.
Acquired from contaminated produce (slugs/snails on lettuce, etc.).
Other causes: Gnathostoma, Baylisascaris, coccidioidomycosis, TB.

TREATMENT

Outline management.
Show structured answer
  • Supportive care.
  • Corticosteroids (prednisone) — modulate inflammatory response; standard.
  • Albendazole — controversial; theoretically worsens by killing worms releasing antigen; case-by-case.
  • Symptomatic management of headache, ICP.
  • Public health reporting; investigate source.
SAQ 275 — Acute Bacterial Meningitis — Empiric Therapy MODERATE
A 3-year-old with fever, photophobia, meningismus, decreased LOC.

TREATMENT

Outline empiric antibiotics by age.
Show structured answer
Neonates (< 1 month): ampicillin + cefotaxime/ceftazidime + gentamicin (covers Group B Strep, E. coli, Listeria).

Infants 1–3 months: ampicillin + cefotaxime/ceftriaxone.

Children > 3 months: CEFTRIAXONE 100 mg/kg/day (or cefotaxime) + VANCOMYCIN (cover penicillin-resistant pneumococcus).

Add ampicillin if Listeria risk (immunocompromised, neonatal age).

Adjunctive DEXAMETHASONE 0.15 mg/kg q6h × 2 days — strongest evidence for reducing SNHL in pediatric Hib meningitis (give with or just before first antibiotic dose).

Duration: 7 days for Neisseria, Haemophilus; 10–14 days for pneumococcus; 14–21 days for Group B Strep, gram-negatives.
SAQ 276 — Lyme Neuroborreliosis MODERATE
A 13-year-old after a Lyme-endemic camping trip develops bilateral facial palsy and lymphocytic meningitis.

DX

Outline early disseminated Lyme features.
Show structured answer
  • Stage 2 (early disseminated) Lyme — days to weeks after bite.
  • Features: cranial neuropathies (especially BILATERAL CN VII), lymphocytic meningitis, radiculopathy, AV block, multiple erythema migrans.
  • Diagnosis: 2-step serology (ELISA then immunoblot); CSF antibody index in neuroborreliosis.

TREATMENT

Outline therapy.
Show structured answer
  • IV ceftriaxone 50–75 mg/kg/day (max 2–4 g) × 14–21 days for neuroborreliosis.
  • IDSA 2020: oral doxycycline acceptable for many neuroborreliosis presentations in children > 8 years.
  • Younger: amoxicillin or cefuroxime.
  • For isolated Bell's palsy: doxycycline (> 8 years) or amoxicillin × 14–21 days.
  • Eye care for facial palsy (lubrication, taping at night).
SAQ 277 — Cryptococcal Meningitis — Pediatric HARD
An immunocompromised child has subacute headache, ICP 38 cmH₂O, cryptococcal antigen positive.

TREATMENT

Three-phase antifungal regimen.
Show structured answer
Induction (≥ 2 weeks):
  • Liposomal amphotericin B 3–6 mg/kg/day + flucytosine 100 mg/kg/day.

Consolidation (≥ 8 weeks): fluconazole 10–12 mg/kg/day.

Maintenance: fluconazole 6 mg/kg/day for ≥ 1 year (or until immune recovery, CD4 > 200 sustained).

ICP management critical:
  • Serial therapeutic LPs to remove CSF — target opening pressure < 20 cmH₂O.
  • Lumbar drain if recurrent.
  • VP shunt for refractory.

DO NOT use corticosteroids routinely — worsen outcomes in cryptococcal meningitis.

Restore immune function where possible.
SAQ 278 — Pediatric Mucormycosis HARD
A child with severe DKA develops nasal eschar and orbital cellulitis.

TREATMENT

Outline multimodal management.
Show structured answer
1) Surgical debridement — aggressive, often disfiguring, REPEATED.

2) Antifungal:
  • Liposomal amphotericin B 5–10 mg/kg/day (high-dose).
  • Step-down to oral isavuconazole or posaconazole.
  • Long-term therapy.

3) Reverse predisposing factors:
  • Correct DKA (lower glucose, correct acidosis).
  • Discontinue deferoxamine if present.
  • Reverse immunosuppression where possible.

4) Multidisciplinary: ENT, neurosurgery, ophthalmology, ICU, ID, endocrinology.

Mortality 40–80% even with optimal treatment.
SAQ 279 — CO Poisoning — DNS MODERATE
A child treated for moderate CO poisoning develops new neuropsychiatric symptoms 3 weeks later.

DEFINITION

Outline delayed neuropsychiatric sequelae.
Show structured answer
Delayed Neuropsychiatric Sequelae (DNS) of CO poisoning:
  • Occur 2–40 days (median ~ 3 weeks) after apparent recovery from CO exposure.
  • Features: cognitive impairment, personality change, parkinsonism, gait disturbance, urinary incontinence.
  • Risk factors: loss of consciousness, COHb > 25%, prolonged exposure, age > 40 in adults.
  • MRI: bilateral globus pallidus / subcortical white matter hyperintensity / necrosis.
  • Mechanism: delayed demyelination, oxidative injury.

TREATMENT

Outline reducing risk.
Show structured answer
  • HBO during acute exposure may reduce DNS incidence — strongest support for: LOC at any point, severe neurological deficits, seizures, cardiac ischaemia, COHb > 25%, pregnancy with COHb > 15%, persistent symptoms despite normobaric O₂.
  • Continue 100% O₂ until COHb < 5% AND symptoms resolved.
  • Test all exposed individuals.
  • Investigate and remediate source.
  • Post-discharge: neurocognitive follow-up; surveillance for DNS over 1–2 months.
  • If DNS develops: supportive, neurorehabilitation, slow recovery over months.
SAQ 280 — Methaemoglobinaemia MODERATE
A child develops cyanosis with normal PaO₂ after topical benzocaine.

DX

Outline pathology.
Show structured answer
  • Iron in Hb oxidised from Fe²⁺ to Fe³⁺ (methaemoglobin) → cannot bind O₂ + left-shifts dissociation curve of remaining normal Hb → effective hypoxia.
  • Pulse ox reads ~ 85% regardless of true PaO₂ (cannot distinguish metHb from deoxy-Hb).
  • PaO₂ NORMAL on ABG (only co-oximetry detects metHb).
  • Brown / chocolate arterial blood.
  • Common causes: benzocaine, prilocaine, dapsone, nitrites, nitric oxide.

TREATMENT

Outline antidote.
Show structured answer
  • METHYLENE BLUE 1–2 mg/kg IV slow over 5 min.
  • Mechanism: methylene blue + NADPH (via NADPH-methaemoglobin reductase / G6PD-dependent) → leukomethylene blue, reducing metHb back to Hb.
  • CONTRAINDICATED in G6PD deficiency — cannot generate NADPH; can cause haemolysis without working.
  • In G6PD deficiency: exchange transfusion or hyperbaric oxygen.
  • Stop offending agent.
  • Repeat methylene blue at 1 h if needed; max 7 mg/kg total.
  • Indications: symptomatic metHb > 20% OR any metHb > 30%.
SAQ 281 — Lithium-Induced NDI MODERATE
A teenager on chronic lithium has polyuria and polydipsia.

DEFINITION

Diagnosis.
Show structured answer
Nephrogenic diabetes insipidus from lithium:
  • Lithium accumulates in distal tubule cells via ENaC.
  • Inhibits AVP receptor signalling and aquaporin-2 insertion → reduced water reabsorption.
  • Polyuria, polydipsia, hypernatraemia if water access limited.
  • 30% of chronic lithium users develop some degree; many subclinical.
  • Persistent NDI possible even after lithium cessation.

TREATMENT

Outline management.
Show structured answer
  • Discontinue or reduce lithium if possible.
  • Adequate hydration; encourage fluid access.
  • Amiloride — first-line (blocks lithium entry via ENaC, reduces NDI progression).
  • Thiazides — paradoxically reduce polyuria (volume contraction increases proximal Na/water reabsorption).
  • Low-sodium diet.
  • NSAIDs — reduce polyuria (impair PGE2-mediated AVP inhibition).
  • DDAVP not effective.
  • Long-term: monitor renal function.
SAQ 282 — Cocaine / Sympathomimetic Toxicity MODERATE
An adolescent presents with agitation, hyperthermia, mydriasis, hypertension, sweating after substance use.

DX

Outline sympathomimetic toxidrome.
Show structured answer
Sympathomimetic toxidrome:
  • Agitation, hallucinations, mydriasis (dilated), DIAPHORESIS (distinguishes from anticholinergic), hypertension, tachycardia, hyperthermia, seizures.
  • Drugs: cocaine, amphetamines, MDMA, methamphetamine, synthetic cathinones ('bath salts'), synthetic cannabinoids.
  • Complications: stroke (haemorrhagic and ischaemic), MI, arrhythmias, rhabdomyolysis, hyperthermia.

TREATMENT

Outline management.
Show structured answer
  • BENZODIAZEPINES (lorazepam, diazepam, midazolam) — first-line for agitation, seizures, hypertension, tachycardia.
  • Aggressive COOLING for hyperthermia.
  • IV fluids — for rhabdomyolysis, hyperthermia.
  • AVOID β-BLOCKERS as monotherapy for hypertension — unopposed α-adrenergic activity can worsen vasoconstriction and ischaemia (especially cocaine).
  • Phentolamine (α-blocker) for severe hypertension.
  • Treat MI / stroke per standard care.
  • Sodium bicarbonate for QRS widening (cocaine has Na-channel blockade).
  • Activated charcoal if recent oral ingestion.
  • Psychiatric / substance use counselling.
SAQ 283 — Synthetic Cannabinoid Toxicity MODERATE
A teenager who used 'K2/Spice' presents with agitation, seizures, severe rhabdomyolysis.

DX

Outline features.
Show structured answer
  • Synthetic cannabinoids ('K2', 'Spice', 'Black Mamba'): full agonists at CB1 receptors (vs partial agonist THC) → much more potent.
  • Unpredictable toxicity; product composition variable.
  • Features: agitation, hallucinations, paranoia, hyperthermia, severe rhabdomyolysis, AKI, seizures, MI, ischaemic stroke, sudden death.
  • Standard urine THC may be NEGATIVE — different chemical structure.

TREATMENT

Outline management.
Show structured answer
  • Supportive care.
  • Benzodiazepines for agitation, seizures.
  • Antipsychotics judiciously for severe psychosis.
  • Aggressive cooling for hyperthermia.
  • Rhabdomyolysis management: IV fluids, urinary alkalinisation.
  • Cardiac monitoring.
  • ICU for severe presentations.
  • Psychiatric / substance use counselling.
SAQ 284 — MELAS — L-Arginine in Acute Stroke-Like Episode HARD
A child with MELAS develops acute stroke-like episode with cortical visual changes.

TREATMENT

Outline acute and chronic L-arginine therapy.
Show structured answer
Acute stroke-like episode:
  • IV L-ARGININE 0.5 g/kg bolus over 30–60 min.
  • Then 0.5 g/kg/day continuous infusion × 3–5 days.

Rationale:
  • L-arginine is precursor to nitric oxide.
  • MELAS patients have impaired NO bioavailability → microvascular endothelial dysfunction.
  • L-arginine improves cerebral blood flow during stroke-like episodes.

Chronic prevention:
  • Oral L-arginine + citrulline.
  • CoQ10, riboflavin, thiamine.

Seizure management:
  • Levetiracetam preferred.
  • AVOID VALPROATE (mitotoxic — fulminant hepatic failure in POLG; carnitine depletion).

Avoid mitotoxic drugs: statins, aminoglycosides, linezolid.
SAQ 285 — Niemann-Pick C — Miglustat MODERATE
A child with NPC and progressive ataxia / dystonia.

TREATMENT

Outline miglustat therapy.
Show structured answer
  • MIGLUSTAT (Zavesca) — substrate reduction therapy.
  • Inhibits glucosylceramide synthase → reduces glycosphingolipid accumulation.
  • Approved for NPC neurological manifestations in Europe and many countries (off-label in US).
  • Slows neurological progression in children and adults.
  • Doesn't reverse established damage.
  • Side effects: diarrhoea, weight loss, tremor.

Other therapies:
  • HPβCD (hydroxypropyl-β-cyclodextrin) — investigational; intrathecal.
  • Arimoclomol — HSP induction; in trials.
  • Supportive care: seizures, dystonia, nutrition.
  • Multidisciplinary; family genetic counselling (AR, 25% recurrence).
SAQ 286 — Anti-LGI1 Encephalitis — FBDS MODERATE
An adolescent with memory loss, brief unilateral dystonic jerks of face/arm, and hyponatraemia.

DX

Diagnosis.
Show structured answer
Anti-LGI1 (Leucine-rich glioma-inactivated 1) encephalitis:
  • Antibodies target LGI1, part of VGKC complex.
  • Faciobrachial dystonic seizures (FBDS) — brief seconds-long unilateral dystonic jerks of face and arm; pathognomonic; often precede limbic encephalitis by weeks-months.
  • Limbic encephalitis: memory loss, behavioural change, temporal seizures.
  • Hyponatraemia from SIADH common.
  • MRI: medial temporal T2/FLAIR hyperintensity (similar to HSV but bilateral and without haemorrhage).

TREATMENT

Outline therapy.
Show structured answer
  • First-line: high-dose IV methylprednisolone + IVIG + plasma exchange.
  • Second-line: rituximab, cyclophosphamide.
  • FBDS often respond DRAMATICALLY to immunotherapy alone.
  • AEDs help suppress FBDS but disease itself is autoimmune.
  • Long-term immunotherapy maintenance.
  • Tumour screen (thymoma, SCLC in adults; less common in pediatrics).
SAQ 287 — ICANS — ASTCT Grading HARD
A child post-CAR-T develops neurotoxicity.

CRITERIA

Outline ASTCT 2019 ICANS grading.
Show structured answer
ASTCT consensus ICANS grading — based on worst of:
  • ICE score (10-point cognitive assessment): orientation, naming, following commands, writing, attention.
  • Level of consciousness.
  • Seizures.
  • Motor findings.
  • Elevated ICP / cerebral oedema.

Grades:
  • Grade 1: ICE 7–9; awake.
  • Grade 2: ICE 3–6; awakens to voice.
  • Grade 3: ICE 0–2; awakens only to tactile; clinical seizure resolves; focal oedema on imaging.
  • Grade 4: unarousable; refractory seizures / status; motor weakness; diffuse oedema; Cushing's triad.

TREATMENT

Outline management.
Show structured answer
  • ICU admission for grade ≥ 2.
  • Aspiration precautions; cEEG monitoring.
  • Corticosteroids:
    • Grade 2: oral dexamethasone or IV.
    • Grade 3: dexamethasone 10–20 mg q6h.
    • Grade 4: methylprednisolone 1 g/day × 3 days.
  • Tocilizumab if concurrent CRS — doesn't cross BBB well so direct effect on ICANS limited.
  • Anakinra for refractory ICANS.
  • Seizure control with levetiracetam.
  • ICP management for grade 4.
SAQ 288 — Hashimoto Encephalopathy / SREAT MODERATE
A patient with autoimmune thyroiditis presents with subacute encephalopathy, myoclonus, seizures.

DX

Diagnosis.
Show structured answer
Hashimoto encephalopathy (SREAT — Steroid-Responsive Encephalopathy Associated with Autoimmune Thyroiditis):
  • Autoimmune encephalopathy in patients with anti-thyroid antibodies (anti-TPO, anti-thyroglobulin).
  • Features: subacute cognitive decline, behavioural change, seizures, myoclonus, stroke-like episodes, ataxia, tremor.
  • Mechanism uncertain — likely autoimmune cerebral vasculopathy / encephalopathy associated with autoimmune thyroid disease (not necessarily thyroid dysfunction itself).
  • Thyroid function often normal or only mildly abnormal.
  • CSF: mildly elevated protein in some.
  • MRI usually normal.

TREATMENT

Outline therapy.
Show structured answer
  • High-dose corticosteroids — usually dramatic response (hence 'steroid-responsive').
  • IV methylprednisolone followed by oral prednisone taper.
  • Relapse common — slow taper.
  • Steroid-sparing agents for chronic / refractory: methotrexate, azathioprine, IVIG.
  • Thyroid hormone replacement if dysfunction.
  • AEDs for seizures.
  • Rule out other autoimmune encephalitides (NMDAR, LGI1, etc.) in parallel.
SAQ 289 — Anti-AMPA Encephalitis HARD
A young patient with rare limbic encephalitis and anti-AMPA antibody positivity.

DX

Outline diagnosis.
Show structured answer
Anti-AMPA receptor encephalitis:
  • Antibodies target GluR1/2 subunits of AMPA receptor.
  • Limbic encephalitis: memory loss, behavioural change, seizures, psychosis.
  • Often paraneoplastic in adults (thymoma, breast, lung); less common pediatric paraneoplastic association.
  • Rare in pediatric population.

TREATMENT

Outline therapy.
Show structured answer
  • First-line: high-dose corticosteroids + IVIG + plasma exchange.
  • Second-line: rituximab + cyclophosphamide.
  • Tumour screen (in adults — thymoma, breast, lung; less yield pediatrics but should still screen).
  • Long-term immunotherapy.
  • Multidisciplinary.
SAQ 290 — Susac Syndrome HARD
A teenager with subacute encephalopathy, branch retinal artery occlusion, sensorineural hearing loss.

DX

Diagnosis.
Show structured answer
Susac syndrome:
  • Autoimmune microangiopathy / endotheliopathy of brain, retina, cochlea.
  • Classic triad (often not all simultaneously present):
    • Encephalopathy with cognitive change, headache, behavioural symptoms.
    • Branch retinal artery occlusion(s) — found on fundoscopy / fluorescein angiography.
    • Sensorineural hearing loss (often low-frequency).
  • MRI: characteristic 'snowball' lesions in corpus callosum (linear, central) — pathognomonic in right clinical context.
  • F > M; young adults typically; rare in pediatrics.

TREATMENT

Outline therapy.
Show structured answer
  • High-dose IV methylprednisolone × 3–5 days followed by oral taper.
  • IVIG.
  • Long-term immunosuppression: rituximab, mycophenolate, cyclophosphamide.
  • Antiplatelet (aspirin) for microvascular protection.
  • Multidisciplinary: neurology, ophthalmology, audiology, rehabilitation.
  • Long-term immunotherapy 18–24 months minimum.
SAQ 291 — Behcet Disease — Neurologic Manifestations MODERATE
A teenager with recurrent oral aphthae, genital ulcers, uveitis, and now brainstem syndrome.

DX

Outline neuro-Behcet.
Show structured answer
Neuro-Behcet disease:
  • Behcet = relapsing multi-system vasculitis; HLA-B51 association.
  • Mucocutaneous: oral aphthae, genital ulcers; uveitis; skin lesions.
  • Neurologic involvement in 5–10%:
    • Parenchymal (more common in pediatric): brainstem (most), basal ganglia, cerebrum, spinal cord; subacute onset.
    • Non-parenchymal: cerebral venous sinus thrombosis, intracranial hypertension.
  • MRI parenchymal disease: brainstem T2 hyperintensity, sometimes with oedema; may extend to thalamus / basal ganglia.

TREATMENT

Outline therapy.
Show structured answer
  • Acute parenchymal Behcet: high-dose corticosteroids.
  • Steroid-sparing: azathioprine, mycophenolate, anti-TNF (infliximab, adalimumab).
  • Colchicine for mucocutaneous features.
  • Long-term immunosuppression.
  • AVOID cyclosporine in neuro-Behcet (may worsen).
  • For CVST: anticoagulation in addition to immunosuppression.
  • Multidisciplinary: rheumatology, neurology, ophthalmology.
SAQ 292 — Wilson Disease — Penicillamine Risk MODERATE
A teenager with neurologic Wilson disease started on D-penicillamine experiences worsening neurological symptoms.

PHYSIOLOGY

Outline the paradoxical worsening.
Show structured answer
  • D-penicillamine is a strong copper chelator.
  • Initial mobilisation of copper from peripheral stores → transient INCREASE in CNS copper as it transits → worsening of neurological symptoms in 10–50% of patients.
  • Can occur weeks-months after starting treatment.
  • Some recover, others have persistent worsening.

TREATMENT

Approach.
Show structured answer
  • Use TRIENTINE as initial chelator in NEUROLOGIC Wilson — lower risk of paradoxical worsening.
  • If penicillamine used: START LOW (250 mg/day) and titrate slowly.
  • Consider ZINC as adjunctive maintenance.
  • Monitor closely; reduce dose or switch if worsening.
  • Low-copper diet.
  • Lifelong therapy.
  • Family cascade testing (AR, 25% recurrence).
SAQ 293 — Acute Liver Failure — King's College Criteria HARD
A teenager with paracetamol overdose and progressive liver dysfunction.

CRITERIA

Outline King's College criteria.
Show structured answer
King's College Hospital criteria for transplantation in fulminant hepatic failure:
Paracetamol:
  • Arterial pH < 7.30 (after resuscitation), OR
  • All three of: INR > 6.5 (PT > 100 sec), creatinine > 300 µmol/L (3.4 mg/dL), grade III/IV encephalopathy.

Non-paracetamol:
  • INR > 6.5 alone, OR
  • Three of: INR > 3.5; age < 10 or > 40; jaundice-to-encephalopathy > 7 days; bilirubin > 300 µmol/L; non-A non-B hepatitis or drug reaction.

Pediatric adaptation:
  • Pediatric End-stage Liver Disease (PELD) score for < 12 years.
  • Various paediatric-specific scoring systems used.

TREATMENT

Outline ICU and ICP management.
Show structured answer
  • NAC for paracetamol (early).
  • ICP management: HOB up, hyperosmolar (3% saline targeting Na 145–155), mannitol if applicable.
  • Normothermia / mild hypothermia controversial.
  • Ammonia management: lactulose, rifaximin, CRRT with high-flux filter for severe hyperammonaemia.
  • Coagulopathy: vitamin K, FFP for active bleeding; AVOID prophylactic FFP (confounds prognostic markers).
  • Glycaemic control.
  • Treat infection — high risk.
  • Emergent liver transplant evaluation.
  • Multidisciplinary: hepatology, transplant, ICU, neurology.
SAQ 294 — Cerebral Microdialysis — Mitochondrial vs Ischaemia HARD
Distinguishing patterns on cerebral microdialysis.

PHYSIOLOGY

Outline distinguishing patterns.
Show structured answer
Cerebral microdialysis patterns:
  • NORMAL: L/P < 25, glucose > 1.5 mmol/L, glutamate normal, glycerol normal.
  • ISCHAEMIA: L/P > 40, LOW glucose, elevated glutamate, elevated glycerol. Reflects inadequate substrate delivery and energy crisis from hypoperfusion.
  • MITOCHONDRIAL DYSFUNCTION (without ischaemia): L/P > 40, NORMAL or HIGH glucose, ± elevated glutamate. Tissue has adequate glucose but cannot oxidise pyruvate.
  • HYPERGLYCOLYSIS: elevated lactate and pyruvate proportionally (L/P normal), often with neuronal hyperactivity (seizures, stress).
  • SAMPLING ERROR or technical issues: abrupt changes inconsistent with clinical picture; check probe, flow rate.

TREATMENT

Outline interventions for each.
Show structured answer
Ischaemia pattern:
  • Optimise CPP (raise MAP toward CPPopt).
  • Reduce CMRO₂ (deeper sedation, cool, treat seizures).
  • Optimise oxygen delivery (Hb, SpO₂).
  • Normocapnia.
  • Consider ICP escalation.

Mitochondrial dysfunction:
  • Optimise oxygen delivery.
  • Reduce metabolic demand.
  • Generally less responsive to CPP optimisation.
  • Address underlying (toxic, hypoxic, septic).
SAQ 295 — Glycine Encephalopathy / NKH — Treatment Approach HARD
A neonate with non-ketotic hyperglycinemia.

TREATMENT

Outline therapeutic approach.
Show structured answer
No curative therapy; supportive care.
  • Sodium benzoate 250–750 mg/kg/day — conjugates glycine for renal excretion as hippurate; lowers plasma glycine, less effect on CSF glycine.
  • NMDA antagonists: dextromethorphan (5–15 mg/kg/day), ketamine — reduce excitotoxicity (glycine is NMDA receptor co-agonist).
  • Felbamate (anti-NMDA mechanism) used by some centres.
  • Ketogenic diet emerging.
  • AEDs for seizures (often refractory).
  • Respiratory support — recurrent apnoea common.
  • Nutrition (NG/PEG).

Prognosis:
  • Classic neonatal form: poor — most die in infancy or have severe disability.
  • Attenuated form (later-onset, milder mutations): better.

Genetic counselling (AR, 25% recurrence in carrier parents; sibling testing).
SAQ 296 — Pediatric Decompressive Craniectomy HARD
A child with refractory ICP after severe TBI.

TREATMENT

Outline indications and approach.
Show structured answer
Indications:
  • Refractory ICP > 20–25 mm Hg despite maximal medical management.
  • Imaging evidence of mass effect / midline shift with surgical lesion → primary craniectomy.
  • Diffuse cerebral oedema with effaced cisterns.
  • Salvageable brain (no irreversible global injury, intact brainstem reflexes).
  • Performed within 6–24 hours of refractory ICP (timing controversial).

Technique:
  • LARGE bone flap (12–15 cm or larger) — small flaps inadequate.
  • Bilateral fronto-temporo-parietal for diffuse swelling.
  • Unilateral with duraplasty for hemispheric swelling.
  • Open dura with expansion duraplasty.
  • Bone flap preserved (subcutaneous pouch or frozen) for delayed cranioplasty.

Post-op:
  • Continued ICP monitoring.
  • Watch for: external cerebral herniation, hygromas, hydrocephalus.
  • Helmet protection while defect present.
  • Cranioplasty at 6 weeks – 6 months.

Pediatric evidence (Taylor 2001, Adelson) generally favours DC more strongly than adult RCTs (DECRA / RescueICP).
SAQ 297 — Hypothermia in Pediatric TBI — Cool Kids Trial HARD
Reviewing evidence for hypothermia in pediatric TBI.

CRITERIA

Outline Cool Kids and related trials.
Show structured answer
Cool Kids Trial (Hutchison et al. 2013):
  • Multicentre RCT in children with severe TBI.
  • Compared therapeutic hypothermia 32–34 °C × 24 h vs normothermia.
  • Result: NO benefit; trend toward INCREASED MORTALITY with hypothermia.
  • Stopped early.

Earlier Hutchison 2008 trial: Similar findings — no benefit, signal of harm.

Adelson HypoT trial (2005): Phase II — initial promise but didn't translate to definitive benefit.

Implications:
  • PROPHYLACTIC therapeutic hypothermia is NOT recommended in pediatric severe TBI per BTF 2019.
  • Targeted hypothermia 32–34 °C remains an OPTION for REFRACTORY intracranial hypertension (Tier 3).
  • If used: slow rewarming (≤ 0.5 °C/hour) — rapid rewarming caused much of harm in Cool Kids.

TREATMENT

Outline current recommendation.
Show structured answer
  • BTF 2019 recommends NORMOTHERMIA in initial management.
  • Hypothermia 32–34 °C is an option for refractory ICP only (after Tier 1-2 fail).
  • If hypothermia used: target 32–34 °C; duration 24–48 h; slow rewarming (≤ 0.5 °C/hour or less).
  • Surveillance: arrhythmia, infection, coagulopathy, electrolyte derangement.
  • Avoid hyperthermia at all costs.
  • For HIE (neonatal): hypothermia is established benefit — different evidence base.
SAQ 298 — Pediatric ICP Devices Comparison MODERATE
Choosing an ICP monitoring device.

DEFINITION

Outline ICP monitoring devices.
Show structured answer
Available devices:
  • External Ventricular Drain (EVD):
    • Gold standard — measures global ICP from CSF in ventricle.
    • THERAPEUTIC — allows CSF drainage.
    • Can be re-zeroed.
    • Risks: infection (5–15%), haemorrhage on insertion (~ 5%), malfunction.
    • Preferred when CSF drainage may be needed.
  • Intraparenchymal fiberoptic monitor (Camino, Codman):
    • Inserted into parenchyma (frontal lobe usually).
    • Measures local ICP — may differ from contralateral hemisphere or ventricular ICP if compartmentalisation.
    • Cannot be re-zeroed after insertion (drift).
    • Lower infection risk than EVD.
    • No CSF drainage capacity.
  • Subdural / epidural monitors: less accurate; rarely used.

NEXT STEP

When to use each.
Show structured answer
  • EVD preferred when:
    • Hydrocephalus or risk of hydrocephalus.
    • Need for CSF drainage as therapy.
    • Need ability to re-zero.
    • Most pediatric TBI cases benefit from EVD.
  • Intraparenchymal preferred when:
    • Slit ventricles (cannot cannulate).
    • Coagulopathy (smaller insertion track).
    • Brief monitoring expected.
    • EVD not feasible (anatomic).
  • Multimodal monitoring: often combine ICP with PbtO₂, microdialysis.
  • Catheter care: aseptic, minimise opening, regular infection surveillance.
  • Remove ICP monitor when no longer needed; CT before removal to assess for hydrocephalus.
SAQ 299 — PALICC vs Adult ARDS Definitions MODERATE
Distinguishing pediatric and adult ARDS criteria.

DEFINITION

Outline key differences.
Show structured answer
PALICC-2 (2023, pediatric)Berlin (2012, adult)
TimingWithin 7 daysWithin 1 week
ImagingAny new infiltrateBilateral opacities (CXR / CT)
CauseNot fully explained by cardiac / fluid overloadNot fully explained by cardiac failure / fluid overload
OxygenationOI (invasive) OR OSI (NIV); SpO₂/FiO₂ acceptablePaO₂/FiO₂ only (PEEP ≥ 5)
SeverityMild OI 4–8; Moderate 8–16; Severe ≥ 16Mild P/F 200–300; Moderate 100–200; Severe < 100
NIV inclusionYes (CPAP/BiPAP)Mild only
Special groupsCyanotic CHD, chronic lung disease, immunocompromised stratifiedNone

PHYSIOLOGY

Why does pediatric ARDS need its own definition?
Show structured answer
  • Children have different respiratory physiology, different aetiology spectrum, different outcomes than adults.
  • Berlin criteria require arterial blood gas — frequently unavailable in pediatrics (less arterial access).
  • PALICC allows SpO₂-based criteria (more practical).
  • OI (oxygenation index) accounts for ventilator MAP — better severity stratification for pediatrics.
  • Special populations (CHD, chronic lung disease, immunocompromised) common in pediatric ICU.
  • PALICC-2 (2023) refined PALICC-1 (2015) with more granular guidance and additional populations.
SAQ 300 — Comprehensive Pediatric Brain Death Protocol HARD
Outline the comprehensive pediatric brain-death determination protocol per AAP / CNS / SCCM 2011.

CRITERIA

Outline the full pediatric protocol.
Show structured answer
PRE-REQUISITES (all must be met):
  1. Established cause sufficient to explain irreversible loss of all brain function.
  2. Confounders eliminated:
    • Hypotension (MAP appropriate for age).
    • Hypothermia (core T ≥ 35 °C, preferably 36).
    • Severe metabolic derangement.
    • CNS depressant drugs cleared.
    • Neuromuscular blockade reversed (TOF 4/4).
  3. Observation period:
    • Term newborn (37 weeks) – 30 days: 24 hours.
    • > 30 days – 18 years: 12 hours.

EXAMINATION (× 2 by different attending physicians):
  • Coma — no response to noxious stimulation.
  • Absence of brainstem reflexes:
    • Pupillary (fixed, dilated or mid-position).
    • Corneal.
    • Oculocephalic (doll's eye).
    • Oculovestibular (cold caloric — < 50 mL ice water in each ear).
    • Gag and cough.
  • Absence of motor response (no spontaneous movement; no response to noxious stimulus; spinal reflexes acceptable).
  • Apnoea test (× each exam).

APNOEA TEST:
  • Pre-test: T ≥ 35 °C, SBP appropriate, baseline PaCO₂ 35–45, PaO₂ > 200 on FiO₂ 1.0 × 10 min.
  • Disconnect from ventilator, apnoeic O₂ via T-piece.
  • Observe 5–10 min.
  • POSITIVE: no respiratory effort AND PaCO₂ ≥ 60 AND ≥ 20 above baseline, pH usually < 7.30.
  • Abort if SBP drops > 20%, SpO₂ < 85%, arrhythmia.

ANCILLARY TESTS when apnoea cannot be safely completed or confounders persist:
  • Radionuclide cerebral perfusion scintigraphy (Tc-99m HMPAO).
  • Catheter cerebral angiography.
  • CTA (criteria still refining in pediatrics).
  • EEG NOT recommended as sole ancillary.

DEFINITION

Outline what brain death is — and what it is NOT.
Show structured answer
Brain death = death by neurologic criteria:
  • Irreversible loss of ALL functions of the entire brain, including brainstem.
  • Legally equivalent to cardiopulmonary death in most jurisdictions.
  • Brain death = death — declaring it ends life support after brain-death determination is not 'withdrawal of life support' from a living patient.

Brain death is NOT:
  • Coma.
  • Vegetative state / unresponsive wakefulness syndrome — preserved brainstem function with no awareness.
  • Minimally conscious state — inconsistent awareness.
  • Locked-in syndrome — preserved consciousness with limited motor output.
  • Hypothermia, severe sedation, or metabolic encephalopathy — must be reversed before determination.

Family communication: Multidisciplinary, sensitive, culturally appropriate; involve palliative care; consider donor co-ordination services if family considers organ donation.

Document meticulously; institutional protocols and physician credentialing required.
